164 "TWENTY THOUSAND LEAGUES UNDER THE SEA"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "by"
164 ""
164 "JULES VERNE"
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "PART ONE"
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER I"
164 ""
164 "A SHIFTING REEF"
164 ""
164 "The year 1866 was signalised by a remarkable incident, a mysterious and"
164 "puzzling phenomenon, which doubtless no one has yet forgotten.  Not to"
164 "mention rumours which agitated the maritime population and excited the"
164 "public mind, even in the interior of continents, seafaring men were"
164 "particularly excited.  Merchants, common sailors, captains of vessels,"
164 "skippers, both of Europe and America, naval officers of all countries,"
164 "and the Governments of several States on the two continents, were"
164 "deeply interested in the matter."
164 ""
164 "For some time past vessels had been met by \"an enormous thing,\" a long"
164 "object, spindle-shaped, occasionally phosphorescent, and infinitely"
164 "larger and more rapid in its movements than a whale."
164 ""
164 "The facts relating to this apparition (entered in various log-books)"
164 "agreed in most respects as to the shape of the object or creature in"
164 "question, the untiring rapidity of its movements, its surprising power"
164 "of locomotion, and the peculiar life with which it seemed endowed.  If"
164 "it was a whale, it surpassed in size all those hitherto classified in"
164 "science.  Taking into consideration the mean of observations made at"
164 "divers times--rejecting the timid estimate of those who assigned to"
164 "this object a length of two hundred feet, equally with the exaggerated"
164 "opinions which set it down as a mile in width and three in length--we"
164 "might fairly conclude that this mysterious being surpassed greatly all"
164 "dimensions admitted by the learned ones of the day, if it existed at"
164 "all.  And that it DID exist was an undeniable fact; and, with that"
164 "tendency which disposes the human mind in favour of the marvellous, we"
164 "can understand the excitement produced in the entire world by this"
164 "supernatural apparition.  As to classing it in the list of fables, the"
164 "idea was out of the question."
164 ""
164 "On the 20th of July, 1866, the steamer Governor Higginson, of the"
164 "Calcutta and Burnach Steam Navigation Company, had met this moving mass"
164 "five miles off the east coast of Australia.  Captain Baker thought at"
164 "first that he was in the presence of an unknown sandbank; he even"
164 "prepared to determine its exact position when two columns of water,"
164 "projected by the mysterious object, shot with a hissing noise a hundred"
164 "and fifty feet up into the air.  Now, unless the sandbank had been"
164 "submitted to the intermittent eruption of a geyser, the Governor"
164 "Higginson had to do neither more nor less than with an aquatic mammal,"
164 "unknown till then, which threw up from its blow-holes columns of water"
164 "mixed with air and vapour."
164 ""
164 "Similar facts were observed on the 23rd of July in the same year, in"
164 "the Pacific Ocean, by the Columbus, of the West India and Pacific Steam"
164 "Navigation Company.  But this extraordinary creature could transport"
164 "itself from one place to another with surprising velocity; as, in an"
164 "interval of three days, the Governor Higginson and the Columbus had"
164 "observed it at two different points of the chart, separated by a"
164 "distance of more than seven hundred nautical leagues."
164 ""
164 "Fifteen days later, two thousand miles farther off, the Helvetia, of"
164 "the Compagnie-Nationale, and the Shannon, of the Royal Mail Steamship"
164 "Company, sailing to windward in that portion of the Atlantic lying"
164 "between the United States and Europe, respectively signalled the"
164 "monster to each other in 42 deg. 15' N. lat. and 60 deg. 35' W. long."
164 "In these simultaneous observations they thought themselves justified in"
164 "estimating the minimum length of the mammal at more than three hundred"
164 "and fifty feet, as the Shannon and Helvetia were of smaller dimensions"
164 "than it, though they measured three hundred feet over all."
164 ""
164 "Now the largest whales, those which frequent those parts of the sea"
164 "round the Aleutian, Kulammak, and Umgullich islands, have never"
164 "exceeded the length of sixty yards, if they attain that."
164 ""
164 "In every place of great resort the monster was the fashion.  They sang"
164 "of it in the cafes, ridiculed it in the papers, and represented it on"
164 "the stage.  All kinds of stories were circulated regarding it.  There"
164 "appeared in the papers caricatures of every gigantic and imaginary"
164 "creature, from the white whale, the terrible \"Moby Dick\" of sub-arctic"
164 "regions, to the immense kraken, whose tentacles could entangle a ship"
164 "of five hundred tons and hurry it into the abyss of the ocean.  The"
164 "legends of ancient times were even revived."
164 ""
164 "Then burst forth the unending argument between the believers and the"
164 "unbelievers in the societies of the wise and the scientific journals."
164 "\"The question of the monster\" inflamed all minds.  Editors of"
164 "scientific journals, quarrelling with believers in the supernatural,"
164 "spilled seas of ink during this memorable campaign, some even drawing"
164 "blood; for from the sea-serpent they came to direct personalities."
164 ""
164 "During the first months of the year 1867 the question seemed buried,"
164 "never to revive, when new facts were brought before the public.  It was"
164 "then no longer a scientific problem to be solved, but a real danger"
164 "seriously to be avoided.  The question took quite another shape.  The"
164 "monster became a small island, a rock, a reef, but a reef of indefinite"
164 "and shifting proportions."
164 ""
164 "On the 5th of March, 1867, the Moravian, of the Montreal Ocean Company,"
164 "finding herself during the night in 27 deg. 30' lat. and 72 deg. 15'"
164 "long., struck on her starboard quarter a rock, marked in no chart for"
164 "that part of the sea.  Under the combined efforts of the wind and its"
164 "four hundred horse power, it was going at the rate of thirteen knots."
164 "Had it not been for the superior strength of the hull of the Moravian,"
164 "she would have been broken by the shock and gone down with the 237"
164 "passengers she was bringing home from Canada."
164 ""
164 "The accident happened about five o'clock in the morning, as the day was"
164 "breaking.  The officers of the quarter-deck hurried to the after-part"
164 "of the vessel.  They examined the sea with the most careful attention."
164 "They saw nothing but a strong eddy about three cables' length distant,"
164 "as if the surface had been violently agitated.  The bearings of the"
164 "place were taken exactly, and the Moravian continued its route without"
164 "apparent damage.  Had it struck on a submerged rock, or on an enormous"
164 "wreck?  They could not tell; but, on examination of the ship's bottom"
164 "when undergoing repairs, it was found that part of her keel was broken."
164 ""
164 "This fact, so grave in itself, might perhaps have been forgotten like"
164 "many others if, three weeks after, it had not been re-enacted under"
164 "similar circumstances.  But, thanks to the nationality of the victim of"
164 "the shock, thanks to the reputation of the company to which the vessel"
164 "belonged, the circumstance became extensively circulated."
164 ""
164 "The 13th of April, 1867, the sea being beautiful, the breeze"
164 "favourable, the Scotia, of the Cunard Company's line, found herself in"
164 "15 deg. 12' long. and 45 deg. 37' lat.  She was going at the speed of"
164 "thirteen knots and a half."
164 ""
164 "At seventeen minutes past four in the afternoon, whilst the passengers"
164 "were assembled at lunch in the great saloon, a slight shock was felt on"
164 "the hull of the Scotia, on her quarter, a little aft of the port-paddle."
164 ""
164 "The Scotia had not struck, but she had been struck, and seemingly by"
164 "something rather sharp and penetrating than blunt.  The shock had been"
164 "so slight that no one had been alarmed, had it not been for the shouts"
164 "of the carpenter's watch, who rushed on to the bridge, exclaiming, \"We"
164 "are sinking! we are sinking!\"  At first the passengers were much"
164 "frightened, but Captain Anderson hastened to reassure them.  The danger"
164 "could not be imminent.  The Scotia, divided into seven compartments by"
164 "strong partitions, could brave with impunity any leak.  Captain"
164 "Anderson went down immediately into the hold.  He found that the sea"
164 "was pouring into the fifth compartment; and the rapidity of the influx"
164 "proved that the force of the water was considerable.  Fortunately this"
164 "compartment did not hold the boilers, or the fires would have been"
164 "immediately extinguished.  Captain Anderson ordered the engines to be"
164 "stopped at once, and one of the men went down to ascertain the extent"
164 "of the injury.  Some minutes afterwards they discovered the existence"
164 "of a large hole, two yards in diameter, in the ship's bottom.  Such a"
164 "leak could not be stopped; and the Scotia, her paddles half submerged,"
164 "was obliged to continue her course.  She was then three hundred miles"
164 "from Cape Clear, and, after three days' delay, which caused great"
164 "uneasiness in Liverpool, she entered the basin of the company."
164 ""
164 "The engineers visited the Scotia, which was put in dry dock.  They"
164 "could scarcely believe it possible; at two yards and a half below"
164 "water-mark was a regular rent, in the form of an isosceles triangle."
164 "The broken place in the iron plates was so perfectly defined that it"
164 "could not have been more neatly done by a punch.  It was clear, then,"
164 "that the instrument producing the perforation was not of a common stamp"
164 "and, after having been driven with prodigious strength, and piercing an"
164 "iron plate 1 3/8 inches thick, had withdrawn itself by a backward"
164 "motion."
164 ""
164 "Such was the last fact, which resulted in exciting once more the"
164 "torrent of public opinion.  From this moment all unlucky casualties"
164 "which could not be otherwise accounted for were put down to the monster."
164 ""
164 "Upon this imaginary creature rested the responsibility of all these"
164 "shipwrecks, which unfortunately were considerable; for of three"
164 "thousand ships whose loss was annually recorded at Lloyd's, the number"
164 "of sailing and steam-ships supposed to be totally lost, from the"
164 "absence of all news, amounted to not less than two hundred!"
164 ""
164 "Now, it was the \"monster\" who, justly or unjustly, was accused of their"
164 "disappearance, and, thanks to it, communication between the different"
164 "continents became more and more dangerous.  The public demanded sharply"
164 "that the seas should at any price be relieved from this formidable"
164 "cetacean.[1]"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "[1] Member of the whale family."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER II"
164 ""
164 "PRO AND CON"
164 ""
164 "At the period when these events took place, I had just returned from a"
164 "scientific research in the disagreeable territory of Nebraska, in the"
164 "United States.  In virtue of my office as Assistant Professor in the"
164 "Museum of Natural History in Paris, the French Government had attached"
164 "me to that expedition.  After six months in Nebraska, I arrived in New"
164 "York towards the end of March, laden with a precious collection.  My"
164 "departure for France was fixed for the first days in May.  Meanwhile I"
164 "was occupying myself in classifying my mineralogical, botanical, and"
164 "zoological riches, when the accident happened to the Scotia."
164 ""
164 "I was perfectly up in the subject which was the question of the day."
164 "How could I be otherwise?  I had read and reread all the American and"
164 "European papers without being any nearer a conclusion.  This mystery"
164 "puzzled me.  Under the impossibility of forming an opinion, I jumped"
164 "from one extreme to the other.  That there really was something could"
164 "not be doubted, and the incredulous were invited to put their finger on"
164 "the wound of the Scotia."
164 ""
164 "On my arrival at New York the question was at its height.  The theory"
164 "of the floating island, and the unapproachable sandbank, supported by"
164 "minds little competent to form a judgment, was abandoned.  And, indeed,"
164 "unless this shoal had a machine in its stomach, how could it change its"
164 "position with such astonishing rapidity?"
164 ""
164 "From the same cause, the idea of a floating hull of an enormous wreck"
164 "was given up."
164 ""
164 "There remained, then, only two possible solutions of the question,"
164 "which created two distinct parties:  on one side, those who were for a"
164 "monster of colossal strength; on the other, those who were for a"
164 "submarine vessel of enormous motive power."
164 ""
164 "But this last theory, plausible as it was, could not stand against"
164 "inquiries made in both worlds.  That a private gentleman should have"
164 "such a machine at his command was not likely.  Where, when, and how was"
164 "it built? and how could its construction have been kept secret?"
164 "Certainly a Government might possess such a destructive machine.  And"
164 "in these disastrous times, when the ingenuity of man has multiplied the"
164 "power of weapons of war, it was possible that, without the knowledge of"
164 "others, a State might try to work such a formidable engine."
164 ""
164 "But the idea of a war machine fell before the declaration of"
164 "Governments.  As public interest was in question, and transatlantic"
164 "communications suffered, their veracity could not be doubted.  But how"
164 "admit that the construction of this submarine boat had escaped the"
164 "public eye?  For a private gentleman to keep the secret under such"
164 "circumstances would be very difficult, and for a State whose every act"
164 "is persistently watched by powerful rivals, certainly impossible."
164 ""
164 "Upon my arrival in New York several persons did me the honour of"
164 "consulting me on the phenomenon in question.  I had published in France"
164 "a work in quarto, in two volumes, entitled Mysteries of the Great"
164 "Submarine Grounds.  This book, highly approved of in the learned world,"
164 "gained for me a special reputation in this rather obscure branch of"
164 "Natural History.  My advice was asked.  As long as I could deny the"
164 "reality of the fact, I confined myself to a decided negative.  But"
164 "soon, finding myself driven into a corner, I was obliged to explain"
164 "myself point by point.  I discussed the question in all its forms,"
164 "politically and scientifically; and I give here an extract from a"
164 "carefully-studied article which I published in the number of the 30th"
164 "of April.  It ran as follows:"
164 ""
164 "\"After examining one by one the different theories, rejecting all other"
164 "suggestions, it becomes necessary to admit the existence of a marine"
164 "animal of enormous power."
164 ""
164 "\"The great depths of the ocean are entirely unknown to us.  Soundings"
164 "cannot reach them.  What passes in those remote depths--what beings"
164 "live, or can live, twelve or fifteen miles beneath the surface of the"
164 "waters--what is the organisation of these animals, we can scarcely"
164 "conjecture.  However, the solution of the problem submitted to me may"
164 "modify the form of the dilemma.  Either we do know all the varieties of"
164 "beings which people our planet, or we do not.  If we do NOT know them"
164 "all--if Nature has still secrets in the deeps for us, nothing is more"
164 "conformable to reason than to admit the existence of fishes, or"
164 "cetaceans of other kinds, or even of new species, of an organisation"
164 "formed to inhabit the strata inaccessible to soundings, and which an"
164 "accident of some sort has brought at long intervals to the upper level"
164 "of the ocean."
164 ""
164 "\"If, on the contrary, we DO know all living kinds, we must necessarily"
164 "seek for the animal in question amongst those marine beings already"
164 "classed; and, in that case, I should be disposed to admit the existence"
164 "of a gigantic narwhal."
164 ""
164 "\"The common narwhal, or unicorn of the sea, often attains a length of"
164 "sixty feet.  Increase its size fivefold or tenfold, give it strength"
164 "proportionate to its size, lengthen its destructive weapons, and you"
164 "obtain the animal required.  It will have the proportions determined by"
164 "the officers of the Shannon, the instrument required by the perforation"
164 "of the Scotia, and the power necessary to pierce the hull of the"
164 "steamer."
164 ""
164 "\"Indeed, the narwhal is armed with a sort of ivory sword, a halberd,"
164 "according to the expression of certain naturalists.  The principal tusk"
164 "has the hardness of steel.  Some of these tusks have been found buried"
164 "in the bodies of whales, which the unicorn always attacks with success."
164 "Others have been drawn out, not without trouble, from the bottoms of"
164 "ships, which they had pierced through and through, as a gimlet pierces"
164 "a barrel.  The Museum of the Faculty of Medicine of Paris possesses one"
164 "of these defensive weapons, two yards and a quarter in length, and"
164 "fifteen inches in diameter at the base."
164 ""
164 "\"Very well! suppose this weapon to be six times stronger and the animal"
164 "ten times more powerful; launch it at the rate of twenty miles an hour,"
164 "and you obtain a shock capable of producing the catastrophe required."
164 "Until further information, therefore, I shall maintain it to be a"
164 "sea-unicorn of colossal dimensions, armed not with a halberd, but with"
164 "a real spur, as the armoured frigates, or the `rams' of war, whose"
164 "massiveness and motive power it would possess at the same time.  Thus"
164 "may this puzzling phenomenon be explained, unless there be something"
164 "over and above all that one has ever conjectured, seen, perceived, or"
164 "experienced; which is just within the bounds of possibility.\""
164 ""
164 "These last words were cowardly on my part; but, up to a certain point,"
164 "I wished to shelter my dignity as professor, and not give too much"
164 "cause for laughter to the Americans, who laugh well when they do laugh."
164 "I reserved for myself a way of escape.  In effect, however, I admitted"
164 "the existence of the \"monster.\" My article was warmly discussed, which"
164 "procured it a high reputation.  It rallied round it a certain number of"
164 "partisans.  The solution it proposed gave, at least, full liberty to"
164 "the imagination.  The human mind delights in grand conceptions of"
164 "supernatural beings.  And the sea is precisely their best vehicle, the"
164 "only medium through which these giants (against which terrestrial"
164 "animals, such as elephants or rhinoceroses, are as nothing) can be"
164 "produced or developed."
164 ""
164 "The industrial and commercial papers treated the question chiefly from"
164 "this point of view.  The Shipping and Mercantile Gazette, the Lloyd's"
164 "List, the Packet-Boat, and the Maritime and Colonial Review, all papers"
164 "devoted to insurance companies which threatened to raise their rates of"
164 "premium, were unanimous on this point.  Public opinion had been"
164 "pronounced.  The United States were the first in the field; and in New"
164 "York they made preparations for an expedition destined to pursue this"
164 "narwhal.  A frigate of great speed, the Abraham Lincoln, was put in"
164 "commission as soon as possible.  The arsenals were opened to Commander"
164 "Farragut, who hastened the arming of his frigate; but, as it always"
164 "happens, the moment it was decided to pursue the monster, the monster"
164 "did not appear.  For two months no one heard it spoken of.  No ship met"
164 "with it.  It seemed as if this unicorn knew of the plots weaving around"
164 "it.  It had been so much talked of, even through the Atlantic cable,"
164 "that jesters pretended that this slender fly had stopped a telegram on"
164 "its passage and was making the most of it."
164 ""
164 "So when the frigate had been armed for a long campaign, and provided"
164 "with formidable fishing apparatus, no one could tell what course to"
164 "pursue.  Impatience grew apace, when, on the 2nd of July, they learned"
164 "that a steamer of the line of San Francisco, from California to"
164 "Shanghai, had seen the animal three weeks before in the North Pacific"
164 "Ocean.  The excitement caused by this news was extreme.  The ship was"
164 "revictualled and well stocked with coal."
164 ""
164 "Three hours before the Abraham Lincoln left Brooklyn pier, I received a"
164 "letter worded as follows:"
164 ""
164 "To M. ARONNAX, Professor in the Museum of Paris, Fifth Avenue Hotel,"
164 "New York."
164 ""
164 "SIR,--If you will consent to join the Abraham Lincoln in this"
164 "expedition, the Government of the United States will with pleasure see"
164 "France represented in the enterprise.  Commander Farragut has a cabin"
164 "at your disposal."
164 ""
164 "Very cordially yours, J.B. HOBSON, Secretary of Marine."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER III"
164 ""
164 "I FORM MY RESOLUTION"
164 ""
164 "Three seconds before the arrival of J. B. Hobson's letter I no more"
164 "thought of pursuing the unicorn than of attempting the passage of the"
164 "North Sea.  Three seconds after reading the letter of the honourable"
164 "Secretary of Marine, I felt that my true vocation, the sole end of my"
164 "life, was to chase this disturbing monster and purge it from the world."
164 ""
164 "But I had just returned from a fatiguing journey, weary and longing for"
164 "repose.  I aspired to nothing more than again seeing my country, my"
164 "friends, my little lodging by the Jardin des Plantes, my dear and"
164 "precious collections--but nothing could keep me back!  I forgot"
164 "all--fatigue, friends and collections--and accepted without hesitation"
164 "the offer of the American Government."
164 ""
164 "\"Besides,\" thought I, \"all roads lead back to Europe; and the unicorn"
164 "may be amiable enough to hurry me towards the coast of France.  This"
164 "worthy animal may allow itself to be caught in the seas of Europe (for"
164 "my particular benefit), and I will not bring back less than half a yard"
164 "of his ivory halberd to the Museum of Natural History.\" But in the"
164 "meanwhile I must seek this narwhal in the North Pacific Ocean, which,"
164 "to return to France, was taking the road to the antipodes."
164 ""
164 "\"Conseil,\" I called in an impatient voice."
164 ""
164 "Conseil was my servant, a true, devoted Flemish boy, who had"
164 "accompanied me in all my travels.  I liked him, and he returned the"
164 "liking well.  He was quiet by nature, regular from principle, zealous"
164 "from habit, evincing little disturbance at the different surprises of"
164 "life, very quick with his hands, and apt at any service required of"
164 "him; and, despite his name, never giving advice--even when asked for it."
164 ""
164 "Conseil had followed me for the last ten years wherever science led."
164 "Never once did he complain of the length or fatigue of a journey, never"
164 "make an objection to pack his portmanteau for whatever country it might"
164 "be, or however far away, whether China or Congo.  Besides all this, he"
164 "had good health, which defied all sickness, and solid muscles, but no"
164 "nerves; good morals are understood.  This boy was thirty years old, and"
164 "his age to that of his master as fifteen to twenty.  May I be excused"
164 "for saying that I was forty years old?"
164 ""
164 "But Conseil had one fault:  he was ceremonious to a degree, and would"
164 "never speak to me but in the third person, which was sometimes"
164 "provoking."
164 ""
164 "\"Conseil,\" said I again, beginning with feverish hands to make"
164 "preparations for my departure."
164 ""
164 "Certainly I was sure of this devoted boy.  As a rule, I never asked him"
164 "if it were convenient for him or not to follow me in my travels; but"
164 "this time the expedition in question might be prolonged, and the"
164 "enterprise might be hazardous in pursuit of an animal capable of"
164 "sinking a frigate as easily as a nutshell.  Here there was matter for"
164 "reflection even to the most impassive man in the world.  What would"
164 "Conseil say?"
164 ""
164 "\"Conseil,\" I called a third time."
164 ""
164 "Conseil appeared."
164 ""
164 "\"Did you call, sir?\" said he, entering."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, my boy; make preparations for me and yourself too.  We leave in"
164 "two hours.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you please, sir,\" replied Conseil, quietly."
164 ""
164 "\"Not an instant to lose; lock in my trunk all travelling utensils,"
164 "coats, shirts, and stockings--without counting, as many as you can, and"
164 "make haste.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And your collections, sir?\" observed Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"They will keep them at the hotel.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We are not returning to Paris, then?\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Oh! certainly,\" I answered, evasively, \"by making a curve.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Will the curve please you, sir?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Oh! it will be nothing; not quite so direct a road, that is all.  We"
164 "take our passage in the Abraham, Lincoln.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you think proper, sir,\" coolly replied Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"You see, my friend, it has to do with the monster--the famous narwhal."
164 "We are going to purge it from the seas.  A glorious mission, but a"
164 "dangerous one!  We cannot tell where we may go; these animals can be"
164 "very capricious.  But we will go whether or no; we have got a captain"
164 "who is pretty wide-awake.\""
164 ""
164 "Our luggage was transported to the deck of the frigate immediately.  I"
164 "hastened on board and asked for Commander Farragut.  One of the sailors"
164 "conducted me to the poop, where I found myself in the presence of a"
164 "good-looking officer, who held out his hand to me."
164 ""
164 "\"Monsieur Pierre Aronnax?\" said he."
164 ""
164 "\"Himself,\" replied I. \"Commander Farragut?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You are welcome, Professor; your cabin is ready for you.\""
164 ""
164 "I bowed, and desired to be conducted to the cabin destined for me."
164 ""
164 "The Abraham Lincoln had been well chosen and equipped for her new"
164 "destination.  She was a frigate of great speed, fitted with"
164 "high-pressure engines which admitted a pressure of seven atmospheres."
164 "Under this the Abraham Lincoln attained the mean speed of nearly"
164 "eighteen knots and a third an hour--a considerable speed, but,"
164 "nevertheless, insufficient to grapple with this gigantic cetacean."
164 ""
164 "The interior arrangements of the frigate corresponded to its nautical"
164 "qualities.  I was well satisfied with my cabin, which was in the after"
164 "part, opening upon the gunroom."
164 ""
164 "\"We shall be well off here,\" said I to Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"As well, by your honour's leave, as a hermit-crab in the shell of a"
164 "whelk,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "I left Conseil to stow our trunks conveniently away, and remounted the"
164 "poop in order to survey the preparations for departure."
164 ""
164 "At that moment Commander Farragut was ordering the last moorings to be"
164 "cast loose which held the Abraham Lincoln to the pier of Brooklyn.  So"
164 "in a quarter of an hour, perhaps less, the frigate would have sailed"
164 "without me.  I should have missed this extraordinary, supernatural, and"
164 "incredible expedition, the recital of which may well meet with some"
164 "suspicion."
164 ""
164 "But Commander Farragut would not lose a day nor an hour in scouring the"
164 "seas in which the animal had been sighted.  He sent for the engineer."
164 ""
164 "\"Is the steam full on?\" asked he."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir,\" replied the engineer."
164 ""
164 "\"Go ahead,\" cried Commander Farragut."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER IV"
164 ""
164 "NED LAND"
164 ""
164 "Captain Farragut was a good seaman, worthy of the frigate he commanded."
164 "His vessel and he were one.  He was the soul of it.  On the question of"
164 "the monster there was no doubt in his mind, and he would not allow the"
164 "existence of the animal to be disputed on board.  He believed in it, as"
164 "certain good women believe in the leviathan--by faith, not by reason."
164 "The monster did exist, and he had sworn to rid the seas of it.  Either"
164 "Captain Farragut would kill the narwhal, or the narwhal would kill the"
164 "captain.  There was no third course."
164 ""
164 "The officers on board shared the opinion of their chief.  They were"
164 "ever chatting, discussing, and calculating the various chances of a"
164 "meeting, watching narrowly the vast surface of the ocean.  More than"
164 "one took up his quarters voluntarily in the cross-trees, who would have"
164 "cursed such a berth under any other circumstances.  As long as the sun"
164 "described its daily course, the rigging was crowded with sailors, whose"
164 "feet were burnt to such an extent by the heat of the deck as to render"
164 "it unbearable; still the Abraham Lincoln had not yet breasted the"
164 "suspected waters of the Pacific.  As to the ship's company, they"
164 "desired nothing better than to meet the unicorn, to harpoon it, hoist"
164 "it on board, and despatch it.  They watched the sea with eager"
164 "attention."
164 ""
164 "Besides, Captain Farragut had spoken of a certain sum of two thousand"
164 "dollars, set apart for whoever should first sight the monster, were he"
164 "cabin-boy, common seaman, or officer."
164 ""
164 "I leave you to judge how eyes were used on board the Abraham Lincoln."
164 ""
164 "For my own part I was not behind the others, and, left to no one my"
164 "share of daily observations.  The frigate might have been called the"
164 "Argus, for a hundred reasons.  Only one amongst us, Conseil, seemed to"
164 "protest by his indifference against the question which so interested us"
164 "all, and seemed to be out of keeping with the general enthusiasm on"
164 "board."
164 ""
164 "I have said that Captain Farragut had carefully provided his ship with"
164 "every apparatus for catching the gigantic cetacean.  No whaler had ever"
164 "been better armed.  We possessed every known engine, from the harpoon"
164 "thrown by the hand to the barbed arrows of the blunderbuss, and the"
164 "explosive balls of the duck-gun.  On the forecastle lay the perfection"
164 "of a breech-loading gun, very thick at the breech, and very narrow in"
164 "the bore, the model of which had been in the Exhibition of 1867.  This"
164 "precious weapon of American origin could throw with ease a conical"
164 "projectile of nine pounds to a mean distance of ten miles."
164 ""
164 "Thus the Abraham Lincoln wanted for no means of destruction; and, what"
164 "was better still she had on board Ned Land, the prince of harpooners."
164 ""
164 "Ned Land was a Canadian, with an uncommon quickness of hand, and who"
164 "knew no equal in his dangerous occupation.  Skill, coolness, audacity,"
164 "and cunning he possessed in a superior degree, and it must be a cunning"
164 "whale to escape the stroke of his harpoon."
164 ""
164 "Ned Land was about forty years of age; he was a tall man (more than six"
164 "feet high), strongly built, grave and taciturn, occasionally violent,"
164 "and very passionate when contradicted.  His person attracted attention,"
164 "but above all the boldness of his look, which gave a singular"
164 "expression to his face."
164 ""
164 "Who calls himself Canadian calls himself French; and, little"
164 "communicative as Ned Land was, I must admit that he took a certain"
164 "liking for me.  My nationality drew him to me, no doubt.  It was an"
164 "opportunity for him to talk, and for me to hear, that old language of"
164 "Rabelais, which is still in use in some Canadian provinces.  The"
164 "harpooner's family was originally from Quebec, and was already a tribe"
164 "of hardy fishermen when this town belonged to France."
164 ""
164 "Little by little, Ned Land acquired a taste for chatting, and I loved"
164 "to hear the recital of his adventures in the polar seas.  He related"
164 "his fishing, and his combats, with natural poetry of expression; his"
164 "recital took the form of an epic poem, and I seemed to be listening to"
164 "a Canadian Homer singing the Iliad of the regions of the North."
164 ""
164 "I am portraying this hardy companion as I really knew him.  We are old"
164 "friends now, united in that unchangeable friendship which is born and"
164 "cemented amidst extreme dangers.  Ah, brave Ned!  I ask no more than to"
164 "live a hundred years longer, that I may have more time to dwell the"
164 "longer on your memory."
164 ""
164 "Now, what was Ned Land's opinion upon the question of the marine"
164 "monster?  I must admit that he did not believe in the unicorn, and was"
164 "the only one on board who did not share that universal conviction.  He"
164 "even avoided the subject, which I one day thought it my duty to press"
164 "upon him.  One magnificent evening, the 30th July (that is to say,"
164 "three weeks after our departure), the frigate was abreast of Cape"
164 "Blanc, thirty miles to leeward of the coast of Patagonia.  We had"
164 "crossed the tropic of Capricorn, and the Straits of Magellan opened"
164 "less than seven hundred miles to the south.  Before eight days were"
164 "over the Abraham Lincoln would be ploughing the waters of the Pacific."
164 ""
164 "Seated on the poop, Ned Land and I were chatting of one thing and"
164 "another as we looked at this mysterious sea, whose great depths had up"
164 "to this time been inaccessible to the eye of man.  I naturally led up"
164 "the conversation to the giant unicorn, and examined the various chances"
164 "of success or failure of the expedition.  But, seeing that Ned Land let"
164 "me speak without saying too much himself, I pressed him more closely."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Ned,\" said I, \"is it possible that you are not convinced of the"
164 "existence of this cetacean that we are following?  Have you any"
164 "particular reason for being so incredulous?\""
164 ""
164 "The harpooner looked at me fixedly for some moments before answering,"
164 "struck his broad forehead with his hand (a habit of his), as if to"
164 "collect himself, and said at last, \"Perhaps I have, Mr. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, Ned, you, a whaler by profession, familiarised with all the great"
164 "marine mammalia--YOU ought to be the last to doubt under such"
164 "circumstances!\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is just what deceives you, Professor,\" replied Ned.  \"As a whaler"
164 "I have followed many a cetacean, harpooned a great number, and killed"
164 "several; but, however strong or well-armed they may have been, neither"
164 "their tails nor their weapons would have been able even to scratch the"
164 "iron plates of a steamer.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, Ned, they tell of ships which the teeth of the narwhal have"
164 "pierced through and through.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Wooden ships--that is possible,\" replied the Canadian, \"but I have"
164 "never seen it done; and, until further proof, I deny that whales,"
164 "cetaceans, or sea-unicorns could ever produce the effect you describe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Ned, I repeat it with a conviction resting on the logic of"
164 "facts.  I believe in the existence of a mammal power fully organised,"
164 "belonging to the branch of vertebrata, like the whales, the cachalots,"
164 "or the dolphins, and furnished with a horn of defence of great"
164 "penetrating power.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Hum!\" said the harpooner, shaking his head with the air of a man who"
164 "would not be convinced."
164 ""
164 "\"Notice one thing, my worthy Canadian,\" I resumed.  \"If such an animal"
164 "is in existence, if it inhabits the depths of the ocean, if it"
164 "frequents the strata lying miles below the surface of the water, it"
164 "must necessarily possess an organisation the strength of which would"
164 "defy all comparison.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And why this powerful organisation?\" demanded Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"Because it requires incalculable strength to keep one's self in these"
164 "strata and resist their pressure.  Listen to me.  Let us admit that the"
164 "pressure of the atmosphere is represented by the weight of a column of"
164 "water thirty-two feet high.  In reality the column of water would be"
164 "shorter, as we are speaking of sea water, the density of which is"
164 "greater than that of fresh water.  Very well, when you dive, Ned, as"
164 "many times 32 feet of water as there are above you, so many times does"
164 "your body bear a pressure equal to that of the atmosphere, that is to"
164 "say, 15 lb. for each square inch of its surface.  It follows, then,"
164 "that at 320 feet this pressure equals that of 10 atmospheres, of 100"
164 "atmospheres at 3,200 feet, and of 1,000 atmospheres at 32,000 feet,"
164 "that is, about 6 miles; which is equivalent to saying that if you could"
164 "attain this depth in the ocean, each square three-eighths of an inch of"
164 "the surface of your body would bear a pressure of 5,600 lb.  Ah! my"
164 "brave Ned, do you know how many square inches you carry on the surface"
164 "of your body?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I have no idea, Mr. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "\"About 6,500; and as in reality the atmospheric pressure is about 15"
164 "lb.  to the square inch, your 6,500 square inches bear at this moment a"
164 "pressure of 97,500 lb.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Without my perceiving it?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Without your perceiving it.  And if you are not crushed by such a"
164 "pressure, it is because the air penetrates the interior of your body"
164 "with equal pressure.  Hence perfect equilibrium between the interior"
164 "and exterior pressure, which thus neutralise each other, and which"
164 "allows you to bear it without inconvenience.  But in the water it is"
164 "another thing.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, I understand,\" replied Ned, becoming more attentive; \"because the"
164 "water surrounds me, but does not penetrate.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Precisely, Ned:  so that at 32 feet beneath the surface of the sea you"
164 "would undergo a pressure of 97,500 lb.; at 320 feet, ten times that"
164 "pressure; at 3,200 feet, a hundred times that pressure; lastly, at"
164 "32,000 feet, a thousand times that pressure would be 97,500,000"
164 "lb.--that is to say, that you would be flattened as if you had been"
164 "drawn from the plates of a hydraulic machine!\""
164 ""
164 "\"The devil!\" exclaimed Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"Very well, my worthy harpooner, if some vertebrate, several hundred"
164 "yards long, and large in proportion, can maintain itself in such"
164 "depths--of those whose surface is represented by millions of square"
164 "inches, that is by tens of millions of pounds, we must estimate the"
164 "pressure they undergo.  Consider, then, what must be the resistance of"
164 "their bony structure, and the strength of their organisation to"
164 "withstand such pressure!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why!\" exclaimed Ned Land, \"they must be made of iron plates eight"
164 "inches thick, like the armoured frigates.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you say, Ned.  And think what destruction such a mass would cause,"
164 "if hurled with the speed of an express train against the hull of a"
164 "vessel.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes--certainly--perhaps,\" replied the Canadian, shaken by these"
164 "figures, but not yet willing to give in."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, have I convinced you?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You have convinced me of one thing, sir, which is that, if such"
164 "animals do exist at the bottom of the seas, they must necessarily be as"
164 "strong as you say.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But if they do not exist, mine obstinate harpooner, how explain the"
164 "accident to the Scotia?\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER V"
164 ""
164 "AT A VENTURE"
164 ""
164 "The voyage of the Abraham Lincoln was for a long time marked by no"
164 "special incident.  But one circumstance happened which showed the"
164 "wonderful dexterity of Ned Land, and proved what confidence we might"
164 "place in him."
164 ""
164 "The 30th of June, the frigate spoke some American whalers, from whom we"
164 "learned that they knew nothing about the narwhal.  But one of them, the"
164 "captain of the Monroe, knowing that Ned Land had shipped on board the"
164 "Abraham Lincoln, begged for his help in chasing a whale they had in"
164 "sight.  Commander Farragut, desirous of seeing Ned Land at work, gave"
164 "him permission to go on board the Monroe.  And fate served our Canadian"
164 "so well that, instead of one whale, he harpooned two with a double"
164 "blow, striking one straight to the heart, and catching the other after"
164 "some minutes' pursuit."
164 ""
164 "Decidedly, if the monster ever had to do with Ned Land's harpoon, I"
164 "would not bet in its favour."
164 ""
164 "The frigate skirted the south-east coast of America with great"
164 "rapidity.  The 3rd of July we were at the opening of the Straits of"
164 "Magellan, level with Cape Vierges.  But Commander Farragut would not"
164 "take a tortuous passage, but doubled Cape Horn."
164 ""
164 "The ship's crew agreed with him.  And certainly it was possible that"
164 "they might meet the narwhal in this narrow pass.  Many of the sailors"
164 "affirmed that the monster could not pass there, \"that he was too big"
164 "for that!\""
164 ""
164 "The 6th of July, about three o'clock in the afternoon, the Abraham"
164 "Lincoln, at fifteen miles to the south, doubled the solitary island,"
164 "this lost rock at the extremity of the American continent, to which"
164 "some Dutch sailors gave the name of their native town, Cape Horn.  The"
164 "course was taken towards the north-west, and the next day the screw of"
164 "the frigate was at last beating the waters of the Pacific."
164 ""
164 "\"Keep your eyes open!\" called out the sailors."
164 ""
164 "And they were opened widely.  Both eyes and glasses, a little dazzled,"
164 "it is true, by the prospect of two thousand dollars, had not an"
164 "instant's repose."
164 ""
164 "I myself, for whom money had no charms, was not the least attentive on"
164 "board.  Giving but few minutes to my meals, but a few hours to sleep,"
164 "indifferent to either rain or sunshine, I did not leave the poop of the"
164 "vessel.  Now leaning on the netting of the forecastle, now on the"
164 "taffrail, I devoured with eagerness the soft foam which whitened the"
164 "sea as far as the eye could reach; and how often have I shared the"
164 "emotion of the majority of the crew, when some capricious whale raised"
164 "its black back above the waves!  The poop of the vessel was crowded on"
164 "a moment.  The cabins poured forth a torrent of sailors and officers,"
164 "each with heaving breast and troubled eye watching the course of the"
164 "cetacean.  I looked and looked till I was nearly blind, whilst Conseil"
164 "kept repeating in a calm voice:"
164 ""
164 "\"If, sir, you would not squint so much, you would see better!\""
164 ""
164 "But vain excitement!  The Abraham Lincoln checked its speed and made"
164 "for the animal signalled, a simple whale, or common cachalot, which"
164 "soon disappeared amidst a storm of abuse."
164 ""
164 "But the weather was good.  The voyage was being accomplished under the"
164 "most favourable auspices.  It was then the bad season in Australia, the"
164 "July of that zone corresponding to our January in Europe, but the sea"
164 "was beautiful and easily scanned round a vast circumference."
164 ""
164 "The 20th of July, the tropic of Capricorn was cut by 105d of longitude,"
164 "and the 27th of the same month we crossed the Equator on the 110th"
164 "meridian.  This passed, the frigate took a more decided westerly"
164 "direction, and scoured the central waters of the Pacific.  Commander"
164 "Farragut thought, and with reason, that it was better to remain in deep"
164 "water, and keep clear of continents or islands, which the beast itself"
164 "seemed to shun (perhaps because there was not enough water for him!"
164 "suggested the greater part of the crew). The frigate passed at some"
164 "distance from the Marquesas and the Sandwich Islands, crossed the"
164 "tropic of Cancer, and made for the China Seas.  We were on the theatre"
164 "of the last diversions of the monster:  and, to say truth, we no longer"
164 "LIVED on board.  The entire ship's crew were undergoing a nervous"
164 "excitement, of which I can give no idea:  they could not eat, they"
164 "could not sleep--twenty times a day, a misconception or an optical"
164 "illusion of some sailor seated on the taffrail, would cause dreadful"
164 "perspirations, and these emotions, twenty times repeated, kept us in a"
164 "state of excitement so violent that a reaction was unavoidable."
164 ""
164 "And truly, reaction soon showed itself.  For three months, during which"
164 "a day seemed an age, the Abraham Lincoln furrowed all the waters of the"
164 "Northern Pacific, running at whales, making sharp deviations from her"
164 "course, veering suddenly from one tack to another, stopping suddenly,"
164 "putting on steam, and backing ever and anon at the risk of deranging"
164 "her machinery, and not one point of the Japanese or American coast was"
164 "left unexplored."
164 ""
164 "The warmest partisans of the enterprise now became its most ardent"
164 "detractors.  Reaction mounted from the crew to the captain himself, and"
164 "certainly, had it not been for the resolute determination on the part"
164 "of Captain Farragut, the frigate would have headed due southward.  This"
164 "useless search could not last much longer.  The Abraham Lincoln had"
164 "nothing to reproach herself with, she had done her best to succeed."
164 "Never had an American ship's crew shown more zeal or patience; its"
164 "failure could not be placed to their charge--there remained nothing but"
164 "to return."
164 ""
164 "This was represented to the commander.  The sailors could not hide"
164 "their discontent, and the service suffered.  I will not say there was a"
164 "mutiny on board, but after a reasonable period of obstinacy, Captain"
164 "Farragut (as Columbus did) asked for three days' patience.  If in three"
164 "days the monster did not appear, the man at the helm should give three"
164 "turns of the wheel, and the Abraham Lincoln would make for the European"
164 "seas."
164 ""
164 "This promise was made on the 2nd of November.  It had the effect of"
164 "rallying the ship's crew.  The ocean was watched with renewed"
164 "attention.  Each one wished for a last glance in which to sum up his"
164 "remembrance.  Glasses were used with feverish activity.  It was a grand"
164 "defiance given to the giant narwhal, and he could scarcely fail to"
164 "answer the summons and \"appear.\""
164 ""
164 "Two days passed, the steam was at half pressure; a thousand schemes"
164 "were tried to attract the attention and stimulate the apathy of the"
164 "animal in case it should be met in those parts.  Large quantities of"
164 "bacon were trailed in the wake of the ship, to the great satisfaction"
164 "(I must say) of the sharks.  Small craft radiated in all directions"
164 "round the Abraham Lincoln as she lay to, and did not leave a spot of"
164 "the sea unexplored.  But the night of the 4th of November arrived"
164 "without the unveiling of this submarine mystery."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 5th of November, at twelve, the delay would (morally"
164 "speaking) expire; after that time, Commander Farragut, faithful to his"
164 "promise, was to turn the course to the south-east and abandon for ever"
164 "the northern regions of the Pacific."
164 ""
164 "The frigate was then in 31 deg. 15' N. lat. and 136 deg. 42' E. long."
164 "The coast of Japan still remained less than two hundred miles to"
164 "leeward.  Night was approaching.  They had just struck eight bells;"
164 "large clouds veiled the face of the moon, then in its first quarter."
164 "The sea undulated peaceably under the stern of the vessel."
164 ""
164 "At that moment I was leaning forward on the starboard netting."
164 "Conseil, standing near me, was looking straight before him.  The crew,"
164 "perched in the ratlines, examined the horizon which contracted and"
164 "darkened by degrees.  Officers with their night glasses scoured the"
164 "growing darkness:  sometimes the ocean sparkled under the rays of the"
164 "moon, which darted between two clouds, then all trace of light was lost"
164 "in the darkness."
164 ""
164 "In looking at Conseil, I could see he was undergoing a little of the"
164 "general influence.  At least I thought so.  Perhaps for the first time"
164 "his nerves vibrated to a sentiment of curiosity."
164 ""
164 "\"Come, Conseil,\" said I, \"this is the last chance of pocketing the two"
164 "thousand dollars.\""
164 ""
164 "\"May I be permitted to say, sir,\" replied Conseil, \"that I never"
164 "reckoned on getting the prize; and, had the government of the Union"
164 "offered a hundred thousand dollars, it would have been none the poorer.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You are right, Conseil.  It is a foolish affair after all, and one"
164 "upon which we entered too lightly.  What time lost, what useless"
164 "emotions!  We should have been back in France six months ago.\""
164 ""
164 "\"In your little room, sir,\" replied Conseil, \"and in your museum, sir;"
164 "and I should have already classed all your fossils, sir.  And the"
164 "Babiroussa would have been installed in its cage in the Jardin des"
164 "Plantes, and have drawn all the curious people of the capital!\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you say, Conseil.  I fancy we shall run a fair chance of being"
164 "laughed at for our pains.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That's tolerably certain,\" replied Conseil, quietly; \"I think they"
164 "will make fun of you, sir.  And, must I say it----?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Go on, my good friend.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir, you will only get your deserts.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Indeed!\""
164 ""
164 "\"When one has the honour of being a _savant_ as you are, sir, one should"
164 "not expose one's self to----\""
164 ""
164 "Conseil had not time to finish his compliment.  In the midst of general"
164 "silence a voice had just been heard.  It was the voice of Ned Land"
164 "shouting:"
164 ""
164 "\"Look out there!  The very thing we are looking for--on our weather"
164 "beam!\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VI"
164 ""
164 "AT FULL STEAM"
164 ""
164 "At this cry the whole ship's crew hurried towards the"
164 "harpooner--commander, officers, masters, sailors, cabin boys; even the"
164 "engineers left their engines, and the stokers their furnaces."
164 ""
164 "The order to stop her had been given, and the frigate now simply went"
164 "on by her own momentum.  The darkness was then profound, and, however"
164 "good the Canadian's eyes were, I asked myself how he had managed to"
164 "see, and what he had been able to see.  My heart beat as if it would"
164 "break.  But Ned Land was not mistaken, and we all perceived the object"
164 "he pointed to.  At two cables' length from the Abraham Lincoln, on the"
164 "starboard quarter, the sea seemed to be illuminated all over.  It was"
164 "not a mere phosphoric phenomenon.  The monster emerged some fathoms"
164 "from the water, and then threw out that very intense but mysterious"
164 "light mentioned in the report of several captains.  This magnificent"
164 "irradiation must have been produced by an agent of great SHINING power."
164 "The luminous part traced on the sea an immense oval, much elongated,"
164 "the centre of which condensed a burning heat, whose overpowering"
164 "brilliancy died out by successive gradations."
164 ""
164 "\"It is only a massing of phosphoric particles,\" cried one of the"
164 "officers."
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir, certainly not,\" I replied.  \"That brightness is of an"
164 "essentially electrical nature.  Besides, see, see! it moves; it is"
164 "moving forwards, backwards; it is darting towards us!\""
164 ""
164 "A general cry arose from the frigate."
164 ""
164 "\"Silence!\" said the captain.  \"Up with the helm, reverse the engines.\""
164 ""
164 "The steam was shut off, and the Abraham Lincoln, beating to port,"
164 "described a semicircle."
164 ""
164 "\"Right the helm, go ahead,\" cried the captain."
164 ""
164 "These orders were executed, and the frigate moved rapidly from the"
164 "burning light."
164 ""
164 "I was mistaken.  She tried to sheer off, but the supernatural animal"
164 "approached with a velocity double her own."
164 ""
164 "We gasped for breath.  Stupefaction more than fear made us dumb and"
164 "motionless.  The animal gained on us, sporting with the waves.  It made"
164 "the round of the frigate, which was then making fourteen knots, and"
164 "enveloped it with its electric rings like luminous dust."
164 ""
164 "Then it moved away two or three miles, leaving a phosphorescent track,"
164 "like those volumes of steam that the express trains leave behind.  All"
164 "at once from the dark line of the horizon whither it retired to gain"
164 "its momentum, the monster rushed suddenly towards the Abraham Lincoln"
164 "with alarming rapidity, stopped suddenly about twenty feet from the"
164 "hull, and died out--not diving under the water, for its brilliancy did"
164 "not abate--but suddenly, and as if the source of this brilliant"
164 "emanation was exhausted.  Then it reappeared on the other side of the"
164 "vessel, as if it had turned and slid under the hull.  Any moment a"
164 "collision might have occurred which would have been fatal to us."
164 "However, I was astonished at the manoeuvres of the frigate.  She fled"
164 "and did not attack."
164 ""
164 "On the captain's face, generally so impassive, was an expression of"
164 "unaccountable astonishment."
164 ""
164 "\"Mr. Aronnax,\" he said, \"I do not know with what formidable being I"
164 "have to deal, and I will not imprudently risk my frigate in the midst"
164 "of this darkness.  Besides, how attack this unknown thing, how defend"
164 "one's self from it?  Wait for daylight, and the scene will change.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You have no further doubt, captain, of the nature of the animal?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; it is evidently a gigantic narwhal, and an electric one.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps,\" added I, \"one can only approach it with a torpedo.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Undoubtedly,\" replied the captain, \"if it possesses such dreadful"
164 "power, it is the most terrible animal that ever was created.  That is"
164 "why, sir, I must be on my guard.\""
164 ""
164 "The crew were on their feet all night.  No one thought of sleep.  The"
164 "Abraham Lincoln, not being able to struggle with such velocity, had"
164 "moderated its pace, and sailed at half speed.  For its part, the"
164 "narwhal, imitating the frigate, let the waves rock it at will, and"
164 "seemed decided not to leave the scene of the struggle.  Towards"
164 "midnight, however, it disappeared, or, to use a more appropriate term,"
164 "it \"died out\" like a large glow-worm. Had it fled?  One could only"
164 "fear, not hope it.  But at seven minutes to one o'clock in the morning"
164 "a deafening whistling was heard, like that produced by a body of water"
164 "rushing with great violence."
164 ""
164 "The captain, Ned Land, and I were then on the poop, eagerly peering"
164 "through the profound darkness."
164 ""
164 "\"Ned Land,\" asked the commander, \"you have often heard the roaring of"
164 "whales?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Often, sir; but never such whales the sight of which brought me in two"
164 "thousand dollars.  If I can only approach within four harpoons' length"
164 "of it!\""
164 ""
164 "\"But to approach it,\" said the commander, \"I ought to put a whaler at"
164 "your disposal?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That will be trifling with the lives of my men.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And mine too,\" simply said the harpooner."
164 ""
164 "Towards two o'clock in the morning, the burning light reappeared, not"
164 "less intense, about five miles to windward of the Abraham Lincoln."
164 "Notwithstanding the distance, and the noise of the wind and sea, one"
164 "heard distinctly the loud strokes of the animal's tail, and even its"
164 "panting breath.  It seemed that, at the moment that the enormous"
164 "narwhal had come to take breath at the surface of the water, the air"
164 "was engulfed in its lungs, like the steam in the vast cylinders of a"
164 "machine of two thousand horse-power."
164 ""
164 "\"Hum!\" thought I, \"a whale with the strength of a cavalry regiment"
164 "would be a pretty whale!\""
164 ""
164 "We were on the qui vive till daylight, and prepared for the combat."
164 "The fishing implements were laid along the hammock nettings.  The"
164 "second lieutenant loaded the blunder busses, which could throw harpoons"
164 "to the distance of a mile, and long duck-guns, with explosive bullets,"
164 "which inflicted mortal wounds even to the most terrible animals.  Ned"
164 "Land contented himself with sharpening his harpoon--a terrible weapon"
164 "in his hands."
164 ""
164 "At six o'clock day began to break; and, with the first glimmer of"
164 "light, the electric light of the narwhal disappeared.  At seven o'clock"
164 "the day was sufficiently advanced, but a very thick sea fog obscured"
164 "our view, and the best spy glasses could not pierce it.  That caused"
164 "disappointment and anger."
164 ""
164 "I climbed the mizzen-mast. Some officers were already perched on the"
164 "mast-heads. At eight o'clock the fog lay heavily on the waves, and its"
164 "thick scrolls rose little by little.  The horizon grew wider and"
164 "clearer at the same time.  Suddenly, just as on the day before, Ned"
164 "Land's voice was heard:"
164 ""
164 "\"The thing itself on the port quarter!\" cried the harpooner."
164 ""
164 "Every eye was turned towards the point indicated.  There, a mile and a"
164 "half from the frigate, a long blackish body emerged a yard above the"
164 "waves.  Its tail, violently agitated, produced a considerable eddy."
164 "Never did a tail beat the sea with such violence.  An immense track, of"
164 "dazzling whiteness, marked the passage of the animal, and described a"
164 "long curve."
164 ""
164 "The frigate approached the cetacean.  I examined it thoroughly."
164 ""
164 "The reports of the Shannon and of the Helvetia had rather exaggerated"
164 "its size, and I estimated its length at only two hundred and fifty"
164 "feet.  As to its dimensions, I could only conjecture them to be"
164 "admirably proportioned.  While I watched this phenomenon, two jets of"
164 "steam and water were ejected from its vents, and rose to the height of"
164 "120 feet; thus I ascertained its way of breathing.  I concluded"
164 "definitely that it belonged to the vertebrate branch, class mammalia."
164 ""
164 "The crew waited impatiently for their chief's orders.  The latter,"
164 "after having observed the animal attentively, called the engineer.  The"
164 "engineer ran to him."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said the commander, \"you have steam up?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir,\" answered the engineer."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, make up your fires and put on all steam.\""
164 ""
164 "Three hurrahs greeted this order.  The time for the struggle had"
164 "arrived.  Some moments after, the two funnels of the frigate vomited"
164 "torrents of black smoke, and the bridge quaked under the trembling of"
164 "the boilers."
164 ""
164 "The Abraham Lincoln, propelled by her wonderful screw, went straight at"
164 "the animal.  The latter allowed it to come within half a cable's"
164 "length; then, as if disdaining to dive, it took a little turn, and"
164 "stopped a short distance off."
164 ""
164 "This pursuit lasted nearly three-quarters of an hour, without the"
164 "frigate gaining two yards on the cetacean.  It was quite evident that"
164 "at that rate we should never come up with it."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Mr. Land,\" asked the captain, \"do you advise me to put the boats"
164 "out to sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir,\" replied Ned Land; \"because we shall not take that beast"
164 "easily.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What shall we do then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Put on more steam if you can, sir.  With your leave, I mean to post"
164 "myself under the bowsprit, and, if we get within harpooning distance, I"
164 "shall throw my harpoon.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Go, Ned,\" said the captain.  \"Engineer, put on more pressure.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land went to his post.  The fires were increased, the screw"
164 "revolved forty-three times a minute, and the steam poured out of the"
164 "valves.  We heaved the log, and calculated that the Abraham Lincoln was"
164 "going at the rate of 18 1/2 miles an hour."
164 ""
164 "But the accursed animal swam at the same speed."
164 ""
164 "For a whole hour the frigate kept up this pace, without gaining six"
164 "feet.  It was humiliating for one of the swiftest sailers in the"
164 "American navy.  A stubborn anger seized the crew; the sailors abused"
164 "the monster, who, as before, disdained to answer them; the captain no"
164 "longer contented himself with twisting his beard--he gnawed it."
164 ""
164 "The engineer was called again."
164 ""
164 "\"You have turned full steam on?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir,\" replied the engineer."
164 ""
164 "The speed of the Abraham Lincoln increased.  Its masts trembled down to"
164 "their stepping holes, and the clouds of smoke could hardly find way out"
164 "of the narrow funnels."
164 ""
164 "They heaved the log a second time."
164 ""
164 "\"Well?\" asked the captain of the man at the wheel."
164 ""
164 "\"Nineteen miles and three-tenths, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Clap on more steam.\""
164 ""
164 "The engineer obeyed.  The manometer showed ten degrees.  But the"
164 "cetacean grew warm itself, no doubt; for without straining itself, it"
164 "made 19 3/10 miles."
164 ""
164 "What a pursuit!  No, I cannot describe the emotion that vibrated"
164 "through me.  Ned Land kept his post, harpoon in hand.  Several times"
164 "the animal let us gain upon it.--\"We shall catch it! we shall catch"
164 "it!\" cried the Canadian.  But just as he was going to strike, the"
164 "cetacean stole away with a rapidity that could not be estimated at less"
164 "than thirty miles an hour, and even during our maximum of speed, it"
164 "bullied the frigate, going round and round it.  A cry of fury broke"
164 "from everyone!"
164 ""
164 "At noon we were no further advanced than at eight o'clock in the"
164 "morning."
164 ""
164 "The captain then decided to take more direct means."
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" said he, \"that animal goes quicker than the Abraham Lincoln."
164 "Very well! we will see whether it will escape these conical bullets."
164 "Send your men to the forecastle, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "The forecastle gun was immediately loaded and slewed round.  But the"
164 "shot passed some feet above the cetacean, which was half a mile off."
164 ""
164 "\"Another, more to the right,\" cried the commander, \"and five dollars to"
164 "whoever will hit that infernal beast.\""
164 ""
164 "An old gunner with a grey beard--that I can see now--with steady eye"
164 "and grave face, went up to the gun and took a long aim.  A loud report"
164 "was heard, with which were mingled the cheers of the crew."
164 ""
164 "The bullet did its work; it hit the animal, and, sliding off the"
164 "rounded surface, was lost in two miles depth of sea."
164 ""
164 "The chase began again, and the captain, leaning towards me, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"I will pursue that beast till my frigate bursts up.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" answered I; \"and you will be quite right to do it.\""
164 ""
164 "I wished the beast would exhaust itself, and not be insensible to"
164 "fatigue like a steam engine.  But it was of no use.  Hours passed,"
164 "without its showing any signs of exhaustion."
164 ""
164 "However, it must be said in praise of the Abraham Lincoln that she"
164 "struggled on indefatigably.  I cannot reckon the distance she made"
164 "under three hundred miles during this unlucky day, November the 6th."
164 "But night came on, and overshadowed the rough ocean."
164 ""
164 "Now I thought our expedition was at an end, and that we should never"
164 "again see the extraordinary animal.  I was mistaken.  At ten minutes to"
164 "eleven in the evening, the electric light reappeared three miles to"
164 "windward of the frigate, as pure, as intense as during the preceding"
164 "night."
164 ""
164 "The narwhal seemed motionless; perhaps, tired with its day's work, it"
164 "slept, letting itself float with the undulation of the waves.  Now was"
164 "a chance of which the captain resolved to take advantage."
164 ""
164 "He gave his orders.  The Abraham Lincoln kept up half steam, and"
164 "advanced cautiously so as not to awake its adversary.  It is no rare"
164 "thing to meet in the middle of the ocean whales so sound asleep that"
164 "they can be successfully attacked, and Ned Land had harpooned more than"
164 "one during its sleep.  The Canadian went to take his place again under"
164 "the bowsprit."
164 ""
164 "The frigate approached noiselessly, stopped at two cables' lengths from"
164 "the animal, and following its track.  No one breathed; a deep silence"
164 "reigned on the bridge.  We were not a hundred feet from the burning"
164 "focus, the light of which increased and dazzled our eyes."
164 ""
164 "At this moment, leaning on the forecastle bulwark, I saw below me Ned"
164 "Land grappling the martingale in one hand, brandishing his terrible"
164 "harpoon in the other, scarcely twenty feet from the motionless animal."
164 "Suddenly his arm straightened, and the harpoon was thrown; I heard the"
164 "sonorous stroke of the weapon, which seemed to have struck a hard body."
164 "The electric light went out suddenly, and two enormous waterspouts"
164 "broke over the bridge of the frigate, rushing like a torrent from stem"
164 "to stern, overthrowing men, and breaking the lashings of the spars.  A"
164 "fearful shock followed, and, thrown over the rail without having time"
164 "to stop myself, I fell into the sea."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VII"
164 ""
164 "AN UNKNOWN SPECIES OF WHALE"
164 ""
164 "This unexpected fall so stunned me that I have no clear recollection of"
164 "my sensations at the time.  I was at first drawn down to a depth of"
164 "about twenty feet.  I am a good swimmer (though without pretending to"
164 "rival Byron or Edgar Poe, who were masters of the art), and in that"
164 "plunge I did not lose my presence of mind.  Two vigorous strokes"
164 "brought me to the surface of the water.  My first care was to look for"
164 "the frigate.  Had the crew seen me disappear?  Had the Abraham Lincoln"
164 "veered round?  Would the captain put out a boat?  Might I hope to be"
164 "saved?"
164 ""
164 "The darkness was intense.  I caught a glimpse of a black mass"
164 "disappearing in the east, its beacon lights dying out in the distance."
164 "It was the frigate!  I was lost."
164 ""
164 "\"Help, help!\"  I shouted, swimming towards the Abraham Lincoln in"
164 "desperation."
164 ""
164 "My clothes encumbered me; they seemed glued to my body, and paralysed"
164 "my movements."
164 ""
164 "I was sinking!  I was suffocating!"
164 ""
164 "\"Help!\""
164 ""
164 "This was my last cry.  My mouth filled with water; I struggled against"
164 "being drawn down the abyss.  Suddenly my clothes were seized by a"
164 "strong hand, and I felt myself quickly drawn up to the surface of the"
164 "sea; and I heard, yes, I heard these words pronounced in my ear:"
164 ""
164 "\"If master would be so good as to lean on my shoulder, master would"
164 "swim with much greater ease.\""
164 ""
164 "I seized with one hand my faithful Conseil's arm."
164 ""
164 "\"Is it you?\" said I, \"you?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Myself,\" answered Conseil; \"and waiting master's orders.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That shock threw you as well as me into the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No; but, being in my master's service, I followed him.\""
164 ""
164 "The worthy fellow thought that was but natural."
164 ""
164 "\"And the frigate?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"The frigate?\" replied Conseil, turning on his back; \"I think that"
164 "master had better not count too much on her.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You think so?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I say that, at the time I threw myself into the sea, I heard the men"
164 "at the wheel say, `The screw and the rudder are broken.'"
164 ""
164 "\"Broken?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, broken by the monster's teeth.  It is the only injury the Abraham"
164 "Lincoln has sustained.  But it is a bad look-out for us--she no longer"
164 "answers her helm.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then we are lost!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps so,\" calmly answered Conseil.  \"However, we have still several"
164 "hours before us, and one can do a good deal in some hours.\""
164 ""
164 "Conseil's imperturbable coolness set me up again.  I swam more"
164 "vigorously; but, cramped by my clothes, which stuck to me like a leaden"
164 "weight, I felt great difficulty in bearing up.  Conseil saw this."
164 ""
164 "\"Will master let me make a slit?\" said he; and, slipping an open knife"
164 "under my clothes, he ripped them up from top to bottom very rapidly."
164 "Then he cleverly slipped them off me, while I swam for both of us."
164 ""
164 "Then I did the same for Conseil, and we continued to swim near to each"
164 "other."
164 ""
164 "Nevertheless, our situation was no less terrible.  Perhaps our"
164 "disappearance had not been noticed; and, if it had been, the frigate"
164 "could not tack, being without its helm.  Conseil argued on this"
164 "supposition, and laid his plans accordingly.  This quiet boy was"
164 "perfectly self-possessed. We then decided that, as our only chance of"
164 "safety was being picked up by the Abraham Lincoln's boats, we ought to"
164 "manage so as to wait for them as long as possible.  I resolved then to"
164 "husband our strength, so that both should not be exhausted at the same"
164 "time; and this is how we managed:  while one of us lay on our back,"
164 "quite still, with arms crossed, and legs stretched out, the other would"
164 "swim and push the other on in front.  This towing business did not last"
164 "more than ten minutes each; and relieving each other thus, we could"
164 "swim on for some hours, perhaps till day-break. Poor chance! but hope"
164 "is so firmly rooted in the heart of man!  Moreover, there were two of"
164 "us.  Indeed I declare (though it may seem improbable) if I sought to"
164 "destroy all hope--if I wished to despair, I could not."
164 ""
164 "The collision of the frigate with the cetacean had occurred about"
164 "eleven o'clock in the evening before.  I reckoned then we should have"
164 "eight hours to swim before sunrise, an operation quite practicable if"
164 "we relieved each other.  The sea, very calm, was in our favour."
164 "Sometimes I tried to pierce the intense darkness that was only"
164 "dispelled by the phosphorescence caused by our movements.  I watched"
164 "the luminous waves that broke over my hand, whose mirror-like surface"
164 "was spotted with silvery rings.  One might have said that we were in a"
164 "bath of quicksilver."
164 ""
164 "Near one o'clock in the morning, I was seized with dreadful fatigue."
164 "My limbs stiffened under the strain of violent cramp.  Conseil was"
164 "obliged to keep me up, and our preservation devolved on him alone.  I"
164 "heard the poor boy pant; his breathing became short and hurried.  I"
164 "found that he could not keep up much longer."
164 ""
164 "\"Leave me! leave me!\"  I said to him."
164 ""
164 "\"Leave my master?  Never!\" replied he.  \"I would drown first.\""
164 ""
164 "Just then the moon appeared through the fringes of a thick cloud that"
164 "the wind was driving to the east.  The surface of the sea glittered"
164 "with its rays.  This kindly light reanimated us.  My head got better"
164 "again.  I looked at all points of the horizon.  I saw the frigate!  She"
164 "was five miles from us, and looked like a dark mass, hardly"
164 "discernible.  But no boats!"
164 ""
164 "I would have cried out.  But what good would it have been at such a"
164 "distance!  My swollen lips could utter no sounds.  Conseil could"
164 "articulate some words, and I heard him repeat at intervals, \"Help!"
164 "help!\""
164 ""
164 "Our movements were suspended for an instant; we listened.  It might be"
164 "only a singing in the ear, but it seemed to me as if a cry answered the"
164 "cry from Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Did you hear?\"  I murmured."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes!  Yes!\""
164 ""
164 "And Conseil gave one more despairing cry."
164 ""
164 "This time there was no mistake!  A human voice responded to ours!  Was"
164 "it the voice of another unfortunate creature, abandoned in the middle"
164 "of the ocean, some other victim of the shock sustained by the vessel?"
164 "Or rather was it a boat from the frigate, that was hailing us in the"
164 "darkness?"
164 ""
164 "Conseil made a last effort, and, leaning on my shoulder, while I struck"
164 "out in a desperate effort, he raised himself half out of the water,"
164 "then fell back exhausted."
164 ""
164 "\"What did you see?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I saw----\" murmured he; \"I saw--but do not talk--reserve all your"
164 "strength!\""
164 ""
164 "What had he seen?  Then, I know not why, the thought of the monster"
164 "came into my head for the first time!  But that voice!  The time is"
164 "past for Jonahs to take refuge in whales' bellies!  However, Conseil"
164 "was towing me again.  He raised his head sometimes, looked before us,"
164 "and uttered a cry of recognition, which was responded to by a voice"
164 "that came nearer and nearer.  I scarcely heard it.  My strength was"
164 "exhausted; my fingers stiffened; my hand afforded me support no longer;"
164 "my mouth, convulsively opening, filled with salt water.  Cold crept"
164 "over me.  I raised my head for the last time, then I sank."
164 ""
164 "At this moment a hard body struck me.  I clung to it: then I felt that"
164 "I was being drawn up, that I was brought to the surface of the water,"
164 "that my chest collapsed--I fainted."
164 ""
164 "It is certain that I soon came to, thanks to the vigorous rubbings that"
164 "I received.  I half opened my eyes."
164 ""
164 "\"Conseil!\"  I murmured."
164 ""
164 "\"Does master call me?\" asked Conseil."
164 ""
164 "Just then, by the waning light of the moon which was sinking down to"
164 "the horizon, I saw a face which was not Conseil's and which I"
164 "immediately recognised."
164 ""
164 "\"Ned!\"  I cried."
164 ""
164 "\"The same, sir, who is seeking his prize!\" replied the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Were you thrown into the sea by the shock to the frigate?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Professor; but more fortunate than you, I was able to find a"
164 "footing almost directly upon a floating island.\""
164 ""
164 "\"An island?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Or, more correctly speaking, on our gigantic narwhal.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Explain yourself, Ned!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Only I soon found out why my harpoon had not entered its skin and was"
164 "blunted.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why, Ned, why?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Because, Professor, that beast is made of sheet iron.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian's last words produced a sudden revolution in my brain.  I"
164 "wriggled myself quickly to the top of the being, or object, half out of"
164 "the water, which served us for a refuge.  I kicked it.  It was"
164 "evidently a hard, impenetrable body, and not the soft substance that"
164 "forms the bodies of the great marine mammalia.  But this hard body"
164 "might be a bony covering, like that of the antediluvian animals; and I"
164 "should be free to class this monster among amphibious reptiles, such as"
164 "tortoises or alligators."
164 ""
164 "Well, no! the blackish back that supported me was smooth, polished,"
164 "without scales.  The blow produced a metallic sound; and, incredible"
164 "though it may be, it seemed, I might say, as if it was made of riveted"
164 "plates."
164 ""
164 "There was no doubt about it!  This monster, this natural phenomenon"
164 "that had puzzled the learned world, and over thrown and misled the"
164 "imagination of seamen of both hemispheres, it must be owned was a still"
164 "more astonishing phenomenon, inasmuch as it was a simply human"
164 "construction."
164 ""
164 "We had no time to lose, however.  We were lying upon the back of a sort"
164 "of submarine boat, which appeared (as far as I could judge) like a huge"
164 "fish of steel.  Ned Land's mind was made up on this point.  Conseil and"
164 "I could only agree with him."
164 ""
164 "Just then a bubbling began at the back of this strange thing (which was"
164 "evidently propelled by a screw), and it began to move.  We had only"
164 "just time to seize hold of the upper part, which rose about seven feet"
164 "out of the water, and happily its speed was not great."
164 ""
164 "\"As long as it sails horizontally,\" muttered Ned Land, \"I do not mind;"
164 "but, if it takes a fancy to dive, I would not give two straws for my"
164 "life.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian might have said still less.  It became really necessary to"
164 "communicate with the beings, whatever they were, shut up inside the"
164 "machine.  I searched all over the outside for an aperture, a panel, or"
164 "a manhole, to use a technical expression; but the lines of the iron"
164 "rivets, solidly driven into the joints of the iron plates, were clear"
164 "and uniform.  Besides, the moon disappeared then, and left us in total"
164 "darkness."
164 ""
164 "At last this long night passed.  My indistinct remembrance prevents my"
164 "describing all the impressions it made.  I can only recall one"
164 "circumstance.  During some lulls of the wind and sea, I fancied I heard"
164 "several times vague sounds, a sort of fugitive harmony produced by"
164 "words of command.  What was, then, the mystery of this submarine craft,"
164 "of which the whole world vainly sought an explanation?  What kind of"
164 "beings existed in this strange boat?  What mechanical agent caused its"
164 "prodigious speed?"
164 ""
164 "Daybreak appeared.  The morning mists surrounded us, but they soon"
164 "cleared off.  I was about to examine the hull, which formed on deck a"
164 "kind of horizontal platform, when I felt it gradually sinking."
164 ""
164 "\"Oh! confound it!\" cried Ned Land, kicking the resounding plate."
164 "\"Open, you inhospitable rascals!\""
164 ""
164 "Happily the sinking movement ceased.  Suddenly a noise, like iron works"
164 "violently pushed aside, came from the interior of the boat.  One iron"
164 "plate was moved, a man appeared, uttered an odd cry, and disappeared"
164 "immediately."
164 ""
164 "Some moments after, eight strong men, with masked faces, appeared"
164 "noiselessly, and drew us down into their formidable machine."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VIII"
164 ""
164 "MOBILIS IN MOBILI"
164 ""
164 "This forcible abduction, so roughly carried out, was accomplished with"
164 "the rapidity of lightning.  I shivered all over.  Whom had we to deal"
164 "with?  No doubt some new sort of pirates, who explored the sea in their"
164 "own way.  Hardly had the narrow panel closed upon me, when I was"
164 "enveloped in darkness.  My eyes, dazzled with the outer light, could"
