164 "distinguish nothing.  I felt my naked feet cling to the rungs of an"
164 "iron ladder.  Ned Land and Conseil, firmly seized, followed me.  At the"
164 "bottom of the ladder, a door opened, and shut after us immediately with"
164 "a bang."
164 ""
164 "We were alone.  Where, I could not say, hardly imagine.  All was black,"
164 "and such a dense black that, after some minutes, my eyes had not been"
164 "able to discern even the faintest glimmer."
164 ""
164 "Meanwhile, Ned Land, furious at these proceedings, gave free vent to"
164 "his indignation."
164 ""
164 "\"Confound it!\" cried he, \"here are people who come up to the Scotch for"
164 "hospitality.  They only just miss being cannibals.  I should not be"
164 "surprised at it, but I declare that they shall not eat me without my"
164 "protesting.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Calm yourself, friend Ned, calm yourself,\" replied Conseil, quietly."
164 "\"Do not cry out before you are hurt.  We are not quite done for yet.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Not quite,\" sharply replied the Canadian, \"but pretty near, at all"
164 "events.  Things look black.  Happily, my bowie knife I have still, and"
164 "I can always see well enough to use it.  The first of these pirates who"
164 "lays a hand on me----\""
164 ""
164 "\"Do not excite yourself, Ned,\" I said to the harpooner, \"and do not"
164 "compromise us by useless violence.  Who knows that they will not listen"
164 "to us?  Let us rather try to find out where we are.\""
164 ""
164 "I groped about.  In five steps I came to an iron wall, made of plates"
164 "bolted together.  Then turning back I struck against a wooden table,"
164 "near which were ranged several stools.  The boards of this prison were"
164 "concealed under a thick mat, which deadened the noise of the feet.  The"
164 "bare walls revealed no trace of window or door.  Conseil, going round"
164 "the reverse way, met me, and we went back to the middle of the cabin,"
164 "which measured about twenty feet by ten.  As to its height, Ned Land,"
164 "in spite of his own great height, could not measure it."
164 ""
164 "Half an hour had already passed without our situation being bettered,"
164 "when the dense darkness suddenly gave way to extreme light.  Our prison"
164 "was suddenly lighted, that is to say, it became filled with a luminous"
164 "matter, so strong that I could not bear it at first.  In its whiteness"
164 "and intensity I recognised that electric light which played round the"
164 "submarine boat like a magnificent phenomenon of phosphorescence.  After"
164 "shutting my eyes involuntarily, I opened them, and saw that this"
164 "luminous agent came from a half globe, unpolished, placed in the roof"
164 "of the cabin."
164 ""
164 "\"At last one can see,\" cried Ned Land, who, knife in hand, stood on the"
164 "defensive."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" said I; \"but we are still in the dark about ourselves.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Let master have patience,\" said the imperturbable Conseil."
164 ""
164 "The sudden lighting of the cabin enabled me to examine it minutely.  It"
164 "only contained a table and five stools.  The invisible door might be"
164 "hermetically sealed.  No noise was heard.  All seemed dead in the"
164 "interior of this boat.  Did it move, did it float on the surface of the"
164 "ocean, or did it dive into its depths?  I could not guess."
164 ""
164 "A noise of bolts was now heard, the door opened, and two men appeared."
164 ""
164 "One was short, very muscular, broad-shouldered, with robust limbs,"
164 "strong head, an abundance of black hair, thick moustache, a quick"
164 "penetrating look, and the vivacity which characterises the population"
164 "of Southern France."
164 ""
164 "The second stranger merits a more detailed description.  I made out his"
164 "prevailing qualities directly:  self-confidence--because his head was"
164 "well set on his shoulders, and his black eyes looked around with cold"
164 "assurance; calmness--for his skin, rather pale, showed his coolness of"
164 "blood; energy--evinced by the rapid contraction of his lofty brows; and"
164 "courage--because his deep breathing denoted great power of lungs."
164 ""
164 "Whether this person was thirty-five or fifty years of age, I could not"
164 "say.  He was tall, had a large forehead, straight nose, a clearly cut"
164 "mouth, beautiful teeth, with fine taper hands, indicative of a highly"
164 "nervous temperament.  This man was certainly the most admirable"
164 "specimen I had ever met.  One particular feature was his eyes, rather"
164 "far from each other, and which could take in nearly a quarter of the"
164 "horizon at once."
164 ""
164 "This faculty--(I verified it later)--gave him a range of vision far"
164 "superior to Ned Land's. When this stranger fixed upon an object, his"
164 "eyebrows met, his large eyelids closed around so as to contract the"
164 "range of his vision, and he looked as if he magnified the objects"
164 "lessened by distance, as if he pierced those sheets of water so opaque"
164 "to our eyes, and as if he read the very depths of the seas."
164 ""
164 "The two strangers, with caps made from the fur of the sea otter, and"
164 "shod with sea boots of seal's skin, were dressed in clothes of a"
164 "particular texture, which allowed free movement of the limbs.  The"
164 "taller of the two, evidently the chief on board, examined us with great"
164 "attention, without saying a word; then, turning to his companion,"
164 "talked with him in an unknown tongue.  It was a sonorous, harmonious,"
164 "and flexible dialect, the vowels seeming to admit of very varied"
164 "accentuation."
164 ""
164 "The other replied by a shake of the head, and added two or three"
164 "perfectly incomprehensible words.  Then he seemed to question me by a"
164 "look."
164 ""
164 "I replied in good French that I did not know his language; but he"
164 "seemed not to understand me, and my situation became more embarrassing."
164 ""
164 "\"If master were to tell our story,\" said Conseil, \"perhaps these"
164 "gentlemen may understand some words.\""
164 ""
164 "I began to tell our adventures, articulating each syllable clearly, and"
164 "without omitting one single detail.  I announced our names and rank,"
164 "introducing in person Professor Aronnax, his servant Conseil, and"
164 "master Ned Land, the harpooner."
164 ""
164 "The man with the soft calm eyes listened to me quietly, even politely,"
164 "and with extreme attention; but nothing in his countenance indicated"
164 "that he had understood my story.  When I finished, he said not a word."
164 ""
164 "There remained one resource, to speak English.  Perhaps they would know"
164 "this almost universal language.  I knew it--as well as the German"
164 "language--well enough to read it fluently, but not to speak it"
164 "correctly.  But, anyhow, we must make ourselves understood."
164 ""
164 "\"Go on in your turn,\" I said to the harpooner; \"speak your best"
164 "Anglo-Saxon, and try to do better than I.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned did not beg off, and recommenced our story."
164 ""
164 "To his great disgust, the harpooner did not seem to have made himself"
164 "more intelligible than I had.  Our visitors did not stir.  They"
164 "evidently understood neither the language of England nor of France."
164 ""
164 "Very much embarrassed, after having vainly exhausted our speaking"
164 "resources, I knew not what part to take, when Conseil said:"
164 ""
164 "\"If master will permit me, I will relate it in German.\""
164 ""
164 "But in spite of the elegant terms and good accent of the narrator, the"
164 "German language had no success.  At last, nonplussed, I tried to"
164 "remember my first lessons, and to narrate our adventures in Latin, but"
164 "with no better success.  This last attempt being of no avail, the two"
164 "strangers exchanged some words in their unknown language, and retired."
164 ""
164 "The door shut."
164 ""
164 "\"It is an infamous shame,\" cried Ned Land, who broke out for the"
164 "twentieth time.  \"We speak to those rogues in French, English, German,"
164 "and Latin, and not one of them has the politeness to answer!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Calm yourself,\" I said to the impetuous Ned; \"anger will do no good.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But do you see, Professor,\" replied our irascible companion, \"that we"
164 "shall absolutely die of hunger in this iron cage?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Bah!\" said Conseil, philosophically; \"we can hold out some time yet.\""
164 ""
164 "\"My friends,\" I said, \"we must not despair.  We have been worse off"
164 "than this.  Do me the favour to wait a little before forming an opinion"
164 "upon the commander and crew of this boat.\""
164 ""
164 "\"My opinion is formed,\" replied Ned Land, sharply.  \"They are rascals.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good! and from what country?\""
164 ""
164 "\"From the land of rogues!\""
164 ""
164 "\"My brave Ned, that country is not clearly indicated on the map of the"
164 "world; but I admit that the nationality of the two strangers is hard to"
164 "determine.  Neither English, French, nor German, that is quite certain."
164 "However, I am inclined to think that the commander and his companion"
164 "were born in low latitudes.  There is southern blood in them.  But I"
164 "cannot decide by their appearance whether they are Spaniards, Turks,"
164 "Arabians, or Indians.  As to their language, it is quite"
164 "incomprehensible.\""
164 ""
164 "\"There is the disadvantage of not knowing all languages,\" said Conseil,"
164 "\"or the disadvantage of not having one universal language.\""
164 ""
164 "As he said these words, the door opened.  A steward entered.  He"
164 "brought us clothes, coats and trousers, made of a stuff I did not know."
164 "I hastened to dress myself, and my companions followed my example."
164 "During that time, the steward--dumb, perhaps deaf--had arranged the"
164 "table, and laid three plates."
164 ""
164 "\"This is something like!\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Bah!\" said the angry harpooner, \"what do you suppose they eat here?"
164 "Tortoise liver, filleted shark, and beef steaks from seadogs.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We shall see,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "The dishes, of bell metal, were placed on the table, and we took our"
164 "places.  Undoubtedly we had to do with civilised people, and, had it"
164 "not been for the electric light which flooded us, I could have fancied"
164 "I was in the dining-room of the Adelphi Hotel at Liverpool, or at the"
164 "Grand Hotel in Paris.  I must say, however, that there was neither"
164 "bread nor wine.  The water was fresh and clear, but it was water and"
164 "did not suit Ned Land's taste.  Amongst the dishes which were brought"
164 "to us, I recognised several fish delicately dressed; but of some,"
164 "although excellent, I could give no opinion, neither could I tell to"
164 "what kingdom they belonged, whether animal or vegetable.  As to the"
164 "dinner-service, it was elegant, and in perfect taste.  Each"
164 "utensil--spoon, fork, knife, plate--had a letter engraved on it, with a"
164 "motto above it, of which this is an exact facsimile:"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "MOBILIS IN MOBILI N"
164 ""
164 "The letter N was no doubt the initial of the name of the enigmatical"
164 "person who commanded at the bottom of the seas."
164 ""
164 "Ned and Conseil did not reflect much.  They devoured the food, and I"
164 "did likewise.  I was, besides, reassured as to our fate; and it seemed"
164 "evident that our hosts would not let us die of want."
164 ""
164 "However, everything has an end, everything passes away, even the hunger"
164 "of people who have not eaten for fifteen hours.  Our appetites"
164 "satisfied, we felt overcome with sleep."
164 ""
164 "\"Faith!  I shall sleep well,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"So shall I,\" replied Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "My two companions stretched themselves on the cabin carpet, and were"
164 "soon sound asleep.  For my own part, too many thoughts crowded my"
164 "brain, too many insoluble questions pressed upon me, too many fancies"
164 "kept my eyes half open.  Where were we?  What strange power carried us"
164 "on?  I felt--or rather fancied I felt--the machine sinking down to the"
164 "lowest beds of the sea.  Dreadful nightmares beset me; I saw in these"
164 "mysterious asylums a world of unknown animals, amongst which this"
164 "submarine boat seemed to be of the same kind, living, moving, and"
164 "formidable as they.  Then my brain grew calmer, my imagination wandered"
164 "into vague unconsciousness, and I soon fell into a deep sleep."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER IX"
164 ""
164 "NED LAND'S TEMPERS"
164 ""
164 "How long we slept I do not know; but our sleep must have lasted long,"
164 "for it rested us completely from our fatigues.  I woke first.  My"
164 "companions had not moved, and were still stretched in their corner."
164 ""
164 "Hardly roused from my somewhat hard couch, I felt my brain freed, my"
164 "mind clear.  I then began an attentive examination of our cell."
164 "Nothing was changed inside.  The prison was still a prison--the"
164 "prisoners, prisoners.  However, the steward, during our sleep, had"
164 "cleared the table.  I breathed with difficulty.  The heavy air seemed"
164 "to oppress my lungs.  Although the cell was large, we had evidently"
164 "consumed a great part of the oxygen that it contained.  Indeed, each"
164 "man consumes, in one hour, the oxygen contained in more than 176 pints"
164 "of air, and this air, charged (as then) with a nearly equal quantity of"
164 "carbonic acid, becomes unbreathable."
164 ""
164 "It became necessary to renew the atmosphere of our prison, and no doubt"
164 "the whole in the submarine boat.  That gave rise to a question in my"
164 "mind.  How would the commander of this floating dwelling-place proceed?"
164 "Would he obtain air by chemical means, in getting by heat the oxygen"
164 "contained in chlorate of potash, and in absorbing carbonic acid by"
164 "caustic potash?  Or--a more convenient, economical, and consequently"
164 "more probable alternative--would he be satisfied to rise and take"
164 "breath at the surface of the water, like a whale, and so renew for"
164 "twenty-four hours the atmospheric provision?"
164 ""
164 "In fact, I was already obliged to increase my respirations to eke out"
164 "of this cell the little oxygen it contained, when suddenly I was"
164 "refreshed by a current of pure air, and perfumed with saline"
164 "emanations.  It was an invigorating sea breeze, charged with iodine.  I"
164 "opened my mouth wide, and my lungs saturated themselves with fresh"
164 "particles."
164 ""
164 "At the same time I felt the boat rolling.  The iron-plated monster had"
164 "evidently just risen to the surface of the ocean to breathe, after the"
164 "fashion of whales.  I found out from that the mode of ventilating the"
164 "boat."
164 ""
164 "When I had inhaled this air freely, I sought the conduit pipe, which"
164 "conveyed to us the beneficial whiff, and I was not long in finding it."
164 "Above the door was a ventilator, through which volumes of fresh air"
164 "renewed the impoverished atmosphere of the cell."
164 ""
164 "I was making my observations, when Ned and Conseil awoke almost at the"
164 "same time, under the influence of this reviving air.  They rubbed their"
164 "eyes, stretched themselves, and were on their feet in an instant."
164 ""
164 "\"Did master sleep well?\" asked Conseil, with his usual politeness."
164 ""
164 "\"Very well, my brave boy.  And you, Mr. Land?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Soundly, Professor.  But, I don't know if I am right or not, there"
164 "seems to be a sea breeze!\""
164 ""
164 "A seaman could not be mistaken, and I told the Canadian all that had"
164 "passed during his sleep."
164 ""
164 "\"Good!\" said he.  \"That accounts for those roarings we heard, when the"
164 "supposed narwhal sighted the Abraham Lincoln.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Quite so, Master Land; it was taking breath.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Only, Mr. Aronnax, I have no idea what o'clock it is, unless it is"
164 "dinner-time.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Dinner-time! my good fellow?  Say rather breakfast-time, for we"
164 "certainly have begun another day.\""
164 ""
164 "\"So,\" said Conseil, \"we have slept twenty-four hours?\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is my opinion.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I will not contradict you,\" replied Ned Land.  \"But, dinner or"
164 "breakfast, the steward will be welcome, whichever he brings.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Master Land, we must conform to the rules on board, and I suppose our"
164 "appetites are in advance of the dinner hour.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is just like you, friend Conseil,\" said Ned, impatiently.  \"You"
164 "are never out of temper, always calm; you would return thanks before"
164 "grace, and die of hunger rather than complain!\""
164 ""
164 "Time was getting on, and we were fearfully hungry; and this time the"
164 "steward did not appear.  It was rather too long to leave us, if they"
164 "really had good intentions towards us.  Ned Land, tormented by the"
164 "cravings of hunger, got still more angry; and, notwithstanding his"
164 "promise, I dreaded an explosion when he found himself with one of the"
164 "crew."
164 ""
164 "For two hours more Ned Land's temper increased; he cried, he shouted,"
164 "but in vain.  The walls were deaf.  There was no sound to be heard in"
164 "the boat; all was still as death.  It did not move, for I should have"
164 "felt the trembling motion of the hull under the influence of the screw."
164 "Plunged in the depths of the waters, it belonged no longer to earth:"
164 "this silence was dreadful."
164 ""
164 "I felt terrified, Conseil was calm, Ned Land roared."
164 ""
164 "Just then a noise was heard outside.  Steps sounded on the metal flags."
164 "The locks were turned, the door opened, and the steward appeared."
164 ""
164 "Before I could rush forward to stop him, the Canadian had thrown him"
164 "down, and held him by the throat.  The steward was choking under the"
164 "grip of his powerful hand."
164 ""
164 "Conseil was already trying to unclasp the harpooner's hand from his"
164 "half-suffocated victim, and I was going to fly to the rescue, when"
164 "suddenly I was nailed to the spot by hearing these words in French:"
164 ""
164 "\"Be quiet, Master Land; and you, Professor, will you be so good as to"
164 "listen to me?\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER X"
164 ""
164 "THE MAN OF THE SEAS"
164 ""
164 "It was the commander of the vessel who thus spoke."
164 ""
164 "At these words, Ned Land rose suddenly.  The steward, nearly strangled,"
164 "tottered out on a sign from his master.  But such was the power of the"
164 "commander on board, that not a gesture betrayed the resentment which"
164 "this man must have felt towards the Canadian.  Conseil interested in"
164 "spite of himself, I stupefied, awaited in silence the result of this"
164 "scene."
164 ""
164 "The commander, leaning against the corner of a table with his arms"
164 "folded, scanned us with profound attention.  Did he hesitate to speak?"
164 "Did he regret the words which he had just spoken in French?  One might"
164 "almost think so."
164 ""
164 "After some moments of silence, which not one of us dreamed of breaking,"
164 "\"Gentlemen,\" said he, in a calm and penetrating voice, \"I speak French,"
164 "English, German, and Latin equally well.  I could, therefore, have"
164 "answered you at our first interview, but I wished to know you first,"
164 "then to reflect.  The story told by each one, entirely agreeing in the"
164 "main points, convinced me of your identity.  I know now that chance has"
164 "brought before me M. Pierre Aronnax, Professor of Natural History at"
164 "the Museum of Paris, entrusted with a scientific mission abroad,"
164 "Conseil, his servant, and Ned Land, of Canadian origin, harpooner on"
164 "board the frigate Abraham Lincoln of the navy of the United States of"
164 "America.\""
164 ""
164 "I bowed assent.  It was not a question that the commander put to me."
164 "Therefore there was no answer to be made.  This man expressed himself"
164 "with perfect ease, without any accent.  His sentences were well turned,"
164 "his words clear, and his fluency of speech remarkable.  Yet, I did not"
164 "recognise in him a fellow-countryman."
164 ""
164 "He continued the conversation in these terms:"
164 ""
164 "\"You have doubtless thought, sir, that I have delayed long in paying"
164 "you this second visit.  The reason is that, your identity recognised, I"
164 "wished to weigh maturely what part to act towards you.  I have"
164 "hesitated much.  Most annoying circumstances have brought you into the"
164 "presence of a man who has broken all the ties of humanity.  You have"
164 "come to trouble my existence.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Unintentionally!\" said I."
164 ""
164 "\"Unintentionally?\" replied the stranger, raising his voice a little."
164 "\"Was it unintentionally that the Abraham Lincoln pursued me all over"
164 "the seas?  Was it unintentionally that you took passage in this"
164 "frigate?  Was it unintentionally that your cannon-balls rebounded off"
164 "the plating of my vessel?  Was it unintentionally that Mr. Ned Land"
164 "struck me with his harpoon?\""
164 ""
164 "I detected a restrained irritation in these words.  But to these"
164 "recriminations I had a very natural answer to make, and I made it."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said I, \"no doubt you are ignorant of the discussions which have"
164 "taken place concerning you in America and Europe.  You do not know that"
164 "divers accidents, caused by collisions with your submarine machine,"
164 "have excited public feeling in the two continents.  I omit the theories"
164 "without number by which it was sought to explain that of which you"
164 "alone possess the secret.  But you must understand that, in pursuing"
164 "you over the high seas of the Pacific, the Abraham Lincoln believed"
164 "itself to be chasing some powerful sea-monster, of which it was"
164 "necessary to rid the ocean at any price.\""
164 ""
164 "A half-smile curled the lips of the commander:  then, in a calmer tone:"
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax,\" he replied, \"dare you affirm that your frigate would not"
164 "as soon have pursued and cannonaded a submarine boat as a monster?\""
164 ""
164 "This question embarrassed me, for certainly Captain Farragut might not"
164 "have hesitated.  He might have thought it his duty to destroy a"
164 "contrivance of this kind, as he would a gigantic narwhal."
164 ""
164 "\"You understand then, sir,\" continued the stranger, \"that I have the"
164 "right to treat you as enemies?\""
164 ""
164 "I answered nothing, purposely.  For what good would it be to discuss"
164 "such a proposition, when force could destroy the best arguments?"
164 ""
164 "\"I have hesitated some time,\" continued the commander; \"nothing obliged"
164 "me to show you hospitality.  If I chose to separate myself from you, I"
164 "should have no interest in seeing you again; I could place you upon the"
164 "deck of this vessel which has served you as a refuge, I could sink"
164 "beneath the waters, and forget that you had ever existed.  Would not"
164 "that be my right?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It might be the right of a savage,\" I answered, \"but not that of a"
164 "civilised man.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Professor,\" replied the commander, quickly, \"I am not what you call a"
164 "civilised man!  I have done with society entirely, for reasons which I"
164 "alone have the right of appreciating.  I do not, therefore, obey its"
164 "laws, and I desire you never to allude to them before me again!\""
164 ""
164 "This was said plainly.  A flash of anger and disdain kindled in the"
164 "eyes of the Unknown, and I had a glimpse of a terrible past in the life"
164 "of this man.  Not only had he put himself beyond the pale of human"
164 "laws, but he had made himself independent of them, free in the"
164 "strictest acceptation of the word, quite beyond their reach!  Who then"
164 "would dare to pursue him at the bottom of the sea, when, on its"
164 "surface, he defied all attempts made against him?"
164 ""
164 "What vessel could resist the shock of his submarine monitor?  What"
164 "cuirass, however thick, could withstand the blows of his spur?  No man"
164 "could demand from him an account of his actions; God, if he believed in"
164 "one--his conscience, if he had one--were the sole judges to whom he was"
164 "answerable."
164 ""
164 "These reflections crossed my mind rapidly, whilst the stranger"
164 "personage was silent, absorbed, and as if wrapped up in himself.  I"
164 "regarded him with fear mingled with interest, as, doubtless, OEdiphus"
164 "regarded the Sphinx."
164 ""
164 "After rather a long silence, the commander resumed the conversation."
164 ""
164 "\"I have hesitated,\" said he, \"but I have thought that my interest might"
164 "be reconciled with that pity to which every human being has a right."
164 "You will remain on board my vessel, since fate has cast you there.  You"
164 "will be free; and, in exchange for this liberty, I shall only impose"
164 "one single condition.  Your word of honour to submit to it will"
164 "suffice.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Speak, sir,\" I answered.  \"I suppose this condition is one which a man"
164 "of honour may accept?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir; it is this:  It is possible that certain events, unforeseen,"
164 "may oblige me to consign you to your cabins for some hours or some"
164 "days, as the case may be.  As I desire never to use violence, I expect"
164 "from you, more than all the others, a passive obedience.  In thus"
164 "acting, I take all the responsibility:  I acquit you entirely, for I"
164 "make it an impossibility for you to see what ought not to be seen.  Do"
164 "you accept this condition?\""
164 ""
164 "Then things took place on board which, to say the least, were singular,"
164 "and which ought not to be seen by people who were not placed beyond the"
164 "pale of social laws.  Amongst the surprises which the future was"
164 "preparing for me, this might not be the least."
164 ""
164 "\"We accept,\" I answered; \"only I will ask your permission, sir, to"
164 "address one question to you--one only.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Speak, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You said that we should be free on board.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Entirely.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I ask you, then, what you mean by this liberty?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just the liberty to go, to come, to see, to observe even all that"
164 "passes here save under rare circumstances--the liberty, in short, which"
164 "we enjoy ourselves, my companions and I.\""
164 ""
164 "It was evident that we did not understand one another."
164 ""
164 "\"Pardon me, sir,\" I resumed, \"but this liberty is only what every"
164 "prisoner has of pacing his prison.  It cannot suffice us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It must suffice you, however.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What! we must renounce for ever seeing our country, our friends, our"
164 "relations again?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.  But to renounce that unendurable worldly yoke which men"
164 "believe to be liberty is not perhaps so painful as you think.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well,\" exclaimed Ned Land, \"never will I give my word of honour not to"
164 "try to escape.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I did not ask you for your word of honour, Master Land,\" answered the"
164 "commander, coldly."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" I replied, beginning to get angry in spite of my self, \"you"
164 "abuse your situation towards us; it is cruelty.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir, it is clemency.  You are my prisoners of war.  I keep you,"
164 "when I could, by a word, plunge you into the depths of the ocean.  You"
164 "attacked me.  You came to surprise a secret which no man in the world"
164 "must penetrate--the secret of my whole existence.  And you think that I"
164 "am going to send you back to that world which must know me no more?"
164 "Never!  In retaining you, it is not you whom I guard--it is myself.\""
164 ""
164 "These words indicated a resolution taken on the part of the commander,"
164 "against which no arguments would prevail."
164 ""
164 "\"So, sir,\" I rejoined, \"you give us simply the choice between life and"
164 "death?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Simply.\""
164 ""
164 "\"My friends,\" said I, \"to a question thus put, there is nothing to"
164 "answer.  But no word of honour binds us to the master of this vessel.\""
164 ""
164 "\"None, sir,\" answered the Unknown."
164 ""
164 "Then, in a gentler tone, he continued:"
164 ""
164 "\"Now, permit me to finish what I have to say to you.  I know you, M."
164 "Aronnax.  You and your companions will not, perhaps, have so much to"
164 "complain of in the chance which has bound you to my fate.  You will"
164 "find amongst the books which are my favourite study the work which you"
164 "have published on `the depths of the sea.'  I have often read it.  You"
164 "have carried out your work as far as terrestrial science permitted you."
164 "But you do not know all--you have not seen all.  Let me tell you then,"
164 "Professor, that you will not regret the time passed on board my vessel."
164 "You are going to visit the land of marvels.\""
164 ""
164 "These words of the commander had a great effect upon me.  I cannot deny"
164 "it.  My weak point was touched; and I forgot, for a moment, that the"
164 "contemplation of these sublime subjects was not worth the loss of"
164 "liberty.  Besides, I trusted to the future to decide this grave"
164 "question.  So I contented myself with saying:"
164 ""
164 "\"By what name ought I to address you?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" replied the commander, \"I am nothing to you but Captain Nemo;"
164 "and you and your companions are nothing to me but the passengers of the"
164 "Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo called.  A steward appeared.  The captain gave him his"
164 "orders in that strange language which I did not understand.  Then,"
164 "turning towards the Canadian and Conseil:"
164 ""
164 "\"A repast awaits you in your cabin,\" said he.  \"Be so good as to follow"
164 "this man."
164 ""
164 "\"And now, M. Aronnax, our breakfast is ready.  Permit me to lead the"
164 "way.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I am at your service, Captain.\""
164 ""
164 "I followed Captain Nemo; and as soon as I had passed through the door,"
164 "I found myself in a kind of passage lighted by electricity, similar to"
164 "the waist of a ship.  After we had proceeded a dozen yards, a second"
164 "door opened before me."
164 ""
164 "I then entered a dining-room, decorated and furnished in severe taste."
164 "High oaken sideboards, inlaid with ebony, stood at the two extremities"
164 "of the room, and upon their shelves glittered china, porcelain, and"
164 "glass of inestimable value.  The plate on the table sparkled in the"
164 "rays which the luminous ceiling shed around, while the light was"
164 "tempered and softened by exquisite paintings."
164 ""
164 "In the centre of the room was a table richly laid out.  Captain Nemo"
164 "indicated the place I was to occupy."
164 ""
164 "The breakfast consisted of a certain number of dishes, the contents of"
164 "which were furnished by the sea alone; and I was ignorant of the nature"
164 "and mode of preparation of some of them.  I acknowledged that they were"
164 "good, but they had a peculiar flavour, which I easily became accustomed"
164 "to.  These different aliments appeared to me to be rich in phosphorus,"
164 "and I thought they must have a marine origin."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo looked at me.  I asked him no questions, but he guessed my"
164 "thoughts, and answered of his own accord the questions which I was"
164 "burning to address to him."
164 ""
164 "\"The greater part of these dishes are unknown to you,\" he said to me."
164 "\"However, you may partake of them without fear.  They are wholesome and"
164 "nourishing.  For a long time I have renounced the food of the earth,"
164 "and I am never ill now.  My crew, who are healthy, are fed on the same"
164 "food.\""
164 ""
164 "\"So,\" said I, \"all these eatables are the produce of the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Professor, the sea supplies all my wants.  Sometimes I cast my"
164 "nets in tow, and I draw them in ready to break.  Sometimes I hunt in"
164 "the midst of this element, which appears to be inaccessible to man, and"
164 "quarry the game which dwells in my submarine forests.  My flocks, like"
164 "those of Neptune's old shepherds, graze fearlessly in the immense"
164 "prairies of the ocean.  I have a vast property there, which I cultivate"
164 "myself, and which is always sown by the hand of the Creator of all"
164 "things.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I can understand perfectly, sir, that your nets furnish excellent fish"
164 "for your table; I can understand also that you hunt aquatic game in"
164 "your submarine forests; but I cannot understand at all how a particle"
164 "of meat, no matter how small, can figure in your bill of fare.\""
164 ""
164 "\"This, which you believe to be meat, Professor, is nothing else than"
164 "fillet of turtle.  Here are also some dolphins' livers, which you take"
164 "to be ragout of pork.  My cook is a clever fellow, who excels in"
164 "dressing these various products of the ocean.  Taste all these dishes."
164 "Here is a preserve of sea-cucumber, which a Malay would declare to be"
164 "unrivalled in the world; here is a cream, of which the milk has been"
164 "furnished by the cetacea, and the sugar by the great fucus of the North"
164 "Sea; and, lastly, permit me to offer you some preserve of anemones,"
164 "which is equal to that of the most delicious fruits.\""
164 ""
164 "I tasted, more from curiosity than as a connoisseur, whilst Captain"
164 "Nemo enchanted me with his extraordinary stories."
164 ""
164 "\"You like the sea, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; I love it!  The sea is everything.  It covers seven tenths of the"
164 "terrestrial globe.  Its breath is pure and healthy.  It is an immense"
164 "desert, where man is never lonely, for he feels life stirring on all"
164 "sides.  The sea is only the embodiment of a supernatural and wonderful"
164 "existence.  It is nothing but love and emotion; it is the `Living"
164 "Infinite,' as one of your poets has said.  In fact, Professor, Nature"
164 "manifests herself in it by her three kingdoms--mineral, vegetable, and"
164 "animal.  The sea is the vast reservoir of Nature.  The globe began with"
164 "sea, so to speak; and who knows if it will not end with it?  In it is"
164 "supreme tranquillity.  The sea does not belong to despots.  Upon its"
164 "surface men can still exercise unjust laws, fight, tear one another to"
164 "pieces, and be carried away with terrestrial horrors.  But at thirty"
164 "feet below its level, their reign ceases, their influence is quenched,"
164 "and their power disappears.  Ah! sir, live--live in the bosom of the"
164 "waters!  There only is independence!  There I recognise no masters!"
164 "There I am free!\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo suddenly became silent in the midst of this enthusiasm, by"
164 "which he was quite carried away.  For a few moments he paced up and"
164 "down, much agitated.  Then he became more calm, regained his accustomed"
164 "coldness of expression, and turning towards me:"
164 ""
164 "\"Now, Professor,\" said he, \"if you wish to go over the Nautilus, I am"
164 "at your service.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo rose.  I followed him.  A double door, contrived at the"
164 "back of the dining-room, opened, and I entered a room equal in"
164 "dimensions to that which I had just quitted."
164 ""
164 "It was a library.  High pieces of furniture, of black violet ebony"
164 "inlaid with brass, supported upon their wide shelves a great number of"
164 "books uniformly bound.  They followed the shape of the room,"
164 "terminating at the lower part in huge divans, covered with brown"
164 "leather, which were curved, to afford the greatest comfort.  Light"
164 "movable desks, made to slide in and out at will, allowed one to rest"
164 "one's book while reading.  In the centre stood an immense table,"
164 "covered with pamphlets, amongst which were some newspapers, already of"
164 "old date.  The electric light flooded everything; it was shed from four"
164 "unpolished globes half sunk in the volutes of the ceiling.  I looked"
164 "with real admiration at this room, so ingeniously fitted up, and I"
164 "could scarcely believe my eyes."
164 ""
164 "\"Captain Nemo,\" said I to my host, who had just thrown himself on one"
164 "of the divans, \"this is a library which would do honour to more than"
164 "one of the continental palaces, and I am absolutely astounded when I"
164 "consider that it can follow you to the bottom of the seas.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Where could one find greater solitude or silence, Professor?\" replied"
164 "Captain Nemo.  \"Did your study in the Museum afford you such perfect"
164 "quiet?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; and I must confess that it is a very poor one after yours."
164 "You must have six or seven thousand volumes here.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Twelve thousand, M. Aronnax.  These are the only ties which bind me to"
164 "the earth.  But I had done with the world on the day when my Nautilus"
164 "plunged for the first time beneath the waters.  That day I bought my"
164 "last volumes, my last pamphlets, my last papers, and from that time I"
164 "wish to think that men no longer think or write.  These books,"
164 "Professor, are at your service besides, and you can make use of them"
164 "freely.\""
164 ""
164 "I thanked Captain Nemo, and went up to the shelves of the library."
164 "Works on science, morals, and literature abounded in every language;"
164 "but I did not see one single work on political economy; that subject"
164 "appeared to be strictly proscribed.  Strange to say, all these books"
164 "were irregularly arranged, in whatever language they were written; and"
164 "this medley proved that the Captain of the Nautilus must have read"
164 "indiscriminately the books which he took up by chance."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said I to the Captain, \"I thank you for having placed this"
164 "library at my disposal.  It contains treasures of science, and I shall"
164 "profit by them.\""
164 ""
164 "\"This room is not only a library,\" said Captain Nemo, \"it is also a"
164 "smoking-room.\""
164 ""
164 "\"A smoking-room!\" I cried.  \"Then one may smoke on board?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then, sir, I am forced to believe that you have kept up a"
164 "communication with Havannah.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Not any,\" answered the Captain.  \"Accept this cigar, M. Aronnax; and,"
164 "though it does not come from Havannah, you will be pleased with it, if"
164 "you are a connoisseur.\""
164 ""
164 "I took the cigar which was offered me; its shape recalled the London"
164 "ones, but it seemed to be made of leaves of gold.  I lighted it at a"
164 "little brazier, which was supported upon an elegant bronze stem, and"
164 "drew the first whiffs with the delight of a lover of smoking who has"
164 "not smoked for two days."
164 ""
164 "\"It is excellent, but it is not tobacco.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No!\" answered the Captain, \"this tobacco comes neither from Havannah"
164 "nor from the East.  It is a kind of sea-weed, rich in nicotine, with"
164 "which the sea provides me, but somewhat sparingly.\""
164 ""
164 "At that moment Captain Nemo opened a door which stood opposite to that"
164 "by which I had entered the library, and I passed into an immense"
164 "drawing-room splendidly lighted."
164 ""
164 "It was a vast, four-sided room, thirty feet long, eighteen wide, and"
164 "fifteen high.  A luminous ceiling, decorated with light arabesques,"
164 "shed a soft clear light over all the marvels accumulated in this"
164 "museum.  For it was in fact a museum, in which an intelligent and"
164 "prodigal hand had gathered all the treasures of nature and art, with"
164 "the artistic confusion which distinguishes a painter's studio."
164 ""
164 "Thirty first-rate pictures, uniformly framed, separated by bright"
164 "drapery, ornamented the walls, which were hung with tapestry of severe"
164 "design. I saw works of great value, the greater part of which I had"
164 "admired in the special collections of Europe, and in the exhibitions of"
164 "paintings. The several schools of the old masters were represented by a"
164 "Madonna of Raphael, a Virgin of Leonardo da Vinci, a nymph of Corregio,"
164 "a woman of Titan, an Adoration of Veronese, an Assumption of Murillo, a"
164 "portrait of Holbein, a monk of Velasquez, a martyr of Ribera, a fair of"
164 "Rubens, two Flemish landscapes of Teniers, three little \"genre\""
164 "pictures of Gerard Dow, Metsu, and Paul Potter, two specimens of"
164 "Gericault and Prudhon, and some sea-pieces of Backhuysen and Vernet."
164 "Amongst the works of modern painters were pictures with the signatures"
164 "of Delacroix, Ingres, Decamps, Troyon, Meissonier, Daubigny, etc.; and"
164 "some admirable statues in marble and bronze, after the finest antique"
164 "models, stood upon pedestals in the corners of this magnificent museum."
164 "Amazement, as the Captain of the Nautilus had predicted, had already"
164 "begun to take possession of me."
164 ""
164 "\"Professor,\" said this strange man, \"you must excuse the unceremonious"
164 "way in which I receive you, and the disorder of this room.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" I answered, \"without seeking to know who you are, I recognise in"
164 "you an artist.\""
164 ""
164 "\"An amateur, nothing more, sir.  Formerly I loved to collect these"
164 "beautiful works created by the hand of man.  I sought them greedily,"
164 "and ferreted them out indefatigably, and I have been able to bring"
164 "together some objects of great value.  These are my last souvenirs of"
164 "that world which is dead to me.  In my eyes, your modern artists are"
164 "already old; they have two or three thousand years of existence; I"
164 "confound them in my own mind.  Masters have no age.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And these musicians?\" said I, pointing out some works of Weber,"
164 "Rossini, Mozart, Beethoven, Haydn, Meyerbeer, Herold, Wagner, Auber,"
164 "Gounod, and a number of others, scattered over a large model"
164 "piano-organ which occupied one of the panels of the drawing-room."
164 ""
164 "\"These musicians,\" replied Captain Nemo, \"are the contemporaries of"
164 "Orpheus; for in the memory of the dead all chronological differences"
164 "are effaced; and I am dead, Professor; as much dead as those of your"
164 "friends who are sleeping six feet under the earth!\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo was silent, and seemed lost in a profound reverie. I"
164 "contemplated him with deep interest, analysing in silence the strange"
164 "expression of his countenance. Leaning on his elbow against an angle of"
164 "a costly mosaic table, he no longer saw me,--he had forgotten my"
164 "presence."
164 ""
164 "I did not disturb this reverie, and continued my observation of the"
164 "curiosities which enriched this drawing-room."
164 ""
164 "Under elegant glass cases, fixed by copper rivets, were classed and"
164 "labelled the most precious productions of the sea which had ever been"
164 "presented to the eye of a naturalist.  My delight as a professor may be"
164 "conceived."
164 ""
164 "The division containing the zoophytes presented the most curious"
164 "specimens of the two groups of polypi and echinodermes. In the first"
164 "group, the tubipores, were gorgones arranged like a fan, soft sponges"
164 "of Syria, ises of the Moluccas, pennatules, an admirable virgularia of"
164 "the Norwegian seas, variegated unbellulairae, alcyonariae, a whole"
164 "series of madrepores, which my master Milne Edwards has so cleverly"
164 "classified, amongst which I remarked some wonderful flabellinae"
164 "oculinae of the Island of Bourbon, the \"Neptune's car\" of the Antilles,"
164 "superb varieties of corals--in short, every species of those curious"
164 "polypi of which entire islands are formed, which will one day become"
164 "continents. Of the echinodermes, remarkable for their coating of"
164 "spines, asteri, sea-stars, pantacrinae, comatules, asterophons, echini,"
164 "holothuri, etc., represented individually a complete collection of this"
164 "group."
164 ""
164 "A somewhat nervous conchyliologist would certainly have fainted before"
164 "other more numerous cases, in which were classified the specimens of"
164 "molluscs. It was a collection of inestimable value, which time fails me"
164 "to describe minutely. Amongst these specimens I will quote from memory"
164 "only the elegant royal hammer-fish of the Indian Ocean, whose regular"
164 "white spots stood out brightly on a red and brown ground, an imperial"
164 "spondyle, bright-coloured, bristling with spines, a rare specimen in"
164 "the European museums--(I estimated its value at not less than L1000); a"
164 "common hammer-fish of the seas of New Holland, which is only procured"
164 "with difficulty; exotic buccardia of Senegal; fragile white bivalve"
164 "shells, which a breath might shatter like a soap-bubble; several"
164 "varieties of the aspirgillum of Java, a kind of calcareous tube, edged"
164 "with leafy folds, and much debated by amateurs; a whole series of"
164 "trochi, some a greenish-yellow, found in the American seas, others a"
164 "reddish-brown, natives of Australian waters; others from the Gulf of"
164 "Mexico, remarkable for their imbricated shell; stellari found in the"
164 "Southern Seas; and last, the rarest of all, the magnificent spur of New"
164 "Zealand; and every description of delicate and fragile shells to which"
164 "science has given appropriate names."
164 ""
164 "Apart, in separate compartments, were spread out chaplets of pearls of"
164 "the greatest beauty, which reflected the electric light in little"
164 "sparks of fire; pink pearls, torn from the pinna-marina of the Red Sea;"
164 "green pearls of the haliotyde iris; yellow, blue and black pearls, the"
164 "curious productions of the divers molluscs of every ocean, and certain"
164 "mussels of the water-courses of the North; lastly, several specimens of"
164 "inestimable value which had been gathered from the rarest pintadines."
164 "Some of these pearls were larger than a pigeon's egg, and were worth as"
164 "much, and more than that which the traveller Tavernier sold to the Shah"
164 "of Persia for three millions, and surpassed the one in the possession"
164 "of the Imaum of Muscat, which I had believed to be unrivalled in the"
164 "world."
164 ""
164 "Therefore, to estimate the value of this collection was simply"
164 "impossible.  Captain Nemo must have expended millions in the"
164 "acquirement of these various specimens, and I was thinking what source"
164 "he could have drawn from, to have been able thus to gratify his fancy"
164 "for collecting, when I was interrupted by these words:"
164 ""
164 "\"You are examining my shells, Professor?  Unquestionably they must be"
164 "interesting to a naturalist; but for me they have a far greater charm,"
164 "for I have collected them all with my own hand, and there is not a sea"
164 "on the face of the globe which has escaped my researches.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I can understand, Captain, the delight of wandering about in the midst"
164 "of such riches.  You are one of those who have collected their"
164 "treasures themselves.  No museum in Europe possesses such a collection"
164 "of the produce of the ocean.  But if I exhaust all my admiration upon"
164 "it, I shall have none left for the vessel which carries it.  I do not"
164 "wish to pry into your secrets:  but I must confess that this Nautilus,"
164 "with the motive power which is confined in it, the contrivances which"
164 "enable it to be worked, the powerful agent which propels it, all excite"
164 "my curiosity to the highest pitch.  I see suspended on the walls of"
164 "this room instruments of whose use I am ignorant.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You will find these same instruments in my own room, Professor, where"
164 "I shall have much pleasure in explaining their use to you.  But first"
164 "come and inspect the cabin which is set apart for your own use.  You"
164 "must see how you will be accommodated on board the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "I followed Captain Nemo who, by one of the doors opening from each"
164 "panel of the drawing-room, regained the waist.  He conducted me towards"
164 "the bow, and there I found, not a cabin, but an elegant room, with a"
164 "bed, dressing-table, and several other pieces of excellent furniture."
164 ""
164 "I could only thank my host."
164 ""
164 "\"Your room adjoins mine,\" said he, opening a door, \"and mine opens into"
164 "the drawing-room that we have just quitted.\""
164 ""
164 "I entered the Captain's room:  it had a severe, almost a monkish"
164 "aspect.  A small iron bedstead, a table, some articles for the toilet;"
164 "the whole lighted by a skylight.  No comforts, the strictest"
164 "necessaries only."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo pointed to a seat."
164 ""
164 "\"Be so good as to sit down,\" he said.  I seated myself, and he began"
164 "thus:"
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XI"
164 ""
164 "ALL BY ELECTRICITY"
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said Captain Nemo, showing me the instruments hanging on the"
164 "walls of his room, \"here are the contrivances required for the"
164 "navigation of the Nautilus.  Here, as in the drawing-room, I have them"
164 "always under my eyes, and they indicate my position and exact direction"
164 "in the middle of the ocean.  Some are known to you, such as the"
164 "thermometer, which gives the internal temperature of the Nautilus; the"
164 "barometer, which indicates the weight of the air and foretells the"
164 "changes of the weather; the hygrometer, which marks the dryness of the"
164 "atmosphere; the storm-glass, the contents of which, by decomposing,"
164 "announce the approach of tempests; the compass, which guides my course;"
164 "the sextant, which shows the latitude by the altitude of the sun;"
164 "chronometers, by which I calculate the longitude; and glasses for day"
164 "and night, which I use to examine the points of the horizon, when the"
164 "Nautilus rises to the surface of the waves.\""
164 ""
164 "\"These are the usual nautical instruments,\" I replied, \"and I know the"
164 "use of them.  But these others, no doubt, answer to the particular"
164 "requirements of the Nautilus.  This dial with movable needle is a"
164 "manometer, is it not?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is actually a manometer.  But by communication with the water,"
164 "whose external pressure it indicates, it gives our depth at the same"
164 "time.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And these other instruments, the use of which I cannot guess?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Here, Professor, I ought to give you some explanations.  Will you be"
164 "kind enough to listen to me?\""
164 ""
164 "He was silent for a few moments, then he said:"
164 ""
164 "\"There is a powerful agent, obedient, rapid, easy, which conforms to"
164 "every use, and reigns supreme on board my vessel.  Everything is done"
164 "by means of it.  It lights, warms it, and is the soul of my mechanical"
164 "apparatus.  This agent is electricity.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Electricity?\"  I cried in surprise."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Nevertheless, Captain, you possess an extreme rapidity of movement,"
164 "which does not agree well with the power of electricity.  Until now,"
164 "its dynamic force has remained under restraint, and has only been able"
164 "to produce a small amount of power.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Professor,\" said Captain Nemo, \"my electricity is not everybody's."
164 "You know what sea-water is composed of.  In a thousand grammes are"
164 "found 96 1/2 per cent. of water, and about 2 2/3 per cent.  of chloride"
164 "of sodium; then, in a smaller quantity, chlorides of magnesium and of"
164 "potassium, bromide of magnesium, sulphate of magnesia, sulphate and"
164 "carbonate of lime.  You see, then, that chloride of sodium forms a"
164 "large part of it.  So it is this sodium that I extract from the"
164 "sea-water, and of which I compose my ingredients.  I owe all to the"
164 "ocean; it produces electricity, and electricity gives heat, light,"
164 "motion, and, in a word, life to the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But not the air you breathe?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Oh!  I could manufacture the air necessary for my consumption, but it"
164 "is useless, because I go up to the surface of the water when I please."
164 "However, if electricity does not furnish me with air to breathe, it"
164 "works at least the powerful pumps that are stored in spacious"
164 "reservoirs, and which enable me to prolong at need, and as long as I"
164 "will, my stay in the depths of the sea.  It gives a uniform and"
164 "unintermittent light, which the sun does not.  Now look at this clock;"
164 "it is electrical, and goes with a regularity that defies the best"
164 "chronometers.  I have divided it into twenty-four hours, like the"
164 "Italian clocks, because for me there is neither night nor day, sun nor"
164 "moon, but only that factitious light that I take with me to the bottom"
164 "of the sea.  Look! just now, it is ten o'clock in the morning.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Exactly.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Another application of electricity.  This dial hanging in front of us"
164 "indicates the speed of the Nautilus.  An electric thread puts it in"
164 "communication with the screw, and the needle indicates the real speed."
164 "Look! now we are spinning along with a uniform speed of fifteen miles"
164 "an hour.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is marvelous!  And I see, Captain, you were right to make use of"
164 "this agent that takes the place of wind, water, and steam.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We have not finished, M. Aronnax,\" said Captain Nemo, rising.  \"If you"
164 "will allow me, we will examine the stern of the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "Really, I knew already the anterior part of this submarine boat, of"
164 "which this is the exact division, starting from the ship's head: the"
164 "dining-room, five yards long, separated from the library by a"
164 "water-tight partition; the library, five yards long; the large"
164 "drawing-room, ten yards long, separated from the Captain's room by a"
164 "second water-tight partition; the said room, five yards in length;"
164 "mine, two and a half yards; and, lastly a reservoir of air, seven and a"
164 "half yards, that extended to the bows.  Total length thirty five yards,"
164 "or one hundred and five feet.  The partitions had doors that were shut"
164 "hermetically by means of india-rubber instruments, and they ensured the"
164 "safety of the Nautilus in case of a leak."
164 ""
164 "I followed Captain Nemo through the waist, and arrived at the centre of"
164 "the boat.  There was a sort of well that opened between two partitions."
164 "An iron ladder, fastened with an iron hook to the partition, led to the"
164 "upper end.  I asked the Captain what the ladder was used for."
164 ""
164 "\"It leads to the small boat,\" he said."
164 ""
164 "\"What! have you a boat?\"  I exclaimed, in surprise."
164 ""
164 "\"Of course; an excellent vessel, light and insubmersible, that serves"
164 "either as a fishing or as a pleasure boat.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But then, when you wish to embark, you are obliged to come to the"
164 "surface of the water?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Not at all.  This boat is attached to the upper part of the hull of"
164 "the Nautilus, and occupies a cavity made for it.  It is decked, quite"
164 "water-tight, and held together by solid bolts.  This ladder leads to a"
164 "man-hole made in the hull of the Nautilus, that corresponds with a"
164 "similar hole made in the side of the boat.  By this double opening I"
164 "get into the small vessel.  They shut the one belonging to the"
164 "Nautilus; I shut the other by means of screw pressure.  I undo the"
164 "bolts, and the little boat goes up to the surface of the sea with"
164 "prodigious rapidity.  I then open the panel of the bridge, carefully"
164 "shut till then; I mast it, hoist my sail, take my oars, and I'm off.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But how do you get back on board?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I do not come back, M. Aronnax; the Nautilus comes to me.\""
164 ""
164 "\"By your orders?\""
164 ""
164 "\"By my orders.  An electric thread connects us.  I telegraph to it, and"
164 "that is enough.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Really,\" I said, astonished at these marvels, \"nothing can be more"
164 "simple.\""
164 ""
164 "After having passed by the cage of the staircase that led to the"
164 "platform, I saw a cabin six feet long, in which Conseil and Ned Land,"
164 "enchanted with their repast, were devouring it with avidity.  Then a"
164 "door opened into a kitchen nine feet long, situated between the large"
164 "store-rooms. There electricity, better than gas itself, did all the"
164 "cooking.  The streams under the furnaces gave out to the sponges of"
164 "platina a heat which was regularly kept up and distributed.  They also"
164 "heated a distilling apparatus, which, by evaporation, furnished"
164 "excellent drinkable water.  Near this kitchen was a bathroom"
164 "comfortably furnished, with hot and cold water taps."
164 ""
164 "Next to the kitchen was the berth-room of the vessel, sixteen feet"
164 "long.  But the door was shut, and I could not see the management of it,"
164 "which might have given me an idea of the number of men employed on"
164 "board the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "At the bottom was a fourth partition that separated this office from"
164 "the engine-room. A door opened, and I found myself in the compartment"
164 "where Captain Nemo--certainly an engineer of a very high order--had"
164 "arranged his locomotive machinery.  This engine-room, clearly lighted,"
164 "did not measure less than sixty-five feet in length.  It was divided"
164 "into two parts; the first contained the materials for producing"
164 "electricity, and the second the machinery that connected it with the"
164 "screw.  I examined it with great interest, in order to understand the"
164 "machinery of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "\"You see,\" said the Captain, \"I use Bunsen's contrivances, not"
164 "Ruhmkorff's. Those would not have been powerful enough.  Bunsen's are"
164 "fewer in number, but strong and large, which experience proves to be"
164 "the best.  The electricity produced passes forward, where it works, by"
164 "electro-magnets of great size, on a system of levers and cog-wheels"
164 "that transmit the movement to the axle of the screw.  This one, the"
164 "diameter of which is nineteen feet, and the thread twenty-three feet,"
164 "performs about 120 revolutions in a second.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And you get then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"A speed of fifty miles an hour.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I have seen the Nautilus manoeuvre before the Abraham Lincoln, and I"
164 "have my own ideas as to its speed.  But this is not enough.  We must"
164 "see where we go.  We must be able to direct it to the right, to the"
164 "left, above, below.  How do you get to the great depths, where you find"
164 "an increasing resistance, which is rated by hundreds of atmospheres?"
164 "How do you return to the surface of the ocean?  And how do you maintain"
164 "yourselves in the requisite medium?  Am I asking too much?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Not at all, Professor,\" replied the Captain, with some hesitation;"
164 "\"since you may never leave this submarine boat.  Come into the saloon,"
164 "it is our usual study, and there you will learn all you want to know"
164 "about the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XII"
164 ""
164 "SOME FIGURES"
164 ""
164 "A moment after we were seated on a divan in the saloon smoking.  The"
164 "Captain showed me a sketch that gave the plan, section, and elevation"
164 "of the Nautilus.  Then he began his description in these words:"
164 ""
164 "\"Here, M. Aronnax, are the several dimensions of the boat you are in."
164 "It is an elongated cylinder with conical ends.  It is very like a cigar"
164 "in shape, a shape already adopted in London in several constructions of"
164 "the same sort.  The length of this cylinder, from stem to stern, is"
164 "exactly 232 feet, and its maximum breadth is twenty-six feet.  It is"
164 "not built quite like your long-voyage steamers, but its lines are"
164 "sufficiently long, and its curves prolonged enough, to allow the water"
164 "to slide off easily, and oppose no obstacle to its passage.  These two"
164 "dimensions enable you to obtain by a simple calculation the surface and"
164 "cubic contents of the Nautilus.  Its area measures 6,032 feet; and its"
164 "contents about 1,500 cubic yards; that is to say, when completely"
164 "immersed it displaces 50,000 feet of water, or weighs 1,500 tons."
164 ""
164 "\"When I made the plans for this submarine vessel, I meant that"
164 "nine-tenths should be submerged:  consequently it ought only to"
164 "displace nine-tenths of its bulk, that is to say, only to weigh that"
164 "number of tons.  I ought not, therefore, to have exceeded that weight,"
164 "constructing it on the aforesaid dimensions."
164 ""
164 "\"The Nautilus is composed of two hulls, one inside, the other outside,"
164 "joined by T-shaped irons, which render it very strong.  Indeed, owing"
164 "to this cellular arrangement it resists like a block, as if it were"
164 "solid.  Its sides cannot yield; it coheres spontaneously, and not by"
164 "the closeness of its rivets; and its perfect union of the materials"
164 "enables it to defy the roughest seas."
164 ""
164 "\"These two hulls are composed of steel plates, whose density is from .7"
164 "to .8 that of water.  The first is not less than two inches and a half"
164 "thick and weighs 394 tons.  The second envelope, the keel, twenty"
164 "inches high and ten thick, weighs only sixty-two tons.  The engine, the"
164 "ballast, the several accessories and apparatus appendages, the"
164 "partitions and bulkheads, weigh 961.62 tons.  Do you follow all this?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I do.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then, when the Nautilus is afloat under these circumstances, one-tenth"
164 "is out of the water.  Now, if I have made reservoirs of a size equal to"
164 "this tenth, or capable of holding 150 tons, and if I fill them with"
164 "water, the boat, weighing then 1,507 tons, will be completely immersed."
164 "That would happen, Professor.  These reservoirs are in the lower part"
164 "of the Nautilus.  I turn on taps and they fill, and the vessel sinks"
164 "that had just been level with the surface.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Captain, but now we come to the real difficulty.  I can"
164 "understand your rising to the surface; but, diving below the surface,"
164 "does not your submarine contrivance encounter a pressure, and"
164 "consequently undergo an upward thrust of one atmosphere for every"
164 "thirty feet of water, just about fifteen pounds per square inch?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then, unless you quite fill the Nautilus, I do not see how you can"
164 "draw it down to those depths.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Professor, you must not confound statics with dynamics or you will be"
164 "exposed to grave errors.  There is very little labour spent in"
164 "attaining the lower regions of the ocean, for all bodies have a"
164 "tendency to sink.  When I wanted to find out the necessary increase of"
164 "weight required to sink the Nautilus, I had only to calculate the"
164 "reduction of volume that sea-water acquires according to the depth.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is evident.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Now, if water is not absolutely incompressible, it is at least capable"
164 "of very slight compression.  Indeed, after the most recent calculations"
164 "this reduction is only .000436 of an atmosphere for each thirty feet of"
164 "depth.  If we want to sink 3,000 feet, I should keep account of the"
164 "reduction of bulk under a pressure equal to that of a column of water"
164 "of a thousand feet.  The calculation is easily verified.  Now, I have"
164 "supplementary reservoirs capable of holding a hundred tons.  Therefore"
164 "I can sink to a considerable depth.  When I wish to rise to the level"
164 "of the sea, I only let off the water, and empty all the reservoirs if I"
164 "want the Nautilus to emerge from the tenth part of her total capacity.\""
164 ""
164 "I had nothing to object to these reasonings."
164 ""
164 "\"I admit your calculations, Captain,\" I replied; \"I should be wrong to"
164 "dispute them since daily experience confirms them; but I foresee a real"
164 "difficulty in the way.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What, sir?\""
164 ""
164 "\"When you are about 1,000 feet deep, the walls of the Nautilus bear a"
164 "pressure of 100 atmospheres.  If, then, just now you were to empty the"
164 "supplementary reservoirs, to lighten the vessel, and to go up to the"
164 "surface, the pumps must overcome the pressure of 100 atmospheres, which"
164 "is 1,500 lbs.  per square inch.  From that a power----\""
164 ""
164 "\"That electricity alone can give,\" said the Captain, hastily.  \"I"
164 "repeat, sir, that the dynamic power of my engines is almost infinite."
164 "The pumps of the Nautilus have an enormous power, as you must have"
164 "observed when their jets of water burst like a torrent upon the Abraham"
164 "Lincoln.  Besides, I use subsidiary reservoirs only to attain a mean"
164 "depth of 750 to 1,000 fathoms, and that with a view of managing my"
164 "machines.  Also, when I have a mind to visit the depths of the ocean"
164 "five or six mlles below the surface, I make use of slower but not less"
164 "infallible means.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What are they, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"That involves my telling you how the Nautilus is worked.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I am impatient to learn.\""
164 ""
164 "\"To steer this boat to starboard or port, to turn, in a word, following"
164 "a horizontal plan, I use an ordinary rudder fixed on the back of the"
164 "stern-post, and with one wheel and some tackle to steer by.  But I can"
164 "also make the Nautilus rise and sink, and sink and rise, by a vertical"
164 "movement by means of two inclined planes fastened to its sides,"
164 "opposite the centre of flotation, planes that move in every direction,"
164 "and that are worked by powerful levers from the interior.  If the"
164 "planes are kept parallel with the boat, it moves horizontally.  If"
164 "slanted, the Nautilus, according to this inclination, and under the"
164 "influence of the screw, either sinks diagonally or rises diagonally as"
164 "it suits me.  And even if I wish to rise more quickly to the surface, I"
164 "ship the screw, and the pressure of the water causes the Nautilus to"
164 "rise vertically like a balloon filled with hydrogen.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Bravo, Captain!  But how can the steersman follow the route in the"
164 "middle of the waters?\""
164 ""
164 "\"The steersman is placed in a glazed box, that is raised about the hull"
164 "of the Nautilus, and furnished with lenses.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Are these lenses capable of resisting such pressure?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perfectly.  Glass, which breaks at a blow, is, nevertheless, capable"
164 "of offering considerable resistance.  During some experiments of"
164 "fishing by electric light in 1864 in the Northern Seas, we saw plates"
164 "less than a third of an inch thick resist a pressure of sixteen"
164 "atmospheres.  Now, the glass that I use is not less than thirty times"
164 "thicker.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Granted.  But, after all, in order to see, the light must exceed the"
164 "darkness, and in the midst of the darkness in the water, how can you"
164 "see?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Behind the steersman's cage is placed a powerful electric reflector,"
164 "the rays from which light up the sea for half a mile in front.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! bravo, bravo, Captain!  Now I can account for this phosphorescence"
164 "in the supposed narwhal that puzzled us so.  I now ask you if the"
164 "boarding of the Nautilus and of the Scotia, that has made such a noise,"
164 "has been the result of a chance rencontre?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Quite accidental, sir.  I was sailing only one fathom below the"
164 "surface of the water when the shock came.  It had no bad result.\""
164 ""
164 "\"None, sir.  But now, about your rencontre with the Abraham Lincoln?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Professor, I am sorry for one of the best vessels in the American"
164 "navy; but they attacked me, and I was bound to defend myself.  I"
164 "contented myself, however, with putting the frigate hors de combat; she"
164 "will not have any difficulty in getting repaired at the next port.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah, Commander! your Nautilus is certainly a marvellous boat.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Professor; and I love it as if it were part of myself.  If danger"
164 "threatens one of your vessels on the ocean, the first impression is the"
164 "feeling of an abyss above and below.  On the Nautilus men's hearts"
164 "never fail them.  No defects to be afraid of, for the double shell is"
164 "as firm as iron; no rigging to attend to; no sails for the wind to"
164 "carry away; no boilers to burst; no fire to fear, for the vessel is"
164 "made of iron, not of wood; no coal to run short, for electricity is the"
164 "only mechanical agent; no collision to fear, for it alone swims in deep"
164 "water; no tempest to brave, for when it dives below the water it"
164 "reaches absolute tranquillity.  There, sir! that is the perfection of"
164 "vessels!  And if it is true that the engineer has more confidence in"
164 "the vessel than the builder, and the builder than the captain himself,"
164 "you understand the trust I repose in my Nautilus; for I am at once"
164 "captain, builder, and engineer.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But how could you construct this wonderful Nautilus in secret?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Each separate portion, M. Aronnax, was brought from different parts of"
164 "the globe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But these parts had to be put together and arranged?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Professor, I had set up my workshops upon a desert island in the"
164 "ocean.  There my workmen, that is to say, the brave men that I"
164 "instructed and educated, and myself have put together our Nautilus."
164 "Then, when the work was finished, fire destroyed all trace of our"
164 "proceedings on this island, that I could have jumped over if I had"
164 "liked.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then the cost of this vessel is great?\""
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax, an iron vessel costs L145 per ton.  Now the Nautilus"
164 "weighed 1,500. It came therefore to L67,500, and L80,000 more for"
164 "fitting it up, and about L200,000, with the works of art and the"
164 "collections it contains.\""
164 ""
164 "\"One last question, Captain Nemo.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ask it, Professor.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You are rich?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Immensely rich, sir; and I could, without missing it, pay the national"
164 "debt of France.\""
164 ""
164 "I stared at the singular person who spoke thus.  Was he playing upon my"
164 "credulity?  The future would decide that."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIII"
164 ""
164 "THE BLACK RIVER"
164 ""
164 "The portion of the terrestrial globe which is covered by water is"
164 "estimated at upwards of eighty millions of acres.  This fluid mass"
164 "comprises two billions two hundred and fifty millions of cubic miles,"
164 "forming a spherical body of a diameter of sixty leagues, the weight of"
164 "which would be three quintillions of tons.  To comprehend the meaning"
164 "of these figures, it is necessary to observe that a quintillion is to a"
164 "billion as a billion is to unity; in other words, there are as many"
164 "billions in a quintillion as there are units in a billion.  This mass"
164 "of fluid is equal to about the quantity of water which would be"
164 "discharged by all the rivers of the earth in forty thousand years."
164 ""
164 "During the geological epochs the ocean originally prevailed everywhere."
164 "Then by degrees, in the silurian period, the tops of the mountains"
164 "began to appear, the islands emerged, then disappeared in partial"
164 "deluges, reappeared, became settled, formed continents, till at length"
164 "the earth became geographically arranged, as we see in the present day."
164 "The solid had wrested from the liquid thirty-seven million six hundred"
164 "and fifty-seven square miles, equal to twelve billions nine hundred and"
164 "sixty millions of acres."
164 ""
164 "The shape of continents allows us to divide the waters into five great"
164 "portions:  the Arctic or Frozen Ocean, the Antarctic, or Frozen Ocean,"
164 "the Indian, the Atlantic, and the Pacific Oceans."
164 ""
164 "The Pacific Ocean extends from north to south between the two Polar"
164 "Circles, and from east to west between Asia and America, over an extent"
164 "of 145 degrees of longitude.  It is the quietest of seas; its currents"
164 "are broad and slow, it has medium tides, and abundant rain.  Such was"
164 "the ocean that my fate destined me first to travel over under these"
164 "strange conditions."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said Captain Nemo, \"we will, if you please, take our bearings"
164 "and fix the starting-point of this voyage.  It is a quarter to twelve;"
164 "I will go up again to the surface.\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain pressed an electric clock three times.  The pumps began to"
164 "drive the water from the tanks; the needle of the manometer marked by a"
164 "different pressure the ascent of the Nautilus, then it stopped."
164 ""
164 "\"We have arrived,\" said the Captain."
164 ""
164 "I went to the central staircase which opened on to the platform,"
164 "clambered up the iron steps, and found myself on the upper part of the"
164 "Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "The platform was only three feet out of water.  The front and back of"
164 "the Nautilus was of that spindle-shape which caused it justly to be"
164 "compared to a cigar.  I noticed that its iron plates, slightly"
164 "overlaying each other, resembled the shell which clothes the bodies of"
164 "our large terrestrial reptiles.  It explained to me how natural it was,"
164 "in spite of all glasses, that this boat should have been taken for a"
164 "marine animal."
164 ""
164 "Toward the middle of the platform the longboat, half buried in the hull"
164 "of the vessel, formed a slight excrescence.  Fore and aft rose two"
164 "cages of medium height with inclined sides, and partly closed by thick"
164 "lenticular glasses; one destined for the steersman who directed the"
164 "Nautilus, the other containing a brilliant lantern to give light on the"
164 "road."
164 ""
164 "The sea was beautiful, the sky pure.  Scarcely could the long vehicle"
164 "feel the broad undulations of the ocean.  A light breeze from the east"
164 "rippled the surface of the waters.  The horizon, free from fog, made"
164 "observation easy.  Nothing was in sight.  Not a quicksand, not an"
164 "island.  A vast desert."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo, by the help of his sextant, took the altitude of the sun,"
164 "which ought also to give the latitude.  He waited for some moments till"
164 "its disc touched the horizon.  Whilst taking observations not a muscle"
164 "moved, the instrument could not have been more motionless in a hand of"
164 "marble."
164 ""
164 "\"Twelve o'clock, sir,\" said he.  \"When you like----\""
164 ""
164 "I cast a last look upon the sea, slightly yellowed by the Japanese"
164 "coast, and descended to the saloon."
164 ""
164 "\"And now, sir, I leave you to your studies,\" added the Captain; \"our"
164 "course is E.N.E., our depth is twenty-six fathoms.  Here are maps on a"
164 "large scale by which you may follow it.  The saloon is at your"
164 "disposal, and, with your permission, I will retire.\"  Captain Nemo"
164 "bowed, and I remained alone, lost in thoughts all bearing on the"
164 "commander of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "For a whole hour was I deep in these reflections, seeking to pierce"
164 "this mystery so interesting to me.  Then my eyes fell upon the vast"
164 "planisphere spread upon the table, and I placed my finger on the very"
164 "spot where the given latitude and longitude crossed."
164 ""
164 "The sea has its large rivers like the continents.  They are special"
164 "currents known by their temperature and their colour.  The most"
164 "remarkable of these is known by the name of the Gulf Stream.  Science"
164 "has decided on the globe the direction of five principal currents: one"
164 "in the North Atlantic, a second in the South, a third in the North"
164 "Pacific, a fourth in the South, and a fifth in the Southern Indian"
164 "Ocean.  It is even probable that a sixth current existed at one time or"
164 "another in the Northern Indian Ocean, when the Caspian and Aral Seas"
164 "formed but one vast sheet of water."
164 ""
164 "At this point indicated on the planisphere one of these currents was"
164 "rolling, the Kuro-Scivo of the Japanese, the Black River, which,"
164 "leaving the Gulf of Bengal, where it is warmed by the perpendicular"
164 "rays of a tropical sun, crosses the Straits of Malacca along the coast"
164 "of Asia, turns into the North Pacific to the Aleutian Islands, carrying"
164 "with it trunks of camphor-trees and other indigenous productions, and"
164 "edging the waves of the ocean with the pure indigo of its warm water."
164 "It was this current that the Nautilus was to follow.  I followed it"
164 "with my eye; saw it lose itself in the vastness of the Pacific, and"
164 "felt myself drawn with it, when Ned Land and Conseil appeared at the"
164 "door of the saloon."
164 ""
164 "My two brave companions remained petrified at the sight of the wonders"
164 "spread before them."
164 ""
164 "\"Where are we, where are we?\" exclaimed the Canadian.  \"In the museum"
164 "at Quebec?\""
164 ""
164 "\"My friends,\" I answered, making a sign for them to enter, \"you are not"
164 "in Canada, but on board the Nautilus, fifty yards below the level of"
164 "the sea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, M. Aronnax,\" said Ned Land, \"can you tell me how many men there"
164 "are on board?  Ten, twenty, fifty, a hundred?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I cannot answer you, Mr. Land; it is better to abandon for a time all"
164 "idea of seizing the Nautilus or escaping from it.  This ship is a"
164 "masterpiece of modern industry, and I should be sorry not to have seen"
164 "it.  Many people would accept the situation forced upon us, if only to"
164 "move amongst such wonders.  So be quiet and let us try and see what"
164 "passes around us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"See!\" exclaimed the harpooner, \"but we can see nothing in this iron"
164 "prison!  We are walking--we are sailing--blindly.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land had scarcely pronounced these words when all was suddenly"
164 "darkness.  The luminous ceiling was gone, and so rapidly that my eyes"
164 "received a painful impression."
164 ""
164 "We remained mute, not stirring, and not knowing what surprise awaited"
164 "us, whether agreeable or disagreeable.  A sliding noise was heard: one"
164 "would have said that panels were working at the sides of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "\"It is the end of the end!\" said Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "Suddenly light broke at each side of the saloon, through two oblong"
164 "openings.  The liquid mass appeared vividly lit up by the electric"
164 "gleam.  Two crystal plates separated us from the sea.  At first I"
164 "trembled at the thought that this frail partition might break, but"
164 "strong bands of copper bound them, giving an almost infinite power of"
164 "resistance."
164 ""
164 "The sea was distinctly visible for a mile all round the Nautilus.  What"
164 "a spectacle!  What pen can describe it?  Who could paint the effects of"
164 "the light through those transparent sheets of water, and the softness"
164 "of the successive gradations from the lower to the superior strata of"
164 "the ocean?"
164 ""
164 "We know the transparency of the sea and that its clearness is far"
