164 "beyond that of rock-water. The mineral and organic substances which it"
164 "holds in suspension heightens its transparency.  In certain parts of"
164 "the ocean at the Antilles, under seventy-five fathoms of water, can be"
164 "seen with surprising clearness a bed of sand.  The penetrating power of"
164 "the solar rays does not seem to cease for a depth of one hundred and"
164 "fifty fathoms.  But in this middle fluid travelled over by the"
164 "Nautilus, the electric brightness was produced even in the bosom of the"
164 "waves.  It was no longer luminous water, but liquid light."
164 ""
164 "On each side a window opened into this unexplored abyss.  The obscurity"
164 "of the saloon showed to advantage the brightness outside, and we looked"
164 "out as if this pure crystal had been the glass of an immense aquarium."
164 ""
164 "\"You wished to see, friend Ned; well, you see now.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Curious! curious!\" muttered the Canadian, who, forgetting his"
164 "ill-temper, seemed to submit to some irresistible attraction; \"and one"
164 "would come further than this to admire such a sight!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" thought I to myself, \"I understand the life of this man; he has"
164 "made a world apart for himself, in which he treasures all his greatest"
164 "wonders.\""
164 ""
164 "For two whole hours an aquatic army escorted the Nautilus.  During"
164 "their games, their bounds, while rivalling each other in beauty,"
164 "brightness, and velocity, I distinguished the green labre; the banded"
164 "mullet, marked by a double line of black; the round-tailed goby, of a"
164 "white colour, with violet spots on the back; the Japanese scombrus, a"
164 "beautiful mackerel of these seas, with a blue body and silvery head;"
164 "the brilliant azurors, whose name alone defies description; some banded"
164 "spares, with variegated fins of blue and yellow; the woodcocks of the"
164 "seas, some specimens of which attain a yard in length; Japanese"
164 "salamanders, spider lampreys, serpents six feet long, with eyes small"
164 "and lively, and a huge mouth bristling with teeth; with many other"
164 "species."
164 ""
164 "Our imagination was kept at its height, interjections followed quickly"
164 "on each other.  Ned named the fish, and Conseil classed them.  I was in"
164 "ecstasies with the vivacity of their movements and the beauty of their"
164 "forms.  Never had it been given to me to surprise these animals, alive"
164 "and at liberty, in their natural element.  I will not mention all the"
164 "varieties which passed before my dazzled eyes, all the collection of"
164 "the seas of China and Japan.  These fish, more numerous than the birds"
164 "of the air, came, attracted, no doubt, by the brilliant focus of the"
164 "electric light."
164 ""
164 "Suddenly there was daylight in the saloon, the iron panels closed"
164 "again, and the enchanting vision disappeared.  But for a long time I"
164 "dreamt on, till my eyes fell on the instruments hanging on the"
164 "partition.  The compass still showed the course to be E.N.E., the"
164 "manometer indicated a pressure of five atmospheres, equivalent to a"
164 "depth of twenty five fathoms, and the electric log gave a speed of"
164 "fifteen miles an hour.  I expected Captain Nemo, but he did not appear."
164 "The clock marked the hour of five."
164 ""
164 "Ned Land and Conseil returned to their cabin, and I retired to my"
164 "chamber.  My dinner was ready.  It was composed of turtle soup made of"
164 "the most delicate hawks bills, of a surmullet served with puff paste"
164 "(the liver of which, prepared by itself, was most delicious), and"
164 "fillets of the emperor-holocanthus, the savour of which seemed to me"
164 "superior even to salmon."
164 ""
164 "I passed the evening reading, writing, and thinking.  Then sleep"
164 "overpowered me, and I stretched myself on my couch of zostera, and"
164 "slept profoundly, whilst the Nautilus was gliding rapidly through the"
164 "current of the Black River."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIV"
164 ""
164 "A NOTE OF INVITATION"
164 ""
164 "The next day was the 9th of November.  I awoke after a long sleep of"
164 "twelve hours.  Conseil came, according to custom, to know \"how I passed"
164 "the night,\" and to offer his services.  He had left his friend the"
164 "Canadian sleeping like a man who had never done anything else all his"
164 "life.  I let the worthy fellow chatter as he pleased, without caring to"
164 "answer him.  I was preoccupied by the absence of the Captain during our"
164 "sitting of the day before, and hoping to see him to-day."
164 ""
164 "As soon as I was dressed I went into the saloon.  It was deserted.  I"
164 "plunged into the study of the shell treasures hidden behind the glasses."
164 ""
164 "The whole day passed without my being honoured by a visit from Captain"
164 "Nemo.  The panels of the saloon did not open.  Perhaps they did not"
164 "wish us to tire of these beautiful things."
164 ""
164 "The course of the Nautilus was E.N.E., her speed twelve knots, the"
164 "depth below the surface between twenty-five and thirty fathoms."
164 ""
164 "The next day, 10th of November, the same desertion, the same solitude."
164 "I did not see one of the ship's crew: Ned and Conseil spent the greater"
164 "part of the day with me.  They were astonished at the puzzling absence"
164 "of the Captain.  Was this singular man ill?--had he altered his"
164 "intentions with regard to us?"
164 ""
164 "After all, as Conseil said, we enjoyed perfect liberty, we were"
164 "delicately and abundantly fed.  Our host kept to his terms of the"
164 "treaty.  We could not complain, and, indeed, the singularity of our"
164 "fate reserved such wonderful compensation for us that we had no right"
164 "to accuse it as yet."
164 ""
164 "That day I commenced the journal of these adventures which has enabled"
164 "me to relate them with more scrupulous exactitude and minute detail."
164 ""
164 "11th November, early in the morning.  The fresh air spreading over the"
164 "interior of the Nautilus told me that we had come to the surface of the"
164 "ocean to renew our supply of oxygen.  I directed my steps to the"
164 "central staircase, and mounted the platform."
164 ""
164 "It was six o'clock, the weather was cloudy, the sea grey, but calm."
164 "Scarcely a billow.  Captain Nemo, whom I hoped to meet, would he be"
164 "there?  I saw no one but the steersman imprisoned in his glass cage."
164 "Seated upon the projection formed by the hull of the pinnace, I inhaled"
164 "the salt breeze with delight."
164 ""
164 "By degrees the fog disappeared under the action of the sun's rays, the"
164 "radiant orb rose from behind the eastern horizon.  The sea flamed under"
164 "its glance like a train of gunpowder.  The clouds scattered in the"
164 "heights were coloured with lively tints of beautiful shades, and"
164 "numerous \"mare's tails,\" which betokened wind for that day.  But what"
164 "was wind to this Nautilus, which tempests could not frighten!"
164 ""
164 "I was admiring this joyous rising of the sun, so gay, and so"
164 "life-giving, when I heard steps approaching the platform.  I was"
164 "prepared to salute Captain Nemo, but it was his second (whom I had"
164 "already seen on the Captain's first visit) who appeared.  He advanced"
164 "on the platform, not seeming to see me.  With his powerful glass to his"
164 "eye, he scanned every point of the horizon with great attention.  This"
164 "examination over, he approached the panel and pronounced a sentence in"
164 "exactly these terms.  I have remembered it, for every morning it was"
164 "repeated under exactly the same conditions.  It was thus worded:"
164 ""
164 "\"Nautron respoc lorni virch.\""
164 ""
164 "What it meant I could not say."
164 ""
164 "These words pronounced, the second descended.  I thought that the"
164 "Nautilus was about to return to its submarine navigation.  I regained"
164 "the panel and returned to my chamber."
164 ""
164 "Five days sped thus, without any change in our situation.  Every"
164 "morning I mounted the platform.  The same phrase was pronounced by the"
164 "same individual.  But Captain Nemo did not appear."
164 ""
164 "I had made up my mind that I should never see him again, when, on the"
164 "16th November, on returning to my room with Ned and Conseil, I found"
164 "upon my table a note addressed to me.  I opened it impatiently.  It was"
164 "written in a bold, clear hand, the characters rather pointed, recalling"
164 "the German type.  The note was worded as follows:"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "TO PROFESSOR ARONNAX, On board the Nautilus.  16th of November, 1867."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo invites Professor Aronnax to a hunting-party, which will"
164 "take place to-morrow morning in the forests of the Island of Crespo."
164 "He hopes that nothing will prevent the Professor from being present,"
164 "and he will with pleasure see him joined by his companions."
164 ""
164 "CAPTAIN NEMO, Commander of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 ""
164 "\"A hunt!\" exclaimed Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"And in the forests of the Island of Crespo!\" added Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Oh! then the gentleman is going on terra firma?\" replied Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "\"That seems to me to be clearly indicated,\" said I, reading the letter"
164 "once more."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, we must accept,\" said the Canadian.  \"But once more on dry"
164 "ground, we shall know what to do.  Indeed, I shall not be sorry to eat"
164 "a piece of fresh venison.\""
164 ""
164 "Without seeking to reconcile what was contradictory between Captain"
164 "Nemo's manifest aversion to islands and continents, and his invitation"
164 "to hunt in a forest, I contented myself with replying:"
164 ""
164 "\"Let us first see where the Island of Crespo is.\""
164 ""
164 "I consulted the planisphere, and in 32 deg. 40' N. lat. and 157 deg."
164 "50' W. long., I found a small island, recognised in 1801 by Captain"
164 "Crespo, and marked in the ancient Spanish maps as Rocca de la Plata,"
164 "the meaning of which is The Silver Rock.  We were then about eighteen"
164 "hundred miles from our starting-point, and the course of the Nautilus,"
164 "a little changed, was bringing it back towards the southeast."
164 ""
164 "I showed this little rock, lost in the midst of the North Pacific, to"
164 "my companions."
164 ""
164 "\"If Captain Nemo does sometimes go on dry ground,\" said I, \"he at least"
164 "chooses desert islands.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land shrugged his shoulders without speaking, and Conseil and he"
164 "left me."
164 ""
164 "After supper, which was served by the steward, mute and impassive, I"
164 "went to bed, not without some anxiety."
164 ""
164 "The next morning, the 17th of November, on awakening, I felt that the"
164 "Nautilus was perfectly still.  I dressed quickly and entered the saloon."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo was there, waiting for me.  He rose, bowed, and asked me"
164 "if it was convenient for me to accompany him.  As he made no allusion"
164 "to his absence during the last eight days, I did not mention it, and"
164 "simply answered that my companions and myself were ready to follow him."
164 ""
164 "We entered the dining-room, where breakfast was served."
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax,\" said the Captain, \"pray, share my breakfast without"
164 "ceremony; we will chat as we eat.  For, though I promised you a walk in"
164 "the forest, I did not undertake to find hotels there.  So breakfast as"
164 "a man who will most likely not have his dinner till very late.\""
164 ""
164 "I did honour to the repast.  It was composed of several kinds of fish,"
164 "and slices of sea-cucumber, and different sorts of seaweed.  Our drink"
164 "consisted of pure water, to which the Captain added some drops of a"
164 "fermented liquor, extracted by the Kamschatcha method from a seaweed"
164 "known under the name of Rhodomenia palmata.  Captain Nemo ate at first"
164 "without saying a word.  Then he began:"
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, when I proposed to you to hunt in my submarine forest of Crespo,"
164 "you evidently thought me mad.  Sir, you should never judge lightly of"
164 "any man.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But Captain, believe me----\""
164 ""
164 "\"Be kind enough to listen, and you will then see whether you have any"
164 "cause to accuse me of folly and contradiction.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I listen.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You know as well as I do, Professor, that man can live under water,"
164 "providing he carries with him a sufficient supply of breathable air."
164 "In submarine works, the workman, clad in an impervious dress, with his"
164 "head in a metal helmet, receives air from above by means of forcing"
164 "pumps and regulators.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is a diving apparatus,\" said I."
164 ""
164 "\"Just so, but under these conditions the man is not at liberty; he is"
164 "attached to the pump which sends him air through an india-rubber tube,"
164 "and if we were obliged to be thus held to the Nautilus, we could not go"
164 "far.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And the means of getting free?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"It is to use the Rouquayrol apparatus, invented by two of your own"
164 "countrymen, which I have brought to perfection for my own use, and"
164 "which will allow you to risk yourself under these new physiological"
164 "conditions without any organ whatever suffering.  It consists of a"
164 "reservoir of thick iron plates, in which I store the air under a"
164 "pressure of fifty atmospheres.  This reservoir is fixed on the back by"
164 "means of braces, like a soldier's knapsack.  Its upper part forms a box"
164 "in which the air is kept by means of a bellows, and therefore cannot"
164 "escape unless at its normal tension.  In the Rouquayrol apparatus such"
164 "as we use, two india rubber pipes leave this box and join a sort of"
164 "tent which holds the nose and mouth; one is to introduce fresh air, the"
164 "other to let out the foul, and the tongue closes one or the other"
164 "according to the wants of the respirator.  But I, in encountering great"
164 "pressures at the bottom of the sea, was obliged to shut my head, like"
164 "that of a diver in a ball of copper; and it is to this ball of copper"
164 "that the two pipes, the inspirator and the expirator, open.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perfectly, Captain Nemo; but the air that you carry with you must soon"
164 "be used; when it only contains fifteen per cent.  of oxygen it is no"
164 "longer fit to breathe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Right!  But I told you, M. Aronnax, that the pumps of the Nautilus"
164 "allow me to store the air under considerable pressure, and on those"
164 "conditions the reservoir of the apparatus can furnish breathable air"
164 "for nine or ten hours.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I have no further objections to make,\" I answered.  \"I will only ask"
164 "you one thing, Captain--how can you light your road at the bottom of"
164 "the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"With the Ruhmkorff apparatus, M. Aronnax; one is carried on the back,"
164 "the other is fastened to the waist.  It is composed of a Bunsen pile,"
164 "which I do not work with bichromate of potash, but with sodium.  A wire"
164 "is introduced which collects the electricity produced, and directs it"
164 "towards a particularly made lantern.  In this lantern is a spiral glass"
164 "which contains a small quantity of carbonic gas.  When the apparatus is"
164 "at work this gas becomes luminous, giving out a white and continuous"
164 "light.  Thus provided, I can breathe and I can see.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Captain Nemo, to all my objections you make such crushing answers that"
164 "I dare no longer doubt.  But, if I am forced to admit the Rouquayrol"
164 "and Ruhmkorff apparatus, I must be allowed some reservations with"
164 "regard to the gun I am to carry.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But it is not a gun for powder,\" answered the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"Then it is an air-gun.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Doubtless!  How would you have me manufacture gun powder on board,"
164 "without either saltpetre, sulphur, or charcoal?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Besides,\" I added, \"to fire under water in a medium eight hundred and"
164 "fifty-five times denser than the air, we must conquer very considerable"
164 "resistance.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That would be no difficulty.  There exist guns, according to Fulton,"
164 "perfected in England by Philip Coles and Burley, in France by Furcy,"
164 "and in Italy by Landi, which are furnished with a peculiar system of"
164 "closing, which can fire under these conditions.  But I repeat, having"
164 "no powder, I use air under great pressure, which the pumps of the"
164 "Nautilus furnish abundantly.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But this air must be rapidly used?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, have I not my Rouquayrol reservoir, which can furnish it at"
164 "need?  A tap is all that is required.  Besides M. Aronnax, you must see"
164 "yourself that, during our submarine hunt, we can spend but little air"
164 "and but few balls.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But it seems to me that in this twilight, and in the midst of this"
164 "fluid, which is very dense compared with the atmosphere, shots could"
164 "not go far, nor easily prove mortal.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, on the contrary, with this gun every blow is mortal; and, however"
164 "lightly the animal is touched, it falls as if struck by a thunderbolt.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Because the balls sent by this gun are not ordinary balls, but little"
164 "cases of glass.  These glass cases are covered with a case of steel,"
164 "and weighted with a pellet of lead; they are real Leyden bottles, into"
164 "which the electricity is forced to a very high tension.  With the"
164 "slightest shock they are discharged, and the animal, however strong it"
164 "may be, falls dead.  I must tell you that these cases are size number"
164 "four, and that the charge for an ordinary gun would be ten.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I will argue no longer,\" I replied, rising from the table.  \"I have"
164 "nothing left me but to take my gun.  At all events, I will go where you"
164 "go.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo then led me aft; and in passing before Ned's and Conseil's"
164 "cabin, I called my two companions, who followed promptly.  We then came"
164 "to a cell near the machinery-room, in which we put on our walking-dress."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XV"
164 ""
164 "A WALK ON THE BOTTOM OF THE SEA"
164 ""
164 "This cell was, to speak correctly, the arsenal and wardrobe of the"
164 "Nautilus.  A dozen diving apparatuses hung from the partition waiting"
164 "our use."
164 ""
164 "Ned Land, on seeing them, showed evident repugnance to dress himself in"
164 "one."
164 ""
164 "\"But, my worthy Ned, the forests of the Island of Crespo are nothing"
164 "but submarine forests.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good!\" said the disappointed harpooner, who saw his dreams of fresh"
164 "meat fade away.  \"And you, M. Aronnax, are you going to dress yourself"
164 "in those clothes?\""
164 ""
164 "\"There is no alternative, Master Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you please, sir,\" replied the harpooner, shrugging his shoulders;"
164 "\"but, as for me, unless I am forced, I will never get into one.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No one will force you, Master Ned,\" said Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"Is Conseil going to risk it?\" asked Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"I follow my master wherever he goes,\" replied Conseil."
164 ""
164 "At the Captain's call two of the ship's crew came to help us dress in"
164 "these heavy and impervious clothes, made of india-rubber without seam,"
164 "and constructed expressly to resist considerable pressure.  One would"
164 "have thought it a suit of armour, both supple and resisting.  This suit"
164 "formed trousers and waistcoat.  The trousers were finished off with"
164 "thick boots, weighted with heavy leaden soles.  The texture of the"
164 "waistcoat was held together by bands of copper, which crossed the"
164 "chest, protecting it from the great pressure of the water, and leaving"
164 "the lungs free to act; the sleeves ended in gloves, which in no way"
164 "restrained the movement of the hands.  There was a vast difference"
164 "noticeable between these consummate apparatuses and the old cork"
164 "breastplates, jackets, and other contrivances in vogue during the"
164 "eighteenth century."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo and one of his companions (a sort of Hercules, who must"
164 "have possessed great strength), Conseil and myself were soon enveloped"
164 "in the dresses.  There remained nothing more to be done but to enclose"
164 "our heads in the metal box.  But, before proceeding to this operation,"
164 "I asked the Captain's permission to examine the guns."
164 ""
164 "One of the Nautilus men gave me a simple gun, the butt end of which,"
164 "made of steel, hollow in the centre, was rather large.  It served as a"
164 "reservoir for compressed air, which a valve, worked by a spring,"
164 "allowed to escape into a metal tube.  A box of projectiles in a groove"
164 "in the thickness of the butt end contained about twenty of these"
164 "electric balls, which, by means of a spring, were forced into the"
164 "barrel of the gun.  As soon as one shot was fired, another was ready."
164 ""
164 "\"Captain Nemo,\" said I, \"this arm is perfect, and easily handled: I"
164 "only ask to be allowed to try it.  But how shall we gain the bottom of"
164 "the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"At this moment, Professor, the Nautilus is stranded in five fathoms,"
164 "and we have nothing to do but to start.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But how shall we get off?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You shall see.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo thrust his head into the helmet, Conseil and I did the"
164 "same, not without hearing an ironical \"Good sport!\" from the Canadian."
164 "The upper part of our dress terminated in a copper collar upon which"
164 "was screwed the metal helmet.  Three holes, protected by thick glass,"
164 "allowed us to see in all directions, by simply turning our head in the"
164 "interior of the head-dress. As soon as it was in position, the"
164 "Rouquayrol apparatus on our backs began to act; and, for my part, I"
164 "could breathe with ease."
164 ""
164 "With the Ruhmkorff lamp hanging from my belt, and the gun in my hand, I"
164 "was ready to set out.  But to speak the truth, imprisoned in these"
164 "heavy garments, and glued to the deck by my leaden soles, it was"
164 "impossible for me to take a step."
164 ""
164 "But this state of things was provided for.  I felt myself being pushed"
164 "into a little room contiguous to the wardrobe room.  My companions"
164 "followed, towed along in the same way.  I heard a water-tight door,"
164 "furnished with stopper plates, close upon us, and we were wrapped in"
164 "profound darkness."
164 ""
164 "After some minutes, a loud hissing was heard.  I felt the cold mount"
164 "from my feet to my chest.  Evidently from some part of the vessel they"
164 "had, by means of a tap, given entrance to the water, which was invading"
164 "us, and with which the room was soon filled.  A second door cut in the"
164 "side of the Nautilus then opened.  We saw a faint light.  In another"
164 "instant our feet trod the bottom of the sea."
164 ""
164 "And now, how can I retrace the impression left upon me by that walk"
164 "under the waters?  Words are impotent to relate such wonders!  Captain"
164 "Nemo walked in front, his companion followed some steps behind."
164 "Conseil and I remained near each other, as if an exchange of words had"
164 "been possible through our metallic cases.  I no longer felt the weight"
164 "of my clothing, or of my shoes, of my reservoir of air, or my thick"
164 "helmet, in the midst of which my head rattled like an almond in its"
164 "shell."
164 ""
164 "The light, which lit the soil thirty feet below the surface of the"
164 "ocean, astonished me by its power.  The solar rays shone through the"
164 "watery mass easily, and dissipated all colour, and I clearly"
164 "distinguished objects at a distance of a hundred and fifty yards."
164 "Beyond that the tints darkened into fine gradations of ultramarine, and"
164 "faded into vague obscurity.  Truly this water which surrounded me was"
164 "but another air denser than the terrestrial atmosphere, but almost as"
164 "transparent.  Above me was the calm surface of the sea.  We were"
164 "walking on fine, even sand, not wrinkled, as on a flat shore, which"
164 "retains the impression of the billows.  This dazzling carpet, really a"
164 "reflector, repelled the rays of the sun with wonderful intensity, which"
164 "accounted for the vibration which penetrated every atom of liquid."
164 "Shall I be believed when I say that, at the depth of thirty feet, I"
164 "could see as if I was in broad daylight?"
164 ""
164 "For a quarter of an hour I trod on this sand, sown with the impalpable"
164 "dust of shells.  The hull of the Nautilus, resembling a long shoal,"
164 "disappeared by degrees; but its lantern, when darkness should overtake"
164 "us in the waters, would help to guide us on board by its distinct rays."
164 ""
164 "Soon forms of objects outlined in the distance were discernible.  I"
164 "recognised magnificent rocks, hung with a tapestry of zoophytes of the"
164 "most beautiful kind, and I was at first struck by the peculiar effect"
164 "of this medium."
164 ""
164 "It was then ten in the morning; the rays of the sun struck the surface"
164 "of the waves at rather an oblique angle, and at the touch of their"
164 "light, decomposed by refraction as through a prism, flowers, rocks,"
164 "plants, shells, and polypi were shaded at the edges by the seven solar"
164 "colours.  It was marvellous, a feast for the eyes, this complication of"
164 "coloured tints, a perfect kaleidoscope of green, yellow, orange,"
164 "violet, indigo, and blue; in one word, the whole palette of an"
164 "enthusiastic colourist!  Why could I not communicate to Conseil the"
164 "lively sensations which were mounting to my brain, and rival him in"
164 "expressions of admiration?  For aught I knew, Captain Nemo and his"
164 "companion might be able to exchange thoughts by means of signs"
164 "previously agreed upon.  So, for want of better, I talked to myself; I"
164 "declaimed in the copper box which covered my head, thereby expending"
164 "more air in vain words than was perhaps wise."
164 ""
164 "Various kinds of isis, clusters of pure tuft-coral, prickly fungi, and"
164 "anemones formed a brilliant garden of flowers, decked with their"
164 "collarettes of blue tentacles, sea-stars studding the sandy bottom.  It"
164 "was a real grief to me to crush under my feet the brilliant specimens"
164 "of molluscs which strewed the ground by thousands, of hammerheads,"
164 "donaciae (veritable bounding shells), of staircases, and red"
164 "helmet-shells, angel-wings, and many others produced by this"
164 "inexhaustible ocean.  But we were bound to walk, so we went on, whilst"
164 "above our heads waved medusae whose umbrellas of opal or rose-pink,"
164 "escalloped with a band of blue, sheltered us from the rays of the sun"
164 "and fiery pelagiae, which, in the darkness, would have strewn our path"
164 "with phosphorescent light."
164 ""
164 "All these wonders I saw in the space of a quarter of a mile, scarcely"
164 "stopping, and following Captain Nemo, who beckoned me on by signs."
164 "Soon the nature of the soil changed; to the sandy plain succeeded an"
164 "extent of slimy mud which the Americans call \"ooze,\" composed of equal"
164 "parts of silicious and calcareous shells.  We then travelled over a"
164 "plain of seaweed of wild and luxuriant vegetation.  This sward was of"
164 "close texture, and soft to the feet, and rivalled the softest carpet"
164 "woven by the hand of man.  But whilst verdure was spread at our feet,"
164 "it did not abandon our heads.  A light network of marine plants, of"
164 "that inexhaustible family of seaweeds of which more than two thousand"
164 "kinds are known, grew on the surface of the water."
164 ""
164 "I noticed that the green plants kept nearer the top of the sea, whilst"
164 "the red were at a greater depth, leaving to the black or brown the care"
164 "of forming gardens and parterres in the remote beds of the ocean."
164 ""
164 "We had quitted the Nautilus about an hour and a half.  It was near"
164 "noon; I knew by the perpendicularity of the sun's rays, which were no"
164 "longer refracted.  The magical colours disappeared by degrees, and the"
164 "shades of emerald and sapphire were effaced.  We walked with a regular"
164 "step, which rang upon the ground with astonishing intensity; the"
164 "slightest noise was transmitted with a quickness to which the ear is"
164 "unaccustomed on the earth; indeed, water is a better conductor of sound"
164 "than air, in the ratio of four to one.  At this period the earth sloped"
164 "downwards; the light took a uniform tint.  We were at a depth of a"
164 "hundred and five yards and twenty inches, undergoing a pressure of six"
164 "atmospheres."
164 ""
164 "At this depth I could still see the rays of the sun, though feebly; to"
164 "their intense brilliancy had succeeded a reddish twilight, the lowest"
164 "state between day and night; but we could still see well enough; it was"
164 "not necessary to resort to the Ruhmkorff apparatus as yet.  At this"
164 "moment Captain Nemo stopped; he waited till I joined him, and then"
164 "pointed to an obscure mass, looming in the shadow, at a short distance."
164 ""
164 "\"It is the forest of the Island of Crespo,\" thought I; and I was not"
164 "mistaken."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVI"
164 ""
164 "A SUBMARINE FOREST"
164 ""
164 "We had at last arrived on the borders of this forest, doubtless one of"
164 "the finest of Captain Nemo's immense domains.  He looked upon it as his"
164 "own, and considered he had the same right over it that the first men"
164 "had in the first days of the world.  And, indeed, who would have"
164 "disputed with him the possession of this submarine property?  What"
164 "other hardier pioneer would come, hatchet in hand, to cut down the dark"
164 "copses?"
164 ""
164 "This forest was composed of large tree-plants; and the moment we"
164 "penetrated under its vast arcades, I was struck by the singular"
164 "position of their branches--a position I had not yet observed."
164 ""
164 "Not an herb which carpeted the ground, not a branch which clothed the"
164 "trees, was either broken or bent, nor did they extend horizontally; all"
164 "stretched up to the surface of the ocean.  Not a filament, not a"
164 "ribbon, however thin they might be, but kept as straight as a rod of"
164 "iron.  The fuci and llianas grew in rigid perpendicular lines, due to"
164 "the density of the element which had produced them.  Motionless yet,"
164 "when bent to one side by the hand, they directly resumed their former"
164 "position.  Truly it was the region of perpendicularity!"
164 ""
164 "I soon accustomed myself to this fantastic position, as well as to the"
164 "comparative darkness which surrounded us.  The soil of the forest"
164 "seemed covered with sharp blocks, difficult to avoid.  The submarine"
164 "flora struck me as being very perfect, and richer even than it would"
164 "have been in the arctic or tropical zones, where these productions are"
164 "not so plentiful.  But for some minutes I involuntarily confounded the"
164 "genera, taking animals for plants; and who would not have been"
164 "mistaken?  The fauna and the flora are too closely allied in this"
164 "submarine world."
164 ""
164 "These plants are self-propagated, and the principle of their existence"
164 "is in the water, which upholds and nourishes them.  The greater number,"
164 "instead of leaves, shoot forth blades of capricious shapes, comprised"
164 "within a scale of colours pink, carmine, green, olive, fawn, and brown."
164 ""
164 "\"Curious anomaly, fantastic element!\" said an ingenious naturalist, \"in"
164 "which the animal kingdom blossoms, and the vegetable does not!\""
164 ""
164 "In about an hour Captain Nemo gave the signal to halt; I, for my part,"
164 "was not sorry, and we stretched ourselves under an arbour of alariae,"
164 "the long thin blades of which stood up like arrows."
164 ""
164 "This short rest seemed delicious to me; there was nothing wanting but"
164 "the charm of conversation; but, impossible to speak, impossible to"
164 "answer, I only put my great copper head to Conseil's.  I saw the worthy"
164 "fellow's eyes glistening with delight, and, to show his satisfaction,"
164 "he shook himself in his breastplate of air, in the most comical way in"
164 "the world."
164 ""
164 "After four hours of this walking, I was surprised not to find myself"
164 "dreadfully hungry.  How to account for this state of the stomach I"
164 "could not tell.  But instead I felt an insurmountable desire to sleep,"
164 "which happens to all divers.  And my eyes soon closed behind the thick"
164 "glasses, and I fell into a heavy slumber, which the movement alone had"
164 "prevented before.  Captain Nemo and his robust companion, stretched in"
164 "the clear crystal, set us the example."
164 ""
164 "How long I remained buried in this drowsiness I cannot judge, but, when"
164 "I woke, the sun seemed sinking towards the horizon.  Captain Nemo had"
164 "already risen, and I was beginning to stretch my limbs, when an"
164 "unexpected apparition brought me briskly to my feet."
164 ""
164 "A few steps off, a monstrous sea-spider, about thirty-eight inches"
164 "high, was watching me with squinting eyes, ready to spring upon me."
164 "Though my diver's dress was thick enough to defend me from the bite of"
164 "this animal, I could not help shuddering with horror.  Conseil and the"
164 "sailor of the Nautilus awoke at this moment.  Captain Nemo pointed out"
164 "the hideous crustacean, which a blow from the butt end of the gun"
164 "knocked over, and I saw the horrible claws of the monster writhe in"
164 "terrible convulsions.  This incident reminded me that other animals"
164 "more to be feared might haunt these obscure depths, against whose"
164 "attacks my diving-dress would not protect me.  I had never thought of"
164 "it before, but I now resolved to be upon my guard.  Indeed, I thought"
164 "that this halt would mark the termination of our walk; but I was"
164 "mistaken, for, instead of returning to the Nautilus, Captain Nemo"
164 "continued his bold excursion.  The ground was still on the incline, its"
164 "declivity seemed to be getting greater, and to be leading us to greater"
164 "depths.  It must have been about three o'clock when we reached a narrow"
164 "valley, between high perpendicular walls, situated about seventy-five"
164 "fathoms deep.  Thanks to the perfection of our apparatus, we were"
164 "forty-five fathoms below the limit which nature seems to have imposed"
164 "on man as to his submarine excursions."
164 ""
164 "I say seventy-five fathoms, though I had no instrument by which to"
164 "judge the distance.  But I knew that even in the clearest waters the"
164 "solar rays could not penetrate further.  And accordingly the darkness"
164 "deepened.  At ten paces not an object was visible.  I was groping my"
164 "way, when I suddenly saw a brilliant white light.  Captain Nemo had"
164 "just put his electric apparatus into use; his companion did the same,"
164 "and Conseil and I followed their example.  By turning a screw I"
164 "established a communication between the wire and the spiral glass, and"
164 "the sea, lit by our four lanterns, was illuminated for a circle of"
164 "thirty-six yards."
164 ""
164 "As we walked I thought the light of our Ruhmkorff apparatus could not"
164 "fail to draw some inhabitant from its dark couch.  But if they did"
164 "approach us, they at least kept at a respectful distance from the"
164 "hunters.  Several times I saw Captain Nemo stop, put his gun to his"
164 "shoulder, and after some moments drop it and walk on.  At last, after"
164 "about four hours, this marvellous excursion came to an end.  A wall of"
164 "superb rocks, in an imposing mass, rose before us, a heap of gigantic"
164 "blocks, an enormous, steep granite shore, forming dark grottos, but"
164 "which presented no practicable slope; it was the prop of the Island of"
164 "Crespo.  It was the earth!  Captain Nemo stopped suddenly.  A gesture"
164 "of his brought us all to a halt; and, however desirous I might be to"
164 "scale the wall, I was obliged to stop.  Here ended Captain Nemo's"
164 "domains.  And he would not go beyond them.  Further on was a portion of"
164 "the globe he might not trample upon."
164 ""
164 "The return began.  Captain Nemo had returned to the head of his little"
164 "band, directing their course without hesitation.  I thought we were not"
164 "following the same road to return to the Nautilus.  The new road was"
164 "very steep, and consequently very painful.  We approached the surface"
164 "of the sea rapidly.  But this return to the upper strata was not so"
164 "sudden as to cause relief from the pressure too rapidly, which might"
164 "have produced serious disorder in our organisation, and brought on"
164 "internal lesions, so fatal to divers.  Very soon light reappeared and"
164 "grew, and, the sun being low on the horizon, the refraction edged the"
164 "different objects with a spectral ring.  At ten yards and a half deep,"
164 "we walked amidst a shoal of little fishes of all kinds, more numerous"
164 "than the birds of the air, and also more agile; but no aquatic game"
164 "worthy of a shot had as yet met our gaze, when at that moment I saw the"
164 "Captain shoulder his gun quickly, and follow a moving object into the"
164 "shrubs.  He fired; I heard a slight hissing, and a creature fell"
164 "stunned at some distance from us.  It was a magnificent sea-otter, an"
164 "enhydrus, the only exclusively marine quadruped.  This otter was five"
164 "feet long, and must have been very valuable.  Its skin, chestnut-brown"
164 "above and silvery underneath, would have made one of those beautiful"
164 "furs so sought after in the Russian and Chinese markets: the fineness"
164 "and the lustre of its coat would certainly fetch L80.  I admired this"
164 "curious mammal, with its rounded head ornamented with short ears, its"
164 "round eyes, and white whiskers like those of a cat, with webbed feet"
164 "and nails, and tufted tail.  This precious animal, hunted and tracked"
164 "by fishermen, has now become very rare, and taken refuge chiefly in the"
164 "northern parts of the Pacific, or probably its race would soon become"
164 "extinct."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo's companion took the beast, threw it over his shoulder,"
164 "and we continued our journey.  For one hour a plain of sand lay"
164 "stretched before us.  Sometimes it rose to within two yards and some"
164 "inches of the surface of the water.  I then saw our image clearly"
164 "reflected, drawn inversely, and above us appeared an identical group"
164 "reflecting our movements and our actions; in a word, like us in every"
164 "point, except that they walked with their heads downward and their feet"
164 "in the air."
164 ""
164 "Another effect I noticed, which was the passage of thick clouds which"
164 "formed and vanished rapidly; but on reflection I understood that these"
164 "seeming clouds were due to the varying thickness of the reeds at the"
164 "bottom, and I could even see the fleecy foam which their broken tops"
164 "multiplied on the water, and the shadows of large birds passing above"
164 "our heads, whose rapid flight I could discern on the surface of the sea."
164 ""
164 "On this occasion I was witness to one of the finest gun shots which"
164 "ever made the nerves of a hunter thrill.  A large bird of great breadth"
164 "of wing, clearly visible, approached, hovering over us.  Captain Nemo's"
164 "companion shouldered his gun and fired, when it was only a few yards"
164 "above the waves.  The creature fell stunned, and the force of its fall"
164 "brought it within the reach of dexterous hunter's grasp.  It was an"
164 "albatross of the finest kind."
164 ""
164 "Our march had not been interrupted by this incident.  For two hours we"
164 "followed these sandy plains, then fields of algae very disagreeable to"
164 "cross.  Candidly, I could do no more when I saw a glimmer of light,"
164 "which, for a half mile, broke the darkness of the waters.  It was the"
164 "lantern of the Nautilus.  Before twenty minutes were over we should be"
164 "on board, and I should be able to breathe with ease, for it seemed that"
164 "my reservoir supplied air very deficient in oxygen.  But I did not"
164 "reckon on an accidental meeting which delayed our arrival for some time."
164 ""
164 "I had remained some steps behind, when I presently saw Captain Nemo"
164 "coming hurriedly towards me.  With his strong hand he bent me to the"
164 "ground, his companion doing the same to Conseil.  At first I knew not"
164 "what to think of this sudden attack, but I was soon reassured by seeing"
164 "the Captain lie down beside me, and remain immovable."
164 ""
164 "I was stretched on the ground, just under the shelter of a bush of"
164 "algae, when, raising my head, I saw some enormous mass, casting"
164 "phosphorescent gleams, pass blusteringly by."
164 ""
164 "My blood froze in my veins as I recognised two formidable sharks which"
164 "threatened us.  It was a couple of tintoreas, terrible creatures, with"
164 "enormous tails and a dull glassy stare, the phosphorescent matter"
164 "ejected from holes pierced around the muzzle.  Monstrous brutes! which"
164 "would crush a whole man in their iron jaws.  I did not know whether"
164 "Conseil stopped to classify them; for my part, I noticed their silver"
164 "bellies, and their huge mouths bristling with teeth, from a very"
164 "unscientific point of view, and more as a possible victim than as a"
164 "naturalist."
164 ""
164 "Happily the voracious creatures do not see well.  They passed without"
164 "seeing us, brushing us with their brownish fins, and we escaped by a"
164 "miracle from a danger certainly greater than meeting a tiger full-face"
164 "in the forest.  Half an hour after, guided by the electric light we"
164 "reached the Nautilus.  The outside door had been left open, and Captain"
164 "Nemo closed it as soon as we had entered the first cell.  He then"
164 "pressed a knob.  I heard the pumps working in the midst of the vessel,"
164 "I felt the water sinking from around me, and in a few moments the cell"
164 "was entirely empty.  The inside door then opened, and we entered the"
164 "vestry."
164 ""
164 "There our diving-dress was taken off, not without some trouble, and,"
164 "fairly worn out from want of food and sleep, I returned to my room, in"
164 "great wonder at this surprising excursion at the bottom of the sea."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVII"
164 ""
164 "FOUR THOUSAND LEAGUES UNDER THE PACIFIC"
164 ""
164 "The next morning, the 18th of November, I had quite recovered from my"
164 "fatigues of the day before, and I went up on to the platform, just as"
164 "the second lieutenant was uttering his daily phrase."
164 ""
164 "I was admiring the magnificent aspect of the ocean when Captain Nemo"
164 "appeared.  He did not seem to be aware of my presence, and began a"
164 "series of astronomical observations.  Then, when he had finished, he"
164 "went and leant on the cage of the watch-light, and gazed abstractedly"
164 "on the ocean.  In the meantime, a number of the sailors of the"
164 "Nautilus, all strong and healthy men, had come up onto the platform."
164 "They came to draw up the nets that had been laid all night.  These"
164 "sailors were evidently of different nations, although the European type"
164 "was visible in all of them.  I recognised some unmistakable Irishmen,"
164 "Frenchmen, some Sclaves, and a Greek, or a Candiote.  They were civil,"
164 "and only used that odd language among themselves, the origin of which I"
164 "could not guess, neither could I question them."
164 ""
164 "The nets were hauled in.  They were a large kind of \"chaluts,\" like"
164 "those on the Normandy coasts, great pockets that the waves and a chain"
164 "fixed in the smaller meshes kept open.  These pockets, drawn by iron"
164 "poles, swept through the water, and gathered in everything in their"
164 "way.  That day they brought up curious specimens from those productive"
164 "coasts."
164 ""
164 "I reckoned that the haul had brought in more than nine hundredweight of"
164 "fish.  It was a fine haul, but not to be wondered at.  Indeed, the nets"
164 "are let down for several hours, and enclose in their meshes an infinite"
164 "variety.  We had no lack of excellent food, and the rapidity of the"
164 "Nautilus and the attraction of the electric light could always renew"
164 "our supply.  These several productions of the sea were immediately"
164 "lowered through the panel to the steward's room, some to be eaten"
164 "fresh, and others pickled."
164 ""
164 "The fishing ended, the provision of air renewed, I thought that the"
164 "Nautilus was about to continue its submarine excursion, and was"
164 "preparing to return to my room, when, without further preamble, the"
164 "Captain turned to me, saying:"
164 ""
164 "\"Professor, is not this ocean gifted with real life?  It has its"
164 "tempers and its gentle moods.  Yesterday it slept as we did, and now it"
164 "has woke after a quiet night.  Look!\" he continued, \"it wakes under the"
164 "caresses of the sun.  It is going to renew its diurnal existence.  It"
164 "is an interesting study to watch the play of its organisation.  It has"
164 "a pulse, arteries, spasms; and I agree with the learned Maury, who"
164 "discovered in it a circulation as real as the circulation of blood in"
164 "animals."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, the ocean has indeed circulation, and to promote it, the Creator"
164 "has caused things to multiply in it--caloric, salt, and animalculae.\""
164 ""
164 "When Captain Nemo spoke thus, he seemed altogether changed, and aroused"
164 "an extraordinary emotion in me."
164 ""
164 "\"Also,\" he added, \"true existence is there; and I can imagine the"
164 "foundations of nautical towns, clusters of submarine houses, which,"
164 "like the Nautilus, would ascend every morning to breathe at the surface"
164 "of the water, free towns, independent cities.  Yet who knows whether"
164 "some despot----\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo finished his sentence with a violent gesture.  Then,"
164 "addressing me as if to chase away some sorrowful thought:"
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax,\" he asked, \"do you know the depth of the ocean?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I only know, Captain, what the principal soundings have taught us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Could you tell me them, so that I can suit them to my purpose?\""
164 ""
164 "\"These are some,\" I replied, \"that I remember.  If I am not mistaken, a"
164 "depth of 8,000 yards has been found in the North Atlantic, and 2,500"
164 "yards in the Mediterranean.  The most remarkable soundings have been"
164 "made in the South Atlantic, near the thirty-fifth parallel, and they"
164 "gave 12,000 yards, 14,000 yards, and 15,000 yards.  To sum up all, it"
164 "is reckoned that if the bottom of the sea were levelled, its mean depth"
164 "would be about one and three-quarter leagues.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Professor,\" replied the Captain, \"we shall show you better than"
164 "that I hope.  As to the mean depth of this part of the Pacific, I tell"
164 "you it is only 4,000 yards.\""
164 ""
164 "Having said this, Captain Nemo went towards the panel, and disappeared"
164 "down the ladder.  I followed him, and went into the large drawing-room."
164 "The screw was immediately put in motion, and the log gave twenty miles"
164 "an hour."
164 ""
164 "During the days and weeks that passed, Captain Nemo was very sparing of"
164 "his visits.  I seldom saw him.  The lieutenant pricked the ship's"
164 "course regularly on the chart, so I could always tell exactly the route"
164 "of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "Nearly every day, for some time, the panels of the drawing-room were"
164 "opened, and we were never tired of penetrating the mysteries of the"
164 "submarine world."
164 ""
164 "The general direction of the Nautilus was south-east, and it kept"
164 "between 100 and 150 yards of depth.  One day, however, I do not know"
164 "why, being drawn diagonally by means of the inclined planes, it touched"
164 "the bed of the sea.  The thermometer indicated a temperature of 4.25"
164 "(cent.): a temperature that at this depth seemed common to all"
164 "latitudes."
164 ""
164 "At three o'clock in the morning of the 26th of November the Nautilus"
164 "crossed the tropic of Cancer at 172 deg. long.  On 27th instant it"
164 "sighted the Sandwich Islands, where Cook died, February 14, 1779.  We"
164 "had then gone 4,860 leagues from our starting-point. In the morning,"
164 "when I went on the platform, I saw two miles to windward, Hawaii, the"
164 "largest of the seven islands that form the group.  I saw clearly the"
164 "cultivated ranges, and the several mountain-chains that run parallel"
164 "with the side, and the volcanoes that overtop Mouna-Rea, which rise"
164 "5,000 yards above the level of the sea.  Besides other things the nets"
164 "brought up, were several flabellariae and graceful polypi, that are"
164 "peculiar to that part of the ocean.  The direction of the Nautilus was"
164 "still to the south-east. It crossed the equator December 1, in 142 deg."
164 "long.; and on the 4th of the same month, after crossing rapidly and"
164 "without anything in particular occurring, we sighted the Marquesas"
164 "group.  I saw, three miles off, Martin's peak in Nouka-Hiva, the"
164 "largest of the group that belongs to France.  I only saw the woody"
164 "mountains against the horizon, because Captain Nemo did not wish to"
164 "bring the ship to the wind.  There the nets brought up beautiful"
164 "specimens of fish:  some with azure fins and tails like gold, the flesh"
164 "of which is unrivalled; some nearly destitute of scales, but of"
164 "exquisite flavour; others, with bony jaws, and yellow-tinged gills, as"
164 "good as bonitos; all fish that would be of use to us.  After leaving"
164 "these charming islands protected by the French flag, from the 4th to"
164 "the 11th of December the Nautilus sailed over about 2,000 miles."
164 ""
164 "During the daytime of the 11th of December I was busy reading in the"
164 "large drawing-room. Ned Land and Conseil watched the luminous water"
164 "through the half-open panels.  The Nautilus was immovable.  While its"
164 "reservoirs were filled, it kept at a depth of 1,000 yards, a region"
164 "rarely visited in the ocean, and in which large fish were seldom seen."
164 ""
164 "I was then reading a charming book by Jean Mace, The Slaves of the"
164 "Stomach, and I was learning some valuable lessons from it, when Conseil"
164 "interrupted me."
164 ""
164 "\"Will master come here a moment?\" he said, in a curious voice."
164 ""
164 "\"What is the matter, Conseil?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I want master to look.\""
164 ""
164 "I rose, went, and leaned on my elbows before the panes and watched."
164 ""
164 "In a full electric light, an enormous black mass, quite immovable, was"
164 "suspended in the midst of the waters.  I watched it attentively,"
164 "seeking to find out the nature of this gigantic cetacean.  But a sudden"
164 "thought crossed my mind.  \"A vessel!\" I said, half aloud."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" replied the Canadian, \"a disabled ship that has sunk"
164 "perpendicularly.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land was right; we were close to a vessel of which the tattered"
164 "shrouds still hung from their chains.  The keel seemed to be in good"
164 "order, and it had been wrecked at most some few hours.  Three stumps of"
164 "masts, broken off about two feet above the bridge, showed that the"
164 "vessel had had to sacrifice its masts.  But, lying on its side, it had"
164 "filled, and it was heeling over to port.  This skeleton of what it had"
164 "once been was a sad spectacle as it lay lost under the waves, but"
164 "sadder still was the sight of the bridge, where some corpses, bound"
164 "with ropes, were still lying.  I counted five--four men, one of whom"
164 "was standing at the helm, and a woman standing by the poop, holding an"
164 "infant in her arms.  She was quite young.  I could distinguish her"
164 "features, which the water had not decomposed, by the brilliant light"
164 "from the Nautilus.  In one despairing effort, she had raised her infant"
164 "above her head--poor little thing!--whose arms encircled its mother's"
164 "neck.  The attitude of the four sailors was frightful, distorted as"
164 "they were by their convulsive movements, whilst making a last effort to"
164 "free themselves from the cords that bound them to the vessel.  The"
164 "steersman alone, calm, with a grave, clear face, his grey hair glued to"
164 "his forehead, and his hand clutching the wheel of the helm, seemed even"
164 "then to be guiding the three broken masts through the depths of the"
164 "ocean."
164 ""
164 "What a scene!  We were dumb; our hearts beat fast before this"
164 "shipwreck, taken as it were from life and photographed in its last"
164 "moments.  And I saw already, coming towards it with hungry eyes,"
164 "enormous sharks, attracted by the human flesh."
164 ""
164 "However, the Nautilus, turning, went round the submerged vessel, and in"
164 "one instant I read on the stern--\"The Florida, Sunderland.\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVIII"
164 ""
164 "VANIKORO"
164 ""
164 "This terrible spectacle was the forerunner of the series of maritime"
164 "catastrophes that the Nautilus was destined to meet with in its route."
164 "As long as it went through more frequented waters, we often saw the"
164 "hulls of shipwrecked vessels that were rotting in the depths, and"
164 "deeper down cannons, bullets, anchors, chains, and a thousand other"
164 "iron materials eaten up by rust.  However, on the 11th of December we"
164 "sighted the Pomotou Islands, the old \"dangerous group\" of Bougainville,"
164 "that extend over a space of 500 leagues at E.S.E. to W.N.W., from the"
164 "Island Ducie to that of Lazareff.  This group covers an area of 370"
164 "square leagues, and it is formed of sixty groups of islands, among"
164 "which the Gambier group is remarkable, over which France exercises"
164 "sway.  These are coral islands, slowly raised, but continuous, created"
164 "by the daily work of polypi.  Then this new island will be joined later"
164 "on to the neighboring groups, and a fifth continent will stretch from"
164 "New Zealand and New Caledonia, and from thence to the Marquesas."
164 ""
164 "One day, when I was suggesting this theory to Captain Nemo, he replied"
164 "coldly:"
164 ""
164 "\"The earth does not want new continents, but new men.\""
164 ""
164 "Chance had conducted the Nautilus towards the Island of"
164 "Clermont-Tonnere, one of the most curious of the group, that was"
164 "discovered in 1822 by Captain Bell of the Minerva. I could study now"
164 "the madreporal system, to which are due the islands in this ocean."
164 ""
164 "Madrepores (which must not be mistaken for corals) have a tissue lined"
164 "with a calcareous crust, and the modifications of its structure have"
164 "induced M. Milne Edwards, my worthy master, to class them into five"
164 "sections. The animalcule that the marine polypus secretes live by"
164 "millions at the bottom of their cells. Their calcareous deposits become"
164 "rocks, reefs, and large and small islands. Here they form a ring,"
164 "surrounding a little inland lake, that communicates with the sea by"
164 "means of gaps. There they make barriers of reefs like those on the"
164 "coasts of New Caledonia and the various Pomoton islands. In other"
164 "places, like those at Reunion and at Maurice, they raise fringed reefs,"
164 "high, straight walls, near which the depth of the ocean is considerable."
164 ""
164 "Some cable-lengths off the shores of the Island of Clermont I admired"
164 "the gigantic work accomplished by these microscopical workers. These"
164 "walls are specially the work of those madrepores known as milleporas,"
164 "porites, madrepores, and astraeas. These polypi are found particularly"
164 "in the rough beds of the sea, near the surface; and consequently it is"
164 "from the upper part that they begin their operations, in which they"
164 "bury themselves by degrees with the debris of the secretions that"
164 "support them. Such is, at least, Darwin's theory, who thus explains the"
164 "formation of the _atolls_, a superior theory (to my mind) to that given"
164 "of the foundation of the madreporical works, summits of mountains or"
164 "volcanoes, that are submerged some feet below the level of the sea."
164 ""
164 "I could observe closely these curious walls, for perpendicularly they"
164 "were more than 300 yards deep, and our electric sheets lighted up this"
164 "calcareous matter brilliantly. Replying to a question Conseil asked me"
164 "as to the time these colossal barriers took to be raised, I astonished"
164 "him much by telling him that learned men reckoned it about the eighth"
164 "of an inch in a hundred years."
164 ""
164 "Towards evening Clermont-Tonnerre was lost in the distance, and the"
164 "route of the Nautilus was sensibly changed. After having crossed the"
164 "tropic of Capricorn in 135 deg. longitude, it sailed W.N.W., making"
164 "again for the tropical zone. Although the summer sun was very strong,"
164 "we did not suffer from heat, for at fifteen or twenty fathoms below the"
164 "surface, the temperature did not rise above from ten to twelve degrees."
164 ""
164 "On 15th of December, we left to the east the bewitching group of the"
164 "Societies and the graceful Tahiti, queen of the Pacific.  I saw in the"
164 "morning, some miles to the windward, the elevated summits of the"
164 "island.  These waters furnished our table with excellent fish,"
164 "mackerel, bonitos, and some varieties of a sea-serpent."
164 ""
164 "On the 25th of December the Nautilus sailed into the midst of the New"
164 "Hebrides, discovered by Quiros in 1606, and that Bougainville explored"
164 "in 1768, and to which Cook gave its present name in 1773.  This group"
164 "is composed principally of nine large islands, that form a band of 120"
164 "leagues N.N.S. to S.S.W., between 15 deg. and 2 deg. S. lat., and 164"
164 "deg. and 168 deg. long.  We passed tolerably near to the Island of"
164 "Aurou, that at noon looked like a mass of green woods, surmounted by a"
164 "peak of great height."
164 ""
164 "That day being Christmas Day, Ned Land seemed to regret sorely the"
164 "non-celebration of \"Christmas,\" the family fete of which Protestants"
164 "are so fond.  I had not seen Captain Nemo for a week, when, on the"
164 "morning of the 27th, he came into the large drawing-room, always"
164 "seeming as if he had seen you five minutes before.  I was busily"
164 "tracing the route of the Nautilus on the planisphere.  The Captain came"
164 "up to me, put his finger on one spot on the chart, and said this single"
164 "word."
164 ""
164 "\"Vanikoro.\""
164 ""
164 "The effect was magical!  It was the name of the islands on which La"
164 "Perouse had been lost!  I rose suddenly."
164 ""
164 "\"The Nautilus has brought us to Vanikoro?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Professor,\" said the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"And I can visit the celebrated islands where the Boussole and the"
164 "Astrolabe struck?\""
164 ""
164 "\"If you like, Professor.\""
164 ""
164 "\"When shall we be there?\""
164 ""
164 "\"We are there now.\""
164 ""
164 "Followed by Captain Nemo, I went up on to the platform, and greedily"
164 "scanned the horizon."
164 ""
164 "To the N.E. two volcanic islands emerged of unequal size, surrounded by"
164 "a coral reef that measured forty miles in circumference.  We were close"
164 "to Vanikoro, really the one to which Dumont d'Urville gave the name of"
164 "Isle de la Recherche, and exactly facing the little harbour of Vanou,"
164 "situated in 16 deg. 4' S. lat., and 164 deg. 32' E. long.  The earth"
164 "seemed covered with verdure from the shore to the summits in the"
164 "interior, that were crowned by Mount Kapogo, 476 feet high.  The"
164 "Nautilus, having passed the outer belt of rocks by a narrow strait,"
164 "found itself among breakers where the sea was from thirty to forty"
164 "fathoms deep.  Under the verdant shade of some mangroves I perceived"
164 "some savages, who appeared greatly surprised at our approach.  In the"
164 "long black body, moving between wind and water, did they not see some"
164 "formidable cetacean that they regarded with suspicion?"
164 ""
164 "Just then Captain Nemo asked me what I knew about the wreck of La"
164 "Perouse."
164 ""
164 "\"Only what everyone knows, Captain,\" I replied."
164 ""
164 "\"And could you tell me what everyone knows about it?\" he inquired,"
164 "ironically."
164 ""
164 "\"Easily.\""
164 ""
164 "I related to him all that the last works of Dumont d'Urville had made"
164 "known--works from which the following is a brief account."
164 ""
164 "La Perouse, and his second, Captain de Langle, were sent by Louis XVI,"
164 "in 1785, on a voyage of circumnavigation.  They embarked in the"
164 "corvettes Boussole and the Astrolabe, neither of which were again heard"
164 "of.  In 1791, the French Government, justly uneasy as to the fate of"
164 "these two sloops, manned two large merchantmen, the Recherche and the"
164 "Esperance, which left Brest the 28th of September under the command of"
164 "Bruni d'Entrecasteaux."
164 ""
164 "Two months after, they learned from Bowen, commander of the Albemarle,"
164 "that the debris of shipwrecked vessels had been seen on the coasts of"
164 "New Georgia.  But D'Entrecasteaux, ignoring this communication--rather"
164 "uncertain, besides--directed his course towards the Admiralty Islands,"
164 "mentioned in a report of Captain Hunter's as being the place where La"
164 "Perouse was wrecked."
164 ""
164 "They sought in vain.  The Esperance and the Recherche passed before"
164 "Vanikoro without stopping there, and, in fact, this voyage was most"
164 "disastrous, as it cost D'Entrecasteaux his life, and those of two of"
164 "his lieutenants, besides several of his crew."
164 ""
164 "Captain Dillon, a shrewd old Pacific sailor, was the first to find"
164 "unmistakable traces of the wrecks.  On the 15th of May, 1824, his"
164 "vessel, the St. Patrick, passed close to Tikopia, one of the New"
164 "Hebrides.  There a Lascar came alongside in a canoe, sold him the"
164 "handle of a sword in silver that bore the print of characters engraved"
164 "on the hilt.  The Lascar pretended that six years before, during a stay"
164 "at Vanikoro, he had seen two Europeans that belonged to some vessels"
164 "that had run aground on the reefs some years ago."
164 ""
164 "Dillon guessed that he meant La Perouse, whose disappearance had"
164 "troubled the whole world.  He tried to get on to Vanikoro, where,"
164 "according to the Lascar, he would find numerous debris of the wreck,"
164 "but winds and tides prevented him."
164 ""
164 "Dillon returned to Calcutta.  There he interested the Asiatic Society"
164 "and the Indian Company in his discovery.  A vessel, to which was given"
164 "the name of the Recherche, was put at his disposal, and he set out,"
164 "23rd January, 1827, accompanied by a French agent."
164 ""
164 "The Recherche, after touching at several points in the Pacific, cast"
164 "anchor before Vanikoro, 7th July, 1827, in that same harbour of Vanou"
164 "where the Nautilus was at this time."
164 ""
164 "There it collected numerous relics of the wreck--iron utensils,"
164 "anchors, pulley-strops, swivel-guns, an 18 lb. shot, fragments of"
164 "astronomical instruments, a piece of crown work, and a bronze clock,"
164 "bearing this inscription--\"Bazin m'a fait,\" the mark of the foundry of"
164 "the arsenal at Brest about 1785.  There could be no further doubt."
164 ""
164 "Dillon, having made all inquiries, stayed in the unlucky place till"
164 "October.  Then he quitted Vanikoro, and directed his course towards New"
164 "Zealand; put into Calcutta, 7th April, 1828, and returned to France,"
164 "where he was warmly welcomed by Charles X."
164 ""
164 "But at the same time, without knowing Dillon's movements, Dumont"
164 "d'Urville had already set out to find the scene of the wreck.  And they"
164 "had learned from a whaler that some medals and a cross of St. Louis had"
164 "been found in the hands of some savages of Louisiade and New Caledonia."
164 "Dumont d'Urville, commander of the Astrolabe, had then sailed, and two"
164 "months after Dillon had left Vanikoro he put into Hobart Town.  There"
164 "he learned the results of Dillon's inquiries, and found that a certain"
164 "James Hobbs, second lieutenant of the Union of Calcutta, after landing"
164 "on an island situated 8 deg. 18' S. lat., and 156 deg. 30' E. long.,"
164 "had seen some iron bars and red stuffs used by the natives of these"
164 "parts.  Dumont d'Urville, much perplexed, and not knowing how to credit"
164 "the reports of low-class journals, decided to follow Dillon's track."
164 ""
164 "On the 10th of February, 1828, the Astrolabe appeared off Tikopia, and"
164 "took as guide and interpreter a deserter found on the island; made his"
164 "way to Vanikoro, sighted it on the 12th inst., lay among the reefs"
164 "until the 14th, and not until the 20th did he cast anchor within the"
164 "barrier in the harbour of Vanou."
164 ""
164 "On the 23rd, several officers went round the island and brought back"
164 "some unimportant trifles.  The natives, adopting a system of denials"
164 "and evasions, refused to take them to the unlucky place.  This"
164 "ambiguous conduct led them to believe that the natives had ill-treated"
164 "the castaways, and indeed they seemed to fear that Dumont d'Urville had"
164 "come to avenge La Perouse and his unfortunate crew."
164 ""
164 "However, on the 26th, appeased by some presents, and understanding that"
164 "they had no reprisals to fear, they led M. Jacquireot to the scene of"
164 "the wreck."
164 ""
164 "There, in three or four fathoms of water, between the reefs of Pacou"
164 "and Vanou, lay anchors, cannons, pigs of lead and iron, embedded in the"
164 "limy concretions.  The large boat and the whaler belonging to the"
164 "Astrolabe were sent to this place, and, not without some difficulty,"
164 "their crews hauled up an anchor weighing 1,800 lbs., a brass gun, some"
164 "pigs of iron, and two copper swivel-guns."
164 ""
164 "Dumont d'Urville, questioning the natives, learned too that La Perouse,"
164 "after losing both his vessels on the reefs of this island, had"
164 "constructed a smaller boat, only to be lost a second time.  Where, no"
164 "one knew."
164 ""
164 "But the French Government, fearing that Dumont d'Urville was not"
164 "acquainted with Dillon's movements, had sent the sloop Bayonnaise,"
164 "commanded by Legoarant de Tromelin, to Vanikoro, which had been"
164 "stationed on the west coast of America.  The Bayonnaise cast her anchor"
164 "before Vanikoro some months after the departure of the Astrolabe, but"
164 "found no new document; but stated that the savages had respected the"
164 "monument to La Perouse.  That is the substance of what I told Captain"
164 "Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"So,\" he said, \"no one knows now where the third vessel perished that"
164 "was constructed by the castaways on the island of Vanikoro?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No one knows.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo said nothing, but signed to me to follow him into the"
164 "large saloon.  The Nautilus sank several yards below the waves, and the"
164 "panels were opened."
164 ""
164 "I hastened to the aperture, and under the crustations of coral, covered"
164 "with fungi, syphonules, alcyons, madrepores, through myriads of"
164 "charming fish--girelles, glyphisidri, pompherides, diacopes, and"
164 "holocentres--I recognised certain debris that the drags had not been"
164 "able to tear up--iron stirrups, anchors, cannons, bullets, capstan"
164 "fittings, the stem of a ship, all objects clearly proving the wreck of"
164 "some vessel, and now carpeted with living flowers. While I was looking"
164 "on this desolate scene, Captain Nemo said, in a sad voice:"
164 ""
164 "\"Commander La Perouse set out 7th December, 1785, with his vessels La"
164 "Boussole and the Astrolabe.  He first cast anchor at Botany Bay,"
164 "visited the Friendly Isles, New Caledonia, then directed his course"
164 "towards Santa Cruz, and put into Namouka, one of the Hapai group.  Then"
164 "his vessels struck on the unknown reefs of Vanikoro.  The Boussole,"
164 "which went first, ran aground on the southerly coast.  The Astrolabe"
164 "went to its help, and ran aground too.  The first vessel was destroyed"
164 "almost immediately.  The second, stranded under the wind, resisted some"
164 "days.  The natives made the castaways welcome.  They installed"
164 "themselves in the island, and constructed a smaller boat with the"
164 "debris of the two large ones.  Some sailors stayed willingly at"
164 "Vanikoro; the others, weak and ill, set out with La Perouse.  They"
164 "directed their course towards the Solomon Islands, and there perished,"
164 "with everything, on the westerly coast of the chief island of the"
164 "group, between Capes Deception and Satisfaction.\""
164 ""
164 "\"How do you know that?\""
164 ""
164 "\"By this, that I found on the spot where was the last wreck.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo showed me a tin-plate box, stamped with the French arms,"
164 "and corroded by the salt water.  He opened it, and I saw a bundle of"
164 "papers, yellow but still readable."
164 ""
164 "They were the instructions of the naval minister to Commander La"
164 "Perouse, annotated in the margin in Louis XVI's handwriting."
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! it is a fine death for a sailor!\" said Captain Nemo, at last.  \"A"
164 "coral tomb makes a quiet grave; and I trust that I and my comrades will"
164 "find no other.\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIX"
164 ""
164 "TORRES STRAITS"
164 ""
164 "During the night of the 27th or 28th of December, the Nautilus left the"
164 "shores of Vanikoro with great speed.  Her course was south-westerly,"
164 "and in three days she had gone over the 750 leagues that separated it"
164 "from La Perouse's group and the south-east point of Papua."
164 ""
164 "Early on the 1st of January, 1863, Conseil joined me on the platform."
164 ""
164 "\"Master, will you permit me to wish you a happy New Year?\""
164 ""
164 "\"What!  Conseil; exactly as if I was at Paris in my study at the Jardin"
164 "des Plantes?  Well, I accept your good wishes, and thank you for them."
164 "Only, I will ask you what you mean by a `Happy New Year' under our"
164 "circumstances?  Do you mean the year that will bring us to the end of"
164 "our imprisonment, or the year that sees us continue this strange"
164 "voyage?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Really, I do not know how to answer, master.  We are sure to see"
164 "curious things, and for the last two months we have not had time for"
164 "dullness.  The last marvel is always the most astonishing; and, if we"
164 "continue this progression, I do not know how it will end.  It is my"
164 "opinion that we shall never again see the like.  I think then, with no"
164 "offence to master, that a happy year would be one in which we could see"
164 "everything.\""
164 ""
164 "On 2nd January we had made 11,340 miles, or 5,250 French leagues, since"
164 "our starting-point in the Japan Seas.  Before the ship's head stretched"
164 "the dangerous shores of the coral sea, on the north-east coast of"
164 "Australia.  Our boat lay along some miles from the redoubtable bank on"
164 "which Cook's vessel was lost, 10th June, 1770.  The boat in which Cook"
164 "was struck on a rock, and, if it did not sink, it was owing to a piece"
164 "of coral that was broken by the shock, and fixed itself in the broken"
164 "keel."
164 ""
164 "I had wished to visit the reef, 360 leagues long, against which the"
164 "sea, always rough, broke with great violence, with a noise like"
164 "thunder.  But just then the inclined planes drew the Nautilus down to a"
164 "great depth, and I could see nothing of the high coral walls.  I had to"
164 "content myself with the different specimens of fish brought up by the"
164 "nets.  I remarked, among others, some germons, a species of mackerel as"
164 "large as a tunny, with bluish sides, and striped with transverse bands,"
164 "that disappear with the animal's life."
164 ""
164 "These fish followed us in shoals, and furnished us with very delicate"
164 "food. We took also a large number of gilt-heads, about one and a half"
164 "inches long, tasting like dorys; and flying pyrapeds like submarine"
164 "swallows, which, in dark nights, light alternately the air and water"
164 "with their phosphorescent light. Among the molluscs and zoophytes, I"
164 "found in the meshes of the net several species of alcyonarians, echini,"
164 "hammers, spurs, dials, cerites, and hyalleae. The flora was represented"
164 "by beautiful floating seaweeds, laminariae, and macrocystes,"
164 "impregnated with the mucilage that transudes through their pores; and"
164 "among which I gathered an admirable Nemastoma Geliniarois, that was"
164 "classed among the natural curiosities of the museum."
164 ""
164 "Two days after crossing the coral sea, 4th January, we sighted the"
164 "Papuan coasts.  On this occasion, Captain Nemo informed me that his"
164 "intention was to get into the Indian Ocean by the Strait of Torres."
164 "His communication ended there."
164 ""
164 "The Torres Straits are nearly thirty-four leagues wide; but they are"
164 "obstructed by an innumerable quantity of islands, islets, breakers, and"
164 "rocks, that make its navigation almost impracticable; so that Captain"
164 "Nemo took all needful precautions to cross them.  The Nautilus,"
164 "floating betwixt wind and water, went at a moderate pace.  Her screw,"
164 "like a cetacean's tail, beat the waves slowly."
164 ""
164 "Profiting by this, I and my two companions went up on to the deserted"
164 "platform.  Before us was the steersman's cage, and I expected that"
164 "Captain Nemo was there directing the course of the Nautilus.  I had"
164 "before me the excellent charts of the Straits of Torres, and I"
164 "consulted them attentively.  Round the Nautilus the sea dashed"
164 "furiously.  The course of the waves, that went from south-east to"
164 "north-west at the rate of two and a half miles, broke on the coral that"
164 "showed itself here and there."
164 ""
164 "\"This is a bad sea!\" remarked Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "\"Detestable indeed, and one that does not suit a boat like the"
164 "Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The Captain must be very sure of his route, for I see there pieces of"
164 "coral that would do for its keel if it only touched them slightly.\""
164 ""
164 "Indeed the situation was dangerous, but the Nautilus seemed to slide"
164 "like magic off these rocks.  It did not follow the routes of the"
164 "Astrolabe and the Zelee exactly, for they proved fatal to Dumont"
164 "d'Urville. It bore more northwards, coasted the Islands of Murray, and"
164 "came back to the south-west towards Cumberland Passage.  I thought it"
164 "was going to pass it by, when, going back to north-west, it went"
164 "through a large quantity of islands and islets little known, towards"
164 "the Island Sound and Canal Mauvais."
164 ""
164 "I wondered if Captain Nemo, foolishly imprudent, would steer his vessel"
164 "into that pass where Dumont d'Urville's two corvettes touched; when,"
164 "swerving again, and cutting straight through to the west, he steered"
164 "for the Island of Gilboa."
164 ""
164 "It was then three in the afternoon.  The tide began to recede, being"
164 "quite full.  The Nautilus approached the island, that I still saw, with"
164 "its remarkable border of screw-pines. He stood off it at about two"
164 "miles distant.  Suddenly a shock overthrew me.  The Nautilus just"
164 "touched a rock, and stayed immovable, laying lightly to port side."
164 ""
164 "When I rose, I perceived Captain Nemo and his lieutenant on the"
164 "platform.  They were examining the situation of the vessel, and"
164 "exchanging words in their incomprehensible dialect."
164 ""
164 "She was situated thus:  Two miles, on the starboard side, appeared"
164 "Gilboa, stretching from north to west like an immense arm.  Towards the"
164 "south and east some coral showed itself, left by the ebb.  We had run"
164 "aground, and in one of those seas where the tides are middling--a sorry"
164 "matter for the floating of the Nautilus.  However, the vessel had not"
164 "suffered, for her keel was solidly joined.  But, if she could neither"
164 "glide off nor move, she ran the risk of being for ever fastened to"
164 "these rocks, and then Captain Nemo's submarine vessel would be done for."
164 ""
164 "I was reflecting thus, when the Captain, cool and calm, always master"
164 "of himself, approached me."
164 ""
164 "\"An accident?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"No; an incident.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But an incident that will oblige you perhaps to become an inhabitant"
164 "of this land from which you flee?\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo looked at me curiously, and made a negative gesture, as"
164 "much as to say that nothing would force him to set foot on terra firma"
164 "again.  Then he said:"
164 ""
164 "\"Besides, M. Aronnax, the Nautilus is not lost; it will carry you yet"
164 "into the midst of the marvels of the ocean.  Our voyage is only begun,"
164 "and I do not wish to be deprived so soon of the honour of your company.\""
164 ""
164 "\"However, Captain Nemo,\" I replied, without noticing the ironical turn"
164 "of his phrase, \"the Nautilus ran aground in open sea.  Now the tides"
164 "are not strong in the Pacific; and, if you cannot lighten the Nautilus,"
164 "I do not see how it will be reinflated.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The tides are not strong in the Pacific:  you are right there,"
164 "Professor; but in Torres Straits one finds still a difference of a yard"
164 "and a half between the level of high and low seas.  To-day is 4th"
164 "January, and in five days the moon will be full.  Now, I shall be very"
164 "much astonished if that satellite does not raise these masses of water"
164 "sufficiently, and render me a service that I should be indebted to her"
164 "for.\""
164 ""
164 "Having said this, Captain Nemo, followed by his lieutenant, redescended"
164 "to the interior of the Nautilus.  As to the vessel, it moved not, and"
164 "was immovable, as if the coralline polypi had already walled it up with"
164 "their in destructible cement."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir?\" said Ned Land, who came up to me after the departure of"
164 "the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, friend Ned, we will wait patiently for the tide on the 9th"
164 "instant; for it appears that the moon will have the goodness to put it"
164 "off again.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Really?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Really.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And this Captain is not going to cast anchor at all since the tide"
164 "will suffice?\" said Conseil, simply."
164 ""
164 "The Canadian looked at Conseil, then shrugged his shoulders."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, you may believe me when I tell you that this piece of iron will"
164 "navigate neither on nor under the sea again; it is only fit to be sold"
164 "for its weight.  I think, therefore, that the time has come to part"
164 "company with Captain Nemo.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Friend Ned, I do not despair of this stout Nautilus, as you do; and in"
