164 "on the horizon, till about four o'clock a steamer running west on our"
164 "counter.  Her masts were visible for an instant, but she could not see"
164 "the Nautilus, being too low in the water.  I fancied this steamboat"
164 "belonged to the P.O. Company, which runs from Ceylon to Sydney,"
164 "touching at King George's Point and Melbourne."
164 ""
164 "At five o'clock in the evening, before that fleeting twilight which"
164 "binds night to day in tropical zones, Conseil and I were astonished by"
164 "a curious spectacle."
164 ""
164 "It was a shoal of argonauts travelling along on the surface of the"
164 "ocean.  We could count several hundreds.  They belonged to the tubercle"
164 "kind which are peculiar to the Indian seas."
164 ""
164 "These graceful molluscs moved backwards by means of their locomotive"
164 "tube, through which they propelled the water already drawn in.  Of"
164 "their eight tentacles, six were elongated, and stretched out floating"
164 "on the water, whilst the other two, rolled up flat, were spread to the"
164 "wing like a light sail.  I saw their spiral-shaped and fluted shells,"
164 "which Cuvier justly compares to an elegant skiff.  A boat indeed!  It"
164 "bears the creature which secretes it without its adhering to it."
164 ""
164 "For nearly an hour the Nautilus floated in the midst of this shoal of"
164 "molluscs.  Then I know not what sudden fright they took.  But as if at"
164 "a signal every sail was furled, the arms folded, the body drawn in, the"
164 "shells turned over, changing their centre of gravity, and the whole"
164 "fleet disappeared under the waves.  Never did the ships of a squadron"
164 "manoeuvre with more unity."
164 ""
164 "At that moment night fell suddenly, and the reeds, scarcely raised by"
164 "the breeze, lay peaceably under the sides of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "The next day, 26th of January, we cut the equator at the eighty-second"
164 "meridian and entered the northern hemisphere.  During the day a"
164 "formidable troop of sharks accompanied us, terrible creatures, which"
164 "multiply in these seas and make them very dangerous.  They were"
164 "\"cestracio philippi\" sharks, with brown backs and whitish bellies,"
164 "armed with eleven rows of teeth--eyed sharks--their throat being marked"
164 "with a large black spot surrounded with white like an eye.  There were"
164 "also some Isabella sharks, with rounded snouts marked with dark spots."
164 "These powerful creatures often hurled themselves at the windows of the"
164 "saloon with such violence as to make us feel very insecure.  At such"
164 "times Ned Land was no longer master of himself.  He wanted to go to the"
164 "surface and harpoon the monsters, particularly certain smooth-hound"
164 "sharks, whose mouth is studded with teeth like a mosaic; and large"
164 "tiger-sharks nearly six yards long, the last named of which seemed to"
164 "excite him more particularly.  But the Nautilus, accelerating her"
164 "speed, easily left the most rapid of them behind."
164 ""
164 "The 27th of January, at the entrance of the vast Bay of Bengal, we met"
164 "repeatedly a forbidding spectacle, dead bodies floating on the surface"
164 "of the water.  They were the dead of the Indian villages, carried by"
164 "the Ganges to the level of the sea, and which the vultures, the only"
164 "undertakers of the country, had not been able to devour.  But the"
164 "sharks did not fail to help them at their funeral work."
164 ""
164 "About seven o'clock in the evening, the Nautilus, half-immersed, was"
164 "sailing in a sea of milk.  At first sight the ocean seemed lactified."
164 "Was it the effect of the lunar rays?  No; for the moon, scarcely two"
164 "days old, was still lying hidden under the horizon in the rays of the"
164 "sun.  The whole sky, though lit by the sidereal rays, seemed black by"
164 "contrast with the whiteness of the waters."
164 ""
164 "Conseil could not believe his eyes, and questioned me as to the cause"
164 "of this strange phenomenon.  Happily I was able to answer him."
164 ""
164 "\"It is called a milk sea,\" I explained.  \"A large extent of white"
164 "wavelets often to be seen on the coasts of Amboyna, and in these parts"
164 "of the sea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, sir,\" said Conseil, \"can you tell me what causes such an effect?"
164 "for I suppose the water is not really turned into milk.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, my boy; and the whiteness which surprises you is caused only by"
164 "the presence of myriads of infusoria, a sort of luminous little worm,"
164 "gelatinous and without colour, of the thickness of a hair, and whose"
164 "length is not more than seven-thousandths of an inch.  These insects"
164 "adhere to one another sometimes for several leagues.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Several leagues!\" exclaimed Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, my boy; and you need not try to compute the number of these"
164 "infusoria.  You will not be able, for, if I am not mistaken, ships have"
164 "floated on these milk seas for more than forty miles.\""
164 ""
164 "Towards midnight the sea suddenly resumed its usual colour; but behind"
164 "us, even to the limits of the horizon, the sky reflected the whitened"
164 "waves, and for a long time seemed impregnated with the vague"
164 "glimmerings of an aurora borealis."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER II"
164 ""
164 "A NOVEL PROPOSAL OF CAPTAIN NEMO'S"
164 ""
164 "On the 28th of February, when at noon the Nautilus came to the surface"
164 "of the sea, in 9 deg. 4' N. lat., there was land in sight about eight"
164 "miles to westward.  The first thing I noticed was a range of mountains"
164 "about two thousand feet high, the shapes of which were most capricious."
164 "On taking the bearings, I knew that we were nearing the island of"
164 "Ceylon, the pearl which hangs from the lobe of the Indian Peninsula."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo and his second appeared at this moment.  The Captain"
164 "glanced at the map.  Then turning to me, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"The Island of Ceylon, noted for its pearl-fisheries. Would you like to"
164 "visit one of them, M. Aronnax?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly, Captain.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, the thing is easy.  Though, if we see the fisheries, we shall"
164 "not see the fishermen.  The annual exportation has not yet begun."
164 "Never mind, I will give orders to make for the Gulf of Manaar, where we"
164 "shall arrive in the night.\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain said something to his second, who immediately went out."
164 "Soon the Nautilus returned to her native element, and the manometer"
164 "showed that she was about thirty feet deep."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir,\" said Captain Nemo, \"you and your companions shall visit"
164 "the Bank of Manaar, and if by chance some fisherman should be there, we"
164 "shall see him at work.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Agreed, Captain!\""
164 ""
164 "\"By the bye, M. Aronnax you are not afraid of sharks?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sharks!\" exclaimed I."
164 ""
164 "This question seemed a very hard one."
164 ""
164 "\"Well?\" continued Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"I admit, Captain, that I am not yet very familiar with that kind of"
164 "fish.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We are accustomed to them,\" replied Captain Nemo, \"and in time you"
164 "will be too.  However, we shall be armed, and on the road we may be"
164 "able to hunt some of the tribe.  It is interesting.  So, till"
164 "to-morrow, sir, and early.\""
164 ""
164 "This said in a careless tone, Captain Nemo left the saloon.  Now, if"
164 "you were invited to hunt the bear in the mountains of Switzerland, what"
164 "would you say?"
164 ""
164 "\"Very well! to-morrow we will go and hunt the bear.\" If you were asked"
164 "to hunt the lion in the plains of Atlas, or the tiger in the Indian"
164 "jungles, what would you say?"
164 ""
164 "\"Ha! ha! it seems we are going to hunt the tiger or the lion!\" But when"
164 "you are invited to hunt the shark in its natural element, you would"
164 "perhaps reflect before accepting the invitation.  As for myself, I"
164 "passed my hand over my forehead, on which stood large drops of cold"
164 "perspiration.  \"Let us reflect,\" said I, \"and take our time.  Hunting"
164 "otters in submarine forests, as we did in the Island of Crespo, will"
164 "pass; but going up and down at the bottom of the sea, where one is"
164 "almost certain to meet sharks, is quite another thing!  I know well"
164 "that in certain countries, particularly in the Andaman Islands, the"
164 "negroes never hesitate to attack them with a dagger in one hand and a"
164 "running noose in the other; but I also know that few who affront those"
164 "creatures ever return alive.  However, I am not a negro, and if I were"
164 "I think a little hesitation in this case would not be ill-timed.\""
164 ""
164 "At this moment Conseil and the Canadian entered, quite composed, and"
164 "even joyous.  They knew not what awaited them."
164 ""
164 "\"Faith, sir,\" said Ned Land, \"your Captain Nemo--the devil take"
164 "him!--has just made us a very pleasant offer.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" said I, \"you know?\""
164 ""
164 "\"If agreeable to you, sir,\" interrupted Conseil, \"the commander of the"
164 "Nautilus has invited us to visit the magnificent Ceylon fisheries"
164 "to-morrow, in your company; he did it kindly, and behaved like a real"
164 "gentleman.\""
164 ""
164 "\"He said nothing more?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Nothing more, sir, except that he had already spoken to you of this"
164 "little walk.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said Conseil, \"would you give us some details of the pearl"
164 "fishery?\""
164 ""
164 "\"As to the fishing itself,\" I asked, \"or the incidents, which?\""
164 ""
164 "\"On the fishing,\" replied the Canadian; \"before entering upon the"
164 "ground, it is as well to know something about it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very well; sit down, my friends, and I will teach you.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned and Conseil seated themselves on an ottoman, and the first thing"
164 "the Canadian asked was:"
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, what is a pearl?\""
164 ""
164 "\"My worthy Ned,\" I answered, \"to the poet, a pearl is a tear of the"
164 "sea; to the Orientals, it is a drop of dew solidified; to the ladies,"
164 "it is a jewel of an oblong shape, of a brilliancy of mother-of-pearl"
164 "substance, which they wear on their fingers, their necks, or their"
164 "ears; for the chemist it is a mixture of phosphate and carbonate of"
164 "lime, with a little gelatine; and lastly, for naturalists, it is simply"
164 "a morbid secretion of the organ that produces the mother-of-pearl"
164 "amongst certain bivalves.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Branch of molluscs,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Precisely so, my learned Conseil; and, amongst these testacea the"
164 "earshell, the tridacnae, the turbots, in a word, all those which"
164 "secrete mother-of-pearl, that is, the blue, bluish, violet, or white"
164 "substance which lines the interior of their shells, are capable of"
164 "producing pearls.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Mussels too?\" asked the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, mussels of certain waters in Scotland, Wales, Ireland, Saxony,"
164 "Bohemia, and France.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good!  For the future I shall pay attention,\" replied the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"But,\" I continued, \"the particular mollusc which secretes the pearl is"
164 "the pearl-oyster, the meleagrina margaritiferct, that precious"
164 "pintadine. The pearl is nothing but a nacreous formation, deposited in"
164 "a globular form, either adhering to the oyster shell, or buried in the"
164 "folds of the creature. On the shell it is fast; in the flesh it is"
164 "loose; but always has for a kernel a small hard substance, may be a"
164 "barren egg, may be a grain of sand, around which the pearly matter"
164 "deposits itself year after year successively, and by thin concentric"
164 "layers.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Are many pearls found in the same oyster?\" asked Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, my boy.  Some are a perfect casket.  One oyster has been"
164 "mentioned, though I allow myself to doubt it, as having contained no"
164 "less than a hundred and fifty sharks.\""
164 ""
164 "\"A hundred and fifty sharks!\" exclaimed Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "\"Did I say sharks?\" said I hurriedly.  \"I meant to say a hundred and"
164 "fifty pearls.  Sharks would not be sense.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly not,\" said Conseil; \"but will you tell us now by what means"
164 "they extract these pearls?\""
164 ""
164 "\"They proceed in various ways.  When they adhere to the shell, the"
164 "fishermen often pull them off with pincers; but the most common way is"
164 "to lay the oysters on mats of the seaweed which covers the banks.  Thus"
164 "they die in the open air; and at the end of ten days they are in a"
164 "forward state of decomposition.  They are then plunged into large"
164 "reservoirs of sea-water; then they are opened and washed.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The price of these pearls varies according to their size?\" asked"
164 "Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Not only according to their size,\" I answered, \"but also according to"
164 "their shape, their water (that is, their colour), and their lustre:"
164 "that is, that bright and diapered sparkle which makes them so charming"
164 "to the eye.  The most beautiful are called virgin pearls, or paragons."
164 "They are formed alone in the tissue of the mollusc, are white, often"
164 "opaque, and sometimes have the transparency of an opal; they are"
164 "generally round or oval.  The round are made into bracelets, the oval"
164 "into pendants, and, being more precious, are sold singly.  Those"
164 "adhering to the shell of the oyster are more irregular in shape, and"
164 "are sold by weight.  Lastly, in a lower order are classed those small"
164 "pearls known under the name of seed-pearls; they are sold by measure,"
164 "and are especially used in embroidery for church ornaments.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But,\" said Conseil, \"is this pearl-fishery dangerous?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No,\" I answered, quickly; \"particularly if certain precautions are"
164 "taken.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What does one risk in such a calling?\" said Ned Land, \"the swallowing"
164 "of some mouthfuls of sea-water?\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you say, Ned.  By the bye,\" said I, trying to take Captain Nemo's"
164 "careless tone, \"are you afraid of sharks, brave Ned?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I!\" replied the Canadian; \"a harpooner by profession?  It is my trade"
164 "to make light of them.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But,\" said I, \"it is not a question of fishing for them with an"
164 "iron-swivel, hoisting them into the vessel, cutting off their tails"
164 "with a blow of a chopper, ripping them up, and throwing their heart"
164 "into the sea!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then, it is a question of----\""
164 ""
164 "\"Precisely.\""
164 ""
164 "\"In the water?\""
164 ""
164 "\"In the water.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Faith, with a good harpoon!  You know, sir, these sharks are"
164 "ill-fashioned beasts.  They turn on their bellies to seize you, and in"
164 "that time----\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land had a way of saying \"seize\" which made my blood run cold."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, and you, Conseil, what do you think of sharks?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Me!\" said Conseil.  \"I will be frank, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"So much the better,\" thought I."
164 ""
164 "\"If you, sir, mean to face the sharks, I do not see why your faithful"
164 "servant should not face them with you.\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER III"
164 ""
164 "A PEARL OF TEN MILLIONS"
164 ""
164 "The next morning at four o'clock I was awakened by the steward whom"
164 "Captain Nemo had placed at my service.  I rose hurriedly, dressed, and"
164 "went into the saloon."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo was awaiting me."
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax,\" said he, \"are you ready to start?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I am ready.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then please to follow me.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And my companions, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"They have been told and are waiting.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Are we not to put on our diver's dresses?\" asked I."
164 ""
164 "\"Not yet.  I have not allowed the Nautilus to come too near this coast,"
164 "and we are some distance from the Manaar Bank; but the boat is ready,"
164 "and will take us to the exact point of disembarking, which will save us"
164 "a long way.  It carries our diving apparatus, which we will put on when"
164 "we begin our submarine journey.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo conducted me to the central staircase, which led on the"
164 "platform.  Ned and Conseil were already there, delighted at the idea of"
164 "the \"pleasure party\" which was preparing.  Five sailors from the"
164 "Nautilus, with their oars, waited in the boat, which had been made fast"
164 "against the side."
164 ""
164 "The night was still dark.  Layers of clouds covered the sky, allowing"
164 "but few stars to be seen.  I looked on the side where the land lay, and"
164 "saw nothing but a dark line enclosing three parts of the horizon, from"
164 "south-west to north west.  The Nautilus, having returned during the"
164 "night up the western coast of Ceylon, was now west of the bay, or"
164 "rather gulf, formed by the mainland and the Island of Manaar.  There,"
164 "under the dark waters, stretched the pintadine bank, an inexhaustible"
164 "field of pearls, the length of which is more than twenty miles."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo, Ned Land, Conseil, and I took our places in the stern of"
164 "the boat.  The master went to the tiller; his four companions leaned on"
164 "their oars, the painter was cast off, and we sheered off."
164 ""
164 "The boat went towards the south; the oarsmen did not hurry.  I noticed"
164 "that their strokes, strong in the water, only followed each other every"
164 "ten seconds, according to the method generally adopted in the navy."
164 "Whilst the craft was running by its own velocity, the liquid drops"
164 "struck the dark depths of the waves crisply like spats of melted lead."
164 "A little billow, spreading wide, gave a slight roll to the boat, and"
164 "some samphire reeds flapped before it."
164 ""
164 "We were silent.  What was Captain Nemo thinking of?  Perhaps of the"
164 "land he was approaching, and which he found too near to him, contrary"
164 "to the Canadian's opinion, who thought it too far off.  As to Conseil,"
164 "he was merely there from curiosity."
164 ""
164 "About half-past five the first tints on the horizon showed the upper"
164 "line of coast more distinctly.  Flat enough in the east, it rose a"
164 "little to the south.  Five miles still lay between us, and it was"
164 "indistinct owing to the mist on the water.  At six o'clock it became"
164 "suddenly daylight, with that rapidity peculiar to tropical regions,"
164 "which know neither dawn nor twilight.  The solar rays pierced the"
164 "curtain of clouds, piled up on the eastern horizon, and the radiant orb"
164 "rose rapidly.  I saw land distinctly, with a few trees scattered here"
164 "and there.  The boat neared Manaar Island, which was rounded to the"
164 "south.  Captain Nemo rose from his seat and watched the sea."
164 ""
164 "At a sign from him the anchor was dropped, but the chain scarcely ran,"
164 "for it was little more than a yard deep, and this spot was one of the"
164 "highest points of the bank of pintadines."
164 ""
164 "\"Here we are, M. Aronnax,\" said Captain Nemo.  \"You see that enclosed"
164 "bay?  Here, in a month will be assembled the numerous fishing boats of"
164 "the exporters, and these are the waters their divers will ransack so"
164 "boldly.  Happily, this bay is well situated for that kind of fishing."
164 "It is sheltered from the strongest winds; the sea is never very rough"
164 "here, which makes it favourable for the diver's work.  We will now put"
164 "on our dresses, and begin our walk.\""
164 ""
164 "I did not answer, and, while watching the suspected waves, began with"
164 "the help of the sailors to put on my heavy sea-dress. Captain Nemo and"
164 "my companions were also dressing.  None of the Nautilus men were to"
164 "accompany us on this new excursion."
164 ""
164 "Soon we were enveloped to the throat in india-rubber clothing; the air"
164 "apparatus fixed to our backs by braces.  As to the Ruhmkorff apparatus,"
164 "there was no necessity for it.  Before putting my head into the copper"
164 "cap, I had asked the question of the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"They would be useless,\" he replied.  \"We are going to no great depth,"
164 "and the solar rays will be enough to light our walk.  Besides, it would"
164 "not be prudent to carry the electric light in these waters; its"
164 "brilliancy might attract some of the dangerous inhabitants of the coast"
164 "most inopportunely.\""
164 ""
164 "As Captain Nemo pronounced these words, I turned to Conseil and Ned"
164 "Land.  But my two friends had already encased their heads in the metal"
164 "cap, and they could neither hear nor answer."
164 ""
164 "One last question remained to ask of Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"And our arms?\" asked I; \"our guns?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Guns!  What for?  Do not mountaineers attack the bear with a dagger in"
164 "their hand, and is not steel surer than lead?  Here is a strong blade;"
164 "put it in your belt, and we start.\""
164 ""
164 "I looked at my companions; they were armed like us, and, more than"
164 "that, Ned Land was brandishing an enormous harpoon, which he had placed"
164 "in the boat before leaving the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "Then, following the Captain's example, I allowed myself to be dressed"
164 "in the heavy copper helmet, and our reservoirs of air were at once in"
164 "activity.  An instant after we were landed, one after the other, in"
164 "about two yards of water upon an even sand.  Captain Nemo made a sign"
164 "with his hand, and we followed him by a gentle declivity till we"
164 "disappeared under the waves."
164 ""
164 "Over our feet, like coveys of snipe in a bog, rose shoals of fish, of"
164 "the genus monoptera, which have no other fins but their tail. I"
164 "recognized the Javanese, a real serpent two and a half feet long, of a"
164 "livid colour underneath, and which might easily be mistaken for a"
164 "conger eel if it were not for the golden stripes on its side. In the"
164 "genus stromateus, whose bodies are very flat and oval, I saw some of"
164 "the most brilliant colours, carrying their dorsal fin like a scythe; an"
164 "excellent eating fish, which, dried and pickled, is known by the name"
164 "of Karawade; then some tranquebars, belonging to the genus"
164 "apsiphoroides, whose body is covered with a shell cuirass of eight"
164 "longitudinal plates."
164 ""
164 "The heightening sun lit the mass of waters more and more. The soil"
164 "changed by degrees. To the fine sand succeeded a perfect causeway of"
164 "boulders, covered with a carpet of molluscs and zoophytes. Amongst the"
164 "specimens of these branches I noticed some placenae, with thin unequal"
164 "shells, a kind of ostracion peculiar to the Red Sea and the Indian"
164 "Ocean; some orange lucinae with rounded shells; rockfish three feet and"
164 "a half long, which raised themselves under the waves like hands ready"
164 "to seize one. There were also some panopyres, slightly luminous; and"
164 "lastly, some oculines, like magnificent fans, forming one of the"
164 "richest vegetations of these seas."
164 ""
164 "In the midst of these living plants, and under the arbours of the"
164 "hydrophytes, were layers of clumsy articulates, particularly some"
164 "raninae, whose carapace formed a slightly rounded triangle; and some"
164 "horrible looking parthenopes."
164 ""
164 "At about seven o'clock we found ourselves at last surveying the"
164 "oyster-banks on which the pearl-oysters are reproduced by millions."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo pointed with his hand to the enormous heap of oysters; and"
164 "I could well understand that this mine was inexhaustible, for Nature's"
164 "creative power is far beyond man's instinct of destruction.  Ned Land,"
164 "faithful to his instinct, hastened to fill a net which he carried by"
164 "his side with some of the finest specimens.  But we could not stop.  We"
164 "must follow the Captain, who seemed to guide him self by paths known"
164 "only to himself.  The ground was sensibly rising, and sometimes, on"
164 "holding up my arm, it was above the surface of the sea.  Then the level"
164 "of the bank would sink capriciously.  Often we rounded high rocks"
164 "scarped into pyramids.  In their dark fractures huge crustacea, perched"
164 "upon their high claws like some war-machine, watched us with fixed"
164 "eyes, and under our feet crawled various kinds of annelides."
164 ""
164 "At this moment there opened before us a large grotto dug in a"
164 "picturesque heap of rocks and carpeted with all the thick warp of the"
164 "submarine flora.  At first it seemed very dark to me.  The solar rays"
164 "seemed to be extinguished by successive gradations, until its vague"
164 "transparency became nothing more than drowned light.  Captain Nemo"
164 "entered; we followed.  My eyes soon accustomed themselves to this"
164 "relative state of darkness.  I could distinguish the arches springing"
164 "capriciously from natural pillars, standing broad upon their granite"
164 "base, like the heavy columns of Tuscan architecture.  Why had our"
164 "incomprehensible guide led us to the bottom of this submarine crypt?  I"
164 "was soon to know.  After descending a rather sharp declivity, our feet"
164 "trod the bottom of a kind of circular pit.  There Captain Nemo stopped,"
164 "and with his hand indicated an object I had not yet perceived.  It was"
164 "an oyster of extraordinary dimensions, a gigantic tridacne, a goblet"
164 "which could have contained a whole lake of holy-water, a basin the"
164 "breadth of which was more than two yards and a half, and consequently"
164 "larger than that ornamenting the saloon of the Nautilus.  I approached"
164 "this extraordinary mollusc.  It adhered by its filaments to a table of"
164 "granite, and there, isolated, it developed itself in the calm waters of"
164 "the grotto.  I estimated the weight of this tridacne at 600 lb.  Such"
164 "an oyster would contain 30 lb. of meat; and one must have the stomach"
164 "of a Gargantua to demolish some dozens of them."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo was evidently acquainted with the existence of this"
164 "bivalve, and seemed to have a particular motive in verifying the actual"
164 "state of this tridacne.  The shells were a little open; the Captain"
164 "came near and put his dagger between to prevent them from closing; then"
164 "with his hand he raised the membrane with its fringed edges, which"
164 "formed a cloak for the creature.  There, between the folded plaits, I"
164 "saw a loose pearl, whose size equalled that of a coco-nut. Its globular"
164 "shape, perfect clearness, and admirable lustre made it altogether a"
164 "jewel of inestimable value.  Carried away by my curiosity, I stretched"
164 "out my hand to seize it, weigh it, and touch it; but the Captain"
164 "stopped me, made a sign of refusal, and quickly withdrew his dagger,"
164 "and the two shells closed suddenly.  I then understood Captain Nemo's"
164 "intention.  In leaving this pearl hidden in the mantle of the tridacne"
164 "he was allowing it to grow slowly.  Each year the secretions of the"
164 "mollusc would add new concentric circles.  I estimated its value at"
164 "L500,000 at least."
164 ""
164 "After ten minutes Captain Nemo stopped suddenly.  I thought he had"
164 "halted previously to returning.  No; by a gesture he bade us crouch"
164 "beside him in a deep fracture of the rock, his hand pointed to one part"
164 "of the liquid mass, which I watched attentively."
164 ""
164 "About five yards from me a shadow appeared, and sank to the ground."
164 "The disquieting idea of sharks shot through my mind, but I was"
164 "mistaken; and once again it was not a monster of the ocean that we had"
164 "anything to do with."
164 ""
164 "It was a man, a living man, an Indian, a fisherman, a poor devil who, I"
164 "suppose, had come to glean before the harvest.  I could see the bottom"
164 "of his canoe anchored some feet above his head.  He dived and went up"
164 "successively.  A stone held between his feet, cut in the shape of a"
164 "sugar loaf, whilst a rope fastened him to his boat, helped him to"
164 "descend more rapidly.  This was all his apparatus.  Reaching the"
164 "bottom, about five yards deep, he went on his knees and filled his bag"
164 "with oysters picked up at random.  Then he went up, emptied it, pulled"
164 "up his stone, and began the operation once more, which lasted thirty"
164 "seconds."
164 ""
164 "The diver did not see us.  The shadow of the rock hid us from sight."
164 "And how should this poor Indian ever dream that men, beings like"
164 "himself, should be there under the water watching his movements and"
164 "losing no detail of the fishing?  Several times he went up in this way,"
164 "and dived again.  He did not carry away more than ten at each plunge,"
164 "for he was obliged to pull them from the bank to which they adhered by"
164 "means of their strong byssus.  And how many of those oysters for which"
164 "he risked his life had no pearl in them!  I watched him closely; his"
164 "manoeuvres were regular; and for the space of half an hour no danger"
164 "appeared to threaten him."
164 ""
164 "I was beginning to accustom myself to the sight of this interesting"
164 "fishing, when suddenly, as the Indian was on the ground, I saw him make"
164 "a gesture of terror, rise, and make a spring to return to the surface"
164 "of the sea."
164 ""
164 "I understood his dread.  A gigantic shadow appeared just above the"
164 "unfortunate diver.  It was a shark of enormous size advancing"
164 "diagonally, his eyes on fire, and his jaws open.  I was mute with"
164 "horror and unable to move."
164 ""
164 "The voracious creature shot towards the Indian, who threw himself on"
164 "one side to avoid the shark's fins; but not its tail, for it struck his"
164 "chest and stretched him on the ground."
164 ""
164 "This scene lasted but a few seconds:  the shark returned, and, turning"
164 "on his back, prepared himself for cutting the Indian in two, when I saw"
164 "Captain Nemo rise suddenly, and then, dagger in hand, walk straight to"
164 "the monster, ready to fight face to face with him.  The very moment the"
164 "shark was going to snap the unhappy fisherman in two, he perceived his"
164 "new adversary, and, turning over, made straight towards him."
164 ""
164 "I can still see Captain Nemo's position.  Holding himself well"
164 "together, he waited for the shark with admirable coolness; and, when it"
164 "rushed at him, threw himself on one side with wonderful quickness,"
164 "avoiding the shock, and burying his dagger deep into its side.  But it"
164 "was not all over.  A terrible combat ensued."
164 ""
164 "The shark had seemed to roar, if I might say so.  The blood rushed in"
164 "torrents from its wound.  The sea was dyed red, and through the opaque"
164 "liquid I could distinguish nothing more.  Nothing more until the moment"
164 "when, like lightning, I saw the undaunted Captain hanging on to one of"
164 "the creature's fins, struggling, as it were, hand to hand with the"
164 "monster, and dealing successive blows at his enemy, yet still unable to"
164 "give a decisive one."
164 ""
164 "The shark's struggles agitated the water with such fury that the"
164 "rocking threatened to upset me."
164 ""
164 "I wanted to go to the Captain's assistance, but, nailed to the spot"
164 "with horror, I could not stir."
164 ""
164 "I saw the haggard eye; I saw the different phases of the fight.  The"
164 "Captain fell to the earth, upset by the enormous mass which leant upon"
164 "him.  The shark's jaws opened wide, like a pair of factory shears, and"
164 "it would have been all over with the Captain; but, quick as thought,"
164 "harpoon in hand, Ned Land rushed towards the shark and struck it with"
164 "its sharp point."
164 ""
164 "The waves were impregnated with a mass of blood.  They rocked under the"
164 "shark's movements, which beat them with indescribable fury.  Ned Land"
164 "had not missed his aim.  It was the monster's death-rattle.  Struck to"
164 "the heart, it struggled in dreadful convulsions, the shock of which"
164 "overthrew Conseil."
164 ""
164 "But Ned Land had disentangled the Captain, who, getting up without any"
164 "wound, went straight to the Indian, quickly cut the cord which held him"
164 "to his stone, took him in his arms, and, with a sharp blow of his heel,"
164 "mounted to the surface."
164 ""
164 "We all three followed in a few seconds, saved by a miracle, and reached"
164 "the fisherman's boat."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo's first care was to recall the unfortunate man to life"
164 "again.  I did not think he could succeed.  I hoped so, for the poor"
164 "creature's immersion was not long; but the blow from the shark's tail"
164 "might have been his death-blow."
164 ""
164 "Happily, with the Captain's and Conseil's sharp friction, I saw"
164 "consciousness return by degrees.  He opened his eyes.  What was his"
164 "surprise, his terror even, at seeing four great copper heads leaning"
164 "over him!  And, above all, what must he have thought when Captain Nemo,"
164 "drawing from the pocket of his dress a bag of pearls, placed it in his"
164 "hand!  This munificent charity from the man of the waters to the poor"
164 "Cingalese was accepted with a trembling hand.  His wondering eyes"
164 "showed that he knew not to what super-human beings he owed both fortune"
164 "and life."
164 ""
164 "At a sign from the Captain we regained the bank, and, following the"
164 "road already traversed, came in about half an hour to the anchor which"
164 "held the canoe of the Nautilus to the earth."
164 ""
164 "Once on board, we each, with the help of the sailors, got rid of the"
164 "heavy copper helmet."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo's first word was to the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Thank you, Master Land,\" said he."
164 ""
164 "\"It was in revenge, Captain,\" replied Ned Land.  \"I owed you that.\""
164 ""
164 "A ghastly smile passed across the Captain's lips, and that was all."
164 ""
164 "\"To the Nautilus,\" said he."
164 ""
164 "The boat flew over the waves.  Some minutes after we met the shark's"
164 "dead body floating.  By the black marking of the extremity of its fins,"
164 "I recognised the terrible melanopteron of the Indian Seas, of the"
164 "species of shark so properly called.  It was more than twenty-five feet"
164 "long; its enormous mouth occupied one-third of its body.  It was an"
164 "adult, as was known by its six rows of teeth placed in an isosceles"
164 "triangle in the upper jaw."
164 ""
164 "Whilst I was contemplating this inert mass, a dozen of these voracious"
164 "beasts appeared round the boat; and, without noticing us, threw"
164 "themselves upon the dead body and fought with one another for the"
164 "pieces."
164 ""
164 "At half-past eight we were again on board the Nautilus.  There I"
164 "reflected on the incidents which had taken place in our excursion to"
164 "the Manaar Bank."
164 ""
164 "Two conclusions I must inevitably draw from it--one bearing upon the"
164 "unparalleled courage of Captain Nemo, the other upon his devotion to a"
164 "human being, a representative of that race from which he fled beneath"
164 "the sea.  Whatever he might say, this strange man had not yet succeeded"
164 "in entirely crushing his heart."
164 ""
164 "When I made this observation to him, he answered in a slightly moved"
164 "tone:"
164 ""
164 "\"That Indian, sir, is an inhabitant of an oppressed country; and I am"
164 "still, and shall be, to my last breath, one of them!\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER IV"
164 ""
164 "THE RED SEA"
164 ""
164 "In the course of the day of the 29th of January, the island of Ceylon"
164 "disappeared under the horizon, and the Nautilus, at a speed of twenty"
164 "miles an hour, slid into the labyrinth of canals which separate the"
164 "Maldives from the Laccadives.  It coasted even the Island of Kiltan, a"
164 "land originally coraline, discovered by Vasco da Gama in 1499, and one"
164 "of the nineteen principal islands of the Laccadive Archipelago,"
164 "situated between 10 deg. and 14 deg. 30' N. lat., and 69 deg. 50' 72\""
164 "E. long."
164 ""
164 "We had made 16,220 miles, or 7,500 (French) leagues from our"
164 "starting-point in the Japanese Seas."
164 ""
164 "The next day (30th January), when the Nautilus went to the surface of"
164 "the ocean there was no land in sight.  Its course was N.N.E., in the"
164 "direction of the Sea of Oman, between Arabia and the Indian Peninsula,"
164 "which serves as an outlet to the Persian Gulf.  It was evidently a"
164 "block without any possible egress.  Where was Captain Nemo taking us"
164 "to?  I could not say.  This, however, did not satisfy the Canadian, who"
164 "that day came to me asking where we were going."
164 ""
164 "\"We are going where our Captain's fancy takes us, Master Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"His fancy cannot take us far, then,\" said the Canadian.  \"The Persian"
164 "Gulf has no outlet:  and, if we do go in, it will not be long before we"
164 "are out again.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very well, then, we will come out again, Master Land; and if, after"
164 "the Persian Gulf, the Nautilus would like to visit the Red Sea, the"
164 "Straits of Bab-el-mandeb are there to give us entrance.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I need not tell you, sir,\" said Ned Land, \"that the Red Sea is as much"
164 "closed as the Gulf, as the Isthmus of Suez is not yet cut; and, if it"
164 "was, a boat as mysterious as ours would not risk itself in a canal cut"
164 "with sluices.  And again, the Red Sea is not the road to take us back"
164 "to Europe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But I never said we were going back to Europe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What do you suppose, then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I suppose that, after visiting the curious coasts of Arabia and Egypt,"
164 "the Nautilus will go down the Indian Ocean again, perhaps cross the"
164 "Channel of Mozambique, perhaps off the Mascarenhas, so as to gain the"
164 "Cape of Good Hope.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And once at the Cape of Good Hope?\" asked the Canadian, with peculiar"
164 "emphasis."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, we shall penetrate into that Atlantic which we do not yet know."
164 "Ah! friend Ned, you are getting tired of this journey under the sea;"
164 "you are surfeited with the incessantly varying spectacle of submarine"
164 "wonders.  For my part, I shall be sorry to see the end of a voyage"
164 "which it is given to so few men to make.\""
164 ""
164 "For four days, till the 3rd of February, the Nautilus scoured the Sea"
164 "of Oman, at various speeds and at various depths.  It seemed to go at"
164 "random, as if hesitating as to which road it should follow, but we"
164 "never passed the Tropic of Cancer."
164 ""
164 "In quitting this sea we sighted Muscat for an instant, one of the most"
164 "important towns of the country of Oman.  I admired its strange aspect,"
164 "surrounded by black rocks upon which its white houses and forts stood"
164 "in relief.  I saw the rounded domes of its mosques, the elegant points"
164 "of its minarets, its fresh and verdant terraces.  But it was only a"
164 "vision!  The Nautilus soon sank under the waves of that part of the sea."
164 ""
164 "We passed along the Arabian coast of Mahrah and Hadramaut, for a"
164 "distance of six miles, its undulating line of mountains being"
164 "occasionally relieved by some ancient ruin.  The 5th of February we at"
164 "last entered the Gulf of Aden, a perfect funnel introduced into the"
164 "neck of Bab-el-mandeb, through which the Indian waters entered the Red"
164 "Sea."
164 ""
164 "The 6th of February, the Nautilus floated in sight of Aden, perched"
164 "upon a promontory which a narrow isthmus joins to the mainland, a kind"
164 "of inaccessible Gibraltar, the fortifications of which were rebuilt by"
164 "the English after taking possession in 1839.  I caught a glimpse of the"
164 "octagon minarets of this town, which was at one time the richest"
164 "commercial magazine on the coast."
164 ""
164 "I certainly thought that Captain Nemo, arrived at this point, would"
164 "back out again; but I was mistaken, for he did no such thing, much to"
164 "my surprise."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 7th of February, we entered the Straits of"
164 "Bab-el-mandeb, the name of which, in the Arab tongue, means The Gate of"
164 "Tears."
164 ""
164 "To twenty miles in breadth, it is only thirty-two in length.  And for"
164 "the Nautilus, starting at full speed, the crossing was scarcely the"
164 "work of an hour.  But I saw nothing, not even the Island of Perim, with"
164 "which the British Government has fortified the position of Aden.  There"
164 "were too many English or French steamers of the line of Suez to Bombay,"
164 "Calcutta to Melbourne, and from Bourbon to the Mauritius, furrowing"
164 "this narrow passage, for the Nautilus to venture to show itself.  So it"
164 "remained prudently below.  At last about noon, we were in the waters of"
164 "the Red Sea."
164 ""
164 "I would not even seek to understand the caprice which had decided"
164 "Captain Nemo upon entering the gulf.  But I quite approved of the"
164 "Nautilus entering it.  Its speed was lessened:  sometimes it kept on"
164 "the surface, sometimes it dived to avoid a vessel, and thus I was able"
164 "to observe the upper and lower parts of this curious sea."
164 ""
164 "The 8th of February, from the first dawn of day, Mocha came in sight,"
164 "now a ruined town, whose walls would fall at a gunshot, yet which"
164 "shelters here and there some verdant date-trees; once an important"
164 "city, containing six public markets, and twenty-six mosques, and whose"
164 "walls, defended by fourteen forts, formed a girdle of two miles in"
164 "circumference."
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus then approached the African shore, where the depth of the"
164 "sea was greater.  There, between two waters clear as crystal, through"
164 "the open panels we were allowed to contemplate the beautiful bushes of"
164 "brilliant coral and large blocks of rock clothed with a splendid fur of"
164 "green variety of sites and landscapes along these sandbanks and algae"
164 "and fuci.  What an indescribable spectacle, and what variety of sites"
164 "and landscapes along these sandbanks and volcanic islands which bound"
164 "the Libyan coast!  But where these shrubs appeared in all their beauty"
164 "was on the eastern coast, which the Nautilus soon gained.  It was on"
164 "the coast of Tehama, for there not only did this display of zoophytes"
164 "flourish beneath the level of the sea, but they also formed picturesque"
164 "interlacings which unfolded themselves about sixty feet above the"
164 "surface, more capricious but less highly coloured than those whose"
164 "freshness was kept up by the vital power of the waters."
164 ""
164 "What charming hours I passed thus at the window of the saloon!  What"
164 "new specimens of submarine flora and fauna did I admire under the"
164 "brightness of our electric lantern!"
164 ""
164 "The 9th of February the Nautilus floated in the broadest part of the"
164 "Red Sea, which is comprised between Souakin, on the west coast, and"
164 "Komfidah, on the east coast, with a diameter of ninety miles."
164 ""
164 "That day at noon, after the bearings were taken, Captain Nemo mounted"
164 "the platform, where I happened to be, and I was determined not to let"
164 "him go down again without at least pressing him regarding his ulterior"
164 "projects.  As soon as he saw me he approached and graciously offered me"
164 "a cigar."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir, does this Red Sea please you?  Have you sufficiently"
164 "observed the wonders it covers, its fishes, its zoophytes, its"
164 "parterres of sponges, and its forests of coral?  Did you catch a"
164 "glimpse of the towns on its borders?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Captain Nemo,\" I replied; \"and the Nautilus is wonderfully fitted"
164 "for such a study.  Ah! it is an intelligent boat!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir, intelligent and invulnerable.  It fears neither the terrible"
164 "tempests of the Red Sea, nor its currents, nor its sandbanks.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" said I, \"this sea is quoted as one of the worst, and in"
164 "the time of the ancients, if I am not mistaken, its reputation was"
164 "detestable.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Detestable, M. Aronnax.  The Greek and Latin historians do not speak"
164 "favourably of it, and Strabo says it is very dangerous during the"
164 "Etesian winds and in the rainy season.  The Arabian Edrisi portrays it"
164 "under the name of the Gulf of Colzoum, and relates that vessels"
164 "perished there in great numbers on the sandbanks and that no one would"
164 "risk sailing in the night.  It is, he pretends, a sea subject to"
164 "fearful hurricanes, strewn with inhospitable islands, and `which offers"
164 "nothing good either on its surface or in its depths.'\""
164 ""
164 "\"One may see,\" I replied, \"that these historians never sailed on board"
164 "the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so,\" replied the Captain, smiling; \"and in that respect moderns"
164 "are not more advanced than the ancients.  It required many ages to find"
164 "out the mechanical power of steam.  Who knows if, in another hundred"
164 "years, we may not see a second Nautilus?  Progress is slow, M. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is true,\" I answered; \"your boat is at least a century before its"
164 "time, perhaps an era.  What a misfortune that the secret of such an"
164 "invention should die with its inventor!\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo did not reply.  After some minutes' silence he continued:"
164 ""
164 "\"You were speaking of the opinions of ancient historians upon the"
164 "dangerous navigation of the Red Sea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is true,\" said I; \"but were not their fears exaggerated?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes and no, M. Aronnax,\" replied Captain Nemo, who seemed to know the"
164 "Red Sea by heart.  \"That which is no longer dangerous for a modern"
164 "vessel, well rigged, strongly built, and master of its own course,"
164 "thanks to obedient steam, offered all sorts of perils to the ships of"
164 "the ancients.  Picture to yourself those first navigators venturing in"
164 "ships made of planks sewn with the cords of the palmtree, saturated"
164 "with the grease of the seadog, and covered with powdered resin!  They"
164 "had not even instruments wherewith to take their bearings, and they"
164 "went by guess amongst currents of which they scarcely knew anything."
164 "Under such conditions shipwrecks were, and must have been, numerous."
164 "But in our time, steamers running between Suez and the South Seas have"
164 "nothing more to fear from the fury of this gulf, in spite of contrary"
164 "trade-winds. The captain and passengers do not prepare for their"
164 "departure by offering propitiatory sacrifices; and, on their return,"
164 "they no longer go ornamented with wreaths and gilt fillets to thank the"
164 "gods in the neighbouring temple.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I agree with you,\" said I; \"and steam seems to have killed all"
164 "gratitude in the hearts of sailors.  But, Captain, since you seem to"
164 "have especially studied this sea, can you tell me the origin of its"
164 "name?\""
164 ""
164 "\"There exist several explanations on the subject, M. Aronnax.  Would"
164 "you like to know the opinion of a chronicler of the fourteenth century?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Willingly.\""
164 ""
164 "\"This fanciful writer pretends that its name was given to it after the"
164 "passage of the Israelites, when Pharaoh perished in the waves which"
164 "closed at the voice of Moses.\""
164 ""
164 "\"A poet's explanation, Captain Nemo,\" I replied; \"but I cannot content"
164 "myself with that.  I ask you for your personal opinion.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Here it is, M. Aronnax.  According to my idea, we must see in this"
164 "appellation of the Red Sea a translation of the Hebrew word `Edom'; and"
164 "if the ancients gave it that name, it was on account of the particular"
164 "colour of its waters.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But up to this time I have seen nothing but transparent waves and"
164 "without any particular colour.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very likely; but as we advance to the bottom of the gulf, you will see"
164 "this singular appearance.  I remember seeing the Bay of Tor entirely"
164 "red, like a sea of blood.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And you attribute this colour to the presence of a microscopic"
164 "seaweed?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes.\""
164 ""
164 "\"So, Captain Nemo, it is not the first time you have overrun the Red"
164 "Sea on board the Nautilus?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you spoke a while ago of the passage of the Israelites and of the"
164 "catastrophe to the Egyptians, I will ask whether you have met with the"
164 "traces under the water of this great historical fact?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; and for a good reason.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What is it?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is that the spot where Moses and his people passed is now so"
164 "blocked up with sand that the camels can barely bathe their legs there."
164 "You can well understand that there would not be water enough for my"
164 "Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And the spot?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"The spot is situated a little above the Isthmus of Suez, in the arm"
164 "which formerly made a deep estuary, when the Red Sea extended to the"
164 "Salt Lakes.  Now, whether this passage were miraculous or not, the"
164 "Israelites, nevertheless, crossed there to reach the Promised Land, and"
164 "Pharaoh's army perished precisely on that spot; and I think that"
164 "excavations made in the middle of the sand would bring to light a large"
164 "number of arms and instruments of Egyptian origin.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is evident,\" I replied; \"and for the sake of archaeologists let"
164 "us hope that these excavations will be made sooner or later, when new"
164 "towns are established on the isthmus, after the construction of the"
164 "Suez Canal; a canal, however, very useless to a vessel like the"
164 "Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very likely; but useful to the whole world,\" said Captain Nemo.  \"The"
164 "ancients well understood the utility of a communication between the Red"
164 "Sea and the Mediterranean for their commercial affairs: but they did"
164 "not think of digging a canal direct, and took the Nile as an"
164 "intermediate.  Very probably the canal which united the Nile to the Red"
164 "Sea was begun by Sesostris, if we may believe tradition.  One thing is"
164 "certain, that in the year 615 before Jesus Christ, Necos undertook the"
164 "works of an alimentary canal to the waters of the Nile across the plain"
164 "of Egypt, looking towards Arabia.  It took four days to go up this"
164 "canal, and it was so wide that two triremes could go abreast.  It was"
164 "carried on by Darius, the son of Hystaspes, and probably finished by"
164 "Ptolemy II.  Strabo saw it navigated:  but its decline from the point"
164 "of departure, near Bubastes, to the Red Sea was so slight that it was"
164 "only navigable for a few months in the year.  This canal answered all"
164 "commercial purposes to the age of Antonius, when it was abandoned and"
164 "blocked up with sand.  Restored by order of the Caliph Omar, it was"
164 "definitely destroyed in 761 or 762 by Caliph Al-Mansor, who wished to"
164 "prevent the arrival of provisions to Mohammed-ben-Abdallah, who had"
164 "revolted against him.  During the expedition into Egypt, your General"
164 "Bonaparte discovered traces of the works in the Desert of Suez; and,"
164 "surprised by the tide, he nearly perished before regaining Hadjaroth,"
164 "at the very place where Moses had encamped three thousand years before"
164 "him.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Captain, what the ancients dared not undertake, this junction"
164 "between the two seas, which will shorten the road from Cadiz to India,"
164 "M. Lesseps has succeeded in doing; and before long he will have changed"
164 "Africa into an immense island.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, M. Aronnax; you have the right to be proud of your countryman."
164 "Such a man brings more honour to a nation than great captains.  He"
164 "began, like so many others, with disgust and rebuffs; but he has"
164 "triumphed, for he has the genius of will.  And it is sad to think that"
164 "a work like that, which ought to have been an international work and"
164 "which would have sufficed to make a reign illustrious, should have"
164 "succeeded by the energy of one man.  All honour to M. Lesseps!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes! honour to the great citizen,\" I replied, surprised by the manner"
164 "in which Captain Nemo had just spoken."
164 ""
164 "\"Unfortunately,\" he continued, \"I cannot take you through the Suez"
164 "Canal; but you will be able to see the long jetty of Port Said after"
164 "to-morrow, when we shall be in the Mediterranean.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The Mediterranean!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir; does that astonish you?\""
164 ""
164 "\"What astonishes me is to think that we shall be there the day after"
164 "to-morrow.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Indeed?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Captain, although by this time I ought to have accustomed myself"
164 "to be surprised at nothing since I have been on board your boat.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But the cause of this surprise?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well! it is the fearful speed you will have to put on the Nautilus, if"
164 "the day after to-morrow she is to be in the Mediterranean, having made"
164 "the round of Africa, and doubled the Cape of Good Hope!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Who told you that she would make the round of Africa and double the"
164 "Cape of Good Hope, sir?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, unless the Nautilus sails on dry land, and passes above the"
164 "isthmus----\""
164 ""
164 "\"Or beneath it, M. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Beneath it?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" replied Captain Nemo quietly.  \"A long time ago Nature"
164 "made under this tongue of land what man has this day made on its"
164 "surface.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What! such a passage exists?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; a subterranean passage, which I have named the Arabian Tunnel."
164 "It takes us beneath Suez and opens into the Gulf of Pelusium.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But this isthmus is composed of nothing but quick sands?\""
164 ""
164 "\"To a certain depth.  But at fifty-five yards only there is a solid"
164 "layer of rock.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Did you discover this passage by chance?\"  I asked more and more"
164 "surprised."
164 ""
164 "\"Chance and reasoning, sir; and by reasoning even more than by chance."
164 "Not only does this passage exist, but I have profited by it several"
164 "times.  Without that I should not have ventured this day into the"
164 "impassable Red Sea.  I noticed that in the Red Sea and in the"
164 "Mediterranean there existed a certain number of fishes of a kind"
164 "perfectly identical.  Certain of the fact, I asked myself was it"
164 "possible that there was no communication between the two seas?  If"
164 "there was, the subterranean current must necessarily run from the Red"
164 "Sea to the Mediterranean, from the sole cause of difference of level."
164 "I caught a large number of fishes in the neighbourhood of Suez.  I"
164 "passed a copper ring through their tails, and threw them back into the"
164 "sea.  Some months later, on the coast of Syria, I caught some of my"
164 "fish ornamented with the ring.  Thus the communication between the two"
164 "was proved.  I then sought for it with my Nautilus; I discovered it,"
164 "ventured into it, and before long, sir, you too will have passed"
164 "through my Arabian tunnel!\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER V"
164 ""
164 "THE ARABIAN TUNNEL"
164 ""
164 "That same evening, in 21 deg. 30' N. lat., the Nautilus floated on the"
164 "surface of the sea, approaching the Arabian coast.  I saw Djeddah, the"
164 "most important counting-house of Egypt, Syria, Turkey, and India.  I"
164 "distinguished clearly enough its buildings, the vessels anchored at the"
164 "quays, and those whose draught of water obliged them to anchor in the"
164 "roads.  The sun, rather low on the horizon, struck full on the houses"
164 "of the town, bringing out their whiteness.  Outside, some wooden"
164 "cabins, and some made of reeds, showed the quarter inhabited by the"
164 "Bedouins.  Soon Djeddah was shut out from view by the shadows of night,"
164 "and the Nautilus found herself under water slightly phosphorescent."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 10th of February, we sighted several ships running to"
164 "windward.  The Nautilus returned to its submarine navigation; but at"
164 "noon, when her bearings were taken, the sea being deserted, she rose"
164 "again to her waterline."
164 ""
164 "Accompanied by Ned and Conseil, I seated myself on the platform.  The"
164 "coast on the eastern side looked like a mass faintly printed upon a"
164 "damp fog."
164 ""
164 "We were leaning on the sides of the pinnace, talking of one thing and"
164 "another, when Ned Land, stretching out his hand towards a spot on the"
164 "sea, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"Do you see anything there, sir?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, Ned,\" I replied; \"but I have not your eyes, you know.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Look well,\" said Ned, \"there, on the starboard beam, about the height"
164 "of the lantern!  Do you not see a mass which seems to move?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" said I, after close attention; \"I see something like a"
164 "long black body on the top of the water.\""
164 ""
164 "And certainly before long the black object was not more than a mile"
164 "from us.  It looked like a great sandbank deposited in the open sea."
164 "It was a gigantic dugong!"
164 ""
164 "Ned Land looked eagerly.  His eyes shone with covetousness at the sight"
164 "of the animal.  His hand seemed ready to harpoon it.  One would have"
164 "thought he was awaiting the moment to throw himself into the sea and"
164 "attack it in its element."
164 ""
164 "At this instant Captain Nemo appeared on the platform.  He saw the"
164 "dugong, understood the Canadian's attitude, and, addressing him, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"If you held a harpoon just now, Master Land, would it not burn your"
164 "hand?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And you would not be sorry to go back, for one day, to your trade of a"
164 "fisherman and to add this cetacean to the list of those you have"
164 "already killed?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I should not, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, you can try.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Thank you, sir,\" said Ned Land, his eyes flaming."
164 ""
164 "\"Only,\" continued the Captain, \"I advise you for your own sake not to"
164 "miss the creature.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Is the dugong dangerous to attack?\"  I asked, in spite of the"
164 "Canadian's shrug of the shoulders."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" replied the Captain; \"sometimes the animal turns upon its"
164 "assailants and overturns their boat.  But for Master Land this danger"
164 "is not to be feared.  His eye is prompt, his arm sure.\""
164 ""
164 "At this moment seven men of the crew, mute and immovable as ever,"
164 "mounted the platform.  One carried a harpoon and a line similar to"
164 "those employed in catching whales.  The pinnace was lifted from the"
164 "bridge, pulled from its socket, and let down into the sea.  Six oarsmen"
164 "took their seats, and the coxswain went to the tiller.  Ned, Conseil,"
164 "and I went to the back of the boat."
164 ""
164 "\"You are not coming, Captain?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; but I wish you good sport.\""
164 ""
164 "The boat put off, and, lifted by the six rowers, drew rapidly towards"
164 "the dugong, which floated about two miles from the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "Arrived some cables-length from the cetacean, the speed slackened, and"
164 "the oars dipped noiselessly into the quiet waters.  Ned Land, harpoon"
164 "in hand, stood in the fore part of the boat.  The harpoon used for"
164 "striking the whale is generally attached to a very long cord which runs"
164 "out rapidly as the wounded creature draws it after him.  But here the"
164 "cord was not more than ten fathoms long, and the extremity was attached"
164 "to a small barrel which, by floating, was to show the course the dugong"
164 "took under the water."
164 ""
164 "I stood and carefully watched the Canadian's adversary.  This dugong,"
164 "which also bears the name of the halicore, closely resembles the"
164 "manatee; its oblong body terminated in a lengthened tail, and its"
164 "lateral fins in perfect fingers.  Its difference from the manatee"
164 "consisted in its upper jaw, which was armed with two long and pointed"
164 "teeth which formed on each side diverging tusks."
164 ""
164 "This dugong which Ned Land was preparing to attack was of colossal"
164 "dimensions; it was more than seven yards long.  It did not move, and"
164 "seemed to be sleeping on the waves, which circumstance made it easier"
164 "to capture."
164 ""
164 "The boat approached within six yards of the animal.  The oars rested on"
164 "the rowlocks.  I half rose.  Ned Land, his body thrown a little back,"
164 "brandished the harpoon in his experienced hand."
164 ""
164 "Suddenly a hissing noise was heard, and the dugong disappeared.  The"
164 "harpoon, although thrown with great force; had apparently only struck"
164 "the water."
164 ""
164 "\"Curse it!\" exclaimed the Canadian furiously; \"I have missed it!\""
164 ""
164 "\"No,\" said I; \"the creature is wounded--look at the blood; but your"
164 "weapon has not stuck in his body.\""
164 ""
164 "\"My harpoon! my harpoon!\" cried Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "The sailors rowed on, and the coxswain made for the floating barrel."
164 "The harpoon regained, we followed in pursuit of the animal."
164 ""
164 "The latter came now and then to the surface to breathe.  Its wound had"
164 "not weakened it, for it shot onwards with great rapidity."
164 ""
164 "The boat, rowed by strong arms, flew on its track.  Several times it"
164 "approached within some few yards, and the Canadian was ready to strike,"
164 "but the dugong made off with a sudden plunge, and it was impossible to"
164 "reach it."
164 ""
164 "Imagine the passion which excited impatient Ned Land!  He hurled at the"
164 "unfortunate creature the most energetic expletives in the English"
164 "tongue.  For my part, I was only vexed to see the dugong escape all our"
164 "attacks."
164 ""
164 "We pursued it without relaxation for an hour, and I began to think it"
164 "would prove difficult to capture, when the animal, possessed with the"
164 "perverse idea of vengeance of which he had cause to repent, turned upon"
164 "the pinnace and assailed us in its turn."
164 ""
164 "This manoeuvre did not escape the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Look out!\" he cried."
164 ""
164 "The coxswain said some words in his outlandish tongue, doubtless"
164 "warning the men to keep on their guard."
164 ""
164 "The dugong came within twenty feet of the boat, stopped, sniffed the"
164 "air briskly with its large nostrils (not pierced at the extremity, but"
164 "in the upper part of its muzzle). Then, taking a spring, he threw"
164 "himself upon us."
164 ""
164 "The pinnace could not avoid the shock, and half upset, shipped at least"
164 "two tons of water, which had to be emptied; but, thanks to the"
164 "coxswain, we caught it sideways, not full front, so we were not quite"
164 "overturned.  While Ned Land, clinging to the bows, belaboured the"
164 "gigantic animal with blows from his harpoon, the creature's teeth were"
164 "buried in the gunwale, and it lifted the whole thing out of the water,"
164 "as a lion does a roebuck.  We were upset over one another, and I know"
164 "not how the adventure would have ended, if the Canadian, still enraged"
164 "with the beast, had not struck it to the heart."
164 ""
164 "I heard its teeth grind on the iron plate, and the dugong disappeared,"
164 "carrying the harpoon with him.  But the barrel soon returned to the"
164 "surface, and shortly after the body of the animal, turned on its back."
164 "The boat came up with it, took it in tow, and made straight for the"
164 "Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "It required tackle of enormous strength to hoist the dugong on to the"
164 "platform.  It weighed 10,000 lb."
164 ""
164 "The next day, 11th February, the larder of the Nautilus was enriched by"
164 "some more delicate game.  A flight of sea-swallows rested on the"
164 "Nautilus.  It was a species of the Sterna nilotica, peculiar to Egypt;"
164 "its beak is black, head grey and pointed, the eye surrounded by white"
164 "spots, the back, wings, and tail of a greyish colour, the belly and"
164 "throat white, and claws red.  They also took some dozen of Nile ducks,"
164 "a wild bird of high flavour, its throat and upper part of the head"
164 "white with black spots."
164 ""
164 "About five o'clock in the evening we sighted to the north the Cape of"
164 "Ras-Mohammed. This cape forms the extremity of Arabia Petraea,"
164 "comprised between the Gulf of Suez and the Gulf of Acabah."
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus penetrated into the Straits of Jubal, which leads to the"
164 "Gulf of Suez.  I distinctly saw a high mountain, towering between the"
164 "two gulfs of Ras-Mohammed. It was Mount Horeb, that Sinai at the top of"
164 "which Moses saw God face to face."
164 ""
164 "At six o'clock the Nautilus, sometimes floating, sometimes immersed,"
164 "passed some distance from Tor, situated at the end of the bay, the"
164 "waters of which seemed tinted with red, an observation already made by"
164 "Captain Nemo.  Then night fell in the midst of a heavy silence,"
164 "sometimes broken by the cries of the pelican and other night-birds, and"
164 "the noise of the waves breaking upon the shore, chafing against the"
164 "rocks, or the panting of some far-off steamer beating the waters of the"
164 "Gulf with its noisy paddles."
164 ""
164 "From eight to nine o'clock the Nautilus remained some fathoms under the"
164 "water.  According to my calculation we must have been very near Suez."
164 "Through the panel of the saloon I saw the bottom of the rocks"
164 "brilliantly lit up by our electric lamp.  We seemed to be leaving the"
164 "Straits behind us more and more."
164 ""
164 "At a quarter-past nine, the vessel having returned to the surface, I"
164 "mounted the platform.  Most impatient to pass through Captain Nemo's"
164 "tunnel, I could not stay in one place, so came to breathe the fresh"
164 "night air."
164 ""
164 "Soon in the shadow I saw a pale light, half discoloured by the fog,"
164 "shining about a mile from us."
164 ""
164 "\"A floating lighthouse!\" said someone near me."
164 ""
164 "I turned, and saw the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"It is the floating light of Suez,\" he continued.  \"It will not be long"
164 "before we gain the entrance of the tunnel.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The entrance cannot be easy?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; for that reason I am accustomed to go into the steersman's"
164 "cage and myself direct our course.  And now, if you will go down, M."
164 "Aronnax, the Nautilus is going under the waves, and will not return to"
164 "the surface until we have passed through the Arabian Tunnel.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo led me towards the central staircase; half way down he"
164 "opened a door, traversed the upper deck, and landed in the pilot's"
164 "cage, which it may be remembered rose at the extremity of the platform."
164 "It was a cabin measuring six feet square, very much like that occupied"
164 "by the pilot on the steamboats of the Mississippi or Hudson.  In the"
164 "midst worked a wheel, placed vertically, and caught to the tiller-rope,"
164 "which ran to the back of the Nautilus.  Four light-ports with"
164 "lenticular glasses, let in a groove in the partition of the cabin,"
164 "allowed the man at the wheel to see in all directions."
164 ""
164 "This cabin was dark; but soon my eyes accustomed themselves to the"
164 "obscurity, and I perceived the pilot, a strong man, with his hands"
164 "resting on the spokes of the wheel.  Outside, the sea appeared vividly"
164 "lit up by the lantern, which shed its rays from the back of the cabin"
164 "to the other extremity of the platform."
164 ""
164 "\"Now,\" said Captain Nemo, \"let us try to make our passage.\""
164 ""
164 "Electric wires connected the pilot's cage with the machinery room, and"
164 "from there the Captain could communicate simultaneously to his Nautilus"
164 "the direction and the speed.  He pressed a metal knob, and at once the"
164 "speed of the screw diminished."
164 ""
164 "I looked in silence at the high straight wall we were running by at"
164 "this moment, the immovable base of a massive sandy coast.  We followed"
164 "it thus for an hour only some few yards off."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo did not take his eye from the knob, suspended by its two"
164 "concentric circles in the cabin.  At a simple gesture, the pilot"
164 "modified the course of the Nautilus every instant."
164 ""
164 "I had placed myself at the port-scuttle, and saw some magnificent"
164 "substructures of coral, zoophytes, seaweed, and fucus, agitating their"
164 "enormous claws, which stretched out from the fissures of the rock."
164 ""
164 "At a quarter-past ten, the Captain himself took the helm.  A large"
164 "gallery, black and deep, opened before us.  The Nautilus went boldly"
164 "into it.  A strange roaring was heard round its sides.  It was the"
164 "waters of the Red Sea, which the incline of the tunnel precipitated"
164 "violently towards the Mediterranean.  The Nautilus went with the"
164 "torrent, rapid as an arrow, in spite of the efforts of the machinery,"
164 "which, in order to offer more effective resistance, beat the waves with"
164 "reversed screw."
164 ""
164 "On the walls of the narrow passage I could see nothing but brilliant"
164 "rays, straight lines, furrows of fire, traced by the great speed, under"
164 "the brilliant electric light.  My heart beat fast."
164 ""
164 "At thirty-five minutes past ten, Captain Nemo quitted the helm, and,"
164 "turning to me, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"The Mediterranean!\""
164 ""
164 "In less than twenty minutes, the Nautilus, carried along by the"
164 "torrent, had passed through the Isthmus of Suez."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VI"
164 ""
164 "THE GRECIAN ARCHIPELAGO"
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 12th of February, at the dawn of day, the Nautilus"
164 "rose to the surface.  I hastened on to the platform.  Three miles to"
164 "the south the dim outline of Pelusium was to be seen.  A torrent had"
164 "carried us from one sea to another.  About seven o'clock Ned and"
164 "Conseil joined me."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Sir Naturalist,\" said the Canadian, in a slightly jovial tone,"
164 "\"and the Mediterranean?\""
164 ""
164 "\"We are floating on its surface, friend Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What!\" said Conseil, \"this very night.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, this very night; in a few minutes we have passed this impassable"
164 "isthmus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I do not believe it,\" replied the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Then you are wrong, Master Land,\" I continued; \"this low coast which"
164 "rounds off to the south is the Egyptian coast.  And you who have such"
164 "good eyes, Ned, you can see the jetty of Port Said stretching into the"
164 "sea.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian looked attentively."
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly you are right, sir, and your Captain is a first-rate man."
164 "We are in the Mediterranean.  Good!  Now, if you please, let us talk of"
164 "our own little affair, but so that no one hears us.\""
164 ""
164 "I saw what the Canadian wanted, and, in any case, I thought it better"
164 "to let him talk, as he wished it; so we all three went and sat down"
164 "near the lantern, where we were less exposed to the spray of the blades."
164 ""
164 "\"Now, Ned, we listen; what have you to tell us?\""
164 ""
164 "\"What I have to tell you is very simple.  We are in Europe; and before"
164 "Captain Nemo's caprices drag us once more to the bottom of the Polar"
164 "Seas, or lead us into Oceania, I ask to leave the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "I wished in no way to shackle the liberty of my companions, but I"
164 "certainly felt no desire to leave Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "Thanks to him, and thanks to his apparatus, I was each day nearer the"
164 "completion of my submarine studies; and I was rewriting my book of"
164 "submarine depths in its very element.  Should I ever again have such an"
164 "opportunity of observing the wonders of the ocean?  No, certainly not!"
164 "And I could not bring myself to the idea of abandoning the Nautilus"
164 "before the cycle of investigation was accomplished."
164 ""
164 "\"Friend Ned, answer me frankly, are you tired of being on board?  Are"
164 "you sorry that destiny has thrown us into Captain Nemo's hands?\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian remained some moments without answering.  Then, crossing"
164 "his arms, he said:"
164 ""
164 "\"Frankly, I do not regret this journey under the seas.  I shall be glad"
164 "to have made it; but, now that it is made, let us have done with it."
164 "That is my idea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It will come to an end, Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Where and when?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Where I do not know--when I cannot say; or, rather, I suppose it will"
164 "end when these seas have nothing more to teach us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then what do you hope for?\" demanded the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"That circumstances may occur as well six months hence as now by which"
164 "we may and ought to profit.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Oh!\" said Ned Land, \"and where shall we be in six months, if you"
164 "please, Sir Naturalist?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps in China; you know the Nautilus is a rapid traveller.  It goes"
164 "through water as swallows through the air, or as an express on the"
164 "land.  It does not fear frequented seas; who can say that it may not"
164 "beat the coasts of France, England, or America, on which flight may be"
164 "attempted as advantageously as here.\""
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax,\" replied the Canadian, \"your arguments are rotten at the"
164 "foundation.  You speak in the future, `We shall be there!  we shall be"
164 "here!'  I speak in the present, `We are here, and we must profit by"
164 "it.'\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land's logic pressed me hard, and I felt myself beaten on that"
164 "ground.  I knew not what argument would now tell in my favour."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" continued Ned, \"let us suppose an impossibility: if Captain Nemo"
164 "should this day offer you your liberty; would you accept it?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I do not know,\" I answered."
164 ""
164 "\"And if,\" he added, \"the offer made you this day was never to be"
164 "renewed, would you accept it?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Friend Ned, this is my answer.  Your reasoning is against me.  We must"
164 "not rely on Captain Nemo's good-will. Common prudence forbids him to"
164 "set us at liberty.  On the other side, prudence bids us profit by the"
164 "first opportunity to leave the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, M. Aronnax, that is wisely said.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Only one observation--just one.  The occasion must be serious, and our"
164 "first attempt must succeed; if it fails, we shall never find another,"
164 "and Captain Nemo will never forgive us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"All that is true,\" replied the Canadian.  \"But your observation"
164 "applies equally to all attempts at flight, whether in two years' time,"
164 "or in two days'. But the question is still this: If a favourable"
164 "opportunity presents itself, it must be seized.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Agreed!  And now, Ned, will you tell me what you mean by a favourable"
164 "opportunity?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It will be that which, on a dark night, will bring the Nautilus a"
164 "short distance from some European coast.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And you will try and save yourself by swimming?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, if we were near enough to the bank, and if the vessel was"
164 "floating at the time.  Not if the bank was far away, and the boat was"
164 "under the water.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And in that case?\""
164 ""
164 "\"In that case, I should seek to make myself master of the pinnace.  I"
164 "know how it is worked.  We must get inside, and the bolts once drawn,"
164 "we shall come to the surface of the water, without even the pilot, who"
164 "is in the bows, perceiving our flight.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Ned, watch for the opportunity; but do not forget that a hitch"
164 "will ruin us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I will not forget, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And now, Ned, would you like to know what I think of your project?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly, M. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, I think--I do not say I hope--I think that this favourable"
164 "opportunity will never present itself.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why not?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Because Captain Nemo cannot hide from himself that we have not given"
164 "up all hope of regaining our liberty, and he will be on his guard,"
164 "above all, in the seas and in the sight of European coasts.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We shall see,\" replied Ned Land, shaking his head determinedly."
164 ""
164 "\"And now, Ned Land,\" I added, \"let us stop here.  Not another word on"
164 "the subject.  The day that you are ready, come and let us know, and we"
164 "will follow you.  I rely entirely upon you.\""
164 ""
164 "Thus ended a conversation which, at no very distant time, led to such"
164 "grave results.  I must say here that facts seemed to confirm my"
164 "foresight, to the Canadian's great despair.  Did Captain Nemo distrust"
164 "us in these frequented seas? or did he only wish to hide himself from"
164 "the numerous vessels, of all nations, which ploughed the Mediterranean?"
164 "I could not tell; but we were oftener between waters and far from the"
164 "coast.  Or, if the Nautilus did emerge, nothing was to be seen but the"
164 "pilot's cage; and sometimes it went to great depths, for, between the"
164 "Grecian Archipelago and Asia Minor we could not touch the bottom by"
164 "more than a thousand fathoms."
164 ""
164 "Thus I only knew we were near the Island of Carpathos, one of the"
164 "Sporades, by Captain Nemo reciting these lines from Virgil:"
164 ""
164 "    \"Est Carpathio Neptuni gurgite vates,"
164 "     Caeruleus Proteus,\""
164 ""
164 "as he pointed to a spot on the planisphere."
164 ""
164 "It was indeed the ancient abode of Proteus, the old shepherd of"
164 "Neptune's flocks, now the Island of Scarpanto, situated between Rhodes"
164 "and Crete.  I saw nothing but the granite base through the glass panels"
164 "of the saloon."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 14th of February, I resolved to employ some hours in"
164 "studying the fishes of the Archipelago; but for some reason or other"
164 "the panels remained hermetically sealed.  Upon taking the course of the"
164 "Nautilus, I found that we were going towards Candia, the ancient Isle"
164 "of Crete.  At the time I embarked on the Abraham Lincoln, the whole of"
164 "this island had risen in insurrection against the despotism of the"
164 "Turks.  But how the insurgents had fared since that time I was"
164 "absolutely ignorant, and it was not Captain Nemo, deprived of all land"
164 "communications, who could tell me."
164 ""
164 "I made no allusion to this event when that night I found myself alone"
164 "with him in the saloon.  Besides, he seemed to be taciturn and"
164 "preoccupied.  Then, contrary to his custom, he ordered both panels to"
164 "be opened, and, going from one to the other, observed the mass of"
164 "waters attentively.  To what end I could not guess; so, on my side, I"
164 "employed my time in studying the fish passing before my eyes."
164 ""
164 "In the midst of the waters a man appeared, a diver, carrying at his"
