164 "belt a leathern purse.  It was not a body abandoned to the waves; it"
164 "was a living man, swimming with a strong hand, disappearing"
164 "occasionally to take breath at the surface."
164 ""
164 "I turned towards Captain Nemo, and in an agitated voice exclaimed:"
164 ""
164 "\"A man shipwrecked!  He must be saved at any price!\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain did not answer me, but came and leaned against the panel."
164 ""
164 "The man had approached, and, with his face flattened against the glass,"
164 "was looking at us."
164 ""
164 "To my great amazement, Captain Nemo signed to him.  The diver answered"
164 "with his hand, mounted immediately to the surface of the water, and did"
164 "not appear again."
164 ""
164 "\"Do not be uncomfortable,\" said Captain Nemo.  \"It is Nicholas of Cape"
164 "Matapan, surnamed Pesca.  He is well known in all the Cyclades.  A bold"
164 "diver! water is his element, and he lives more in it than on land,"
164 "going continually from one island to another, even as far as Crete.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You know him, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why not, M. Aronnax?\""
164 ""
164 "Saying which, Captain Nemo went towards a piece of furniture standing"
164 "near the left panel of the saloon.  Near this piece of furniture, I saw"
164 "a chest bound with iron, on the cover of which was a copper plate,"
164 "bearing the cypher of the Nautilus with its device."
164 ""
164 "At that moment, the Captain, without noticing my presence, opened the"
164 "piece of furniture, a sort of strong box, which held a great many"
164 "ingots."
164 ""
164 "They were ingots of gold.  From whence came this precious metal, which"
164 "represented an enormous sum?  Where did the Captain gather this gold"
164 "from? and what was he going to do with it?"
164 ""
164 "I did not say one word.  I looked.  Captain Nemo took the ingots one by"
164 "one, and arranged them methodically in the chest, which he filled"
164 "entirely.  I estimated the contents at more than 4,000 lb. weight of"
164 "gold, that is to say, nearly L200,000."
164 ""
164 "The chest was securely fastened, and the Captain wrote an address on"
164 "the lid, in characters which must have belonged to Modern Greece."
164 ""
164 "This done, Captain Nemo pressed a knob, the wire of which communicated"
164 "with the quarters of the crew.  Four men appeared, and, not without"
164 "some trouble, pushed the chest out of the saloon.  Then I heard them"
164 "hoisting it up the iron staircase by means of pulleys."
164 ""
164 "At that moment, Captain Nemo turned to me."
164 ""
164 "\"And you were saying, sir?\" said he."
164 ""
164 "\"I was saying nothing, Captain.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then, sir, if you will allow me, I will wish you good night.\""
164 ""
164 "Whereupon he turned and left the saloon."
164 ""
164 "I returned to my room much troubled, as one may believe.  I vainly"
164 "tried to sleep--I sought the connecting link between the apparition of"
164 "the diver and the chest filled with gold.  Soon, I felt by certain"
164 "movements of pitching and tossing that the Nautilus was leaving the"
164 "depths and returning to the surface."
164 ""
164 "Then I heard steps upon the platform; and I knew they were unfastening"
164 "the pinnace and launching it upon the waves.  For one instant it struck"
164 "the side of the Nautilus, then all noise ceased."
164 ""
164 "Two hours after, the same noise, the same going and coming was renewed;"
164 "the boat was hoisted on board, replaced in its socket, and the Nautilus"
164 "again plunged under the waves."
164 ""
164 "So these millions had been transported to their address.  To what point"
164 "of the continent?  Who was Captain Nemo's correspondent?"
164 ""
164 "The next day I related to Conseil and the Canadian the events of the"
164 "night, which had excited my curiosity to the highest degree.  My"
164 "companions were not less surprised than myself."
164 ""
164 "\"But where does he take his millions to?\" asked Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "To that there was no possible answer.  I returned to the saloon after"
164 "having breakfast and set to work.  Till five o'clock in the evening I"
164 "employed myself in arranging my notes.  At that moment--(ought I to"
164 "attribute it to some peculiar idiosyncrasy)--I felt so great a heat"
164 "that I was obliged to take off my coat.  It was strange, for we were"
164 "under low latitudes; and even then the Nautilus, submerged as it was,"
164 "ought to experience no change of temperature.  I looked at the"
164 "manometer; it showed a depth of sixty feet, to which atmospheric heat"
164 "could never attain."
164 ""
164 "I continued my work, but the temperature rose to such a pitch as to be"
164 "intolerable."
164 ""
164 "\"Could there be fire on board?\"  I asked myself."
164 ""
164 "I was leaving the saloon, when Captain Nemo entered; he approached the"
164 "thermometer, consulted it, and, turning to me, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"Forty-two degrees.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I have noticed it, Captain,\" I replied; \"and if it gets much hotter we"
164 "cannot bear it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Oh, sir, it will not get better if we do not wish it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You can reduce it as you please, then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No; but I can go farther from the stove which produces it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is outward, then!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly; we are floating in a current of boiling water.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Is it possible!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"Look.\""
164 ""
164 "The panels opened, and I saw the sea entirely white all round.  A"
164 "sulphurous smoke was curling amid the waves, which boiled like water in"
164 "a copper.  I placed my hand on one of the panes of glass, but the heat"
164 "was so great that I quickly took it off again."
164 ""
164 "\"Where are we?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Near the Island of Santorin, sir,\" replied the Captain.  \"I wished to"
164 "give you a sight of the curious spectacle of a submarine eruption.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I thought,\" said I, \"that the formation of these new islands was"
164 "ended.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Nothing is ever ended in the volcanic parts of the sea,\" replied"
164 "Captain Nemo; \"and the globe is always being worked by subterranean"
164 "fires.  Already, in the nineteenth year of our era, according to"
164 "Cassiodorus and Pliny, a new island, Theia (the divine), appeared in"
164 "the very place where these islets have recently been formed.  Then they"
164 "sank under the waves, to rise again in the year 69, when they again"
164 "subsided.  Since that time to our days the Plutonian work has been"
164 "suspended.  But on the 3rd of February, 1866, a new island, which they"
164 "named George Island, emerged from the midst of the sulphurous vapour"
164 "near Nea Kamenni, and settled again the 6th of the same month.  Seven"
164 "days after, the 13th of February, the Island of Aphroessa appeared,"
164 "leaving between Nea Kamenni and itself a canal ten yards broad.  I was"
164 "in these seas when the phenomenon occurred, and I was able therefore to"
164 "observe all the different phases.  The Island of Aphroessa, of round"
164 "form, measured 300 feet in diameter, and 30 feet in height.  It was"
164 "composed of black and vitreous lava, mixed with fragments of felspar."
164 "And lastly, on the 10th of March, a smaller island, called Reka, showed"
164 "itself near Nea Kamenni, and since then these three have joined"
164 "together, forming but one and the same island.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And the canal in which we are at this moment?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Here it is,\" replied Captain Nemo, showing me a map of the"
164 "Archipelago.  \"You see, I have marked the new islands.\""
164 ""
164 "I returned to the glass.  The Nautilus was no longer moving, the heat"
164 "was becoming unbearable.  The sea, which till now had been white, was"
164 "red, owing to the presence of salts of iron.  In spite of the ship's"
164 "being hermetically sealed, an insupportable smell of sulphur filled the"
164 "saloon, and the brilliancy of the electricity was entirely extinguished"
164 "by bright scarlet flames.  I was in a bath, I was choking, I was"
164 "broiled."
164 ""
164 "\"We can remain no longer in this boiling water,\" said I to the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"It would not be prudent,\" replied the impassive Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "An order was given; the Nautilus tacked about and left the furnace it"
164 "could not brave with impunity.  A quarter of an hour after we were"
164 "breathing fresh air on the surface.  The thought then struck me that,"
164 "if Ned Land had chosen this part of the sea for our flight, we should"
164 "never have come alive out of this sea of fire."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 16th of February, we left the basin which, between"
164 "Rhodes and Alexandria, is reckoned about 1,500 fathoms in depth, and"
164 "the Nautilus, passing some distance from Cerigo, quitted the Grecian"
164 "Archipelago after having doubled Cape Matapan."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VII"
164 ""
164 "THE MEDITERRANEAN IN FORTY-EIGHT HOURS"
164 ""
164 "The Mediterranean, the blue sea par excellence, \"the great sea\" of the"
164 "Hebrews, \"the sea\" of the Greeks, the \"mare nostrum\" of the Romans,"
164 "bordered by orange-trees, aloes, cacti, and sea-pines; embalmed with"
164 "the perfume of the myrtle, surrounded by rude mountains, saturated with"
164 "pure and transparent air, but incessantly worked by underground fires;"
164 "a perfect battlefield in which Neptune and Pluto still dispute the"
164 "empire of the world!"
164 ""
164 "It is upon these banks, and on these waters, says Michelet, that man is"
164 "renewed in one of the most powerful climates of the globe.  But,"
164 "beautiful as it was, I could only take a rapid glance at the basin"
164 "whose superficial area is two million of square yards.  Even Captain"
164 "Nemo's knowledge was lost to me, for this puzzling person did not"
164 "appear once during our passage at full speed.  I estimated the course"
164 "which the Nautilus took under the waves of the sea at about six hundred"
164 "leagues, and it was accomplished in forty-eight hours.  Starting on the"
164 "morning of the 16th of February from the shores of Greece, we had"
164 "crossed the Straits of Gibraltar by sunrise on the 18th."
164 ""
164 "It was plain to me that this Mediterranean, enclosed in the midst of"
164 "those countries which he wished to avoid, was distasteful to Captain"
164 "Nemo.  Those waves and those breezes brought back too many"
164 "remembrances, if not too many regrets.  Here he had no longer that"
164 "independence and that liberty of gait which he had when in the open"
164 "seas, and his Nautilus felt itself cramped between the close shores of"
164 "Africa and Europe."
164 ""
164 "Our speed was now twenty-five miles an hour.  It may be well understood"
164 "that Ned Land, to his great disgust, was obliged to renounce his"
164 "intended flight.  He could not launch the pinnace, going at the rate of"
164 "twelve or thirteen yards every second.  To quit the Nautilus under such"
164 "conditions would be as bad as jumping from a train going at full"
164 "speed--an imprudent thing, to say the least of it.  Besides, our vessel"
164 "only mounted to the surface of the waves at night to renew its stock of"
164 "air; it was steered entirely by the compass and the log."
164 ""
164 "I saw no more of the interior of this Mediterranean than a traveller by"
164 "express train perceives of the landscape which flies before his eyes;"
164 "that is to say, the distant horizon, and not the nearer objects which"
164 "pass like a flash of lightning."
164 ""
164 "We were then passing between Sicily and the coast of Tunis.  In the"
164 "narrow space between Cape Bon and the Straits of Messina the bottom of"
164 "the sea rose almost suddenly.  There was a perfect bank, on which there"
164 "was not more than nine fathoms of water, whilst on either side the"
164 "depth was ninety fathoms."
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus had to manoeuvre very carefully so as not to strike"
164 "against this submarine barrier."
164 ""
164 "I showed Conseil, on the map of the Mediterranean, the spot occupied by"
164 "this reef."
164 ""
164 "\"But if you please, sir,\" observed Conseil, \"it is like a real isthmus"
164 "joining Europe to Africa.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, my boy, it forms a perfect bar to the Straits of Lybia, and the"
164 "soundings of Smith have proved that in former times the continents"
164 "between Cape Boco and Cape Furina were joined.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I can well believe it,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"I will add,\" I continued, \"that a similar barrier exists between"
164 "Gibraltar and Ceuta, which in geological times formed the entire"
164 "Mediterranean.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What if some volcanic burst should one day raise these two barriers"
164 "above the waves?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is not probable, Conseil.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, but allow me to finish, please, sir; if this phenomenon should"
164 "take place, it will be troublesome for M. Lesseps, who has taken so"
164 "much pains to pierce the isthmus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I agree with you; but I repeat, Conseil, this phenomenon will never"
164 "happen.  The violence of subterranean force is ever diminishing."
164 "Volcanoes, so plentiful in the first days of the world, are being"
164 "extinguished by degrees; the internal heat is weakened, the temperature"
164 "of the lower strata of the globe is lowered by a perceptible quantity"
164 "every century to the detriment of our globe, for its heat is its life.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But the sun?\""
164 ""
164 "\"The sun is not sufficient, Conseil.  Can it give heat to a dead body?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Not that I know of.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, my friend, this earth will one day be that cold corpse; it will"
164 "become uninhabitable and uninhabited like the moon, which has long"
164 "since lost all its vital heat.\""
164 ""
164 "\"In how many centuries?\""
164 ""
164 "\"In some hundreds of thousands of years, my boy.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then,\" said Conseil, \"we shall have time to finish our journey--that"
164 "is, if Ned Land does not interfere with it.\""
164 ""
164 "And Conseil, reassured, returned to the study of the bank, which the"
164 "Nautilus was skirting at a moderate speed."
164 ""
164 "During the night of the 16th and 17th February we had entered the"
164 "second Mediterranean basin, the greatest depth of which was 1,450"
164 "fathoms.  The Nautilus, by the action of its crew, slid down the"
164 "inclined planes and buried itself in the lowest depths of the sea."
164 ""
164 "On the 18th of February, about three o'clock in the morning, we were at"
164 "the entrance of the Straits of Gibraltar.  There once existed two"
164 "currents: an upper one, long since recognised, which conveys the waters"
164 "of the ocean into the basin of the Mediterranean; and a lower"
164 "counter-current, which reasoning has now shown to exist.  Indeed, the"
164 "volume of water in the Mediterranean, incessantly added to by the waves"
164 "of the Atlantic and by rivers falling into it, would each year raise"
164 "the level of this sea, for its evaporation is not sufficient to restore"
164 "the equilibrium.  As it is not so, we must necessarily admit the"
164 "existence of an under-current, which empties into the basin of the"
164 "Atlantic through the Straits of Gibraltar the surplus waters of the"
164 "Mediterranean.  A fact indeed; and it was this counter-current by which"
164 "the Nautilus profited.  It advanced rapidly by the narrow pass.  For"
164 "one instant I caught a glimpse of the beautiful ruins of the temple of"
164 "Hercules, buried in the ground, according to Pliny, and with the low"
164 "island which supports it; and a few minutes later we were floating on"
164 "the Atlantic."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER VIII"
164 ""
164 "VIGO BAY"
164 ""
164 "The Atlantic! a vast sheet of water whose superficial area covers"
164 "twenty-five millions of square miles, the length of which is nine"
164 "thousand miles, with a mean breadth of two thousand seven hundred--an"
164 "ocean whose parallel winding shores embrace an immense circumference,"
164 "watered by the largest rivers of the world, the St. Lawrence, the"
164 "Mississippi, the Amazon, the Plata, the Orinoco, the Niger, the"
164 "Senegal, the Elbe, the Loire, and the Rhine, which carry water from the"
164 "most civilised, as well as from the most savage, countries!"
164 "Magnificent field of water, incessantly ploughed by vessels of every"
164 "nation, sheltered by the flags of every nation, and which terminates in"
164 "those two terrible points so dreaded by mariners, Cape Horn and the"
164 "Cape of Tempests."
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus was piercing the water with its sharp spur, after having"
164 "accomplished nearly ten thousand leagues in three months and a half, a"
164 "distance greater than the great circle of the earth.  Where were we"
164 "going now, and what was reserved for the future?  The Nautilus, leaving"
164 "the Straits of Gibraltar, had gone far out.  It returned to the surface"
164 "of the waves, and our daily walks on the platform were restored to us."
164 ""
164 "I mounted at once, accompanied by Ned Land and Conseil.  At a distance"
164 "of about twelve miles, Cape St. Vincent was dimly to be seen, forming"
164 "the south-western point of the Spanish peninsula.  A strong southerly"
164 "gale was blowing.  The sea was swollen and billowy; it made the"
164 "Nautilus rock violently.  It was almost impossible to keep one's foot"
164 "on the platform, which the heavy rolls of the sea beat over every"
164 "instant.  So we descended after inhaling some mouthfuls of fresh air."
164 ""
164 "I returned to my room, Conseil to his cabin; but the Canadian, with a"
164 "preoccupied air, followed me.  Our rapid passage across the"
164 "Mediterranean had not allowed him to put his project into execution,"
164 "and he could not help showing his disappointment.  When the door of my"
164 "room was shut, he sat down and looked at me silently."
164 ""
164 "\"Friend Ned,\" said I, \"I understand you; but you cannot reproach"
164 "yourself.  To have attempted to leave the Nautilus under the"
164 "circumstances would have been folly.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land did not answer; his compressed lips and frowning brow showed"
164 "with him the violent possession this fixed idea had taken of his mind."
164 ""
164 "\"Let us see,\" I continued; \"we need not despair yet.  We are going up"
164 "the coast of Portugal again; France and England are not far off, where"
164 "we can easily find refuge.  Now if the Nautilus, on leaving the Straits"
164 "of Gibraltar, had gone to the south, if it had carried us towards"
164 "regions where there were no continents, I should share your uneasiness."
164 "But we know now that Captain Nemo does not fly from civilised seas, and"
164 "in some days I think you can act with security.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land still looked at me fixedly; at length his fixed lips parted,"
164 "and he said, \"It is for to-night.\""
164 ""
164 "I drew myself up suddenly.  I was, I admit, little prepared for this"
164 "communication.  I wanted to answer the Canadian, but words would not"
164 "come."
164 ""
164 "\"We agreed to wait for an opportunity,\" continued Ned Land, \"and the"
164 "opportunity has arrived.  This night we shall be but a few miles from"
164 "the Spanish coast.  It is cloudy.  The wind blows freely.  I have your"
164 "word, M. Aronnax, and I rely upon you.\""
164 ""
164 "As I was silent, the Canadian approached me."
164 ""
164 "\"To-night, at nine o'clock,\" said he.  \"I have warned Conseil.  At that"
164 "moment Captain Nemo will be shut up in his room, probably in bed."
164 "Neither the engineers nor the ship's crew can see us.  Conseil and I"
164 "will gain the central staircase, and you, M. Aronnax, will remain in"
164 "the library, two steps from us, waiting my signal.  The oars, the mast,"
164 "and the sail are in the canoe.  I have even succeeded in getting some"
164 "provisions.  I have procured an English wrench, to unfasten the bolts"
164 "which attach it to the shell of the Nautilus.  So all is ready, till"
164 "to-night.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The sea is bad.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That I allow,\" replied the Canadian; \"but we must risk that.  Liberty"
164 "is worth paying for; besides, the boat is strong, and a few miles with"
164 "a fair wind to carry us is no great thing.  Who knows but by to-morrow"
164 "we may be a hundred leagues away?  Let circumstances only favour us,"
164 "and by ten or eleven o'clock we shall have landed on some spot of terra"
164 "firma, alive or dead.  But adieu now till to-night.\""
164 ""
164 "With these words the Canadian withdrew, leaving me almost dumb.  I had"
164 "imagined that, the chance gone, I should have time to reflect and"
164 "discuss the matter.  My obstinate companion had given me no time; and,"
164 "after all, what could I have said to him?  Ned Land was perfectly"
164 "right.  There was almost the opportunity to profit by.  Could I retract"
164 "my word, and take upon myself the responsibility of compromising the"
164 "future of my companions?  To-morrow Captain Nemo might take us far from"
164 "all land."
164 ""
164 "At that moment a rather loud hissing noise told me that the reservoirs"
164 "were filling, and that the Nautilus was sinking under the waves of the"
164 "Atlantic."
164 ""
164 "A sad day I passed, between the desire of regaining my liberty of"
164 "action and of abandoning the wonderful Nautilus, and leaving my"
164 "submarine studies incomplete."
164 ""
164 "What dreadful hours I passed thus!  Sometimes seeing myself and"
164 "companions safely landed, sometimes wishing, in spite of my reason,"
164 "that some unforeseen circumstance, would prevent the realisation of Ned"
164 "Land's project."
164 ""
164 "Twice I went to the saloon.  I wished to consult the compass.  I wished"
164 "to see if the direction the Nautilus was taking was bringing us nearer"
164 "or taking us farther from the coast.  But no; the Nautilus kept in"
164 "Portuguese waters."
164 ""
164 "I must therefore take my part and prepare for flight.  My luggage was"
164 "not heavy; my notes, nothing more."
164 ""
164 "As to Captain Nemo, I asked myself what he would think of our escape;"
164 "what trouble, what wrong it might cause him and what he might do in"
164 "case of its discovery or failure.  Certainly I had no cause to complain"
164 "of him; on the contrary, never was hospitality freer than his.  In"
164 "leaving him I could not be taxed with ingratitude.  No oath bound us to"
164 "him.  It was on the strength of circumstances he relied, and not upon"
164 "our word, to fix us for ever."
164 ""
164 "I had not seen the Captain since our visit to the Island of Santorin."
164 "Would chance bring me to his presence before our departure?  I wished"
164 "it, and I feared it at the same time.  I listened if I could hear him"
164 "walking the room contiguous to mine.  No sound reached my ear.  I felt"
164 "an unbearable uneasiness.  This day of waiting seemed eternal.  Hours"
164 "struck too slowly to keep pace with my impatience."
164 ""
164 "My dinner was served in my room as usual.  I ate but little; I was too"
164 "preoccupied.  I left the table at seven o'clock. A hundred and twenty"
164 "minutes (I counted them) still separated me from the moment in which I"
164 "was to join Ned Land.  My agitation redoubled.  My pulse beat"
164 "violently.  I could not remain quiet.  I went and came, hoping to calm"
164 "my troubled spirit by constant movement.  The idea of failure in our"
164 "bold enterprise was the least painful of my anxieties; but the thought"
164 "of seeing our project discovered before leaving the Nautilus, of being"
164 "brought before Captain Nemo, irritated, or (what was worse) saddened,"
164 "at my desertion, made my heart beat."
164 ""
164 "I wanted to see the saloon for the last time.  I descended the stairs"
164 "and arrived in the museum, where I had passed so many useful and"
164 "agreeable hours.  I looked at all its riches, all its treasures, like a"
164 "man on the eve of an eternal exile, who was leaving never to return."
164 ""
164 "These wonders of Nature, these masterpieces of art, amongst which for"
164 "so many days my life had been concentrated, I was going to abandon them"
164 "for ever!  I should like to have taken a last look through the windows"
164 "of the saloon into the waters of the Atlantic:  but the panels were"
164 "hermetically closed, and a cloak of steel separated me from that ocean"
164 "which I had not yet explored."
164 ""
164 "In passing through the saloon, I came near the door let into the angle"
164 "which opened into the Captain's room.  To my great surprise, this door"
164 "was ajar.  I drew back involuntarily.  If Captain Nemo should be in his"
164 "room, he could see me.  But, hearing no sound, I drew nearer.  The room"
164 "was deserted.  I pushed open the door and took some steps forward."
164 "Still the same monklike severity of aspect."
164 ""
164 "Suddenly the clock struck eight.  The first beat of the hammer on the"
164 "bell awoke me from my dreams.  I trembled as if an invisible eye had"
164 "plunged into my most secret thoughts, and I hurried from the room."
164 ""
164 "There my eye fell upon the compass.  Our course was still north.  The"
164 "log indicated moderate speed, the manometer a depth of about sixty feet."
164 ""
164 "I returned to my room, clothed myself warmly--sea boots, an otterskin"
164 "cap, a great coat of byssus, lined with sealskin; I was ready, I was"
164 "waiting.  The vibration of the screw alone broke the deep silence which"
164 "reigned on board.  I listened attentively.  Would no loud voice"
164 "suddenly inform me that Ned Land had been surprised in his projected"
164 "flight.  A mortal dread hung over me, and I vainly tried to regain my"
164 "accustomed coolness."
164 ""
164 "At a few minutes to nine, I put my ear to the Captain's door.  No"
164 "noise.  I left my room and returned to the saloon, which was half in"
164 "obscurity, but deserted."
164 ""
164 "I opened the door communicating with the library.  The same"
164 "insufficient light, the same solitude.  I placed myself near the door"
164 "leading to the central staircase, and there waited for Ned Land's"
164 "signal."
164 ""
164 "At that moment the trembling of the screw sensibly diminished, then it"
164 "stopped entirely.  The silence was now only disturbed by the beatings"
164 "of my own heart.  Suddenly a slight shock was felt; and I knew that the"
164 "Nautilus had stopped at the bottom of the ocean.  My uneasiness"
164 "increased.  The Canadian's signal did not come.  I felt inclined to"
164 "join Ned Land and beg of him to put off his attempt.  I felt that we"
164 "were not sailing under our usual conditions."
164 ""
164 "At this moment the door of the large saloon opened, and Captain Nemo"
164 "appeared.  He saw me, and without further preamble began in an amiable"
164 "tone of voice:"
164 ""
164 "\"Ah, sir!  I have been looking for you.  Do you know the history of"
164 "Spain?\""
164 ""
164 "Now, one might know the history of one's own country by heart; but in"
164 "the condition I was at the time, with troubled mind and head quite"
164 "lost, I could not have said a word of it."
164 ""
164 "\"Well,\" continued Captain Nemo, \"you heard my question!  Do you know"
164 "the history of Spain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very slightly,\" I answered."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, here are learned men having to learn,\" said the Captain.  \"Come,"
164 "sit down, and I will tell you a curious episode in this history.  Sir,"
164 "listen well,\" said he; \"this history will interest you on one side, for"
164 "it will answer a question which doubtless you have not been able to"
164 "solve.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I listen, Captain,\" said I, not knowing what my interlocutor was"
164 "driving at, and asking myself if this incident was bearing on our"
164 "projected flight."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, if you have no objection, we will go back to 1702.  You cannot be"
164 "ignorant that your king, Louis XIV, thinking that the gesture of a"
164 "potentate was sufficient to bring the Pyrenees under his yoke, had"
164 "imposed the Duke of Anjou, his grandson, on the Spaniards.  This prince"
164 "reigned more or less badly under the name of Philip V, and had a strong"
164 "party against him abroad.  Indeed, the preceding year, the royal houses"
164 "of Holland, Austria, and England had concluded a treaty of alliance at"
164 "the Hague, with the intention of plucking the crown of Spain from the"
164 "head of Philip V, and placing it on that of an archduke to whom they"
164 "prematurely gave the title of Charles III."
164 ""
164 "\"Spain must resist this coalition; but she was almost entirely"
164 "unprovided with either soldiers or sailors.  However, money would not"
164 "fail them, provided that their galleons, laden with gold and silver"
164 "from America, once entered their ports.  And about the end of 1702 they"
164 "expected a rich convoy which France was escorting with a fleet of"
164 "twenty-three vessels, commanded by Admiral Chateau-Renaud, for the"
164 "ships of the coalition were already beating the Atlantic.  This convoy"
164 "was to go to Cadiz, but the Admiral, hearing that an English fleet was"
164 "cruising in those waters, resolved to make for a French port."
164 ""
164 "\"The Spanish commanders of the convoy objected to this decision.  They"
164 "wanted to be taken to a Spanish port, and, if not to Cadiz, into Vigo"
164 "Bay, situated on the northwest coast of Spain, and which was not"
164 "blocked."
164 ""
164 "\"Admiral Chateau-Renaud had the rashness to obey this injunction, and"
164 "the galleons entered Vigo Bay."
164 ""
164 "\"Unfortunately, it formed an open road which could not be defended in"
164 "any way.  They must therefore hasten to unload the galleons before the"
164 "arrival of the combined fleet; and time would not have failed them had"
164 "not a miserable question of rivalry suddenly arisen."
164 ""
164 "\"You are following the chain of events?\" asked Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"Perfectly,\" said I, not knowing the end proposed by this historical"
164 "lesson."
164 ""
164 "\"I will continue.  This is what passed.  The merchants of Cadiz had a"
164 "privilege by which they had the right of receiving all merchandise"
164 "coming from the West Indies.  Now, to disembark these ingots at the"
164 "port of Vigo was depriving them of their rights.  They complained at"
164 "Madrid, and obtained the consent of the weak-minded Philip that the"
164 "convoy, without discharging its cargo, should remain sequestered in the"
164 "roads of Vigo until the enemy had disappeared."
164 ""
164 "\"But whilst coming to this decision, on the 22nd of October, 1702, the"
164 "English vessels arrived in Vigo Bay, when Admiral Chateau-Renaud, in"
164 "spite of inferior forces, fought bravely.  But, seeing that the"
164 "treasure must fall into the enemy's hands, he burnt and scuttled every"
164 "galleon, which went to the bottom with their immense riches.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo stopped.  I admit I could not see yet why this history"
164 "should interest me."
164 ""
164 "\"Well?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, M. Aronnax,\" replied Captain Nemo, \"we are in that Vigo Bay; and"
164 "it rests with yourself whether you will penetrate its mysteries.\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain rose, telling me to follow him.  I had had time to recover."
164 "I obeyed.  The saloon was dark, but through the transparent glass the"
164 "waves were sparkling.  I looked."
164 ""
164 "For half a mile around the Nautilus, the waters seemed bathed in"
164 "electric light.  The sandy bottom was clean and bright.  Some of the"
164 "ship's crew in their diving-dresses were clearing away half-rotten"
164 "barrels and empty cases from the midst of the blackened wrecks.  From"
164 "these cases and from these barrels escaped ingots of gold and silver,"
164 "cascades of piastres and jewels.  The sand was heaped up with them."
164 "Laden with their precious booty, the men returned to the Nautilus,"
164 "disposed of their burden, and went back to this inexhaustible fishery"
164 "of gold and silver."
164 ""
164 "I understood now.  This was the scene of the battle of the 22nd of"
164 "October, 1702.  Here on this very spot the galleons laden for the"
164 "Spanish Government had sunk.  Here Captain Nemo came, according to his"
164 "wants, to pack up those millions with which he burdened the Nautilus."
164 "It was for him and him alone America had given up her precious metals."
164 "He was heir direct, without anyone to share, in those treasures torn"
164 "from the Incas and from the conquered of Ferdinand Cortez."
164 ""
164 "\"Did you know, sir,\" he asked, smiling, \"that the sea contained such"
164 "riches?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I knew,\" I answered, \"that they value money held in suspension in"
164 "these waters at two millions.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Doubtless; but to extract this money the expense would be greater than"
164 "the profit.  Here, on the contrary, I have but to pick up what man has"
164 "lost--and not only in Vigo Bay, but in a thousand other ports where"
164 "shipwrecks have happened, and which are marked on my submarine map."
164 "Can you understand now the source of the millions I am worth?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I understand, Captain.  But allow me to tell you that in exploring"
164 "Vigo Bay you have only been beforehand with a rival society.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And which?\""
164 ""
164 "\"A society which has received from the Spanish Government the privilege"
164 "of seeking those buried galleons.  The shareholders are led on by the"
164 "allurement of an enormous bounty, for they value these rich shipwrecks"
164 "at five hundred millions.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Five hundred millions they were,\" answered Captain Nemo, \"but they are"
164 "so no longer.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so,\" said I; \"and a warning to those shareholders would be an act"
164 "of charity.  But who knows if it would be well received?  What gamblers"
164 "usually regret above all is less the loss of their money than of their"
164 "foolish hopes.  After all, I pity them less than the thousands of"
164 "unfortunates to whom so much riches well-distributed would have been"
164 "profitable, whilst for them they will be for ever barren.\""
164 ""
164 "I had no sooner expressed this regret than I felt that it must have"
164 "wounded Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"Barren!\" he exclaimed, with animation.  \"Do you think then, sir, that"
164 "these riches are lost because I gather them?  Is it for myself alone,"
164 "according to your idea, that I take the trouble to collect these"
164 "treasures?  Who told you that I did not make a good use of it?  Do you"
164 "think I am ignorant that there are suffering beings and oppressed races"
164 "on this earth, miserable creatures to console, victims to avenge?  Do"
164 "you not understand?\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo stopped at these last words, regretting perhaps that he"
164 "had spoken so much.  But I had guessed that, whatever the motive which"
164 "had forced him to seek independence under the sea, it had left him"
164 "still a man, that his heart still beat for the sufferings of humanity,"
164 "and that his immense charity was for oppressed races as well as"
164 "individuals.  And I then understood for whom those millions were"
164 "destined which were forwarded by Captain Nemo when the Nautilus was"
164 "cruising in the waters of Crete."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER IX"
164 ""
164 "A VANISHED CONTINENT"
164 ""
164 "The next morning, the 19th of February, I saw the Canadian enter my"
164 "room.  I expected this visit.  He looked very disappointed."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir?\" said he."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Ned, fortune was against us yesterday.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; that Captain must needs stop exactly at the hour we intended"
164 "leaving his vessel.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Ned, he had business at his bankers.\""
164 ""
164 "\"His bankers!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Or rather his banking-house; by that I mean the ocean, where his"
164 "riches are safer than in the chests of the State.\""
164 ""
164 "I then related to the Canadian the incidents of the preceding night,"
164 "hoping to bring him back to the idea of not abandoning the Captain; but"
164 "my recital had no other result than an energetically expressed regret"
164 "from Ned that he had not been able to take a walk on the battlefield of"
164 "Vigo on his own account."
164 ""
164 "\"However,\" said he, \"all is not ended.  It is only a blow of the"
164 "harpoon lost.  Another time we must succeed; and to-night, if"
164 "necessary----\""
164 ""
164 "\"In what direction is the Nautilus going?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"I do not know,\" replied Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, at noon we shall see the point.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian returned to Conseil.  As soon as I was dressed, I went"
164 "into the saloon.  The compass was not reassuring.  The course of the"
164 "Nautilus was S.S.W. We were turning our backs on Europe."
164 ""
164 "I waited with some impatience till the ship's place was pricked on the"
164 "chart.  At about half-past eleven the reservoirs were emptied, and our"
164 "vessel rose to the surface of the ocean.  I rushed towards the"
164 "platform.  Ned Land had preceded me.  No more land in sight.  Nothing"
164 "but an immense sea.  Some sails on the horizon, doubtless those going"
164 "to San Roque in search of favourable winds for doubling the Cape of"
164 "Good Hope.  The weather was cloudy.  A gale of wind was preparing.  Ned"
164 "raved, and tried to pierce the cloudy horizon.  He still hoped that"
164 "behind all that fog stretched the land he so longed for."
164 ""
164 "At noon the sun showed itself for an instant.  The second profited by"
164 "this brightness to take its height.  Then, the sea becoming more"
164 "billowy, we descended, and the panel closed."
164 ""
164 "An hour after, upon consulting the chart, I saw the position of the"
164 "Nautilus was marked at 16 deg. 17' long., and 33 deg. 22' lat., at 150"
164 "leagues from the nearest coast.  There was no means of flight, and I"
164 "leave you to imagine the rage of the Canadian when I informed him of"
164 "our situation."
164 ""
164 "For myself, I was not particularly sorry.  I felt lightened of the load"
164 "which had oppressed me, and was able to return with some degree of"
164 "calmness to my accustomed work."
164 ""
164 "That night, about eleven o'clock, I received a most unexpected visit"
164 "from Captain Nemo.  He asked me very graciously if I felt fatigued from"
164 "my watch of the preceding night.  I answered in the negative."
164 ""
164 "\"Then, M. Aronnax, I propose a curious excursion.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Propose, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You have hitherto only visited the submarine depths by daylight, under"
164 "the brightness of the sun.  Would it suit you to see them in the"
164 "darkness of the night?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Most willingly.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I warn you, the way will be tiring.  We shall have far to walk, and"
164 "must climb a mountain.  The roads are not well kept.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What you say, Captain, only heightens my curiosity; I am ready to"
164 "follow you.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Come then, sir, we will put on our diving-dresses.\""
164 ""
164 "Arrived at the robing-room, I saw that neither of my companions nor any"
164 "of the ship's crew were to follow us on this excursion.  Captain Nemo"
164 "had not even proposed my taking with me either Ned or Conseil."
164 ""
164 "In a few moments we had put on our diving-dresses; they placed on our"
164 "backs the reservoirs, abundantly filled with air, but no electric lamps"
164 "were prepared.  I called the Captain's attention to the fact."
164 ""
164 "\"They will be useless,\" he replied."
164 ""
164 "I thought I had not heard aright, but I could not repeat my"
164 "observation, for the Captain's head had already disappeared in its"
164 "metal case.  I finished harnessing myself.  I felt them put an"
164 "iron-pointed stick into my hand, and some minutes later, after going"
164 "through the usual form, we set foot on the bottom of the Atlantic at a"
164 "depth of 150 fathoms.  Midnight was near.  The waters were profoundly"
164 "dark, but Captain Nemo pointed out in the distance a reddish spot, a"
164 "sort of large light shining brilliantly about two miles from the"
164 "Nautilus.  What this fire might be, what could feed it, why and how it"
164 "lit up the liquid mass, I could not say.  In any case, it did light our"
164 "way, vaguely, it is true, but I soon accustomed myself to the peculiar"
164 "darkness, and I understood, under such circumstances, the uselessness"
164 "of the Ruhmkorff apparatus."
164 ""
164 "As we advanced, I heard a kind of pattering above my head.  The noise"
164 "redoubling, sometimes producing a continual shower, I soon understood"
164 "the cause.  It was rain falling violently, and crisping the surface of"
164 "the waves.  Instinctively the thought flashed across my mind that I"
164 "should be wet through!  By the water! in the midst of the water!  I"
164 "could not help laughing at the odd idea.  But, indeed, in the thick"
164 "diving-dress, the liquid element is no longer felt, and one only seems"
164 "to be in an atmosphere somewhat denser than the terrestrial atmosphere."
164 "Nothing more."
164 ""
164 "After half an hour's walk the soil became stony.  Medusae, microscopic"
164 "crustacea, and pennatules lit it slightly with their phosphorescent"
164 "gleam.  I caught a glimpse of pieces of stone covered with millions of"
164 "zoophytes and masses of sea weed.  My feet often slipped upon this"
164 "sticky carpet of sea weed, and without my iron-tipped stick I should"
164 "have fallen more than once.  In turning round, I could still see the"
164 "whitish lantern of the Nautilus beginning to pale in the distance."
164 ""
164 "But the rosy light which guided us increased and lit up the horizon."
164 "The presence of this fire under water puzzled me in the highest degree."
164 "Was I going towards a natural phenomenon as yet unknown to the _savants_"
164 "of the earth?  Or even (for this thought crossed my brain) had the hand"
164 "of man aught to do with this conflagration?  Had he fanned this flame?"
164 "Was I to meet in these depths companions and friends of Captain Nemo"
164 "whom he was going to visit, and who, like him, led this strange"
164 "existence?  Should I find down there a whole colony of exiles who,"
164 "weary of the miseries of this earth, had sought and found independence"
164 "in the deep ocean?  All these foolish and unreasonable ideas pursued"
164 "me.  And in this condition of mind, over-excited by the succession of"
164 "wonders continually passing before my eyes, I should not have been"
164 "surprised to meet at the bottom of the sea one of those submarine towns"
164 "of which Captain Nemo dreamed."
164 ""
164 "Our road grew lighter and lighter.  The white glimmer came in rays from"
164 "the summit of a mountain about 800 feet high.  But what I saw was"
164 "simply a reflection, developed by the clearness of the waters.  The"
164 "source of this inexplicable light was a fire on the opposite side of"
164 "the mountain."
164 ""
164 "In the midst of this stony maze furrowing the bottom of the Atlantic,"
164 "Captain Nemo advanced without hesitation.  He knew this dreary road."
164 "Doubtless he had often travelled over it, and could not lose himself."
164 "I followed him with unshaken confidence.  He seemed to me like a genie"
164 "of the sea; and, as he walked before me, I could not help admiring his"
164 "stature, which was outlined in black on the luminous horizon."
164 ""
164 "It was one in the morning when we arrived at the first slopes of the"
164 "mountain; but to gain access to them we must venture through the"
164 "difficult paths of a vast copse."
164 ""
164 "Yes; a copse of dead trees, without leaves, without sap, trees"
164 "petrified by the action of the water and here and there overtopped by"
164 "gigantic pines.  It was like a coal-pit still standing, holding by the"
164 "roots to the broken soil, and whose branches, like fine black paper"
164 "cuttings, showed distinctly on the watery ceiling.  Picture to yourself"
164 "a forest in the Hartz hanging on to the sides of the mountain, but a"
164 "forest swallowed up.  The paths were encumbered with seaweed and fucus,"
164 "between which grovelled a whole world of crustacea.  I went along,"
164 "climbing the rocks, striding over extended trunks, breaking the sea"
164 "bind-weed which hung from one tree to the other; and frightening the"
164 "fishes, which flew from branch to branch.  Pressing onward, I felt no"
164 "fatigue.  I followed my guide, who was never tired.  What a spectacle!"
164 "How can I express it? how paint the aspect of those woods and rocks in"
164 "this medium--their under parts dark and wild, the upper coloured with"
164 "red tints, by that light which the reflecting powers of the waters"
164 "doubled?  We climbed rocks which fell directly after with gigantic"
164 "bounds and the low growling of an avalanche.  To right and left ran"
164 "long, dark galleries, where sight was lost.  Here opened vast glades"
164 "which the hand of man seemed to have worked; and I sometimes asked"
164 "myself if some inhabitant of these submarine regions would not suddenly"
164 "appear to me."
164 ""
164 "But Captain Nemo was still mounting.  I could not stay behind.  I"
164 "followed boldly.  My stick gave me good help.  A false step would have"
164 "been dangerous on the narrow passes sloping down to the sides of the"
164 "gulfs; but I walked with firm step, without feeling any giddiness.  Now"
164 "I jumped a crevice, the depth of which would have made me hesitate had"
164 "it been among the glaciers on the land; now I ventured on the unsteady"
164 "trunk of a tree thrown across from one abyss to the other, without"
164 "looking under my feet, having only eyes to admire the wild sites of"
164 "this region."
164 ""
164 "There, monumental rocks, leaning on their regularly-cut bases, seemed"
164 "to defy all laws of equilibrium.  From between their stony knees trees"
164 "sprang, like a jet under heavy pressure, and upheld others which upheld"
164 "them.  Natural towers, large scarps, cut perpendicularly, like a"
164 "\"curtain,\" inclined at an angle which the laws of gravitation could"
164 "never have tolerated in terrestrial regions."
164 ""
164 "Two hours after quitting the Nautilus we had crossed the line of trees,"
164 "and a hundred feet above our heads rose the top of the mountain, which"
164 "cast a shadow on the brilliant irradiation of the opposite slope.  Some"
164 "petrified shrubs ran fantastically here and there.  Fishes got up under"
164 "our feet like birds in the long grass.  The massive rocks were rent"
164 "with impenetrable fractures, deep grottos, and unfathomable holes, at"
164 "the bottom of which formidable creatures might be heard moving.  My"
164 "blood curdled when I saw enormous antennae blocking my road, or some"
164 "frightful claw closing with a noise in the shadow of some cavity."
164 "Millions of luminous spots shone brightly in the midst of the darkness."
164 "They were the eyes of giant crustacea crouched in their holes; giant"
164 "lobsters setting themselves up like halberdiers, and moving their claws"
164 "with the clicking sound of pincers; titanic crabs, pointed like a gun"
164 "on its carriage; and frightful-looking poulps, interweaving their"
164 "tentacles like a living nest of serpents."
164 ""
164 "We had now arrived on the first platform, where other surprises awaited"
164 "me.  Before us lay some picturesque ruins, which betrayed the hand of"
164 "man and not that of the Creator.  There were vast heaps of stone,"
164 "amongst which might be traced the vague and shadowy forms of castles"
164 "and temples, clothed with a world of blossoming zoophytes, and over"
164 "which, instead of ivy, sea-weed and fucus threw a thick vegetable"
164 "mantle.  But what was this portion of the globe which had been"
164 "swallowed by cataclysms?  Who had placed those rocks and stones like"
164 "cromlechs of prehistoric times?  Where was I?  Whither had Captain"
164 "Nemo's fancy hurried me?"
164 ""
164 "I would fain have asked him; not being able to, I stopped him--I seized"
164 "his arm.  But, shaking his head, and pointing to the highest point of"
164 "the mountain, he seemed to say:"
164 ""
164 "\"Come, come along; come higher!\""
164 ""
164 "I followed, and in a few minutes I had climbed to the top, which for a"
164 "circle of ten yards commanded the whole mass of rock."
164 ""
164 "I looked down the side we had just climbed.  The mountain did not rise"
164 "more than seven or eight hundred feet above the level of the plain; but"
164 "on the opposite side it commanded from twice that height the depths of"
164 "this part of the Atlantic.  My eyes ranged far over a large space lit"
164 "by a violent fulguration.  In fact, the mountain was a volcano."
164 ""
164 "At fifty feet above the peak, in the midst of a rain of stones and"
164 "scoriae, a large crater was vomiting forth torrents of lava which fell"
164 "in a cascade of fire into the bosom of the liquid mass.  Thus situated,"
164 "this volcano lit the lower plain like an immense torch, even to the"
164 "extreme limits of the horizon.  I said that the submarine crater threw"
164 "up lava, but no flames.  Flames require the oxygen of the air to feed"
164 "upon and cannot be developed under water; but streams of lava, having"
164 "in themselves the principles of their incandescence, can attain a white"
164 "heat, fight vigorously against the liquid element, and turn it to"
164 "vapour by contact."
164 ""
164 "Rapid currents bearing all these gases in diffusion and torrents of"
164 "lava slid to the bottom of the mountain like an eruption of Vesuvius on"
164 "another Terra del Greco."
164 ""
164 "There indeed under my eyes, ruined, destroyed, lay a town--its roofs"
164 "open to the sky, its temples fallen, its arches dislocated, its columns"
164 "lying on the ground, from which one would still recognise the massive"
164 "character of Tuscan architecture.  Further on, some remains of a"
164 "gigantic aqueduct; here the high base of an Acropolis, with the"
164 "floating outline of a Parthenon; there traces of a quay, as if an"
164 "ancient port had formerly abutted on the borders of the ocean, and"
164 "disappeared with its merchant vessels and its war-galleys. Farther on"
164 "again, long lines of sunken walls and broad, deserted streets--a"
164 "perfect Pompeii escaped beneath the waters.  Such was the sight that"
164 "Captain Nemo brought before my eyes!"
164 ""
164 "Where was I?  Where was I?  I must know at any cost.  I tried to speak,"
164 "but Captain Nemo stopped me by a gesture, and, picking up a piece of"
164 "chalk-stone, advanced to a rock of black basalt, and traced the one"
164 "word:"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "ATLANTIS"
164 ""
164 ""
164 "What a light shot through my mind!  Atlantis! the Atlantis of Plato,"
164 "that continent denied by Origen and Humbolt, who placed its"
164 "disappearance amongst the legendary tales.  I had it there now before"
164 "my eyes, bearing upon it the unexceptionable testimony of its"
164 "catastrophe.  The region thus engulfed was beyond Europe, Asia, and"
164 "Lybia, beyond the columns of Hercules, where those powerful people, the"
164 "Atlantides, lived, against whom the first wars of ancient Greeks were"
164 "waged."
164 ""
164 "Thus, led by the strangest destiny, I was treading under foot the"
164 "mountains of this continent, touching with my hand those ruins a"
164 "thousand generations old and contemporary with the geological epochs."
164 "I was walking on the very spot where the contemporaries of the first"
164 "man had walked."
164 ""
164 "Whilst I was trying to fix in my mind every detail of this grand"
164 "landscape, Captain Nemo remained motionless, as if petrified in mute"
164 "ecstasy, leaning on a mossy stone.  Was he dreaming of those"
164 "generations long since disappeared?  Was he asking them the secret of"
164 "human destiny?  Was it here this strange man came to steep himself in"
164 "historical recollections, and live again this ancient life--he who"
164 "wanted no modern one?  What would I not have given to know his"
164 "thoughts, to share them, to understand them!  We remained for an hour"
164 "at this place, contemplating the vast plains under the brightness of"
164 "the lava, which was some times wonderfully intense.  Rapid tremblings"
164 "ran along the mountain caused by internal bubblings, deep noise,"
164 "distinctly transmitted through the liquid medium were echoed with"
164 "majestic grandeur.  At this moment the moon appeared through the mass"
164 "of waters and threw her pale rays on the buried continent.  It was but"
164 "a gleam, but what an indescribable effect!  The Captain rose, cast one"
164 "last look on the immense plain, and then bade me follow him."
164 ""
164 "We descended the mountain rapidly, and, the mineral forest once passed,"
164 "I saw the lantern of the Nautilus shining like a star.  The Captain"
164 "walked straight to it, and we got on board as the first rays of light"
164 "whitened the surface of the ocean."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER X"
164 ""
164 "THE SUBMARINE COAL-MINES"
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 20th of February, I awoke very late:  the fatigues of"
164 "the previous night had prolonged my sleep until eleven o'clock. I"
164 "dressed quickly, and hastened to find the course the Nautilus was"
164 "taking.  The instruments showed it to be still toward the south, with a"
164 "speed of twenty miles an hour and a depth of fifty fathoms."
164 ""
164 "The species of fishes here did not differ much from those already"
164 "noticed.  There were rays of giant size, five yards long, and endowed"
164 "with great muscular strength, which enabled them to shoot above the"
164 "waves; sharks of many kinds; amongst others, one fifteen feet long,"
164 "with triangular sharp teeth, and whose transparency rendered it almost"
164 "invisible in the water."
164 ""
164 "Amongst bony fish Conseil noticed some about three yards long, armed at"
164 "the upper jaw with a piercing sword; other bright-coloured creatures,"
164 "known in the time of Aristotle by the name of the sea-dragon, which are"
164 "dangerous to capture on account of the spikes on their back."
164 ""
164 "About four o'clock, the soil, generally composed of a thick mud mixed"
164 "with petrified wood, changed by degrees, and it became more stony, and"
164 "seemed strewn with conglomerate and pieces of basalt, with a sprinkling"
164 "of lava.  I thought that a mountainous region was succeeding the long"
164 "plains; and accordingly, after a few evolutions of the Nautilus, I saw"
164 "the southerly horizon blocked by a high wall which seemed to close all"
164 "exit.  Its summit evidently passed the level of the ocean.  It must be"
164 "a continent, or at least an island--one of the Canaries, or of the Cape"
164 "Verde Islands.  The bearings not being yet taken, perhaps designedly, I"
164 "was ignorant of our exact position.  In any case, such a wall seemed to"
164 "me to mark the limits of that Atlantis, of which we had in reality"
164 "passed over only the smallest part."
164 ""
164 "Much longer should I have remained at the window admiring the beauties"
164 "of sea and sky, but the panels closed.  At this moment the Nautilus"
164 "arrived at the side of this high, perpendicular wall.  What it would"
164 "do, I could not guess.  I returned to my room; it no longer moved.  I"
164 "laid myself down with the full intention of waking after a few hours'"
164 "sleep; but it was eight o'clock the next day when I entered the saloon."
164 "I looked at the manometer.  It told me that the Nautilus was floating"
164 "on the surface of the ocean.  Besides, I heard steps on the platform."
164 "I went to the panel.  It was open; but, instead of broad daylight, as I"
164 "expected, I was surrounded by profound darkness.  Where were we?  Was I"
164 "mistaken?  Was it still night?  No; not a star was shining and night"
164 "has not that utter darkness."
164 ""
164 "I knew not what to think, when a voice near me said:"
164 ""
164 "\"Is that you, Professor?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!  Captain,\" I answered, \"where are we?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Underground, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Underground!\"  I exclaimed.  \"And the Nautilus floating still?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It always floats.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But I do not understand.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Wait a few minutes, our lantern will be lit, and, if you like light"
164 "places, you will be satisfied.\""
164 ""
164 "I stood on the platform and waited.  The darkness was so complete that"
164 "I could not even see Captain Nemo; but, looking to the zenith, exactly"
164 "above my head, I seemed to catch an undecided gleam, a kind of twilight"
164 "filling a circular hole.  At this instant the lantern was lit, and its"
164 "vividness dispelled the faint light.  I closed my dazzled eyes for an"
164 "instant, and then looked again.  The Nautilus was stationary, floating"
164 "near a mountain which formed a sort of quay.  The lake, then,"
164 "supporting it was a lake imprisoned by a circle of walls, measuring two"
164 "miles in diameter and six in circumference.  Its level (the manometer"
164 "showed) could only be the same as the outside level, for there must"
164 "necessarily be a communication between the lake and the sea.  The high"
164 "partitions, leaning forward on their base, grew into a vaulted roof"
164 "bearing the shape of an immense funnel turned upside down, the height"
164 "being about five or six hundred yards.  At the summit was a circular"
164 "orifice, by which I had caught the slight gleam of light, evidently"
164 "daylight."
164 ""
164 "\"Where are we?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"In the very heart of an extinct volcano, the interior of which has"
164 "been invaded by the sea, after some great convulsion of the earth."
164 "Whilst you were sleeping, Professor, the Nautilus penetrated to this"
164 "lagoon by a natural canal, which opens about ten yards beneath the"
164 "surface of the ocean.  This is its harbour of refuge, a sure,"
164 "commodious, and mysterious one, sheltered from all gales.  Show me, if"
164 "you can, on the coasts of any of your continents or islands, a road"
164 "which can give such perfect refuge from all storms.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" I replied, \"you are in safety here, Captain Nemo.  Who"
164 "could reach you in the heart of a volcano?  But did I not see an"
164 "opening at its summit?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; its crater, formerly filled with lava, vapour, and flames, and"
164 "which now gives entrance to the life-giving air we breathe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But what is this volcanic mountain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It belongs to one of the numerous islands with which this sea is"
164 "strewn--to vessels a simple sandbank--to us an immense cavern.  Chance"
164 "led me to discover it, and chance served me well.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But of what use is this refuge, Captain?  The Nautilus wants no port.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; but it wants electricity to make it move, and the wherewithal"
164 "to make the electricity--sodium to feed the elements, coal from which"
164 "to get the sodium, and a coal-mine to supply the coal.  And exactly on"
164 "this spot the sea covers entire forests embedded during the geological"
164 "periods, now mineralised and transformed into coal; for me they are an"
164 "inexhaustible mine.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Your men follow the trade of miners here, then, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Exactly so.  These mines extend under the waves like the mines of"
164 "Newcastle.  Here, in their diving-dresses, pick axe and shovel in hand,"
164 "my men extract the coal, which I do not even ask from the mines of the"
164 "earth.  When I burn this combustible for the manufacture of sodium, the"
164 "smoke, escaping from the crater of the mountain, gives it the"
164 "appearance of a still-active volcano.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And we shall see your companions at work?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No; not this time at least; for I am in a hurry to continue our"
164 "submarine tour of the earth.  So I shall content myself with drawing"
164 "from the reserve of sodium I already possess.  The time for loading is"
164 "one day only, and we continue our voyage.  So, if you wish to go over"
164 "the cavern and make the round of the lagoon, you must take advantage of"
164 "to-day, M. Aronnax.\""
164 ""
164 "I thanked the Captain and went to look for my companions, who had not"
164 "yet left their cabin.  I invited them to follow me without saying where"
164 "we were.  They mounted the platform.  Conseil, who was astonished at"
164 "nothing, seemed to look upon it as quite natural that he should wake"
164 "under a mountain, after having fallen asleep under the waves.  But Ned"
164 "Land thought of nothing but finding whether the cavern had any exit."
164 "After breakfast, about ten o'clock, we went down on to the mountain."
164 ""
164 "\"Here we are, once more on land,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"I do not call this land,\" said the Canadian.  \"And besides, we are not"
164 "on it, but beneath it.\""
164 ""
164 "Between the walls of the mountains and the waters of the lake lay a"
164 "sandy shore which, at its greatest breadth, measured five hundred feet."
164 "On this soil one might easily make the tour of the lake.  But the base"
164 "of the high partitions was stony ground, with volcanic locks and"
164 "enormous pumice-stones lying in picturesque heaps.  All these detached"
164 "masses, covered with enamel, polished by the action of the"
164 "subterraneous fires, shone resplendent by the light of our electric"
164 "lantern.  The mica dust from the shore, rising under our feet, flew"
164 "like a cloud of sparks.  The bottom now rose sensibly, and we soon"
164 "arrived at long circuitous slopes, or inclined planes, which took us"
164 "higher by degrees; but we were obliged to walk carefully among these"
164 "conglomerates, bound by no cement, the feet slipping on the glassy"
164 "crystal, felspar, and quartz."
164 ""
164 "The volcanic nature of this enormous excavation was confirmed on all"
164 "sides, and I pointed it out to my companions."
164 ""
164 "\"Picture to yourselves,\" said I, \"what this crater must have been when"
164 "filled with boiling lava, and when the level of the incandescent liquid"
164 "rose to the orifice of the mountain, as though melted on the top of a"
164 "hot plate.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I can picture it perfectly,\" said Conseil.  \"But, sir, will you tell"
164 "me why the Great Architect has suspended operations, and how it is that"
164 "the furnace is replaced by the quiet waters of the lake?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Most probably, Conseil, because some convulsion beneath the ocean"
164 "produced that very opening which has served as a passage for the"
164 "Nautilus.  Then the waters of the Atlantic rushed into the interior of"
164 "the mountain.  There must have been a terrible struggle between the two"
164 "elements, a struggle which ended in the victory of Neptune.  But many"
164 "ages have run out since then, and the submerged volcano is now a"
164 "peaceable grotto.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very well,\" replied Ned Land; \"I accept the explanation, sir; but, in"
164 "our own interests, I regret that the opening of which you speak was not"
164 "made above the level of the sea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, friend Ned,\" said Conseil, \"if the passage had not been under the"
164 "sea, the Nautilus could not have gone through it.\""
164 ""
164 "We continued ascending.  The steps became more and more perpendicular"
164 "and narrow.  Deep excavations, which we were obliged to cross, cut them"
164 "here and there; sloping masses had to be turned.  We slid upon our"
164 "knees and crawled along.  But Conseil's dexterity and the Canadian's"
164 "strength surmounted all obstacles.  At a height of about 31 feet the"
164 "nature of the ground changed without becoming more practicable.  To the"
164 "conglomerate and trachyte succeeded black basalt, the first dispread in"
164 "layers full of bubbles, the latter forming regular prisms, placed like"
164 "a colonnade supporting the spring of the immense vault, an admirable"
164 "specimen of natural architecture.  Between the blocks of basalt wound"
164 "long streams of lava, long since grown cold, encrusted with bituminous"
164 "rays; and in some places there were spread large carpets of sulphur.  A"
164 "more powerful light shone through the upper crater, shedding a vague"
164 "glimmer over these volcanic depressions for ever buried in the bosom of"
164 "this extinguished mountain.  But our upward march was soon stopped at a"
164 "height of about two hundred and fifty feet by impassable obstacles."
164 "There was a complete vaulted arch overhanging us, and our ascent was"
164 "changed to a circular walk.  At the last change vegetable life began to"
164 "struggle with the mineral.  Some shrubs, and even some trees, grew from"
164 "the fractures of the walls.  I recognised some euphorbias, with the"
164 "caustic sugar coming from them; heliotropes, quite incapable of"
164 "justifying their name, sadly drooped their clusters of flowers, both"
164 "their colour and perfume half gone.  Here and there some chrysanthemums"
164 "grew timidly at the foot of an aloe with long, sickly-looking leaves."
164 "But between the streams of lava, I saw some little violets still"
164 "slightly perfumed, and I admit that I smelt them with delight.  Perfume"
164 "is the soul of the flower, and sea-flowers have no soul."
164 ""
164 "We had arrived at the foot of some sturdy dragon-trees, which had"
164 "pushed aside the rocks with their strong roots, when Ned Land exclaimed:"
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! sir, a hive! a hive!\""
164 ""
164 "\"A hive!\"  I replied, with a gesture of incredulity."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, a hive,\" repeated the Canadian, \"and bees humming round it.\""
164 ""
164 "I approached, and was bound to believe my own eyes.  There at a hole"
164 "bored in one of the dragon-trees were some thousands of these ingenious"
164 "insects, so common in all the Canaries, and whose produce is so much"
164 "esteemed.  Naturally enough, the Canadian wished to gather the honey,"
164 "and I could not well oppose his wish.  A quantity of dry leaves, mixed"
164 "with sulphur, he lit with a spark from his flint, and he began to smoke"
164 "out the bees.  The humming ceased by degrees, and the hive eventually"
164 "yielded several pounds of the sweetest honey, with which Ned Land"
164 "filled his haversack."
164 ""
164 "\"When I have mixed this honey with the paste of the bread-fruit,\" said"
164 "he, \"I shall be able to offer you a succulent cake.\""
164 ""
164 "[Transcriber's Note: 'bread-fruit' has been substituted for"
164 "'artocarpus' in this ed.]"
164 ""
164 "\"'Pon my word,\" said Conseil, \"it will be gingerbread.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Never mind the gingerbread,\" said I; \"let us continue our interesting"
164 "walk.\""
164 ""
164 "At every turn of the path we were following, the lake appeared in all"
164 "its length and breadth.  The lantern lit up the whole of its peaceable"
164 "surface, which knew neither ripple nor wave.  The Nautilus remained"
164 "perfectly immovable.  On the platform, and on the mountain, the ship's"
164 "crew were working like black shadows clearly carved against the"
164 "luminous atmosphere.  We were now going round the highest crest of the"
164 "first layers of rock which upheld the roof.  I then saw that bees were"
164 "not the only representatives of the animal kingdom in the interior of"
164 "this volcano.  Birds of prey hovered here and there in the shadows, or"
164 "fled from their nests on the top of the rocks.  There were sparrow"
164 "hawks, with white breasts, and kestrels, and down the slopes scampered,"
164 "with their long legs, several fine fat bustards.  I leave anyone to"
164 "imagine the covetousness of the Canadian at the sight of this savoury"
164 "game, and whether he did not regret having no gun.  But he did his best"
164 "to replace the lead by stones, and, after several fruitless attempts,"
164 "he succeeded in wounding a magnificent bird.  To say that he risked his"
164 "life twenty times before reaching it is but the truth; but he managed"
164 "so well that the creature joined the honey-cakes in his bag.  We were"
164 "now obliged to descend toward the shore, the crest becoming"
164 "impracticable.  Above us the crater seemed to gape like the mouth of a"
164 "well.  From this place the sky could be clearly seen, and clouds,"
164 "dissipated by the west wind, leaving behind them, even on the summit of"
164 "the mountain, their misty remnants--certain proof that they were only"
164 "moderately high, for the volcano did not rise more than eight hundred"
164 "feet above the level of the ocean.  Half an hour after the Canadian's"
164 "last exploit we had regained the inner shore.  Here the flora was"
164 "represented by large carpets of marine crystal, a little umbelliferous"
164 "plant very good to pickle, which also bears the name of pierce-stone"
164 "and sea-fennel. Conseil gathered some bundles of it.  As to the fauna,"
164 "it might be counted by thousands of crustacea of all sorts, lobsters,"
164 "crabs, spider-crabs, chameleon shrimps, and a large number of shells,"
164 "rockfish, and limpets.  Three-quarters of an hour later we had finished"
164 "our circuitous walk and were on board.  The crew had just finished"
164 "loading the sodium, and the Nautilus could have left that instant.  But"
164 "Captain Nemo gave no order.  Did he wish to wait until night, and leave"
164 "the submarine passage secretly?  Perhaps so.  Whatever it might be, the"
164 "next day, the Nautilus, having left its port, steered clear of all land"
164 "at a few yards beneath the waves of the Atlantic."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XI"
164 ""
164 "THE SARGASSO SEA"
164 ""
164 "That day the Nautilus crossed a singular part of the Atlantic Ocean."
164 "No one can be ignorant of the existence of a current of warm water"
164 "known by the name of the Gulf Stream.  After leaving the Gulf of"
164 "Florida, we went in the direction of Spitzbergen.  But before entering"
164 "the Gulf of Mexico, about 45 deg. of N. lat., this current divides into"
164 "two arms, the principal one going towards the coast of Ireland and"
164 "Norway, whilst the second bends to the south about the height of the"
164 "Azores; then, touching the African shore, and describing a lengthened"
164 "oval, returns to the Antilles.  This second arm--it is rather a collar"
164 "than an arm--surrounds with its circles of warm water that portion of"
164 "the cold, quiet, immovable ocean called the Sargasso Sea, a perfect"
164 "lake in the open Atlantic: it takes no less than three years for the"
164 "great current to pass round it.  Such was the region the Nautilus was"
164 "now visiting, a perfect meadow, a close carpet of seaweed, fucus, and"
164 "tropical berries, so thick and so compact that the stem of a vessel"
164 "could hardly tear its way through it.  And Captain Nemo, not wishing to"
164 "entangle his screw in this herbaceous mass, kept some yards beneath the"
164 "surface of the waves.  The name Sargasso comes from the Spanish word"
164 "\"sargazzo\" which signifies kelp.  This kelp, or berry-plant, is the"
164 "principal formation of this immense bank.  And this is the reason why"
164 "these plants unite in the peaceful basin of the Atlantic.  The only"
164 "explanation which can be given, he says, seems to me to result from the"
164 "experience known to all the world.  Place in a vase some fragments of"
164 "cork or other floating body, and give to the water in the vase a"
164 "circular movement, the scattered fragments will unite in a group in the"
164 "centre of the liquid surface, that is to say, in the part least"
164 "agitated.  In the phenomenon we are considering, the Atlantic is the"
164 "vase, the Gulf Stream the circular current, and the Sargasso Sea the"
164 "central point at which the floating bodies unite."
164 ""
164 "I share Maury's opinion, and I was able to study the phenomenon in the"
164 "very midst, where vessels rarely penetrate.  Above us floated products"
164 "of all kinds, heaped up among these brownish plants; trunks of trees"
164 "torn from the Andes or the Rocky Mountains, and floated by the Amazon"
164 "or the Mississippi; numerous wrecks, remains of keels, or ships'"
164 "bottoms, side-planks stove in, and so weighted with shells and"
164 "barnacles that they could not again rise to the surface.  And time will"
164 "one day justify Maury's other opinion, that these substances thus"
164 "accumulated for ages will become petrified by the action of the water"
164 "and will then form inexhaustible coal-mines--a precious reserve"
164 "prepared by far-seeing Nature for the moment when men shall have"
164 "exhausted the mines of continents."
164 ""
164 "In the midst of this inextricable mass of plants and sea weed, I"
164 "noticed some charming pink halcyons and actiniae, with their long"
164 "tentacles trailing after them, and medusae, green, red, and blue."
164 ""
164 "All the day of the 22nd of February we passed in the Sargasso Sea,"
164 "where such fish as are partial to marine plants find abundant"
164 "nourishment.  The next, the ocean had returned to its accustomed"
164 "aspect.  From this time for nineteen days, from the 23rd of February to"
164 "the 12th of March, the Nautilus kept in the middle of the Atlantic,"
164 "carrying us at a constant speed of a hundred leagues in twenty-four"
164 "hours.  Captain Nemo evidently intended accomplishing his submarine"
164 "programme, and I imagined that he intended, after doubling Cape Horn,"
164 "to return to the Australian seas of the Pacific.  Ned Land had cause"
164 "for fear.  In these large seas, void of islands, we could not attempt"
164 "to leave the boat.  Nor had we any means of opposing Captain Nemo's"
164 "will.  Our only course was to submit; but what we could neither gain by"
164 "force nor cunning, I liked to think might be obtained by persuasion."
164 "This voyage ended, would he not consent to restore our liberty, under"
164 "an oath never to reveal his existence?--an oath of honour which we"
164 "should have religiously kept.  But we must consider that delicate"
164 "question with the Captain.  But was I free to claim this liberty?  Had"
164 "he not himself said from the beginning, in the firmest manner, that the"
164 "secret of his life exacted from him our lasting imprisonment on board"
164 "the Nautilus?  And would not my four months' silence appear to him a"
164 "tacit acceptance of our situation?  And would not a return to the"
164 "subject result in raising suspicions which might be hurtful to our"
164 "projects, if at some future time a favourable opportunity offered to"
164 "return to them?"
164 ""
164 "During the nineteen days mentioned above, no incident of any kind"
164 "happened to signalise our voyage.  I saw little of the Captain; he was"
164 "at work.  In the library I often found his books left open, especially"
164 "those on natural history.  My work on submarine depths, conned over by"
164 "him, was covered with marginal notes, often contradicting my theories"
164 "and systems; but the Captain contented himself with thus purging my"
164 "work; it was very rare for him to discuss it with me.  Sometimes I"
164 "heard the melancholy tones of his organ; but only at night, in the"
164 "midst of the deepest obscurity, when the Nautilus slept upon the"
164 "deserted ocean.  During this part of our voyage we sailed whole days on"
164 "the surface of the waves.  The sea seemed abandoned.  A few"
164 "sailing-vessels, on the road to India, were making for the Cape of Good"
164 "Hope.  One day we were followed by the boats of a whaler, who, no"
164 "doubt, took us for some enormous whale of great price; but Captain Nemo"
164 "did not wish the worthy fellows to lose their time and trouble, so"
164 "ended the chase by plunging under the water.  Our navigation continued"
164 "until the 13th of March; that day the Nautilus was employed in taking"
164 "soundings, which greatly interested me.  We had then made about 13,000"
164 "leagues since our departure from the high seas of the Pacific.  The"
164 "bearings gave us 45 deg. 37' S. lat., and 37 deg. 53' W. long.  It was"
164 "the same water in which Captain Denham of the Herald sounded 7,000"
164 "fathoms without finding the bottom.  There, too, Lieutenant Parker, of"
164 "the American frigate Congress, could not touch the bottom with 15,140"
164 "fathoms.  Captain Nemo intended seeking the bottom of the ocean by a"
164 "diagonal sufficiently lengthened by means of lateral planes placed at"
164 "an angle of 45 deg. with the water-line of the Nautilus.  Then the"
164 "screw set to work at its maximum speed, its four blades beating the"
164 "waves with in describable force.  Under this powerful pressure, the"
164 "hull of the Nautilus quivered like a sonorous chord and sank regularly"
164 "under the water."
164 ""
164 "At 7,000 fathoms I saw some blackish tops rising from the midst of the"
164 "waters; but these summits might belong to high mountains like the"
164 "Himalayas or Mont Blanc, even higher; and the depth of the abyss"
164 "remained incalculable.  The Nautilus descended still lower, in spite of"
164 "the great pressure.  I felt the steel plates tremble at the fastenings"
164 "of the bolts; its bars bent, its partitions groaned; the windows of the"
164 "saloon seemed to curve under the pressure of the waters.  And this firm"
164 "structure would doubtless have yielded, if, as its Captain had said, it"
164 "had not been capable of resistance like a solid block.  We had attained"
164 "a depth of 16,000 yards (four leagues), and the sides of the Nautilus"
164 "then bore a pressure of 1,600 atmospheres, that is to say, 3,200 lb."
164 "to each square two-fifths of an inch of its surface."
164 ""
164 "\"What a situation to be in!\"  I exclaimed.  \"To overrun these deep"
164 "regions where man has never trod!  Look, Captain, look at these"
164 "magnificent rocks, these uninhabited grottoes, these lowest receptacles"
164 "of the globe, where life is no longer possible!  What unknown sights"
164 "are here!  Why should we be unable to preserve a remembrance of them?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Would you like to carry away more than the remembrance?\" said Captain"
164 "Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"What do you mean by those words?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I mean to say that nothing is easier than to make a photographic view"
164 "of this submarine region.\""
164 ""
164 "I had not time to express my surprise at this new proposition, when, at"
164 "Captain Nemo's call, an objective was brought into the saloon.  Through"
164 "the widely-opened panel, the liquid mass was bright with electricity,"
164 "which was distributed with such uniformity that not a shadow, not a"
164 "gradation, was to be seen in our manufactured light.  The Nautilus"
164 "remained motionless, the force of its screw subdued by the inclination"
164 "of its planes: the instrument was propped on the bottom of the oceanic"
164 "site, and in a few seconds we had obtained a perfect negative."
164 ""
164 "But, the operation being over, Captain Nemo said, \"Let us go up; we"
164 "must not abuse our position, nor expose the Nautilus too long to such"
164 "great pressure.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Go up again!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"Hold well on.\""
164 ""
