164 "I had not time to understand why the Captain cautioned me thus, when I"
164 "was thrown forward on to the carpet.  At a signal from the Captain, its"
164 "screw was shipped, and its blades raised vertically; the Nautilus shot"
164 "into the air like a balloon, rising with stunning rapidity, and cutting"
164 "the mass of waters with a sonorous agitation.  Nothing was visible; and"
164 "in four minutes it had shot through the four leagues which separated it"
164 "from the ocean, and, after emerging like a flying-fish, fell, making"
164 "the waves rebound to an enormous height."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XII"
164 ""
164 "CACHALOTS AND WHALES"
164 ""
164 "During the nights of the 13th and 14th of March, the Nautilus returned"
164 "to its southerly course.  I fancied that, when on a level with Cape"
164 "Horn, he would turn the helm westward, in order to beat the Pacific"
164 "seas, and so complete the tour of the world.  He did nothing of the"
164 "kind, but continued on his way to the southern regions.  Where was he"
164 "going to?  To the pole?  It was madness!  I began to think that the"
164 "Captain's temerity justified Ned Land's fears.  For some time past the"
164 "Canadian had not spoken to me of his projects of flight; he was less"
164 "communicative, almost silent.  I could see that this lengthened"
164 "imprisonment was weighing upon him, and I felt that rage was burning"
164 "within him.  When he met the Captain, his eyes lit up with suppressed"
164 "anger; and I feared that his natural violence would lead him into some"
164 "extreme.  That day, the 14th of March, Conseil and he came to me in my"
164 "room.  I inquired the cause of their visit."
164 ""
164 "\"A simple question to ask you, sir,\" replied the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Speak, Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"How many men are there on board the Nautilus, do you think?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I cannot tell, my friend.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I should say that its working does not require a large crew.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly, under existing conditions, ten men, at the most, ought to"
164 "be enough.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, why should there be any more?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why?\"  I replied, looking fixedly at Ned Land, whose meaning was easy"
164 "to guess.  \"Because,\" I added, \"if my surmises are correct, and if I"
164 "have well understood the Captain's existence, the Nautilus is not only"
164 "a vessel: it is also a place of refuge for those who, like its"
164 "commander, have broken every tie upon earth.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps so,\" said Conseil; \"but, in any case, the Nautilus can only"
164 "contain a certain number of men.  Could not you, sir, estimate their"
164 "maximum?\""
164 ""
164 "\"How, Conseil?\""
164 ""
164 "\"By calculation; given the size of the vessel, which you know, sir, and"
164 "consequently the quantity of air it contains, knowing also how much"
164 "each man expends at a breath, and comparing these results with the fact"
164 "that the Nautilus is obliged to go to the surface every twenty-four"
164 "hours.\""
164 ""
164 "Conseil had not finished the sentence before I saw what he was driving"
164 "at."
164 ""
164 "\"I understand,\" said I; \"but that calculation, though simple enough,"
164 "can give but a very uncertain result.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Never mind,\" said Ned Land urgently."
164 ""
164 "\"Here it is, then,\" said I. \"In one hour each man consumes the oxygen"
164 "contained in twenty gallons of air; and in twenty-four, that contained"
164 "in 480 gallons.  We must, therefore find how many times 480 gallons of"
164 "air the Nautilus contains.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Or,\" I continued, \"the size of the Nautilus being 1,500 tons; and one"
164 "ton holding 200 gallons, it contains 300,000 gallons of air, which,"
164 "divided by 480, gives a quotient of 625.  Which means to say, strictly"
164 "speaking, that the air contained in the Nautilus would suffice for 625"
164 "men for twenty-four hours.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Six hundred and twenty-five!\" repeated Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"But remember that all of us, passengers, sailors, and officers"
164 "included, would not form a tenth part of that number.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Still too many for three men,\" murmured Conseil."
164 ""
164 "The Canadian shook his head, passed his hand across his forehead, and"
164 "left the room without answering."
164 ""
164 "\"Will you allow me to make one observation, sir?\" said Conseil.  \"Poor"
164 "Ned is longing for everything that he can not have.  His past life is"
164 "always present to him; everything that we are forbidden he regrets."
164 "His head is full of old recollections.  And we must understand him."
164 "What has he to do here?  Nothing; he is not learned like you, sir; and"
164 "has not the same taste for the beauties of the sea that we have.  He"
164 "would risk everything to be able to go once more into a tavern in his"
164 "own country.\""
164 ""
164 "Certainly the monotony on board must seem intolerable to the Canadian,"
164 "accustomed as he was to a life of liberty and activity.  Events were"
164 "rare which could rouse him to any show of spirit; but that day an event"
164 "did happen which recalled the bright days of the harpooner.  About"
164 "eleven in the morning, being on the surface of the ocean, the Nautilus"
164 "fell in with a troop of whales--an encounter which did not astonish me,"
164 "knowing that these creatures, hunted to death, had taken refuge in high"
164 "latitudes."
164 ""
164 "We were seated on the platform, with a quiet sea.  The month of October"
164 "in those latitudes gave us some lovely autumnal days.  It was the"
164 "Canadian--he could not be mistaken--who signalled a whale on the"
164 "eastern horizon.  Looking attentively, one might see its black back"
164 "rise and fall with the waves five miles from the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" exclaimed Ned Land, \"if I was on board a whaler, now such a"
164 "meeting would give me pleasure.  It is one of large size.  See with"
164 "what strength its blow-holes throw up columns of air an steam!"
164 "Confound it, why am I bound to these steel plates?\""
164 ""
164 "\"What, Ned,\" said I, \"you have not forgotten your old ideas of fishing?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Can a whale-fisher ever forget his old trade, sir?  Can he ever tire"
164 "of the emotions caused by such a chase?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You have never fished in these seas, Ned?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Never, sir; in the northern only, and as much in Behring as in Davis"
164 "Straits.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then the southern whale is still unknown to you.  It is the Greenland"
164 "whale you have hunted up to this time, and that would not risk passing"
164 "through the warm waters of the equator.  Whales are localised,"
164 "according to their kinds, in certain seas which they never leave.  And"
164 "if one of these creatures went from Behring to Davis Straits, it must"
164 "be simply because there is a passage from one sea to the other, either"
164 "on the American or the Asiatic side.\""
164 ""
164 "\"In that case, as I have never fished in these seas, I do not know the"
164 "kind of whale frequenting them!\""
164 ""
164 "\"I have told you, Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"A greater reason for making their acquaintance,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Look! look!\" exclaimed the Canadian, \"they approach: they aggravate"
164 "me; they know that I cannot get at them!\""
164 ""
164 "Ned stamped his feet.  His hand trembled, as he grasped an imaginary"
164 "harpoon."
164 ""
164 "\"Are these cetaceans as large as those of the northern seas?\" asked he."
164 ""
164 "\"Very nearly, Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Because I have seen large whales, sir, whales measuring a hundred"
164 "feet.  I have even been told that those of Hullamoch and Umgallick, of"
164 "the Aleutian Islands, are sometimes a hundred and fifty feet long.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That seems to me exaggeration. These creatures are only"
164 "balaeaopterons, provided with dorsal fins; and, like the cachalots, are"
164 "generally much smaller than the Greenland whale.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" exclaimed the Canadian, whose eyes had never left the ocean,"
164 "\"they are coming nearer; they are in the same water as the Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "Then, returning to the conversation, he said:"
164 ""
164 "\"You spoke of the cachalot as a small creature.  I have heard of"
164 "gigantic ones.  They are intelligent cetacea.  It is said of some that"
164 "they cover themselves with seaweed and fucus, and then are taken for"
164 "islands.  People encamp upon them, and settle there; lights a fire----\""
164 ""
164 "\"And build houses,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, joker,\" said Ned Land.  \"And one fine day the creature plunges,"
164 "carrying with it all the inhabitants to the bottom of the sea.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Something like the travels of Sinbad the Sailor,\" I replied, laughing."
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" suddenly exclaimed Ned Land, \"it is not one whale; there are"
164 "ten--there are twenty--it is a whole troop!  And I not able to do"
164 "anything! hands and feet tied!\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, friend Ned,\" said Conseil, \"why do you not ask Captain Nemo's"
164 "permission to chase them?\""
164 ""
164 "Conseil had not finished his sentence when Ned Land had lowered himself"
164 "through the panel to seek the Captain.  A few minutes afterwards the"
164 "two appeared together on the platform."
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo watched the troop of cetacea playing on the waters about a"
164 "mile from the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 "\"They are southern whales,\" said he; \"there goes the fortune of a whole"
164 "fleet of whalers.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir,\" asked the Canadian, \"can I not chase them, if only to"
164 "remind me of my old trade of harpooner?\""
164 ""
164 "\"And to what purpose?\" replied Captain Nemo; \"only to destroy!  We have"
164 "nothing to do with the whale-oil on board.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But, sir,\" continued the Canadian, \"in the Red Sea you allowed us to"
164 "follow the dugong.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then it was to procure fresh meat for my crew.  Here it would be"
164 "killing for killing's sake.  I know that is a privilege reserved for"
164 "man, but I do not approve of such murderous pastime.  In destroying the"
164 "southern whale (like the Greenland whale, an inoffensive creature),"
164 "your traders do a culpable action, Master Land.  They have already"
164 "depopulated the whole of Baffin's Bay, and are annihilating a class of"
164 "useful animals.  Leave the unfortunate cetacea alone.  They have plenty"
164 "of natural enemies--cachalots, swordfish, and sawfish--without you"
164 "troubling them.\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain was right.  The barbarous and inconsiderate greed of these"
164 "fishermen will one day cause the disappearance of the last whale in the"
164 "ocean.  Ned Land whistled \"Yankee-doodle\" between his teeth, thrust his"
164 "hands into his pockets, and turned his back upon us.  But Captain Nemo"
164 "watched the troop of cetacea, and, addressing me, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"I was right in saying that whales had natural enemies enough, without"
164 "counting man.  These will have plenty to do before long.  Do you see,"
164 "M. Aronnax, about eight miles to leeward, those blackish moving points?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Captain,\" I replied."
164 ""
164 "\"Those are cachalots--terrible animals, which I have met in troops of"
164 "two or three hundred.  As to those, they are cruel, mischievous"
164 "creatures; they would be right in exterminating them.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian turned quickly at the last words."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Captain,\" said he, \"it is still time, in the interest of the"
164 "whales.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is useless to expose one's self, Professor.  The Nautilus will"
164 "disperse them.  It is armed with a steel spur as good as Master Land's"
164 "harpoon, I imagine.\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian did not put himself out enough to shrug his shoulders."
164 "Attack cetacea with blows of a spur!  Who had ever heard of such a"
164 "thing?"
164 ""
164 "\"Wait, M. Aronnax,\" said Captain Nemo.  \"We will show you something you"
164 "have never yet seen.  We have no pity for these ferocious creatures."
164 "They are nothing but mouth and teeth.\""
164 ""
164 "Mouth and teeth!  No one could better describe the macrocephalous"
164 "cachalot, which is sometimes more than seventy-five feet long.  Its"
164 "enormous head occupies one-third of its entire body.  Better armed than"
164 "the whale, whose upper jaw is furnished only with whalebone, it is"
164 "supplied with twenty-five large tusks, about eight inches long,"
164 "cylindrical and conical at the top, each weighing two pounds.  It is in"
164 "the upper part of this enormous head, in great cavities divided by"
164 "cartilages, that is to be found from six to eight hundred pounds of"
164 "that precious oil called spermaceti.  The cachalot is a disagreeable"
164 "creature, more tadpole than fish, according to Fredol's description."
164 "It is badly formed, the whole of its left side being (if we may say"
164 "it), a \"failure,\" and being only able to see with its right eye.  But"
164 "the formidable troop was nearing us.  They had seen the whales and were"
164 "preparing to attack them.  One could judge beforehand that the"
164 "cachalots would be victorious, not only because they were better built"
164 "for attack than their inoffensive adversaries, but also because they"
164 "could remain longer under water without coming to the surface.  There"
164 "was only just time to go to the help of the whales.  The Nautilus went"
164 "under water.  Conseil, Ned Land, and I took our places before the"
164 "window in the saloon, and Captain Nemo joined the pilot in his cage to"
164 "work his apparatus as an engine of destruction.  Soon I felt the"
164 "beatings of the screw quicken, and our speed increased.  The battle"
164 "between the cachalots and the whales had already begun when the"
164 "Nautilus arrived.  They did not at first show any fear at the sight of"
164 "this new monster joining in the conflict.  But they soon had to guard"
164 "against its blows.  What a battle!  The Nautilus was nothing but a"
164 "formidable harpoon, brandished by the hand of its Captain.  It hurled"
164 "itself against the fleshy mass, passing through from one part to the"
164 "other, leaving behind it two quivering halves of the animal.  It could"
164 "not feel the formidable blows from their tails upon its sides, nor the"
164 "shock which it produced itself, much more.  One cachalot killed, it ran"
164 "at the next, tacked on the spot that it might not miss its prey, going"
164 "forwards and backwards, answering to its helm, plunging when the"
164 "cetacean dived into the deep waters, coming up with it when it returned"
164 "to the surface, striking it front or sideways, cutting or tearing in"
164 "all directions and at any pace, piercing it with its terrible spur."
164 "What carnage!  What a noise on the surface of the waves!  What sharp"
164 "hissing, and what snorting peculiar to these enraged animals!  In the"
164 "midst of these waters, generally so peaceful, their tails made perfect"
164 "billows.  For one hour this wholesale massacre continued, from which"
164 "the cachalots could not escape.  Several times ten or twelve united"
164 "tried to crush the Nautilus by their weight.  From the window we could"
164 "see their enormous mouths, studded with tusks, and their formidable"
164 "eyes.  Ned Land could not contain himself; he threatened and swore at"
164 "them.  We could feel them clinging to our vessel like dogs worrying a"
164 "wild boar in a copse.  But the Nautilus, working its screw, carried"
164 "them here and there, or to the upper levels of the ocean, without"
164 "caring for their enormous weight, nor the powerful strain on the"
164 "vessel.  At length the mass of cachalots broke up, the waves became"
164 "quiet, and I felt that we were rising to the surface.  The panel"
164 "opened, and we hurried on to the platform.  The sea was covered with"
164 "mutilated bodies.  A formidable explosion could not have divided and"
164 "torn this fleshy mass with more violence.  We were floating amid"
164 "gigantic bodies, bluish on the back and white underneath, covered with"
164 "enormous protuberances.  Some terrified cachalots were flying towards"
164 "the horizon.  The waves were dyed red for several miles, and the"
164 "Nautilus floated in a sea of blood:  Captain Nemo joined us."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Master Land?\" said he."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir,\" replied the Canadian, whose enthusiasm had somewhat"
164 "calmed; \"it is a terrible spectacle, certainly.  But I am not a"
164 "butcher.  I am a hunter, and I call this a butchery.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is a massacre of mischievous creatures,\" replied the Captain; \"and"
164 "the Nautilus is not a butcher's knife.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I like my harpoon better,\" said the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Every one to his own,\" answered the Captain, looking fixedly at Ned"
164 "Land."
164 ""
164 "I feared he would commit some act of violence, which would end in sad"
164 "consequences.  But his anger was turned by the sight of a whale which"
164 "the Nautilus had just come up with.  The creature had not quite escaped"
164 "from the cachalot's teeth.  I recognised the southern whale by its flat"
164 "head, which is entirely black.  Anatomically, it is distinguished from"
164 "the white whale and the North Cape whale by the seven cervical"
164 "vertebrae, and it has two more ribs than its congeners.  The"
164 "unfortunate cetacean was lying on its side, riddled with holes from the"
164 "bites, and quite dead.  From its mutilated fin still hung a young whale"
164 "which it could not save from the massacre.  Its open mouth let the"
164 "water flow in and out, murmuring like the waves breaking on the shore."
164 "Captain Nemo steered close to the corpse of the creature.  Two of his"
164 "men mounted its side, and I saw, not without surprise, that they were"
164 "drawing from its breasts all the milk which they contained, that is to"
164 "say, about two or three tons.  The Captain offered me a cup of the"
164 "milk, which was still warm.  I could not help showing my repugnance to"
164 "the drink; but he assured me that it was excellent, and not to be"
164 "distinguished from cow's milk.  I tasted it, and was of his opinion."
164 "It was a useful reserve to us, for in the shape of salt butter or"
164 "cheese it would form an agreeable variety from our ordinary food.  From"
164 "that day I noticed with uneasiness that Ned Land's ill-will towards"
164 "Captain Nemo increased, and I resolved to watch the Canadian's gestures"
164 "closely."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIII"
164 ""
164 "THE ICEBERG"
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus was steadily pursuing its southerly course, following the"
164 "fiftieth meridian with considerable speed.  Did he wish to reach the"
164 "pole?  I did not think so, for every attempt to reach that point had"
164 "hitherto failed.  Again, the season was far advanced, for in the"
164 "Antarctic regions the 13th of March corresponds with the 13th of"
164 "September of northern regions, which begin at the equinoctial season."
164 "On the 14th of March I saw floating ice in latitude 55 deg., merely"
164 "pale bits of debris from twenty to twenty-five feet long, forming banks"
164 "over which the sea curled.  The Nautilus remained on the surface of the"
164 "ocean.  Ned Land, who had fished in the Arctic Seas, was familiar with"
164 "its icebergs; but Conseil and I admired them for the first time.  In"
164 "the atmosphere towards the southern horizon stretched a white dazzling"
164 "band.  English whalers have given it the name of \"ice blink.\"  However"
164 "thick the clouds may be, it is always visible, and announces the"
164 "presence of an ice pack or bank.  Accordingly, larger blocks soon"
164 "appeared, whose brilliancy changed with the caprices of the fog.  Some"
164 "of these masses showed green veins, as if long undulating lines had"
164 "been traced with sulphate of copper; others resembled enormous"
164 "amethysts with the light shining through them.  Some reflected the"
164 "light of day upon a thousand crystal facets.  Others shaded with vivid"
164 "calcareous reflections resembled a perfect town of marble.  The more we"
164 "neared the south the more these floating islands increased both in"
164 "number and importance."
164 ""
164 "At 60 deg. lat. every pass had disappeared.  But, seeking carefully,"
164 "Captain Nemo soon found a narrow opening, through which he boldly"
164 "slipped, knowing, however, that it would close behind him.  Thus,"
164 "guided by this clever hand, the Nautilus passed through all the ice"
164 "with a precision which quite charmed Conseil; icebergs or mountains,"
164 "ice-fields or smooth plains, seeming to have no limits, drift-ice or"
164 "floating ice-packs, plains broken up, called palchs when they are"
164 "circular, and streams when they are made up of long strips.  The"
164 "temperature was very low; the thermometer exposed to the air marked 2"
164 "deg. or 3 deg. below zero, but we were warmly clad with fur, at the"
164 "expense of the sea-bear and seal.  The interior of the Nautilus, warmed"
164 "regularly by its electric apparatus, defied the most intense cold."
164 "Besides, it would only have been necessary to go some yards beneath the"
164 "waves to find a more bearable temperature.  Two months earlier we"
164 "should have had perpetual daylight in these latitudes; but already we"
164 "had had three or four hours of night, and by and by there would be six"
164 "months of darkness in these circumpolar regions.  On the 15th of March"
164 "we were in the latitude of New Shetland and South Orkney.  The Captain"
164 "told me that formerly numerous tribes of seals inhabited them; but that"
164 "English and American whalers, in their rage for destruction, massacred"
164 "both old and young; thus, where there was once life and animation, they"
164 "had left silence and death."
164 ""
164 "About eight o'clock on the morning of the 16th of March the Nautilus,"
164 "following the fifty-fifth meridian, cut the Antarctic polar circle."
164 "Ice surrounded us on all sides, and closed the horizon.  But Captain"
164 "Nemo went from one opening to another, still going higher.  I cannot"
164 "express my astonishment at the beauties of these new regions.  The ice"
164 "took most surprising forms.  Here the grouping formed an oriental town,"
164 "with innumerable mosques and minarets; there a fallen city thrown to"
164 "the earth, as it were, by some convulsion of nature.  The whole aspect"
164 "was constantly changed by the oblique rays of the sun, or lost in the"
164 "greyish fog amidst hurricanes of snow.  Detonations and falls were"
164 "heard on all sides, great overthrows of icebergs, which altered the"
164 "whole landscape like a diorama.  Often seeing no exit, I thought we"
164 "were definitely prisoners; but, instinct guiding him at the slightest"
164 "indication, Captain Nemo would discover a new pass.  He was never"
164 "mistaken when he saw the thin threads of bluish water trickling along"
164 "the ice-fields; and I had no doubt that he had already ventured into"
164 "the midst of these Antarctic seas before.  On the 16th of March,"
164 "however, the ice-fields absolutely blocked our road.  It was not the"
164 "iceberg itself, as yet, but vast fields cemented by the cold.  But this"
164 "obstacle could not stop Captain Nemo: he hurled himself against it with"
164 "frightful violence.  The Nautilus entered the brittle mass like a"
164 "wedge, and split it with frightful crackings.  It was the battering ram"
164 "of the ancients hurled by infinite strength.  The ice, thrown high in"
164 "the air, fell like hail around us.  By its own power of impulsion our"
164 "apparatus made a canal for itself; some times carried away by its own"
164 "impetus, it lodged on the ice-field, crushing it with its weight, and"
164 "sometimes buried beneath it, dividing it by a simple pitching movement,"
164 "producing large rents in it.  Violent gales assailed us at this time,"
164 "accompanied by thick fogs, through which, from one end of the platform"
164 "to the other, we could see nothing.  The wind blew sharply from all"
164 "parts of the compass, and the snow lay in such hard heaps that we had"
164 "to break it with blows of a pickaxe.  The temperature was always at 5"
164 "deg. below zero; every outward part of the Nautilus was covered with"
164 "ice.  A rigged vessel would have been entangled in the blocked up"
164 "gorges.  A vessel without sails, with electricity for its motive power,"
164 "and wanting no coal, could alone brave such high latitudes.  At length,"
164 "on the 18th of March, after many useless assaults, the Nautilus was"
164 "positively blocked.  It was no longer either streams, packs, or"
164 "ice-fields, but an interminable and immovable barrier, formed by"
164 "mountains soldered together."
164 ""
164 "\"An iceberg!\" said the Canadian to me."
164 ""
164 "I knew that to Ned Land, as well as to all other navigators who had"
164 "preceded us, this was an inevitable obstacle.  The sun appearing for an"
164 "instant at noon, Captain Nemo took an observation as near as possible,"
164 "which gave our situation at 51 deg. 30' long. and 67 deg. 39' of S."
164 "lat.  We had advanced one degree more in this Antarctic region.  Of the"
164 "liquid surface of the sea there was no longer a glimpse.  Under the"
164 "spur of the Nautilus lay stretched a vast plain, entangled with"
164 "confused blocks.  Here and there sharp points and slender needles"
164 "rising to a height of 200 feet; further on a steep shore, hewn as it"
164 "were with an axe and clothed with greyish tints; huge mirrors,"
164 "reflecting a few rays of sunshine, half drowned in the fog.  And over"
164 "this desolate face of nature a stern silence reigned, scarcely broken"
164 "by the flapping of the wings of petrels and puffins.  Everything was"
164 "frozen--even the noise.  The Nautilus was then obliged to stop in its"
164 "adventurous course amid these fields of ice.  In spite of our efforts,"
164 "in spite of the powerful means employed to break up the ice, the"
164 "Nautilus remained immovable.  Generally, when we can proceed no"
164 "further, we have return still open to us; but here return was as"
164 "impossible as advance, for every pass had closed behind us; and for the"
164 "few moments when we were stationary, we were likely to be entirely"
164 "blocked, which did indeed happen about two o'clock in the afternoon,"
164 "the fresh ice forming around its sides with astonishing rapidity.  I"
164 "was obliged to admit that Captain Nemo was more than imprudent.  I was"
164 "on the platform at that moment.  The Captain had been observing our"
164 "situation for some time past, when he said to me:"
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir, what do you think of this?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I think that we are caught, Captain.\""
164 ""
164 "\"So, M. Aronnax, you really think that the Nautilus cannot disengage"
164 "itself?\""
164 ""
164 "\"With difficulty, Captain; for the season is already too far advanced"
164 "for you to reckon on the breaking of the ice.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! sir,\" said Captain Nemo, in an ironical tone, \"you will always be"
164 "the same.  You see nothing but difficulties and obstacles.  I affirm"
164 "that not only can the Nautilus disengage itself, but also that it can"
164 "go further still.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Further to the South?\"  I asked, looking at the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir; it shall go to the pole.\""
164 ""
164 "\"To the pole!\"  I exclaimed, unable to repress a gesture of incredulity."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" replied the Captain, coldly, \"to the Antarctic pole--to that"
164 "unknown point from whence springs every meridian of the globe.  You"
164 "know whether I can do as I please with the Nautilus!\""
164 ""
164 "Yes, I knew that.  I knew that this man was bold, even to rashness."
164 "But to conquer those obstacles which bristled round the South Pole,"
164 "rendering it more inaccessible than the North, which had not yet been"
164 "reached by the boldest navigators--was it not a mad enterprise, one"
164 "which only a maniac would have conceived?  It then came into my head to"
164 "ask Captain Nemo if he had ever discovered that pole which had never"
164 "yet been trodden by a human creature?"
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir,\" he replied; \"but we will discover it together.  Where others"
164 "have failed, I will not fail.  I have never yet led my Nautilus so far"
164 "into southern seas; but, I repeat, it shall go further yet.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I can well believe you, Captain,\" said I, in a slightly ironical tone."
164 "\"I believe you!  Let us go ahead!  There are no obstacles for us!  Let"
164 "us smash this iceberg!  Let us blow it up; and, if it resists, let us"
164 "give the Nautilus wings to fly over it!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Over it, sir!\" said Captain Nemo, quietly; \"no, not over it, but under"
164 "it!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Under it!\"  I exclaimed, a sudden idea of the Captain's projects"
164 "flashing upon my mind.  I understood; the wonderful qualities of the"
164 "Nautilus were going to serve us in this superhuman enterprise."
164 ""
164 "\"I see we are beginning to understand one another, sir,\" said the"
164 "Captain, half smiling.  \"You begin to see the possibility--I should say"
164 "the success--of this attempt.  That which is impossible for an ordinary"
164 "vessel is easy to the Nautilus.  If a continent lies before the pole,"
164 "it must stop before the continent; but if, on the contrary, the pole is"
164 "washed by open sea, it will go even to the pole.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" said I, carried away by the Captain's reasoning; \"if the"
164 "surface of the sea is solidified by the ice, the lower depths are free"
164 "by the Providential law which has placed the maximum of density of the"
164 "waters of the ocean one degree higher than freezing-point; and, if I am"
164 "not mistaken, the portion of this iceberg which is above the water is"
164 "as one to four to that which is below.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very nearly, sir; for one foot of iceberg above the sea there are"
164 "three below it.  If these ice mountains are not more than 300 feet"
164 "above the surface, they are not more than 900 beneath.  And what are"
164 "900 feet to the Nautilus?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Nothing, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"It could even seek at greater depths that uniform temperature of"
164 "sea-water, and there brave with impunity the thirty or forty degrees of"
164 "surface cold.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so, sir--just so,\" I replied, getting animated."
164 ""
164 "\"The only difficulty,\" continued Captain Nemo, \"is that of remaining"
164 "several days without renewing our provision of air.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Is that all?  The Nautilus has vast reservoirs; we can fill them, and"
164 "they will supply us with all the oxygen we want.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well thought of, M. Aronnax,\" replied the Captain, smiling.  \"But, not"
164 "wishing you to accuse me of rashness, I will first give you all my"
164 "objections.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Have you any more to make?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Only one.  It is possible, if the sea exists at the South Pole, that"
164 "it may be covered; and, consequently, we shall be unable to come to the"
164 "surface.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good, sir! but do you forget that the Nautilus is armed with a"
164 "powerful spur, and could we not send it diagonally against these fields"
164 "of ice, which would open at the shocks.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! sir, you are full of ideas to-day.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Besides, Captain,\" I added, enthusiastically, \"why should we not find"
164 "the sea open at the South Pole as well as at the North?  The frozen"
164 "poles of the earth do not coincide, either in the southern or in the"
164 "northern regions; and, until it is proved to the contrary, we may"
164 "suppose either a continent or an ocean free from ice at these two"
164 "points of the globe.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I think so too, M. Aronnax,\" replied Captain Nemo.  \"I only wish you"
164 "to observe that, after having made so many objections to my project,"
164 "you are now crushing me with arguments in its favour!\""
164 ""
164 "The preparations for this audacious attempt now began.  The powerful"
164 "pumps of the Nautilus were working air into the reservoirs and storing"
164 "it at high pressure.  About four o'clock, Captain Nemo announced the"
164 "closing of the panels on the platform.  I threw one last look at the"
164 "massive iceberg which we were going to cross.  The weather was clear,"
164 "the atmosphere pure enough, the cold very great, being 12 deg. below"
164 "zero; but, the wind having gone down, this temperature was not so"
164 "unbearable.  About ten men mounted the sides of the Nautilus, armed"
164 "with pickaxes to break the ice around the vessel, which was soon free."
164 "The operation was quickly performed, for the fresh ice was still very"
164 "thin.  We all went below.  The usual reservoirs were filled with the"
164 "newly-liberated water, and the Nautilus soon descended.  I had taken my"
164 "place with Conseil in the saloon; through the open window we could see"
164 "the lower beds of the Southern Ocean.  The thermometer went up, the"
164 "needle of the compass deviated on the dial.  At about 900 feet, as"
164 "Captain Nemo had foreseen, we were floating beneath the undulating"
164 "bottom of the iceberg.  But the Nautilus went lower still--it went to"
164 "the depth of four hundred fathoms.  The temperature of the water at the"
164 "surface showed twelve degrees, it was now only ten; we had gained two."
164 "I need not say the temperature of the Nautilus was raised by its"
164 "heating apparatus to a much higher degree; every manoeuvre was"
164 "accomplished with wonderful precision."
164 ""
164 "\"We shall pass it, if you please, sir,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"I believe we shall,\" I said, in a tone of firm conviction."
164 ""
164 "In this open sea, the Nautilus had taken its course direct to the pole,"
164 "without leaving the fifty-second meridian.  From 67 deg. 30' to 90"
164 "deg., twenty-two degrees and a half of latitude remained to travel;"
164 "that is, about five hundred leagues.  The Nautilus kept up a mean speed"
164 "of twenty-six miles an hour--the speed of an express train.  If that"
164 "was kept up, in forty hours we should reach the pole."
164 ""
164 "For a part of the night the novelty of the situation kept us at the"
164 "window.  The sea was lit with the electric lantern; but it was"
164 "deserted; fishes did not sojourn in these imprisoned waters; they only"
164 "found there a passage to take them from the Antarctic Ocean to the open"
164 "polar sea.  Our pace was rapid; we could feel it by the quivering of"
164 "the long steel body.  About two in the morning I took some hours'"
164 "repose, and Conseil did the same.  In crossing the waist I did not meet"
164 "Captain Nemo: I supposed him to be in the pilot's cage.  The next"
164 "morning, the 19th of March, I took my post once more in the saloon."
164 "The electric log told me that the speed of the Nautilus had been"
164 "slackened.  It was then going towards the surface; but prudently"
164 "emptying its reservoirs very slowly.  My heart beat fast.  Were we"
164 "going to emerge and regain the open polar atmosphere?  No!  A shock"
164 "told me that the Nautilus had struck the bottom of the iceberg, still"
164 "very thick, judging from the deadened sound.  We had in deed \"struck,\""
164 "to use a sea expression, but in an inverse sense, and at a thousand"
164 "feet deep.  This would give three thousand feet of ice above us; one"
164 "thousand being above the water-mark. The iceberg was then higher than"
164 "at its borders--not a very reassuring fact.  Several times that day the"
164 "Nautilus tried again, and every time it struck the wall which lay like"
164 "a ceiling above it.  Sometimes it met with but 900 yards, only 200 of"
164 "which rose above the surface.  It was twice the height it was when the"
164 "Nautilus had gone under the waves.  I carefully noted the different"
164 "depths, and thus obtained a submarine profile of the chain as it was"
164 "developed under the water.  That night no change had taken place in our"
164 "situation.  Still ice between four and five hundred yards in depth!  It"
164 "was evidently diminishing, but, still, what a thickness between us and"
164 "the surface of the ocean!  It was then eight.  According to the daily"
164 "custom on board the Nautilus, its air should have been renewed four"
164 "hours ago; but I did not suffer much, although Captain Nemo had not yet"
164 "made any demand upon his reserve of oxygen.  My sleep was painful that"
164 "night; hope and fear besieged me by turns: I rose several times.  The"
164 "groping of the Nautilus continued.  About three in the morning, I"
164 "noticed that the lower surface of the iceberg was only about fifty feet"
164 "deep.  One hundred and fifty feet now separated us from the surface of"
164 "the waters.  The iceberg was by degrees becoming an ice-field, the"
164 "mountain a plain.  My eyes never left the manometer.  We were still"
164 "rising diagonally to the surface, which sparkled under the electric"
164 "rays.  The iceberg was stretching both above and beneath into"
164 "lengthening slopes; mile after mile it was getting thinner.  At length,"
164 "at six in the morning of that memorable day, the 19th of March, the"
164 "door of the saloon opened, and Captain Nemo appeared."
164 ""
164 "\"The sea is open!!\" was all he said."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIV"
164 ""
164 "THE SOUTH POLE"
164 ""
164 "I rushed on to the platform.  Yes! the open sea, with but a few"
164 "scattered pieces of ice and moving icebergs--a long stretch of sea; a"
164 "world of birds in the air, and myriads of fishes under those waters,"
164 "which varied from intense blue to olive green, according to the bottom."
164 "The thermometer marked 3 deg. C. above zero.  It was comparatively"
164 "spring, shut up as we were behind this iceberg, whose lengthened mass"
164 "was dimly seen on our northern horizon."
164 ""
164 "\"Are we at the pole?\"  I asked the Captain, with a beating heart."
164 ""
164 "\"I do not know,\" he replied.  \"At noon I will take our bearings.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But will the sun show himself through this fog?\" said I, looking at"
164 "the leaden sky."
164 ""
164 "\"However little it shows, it will be enough,\" replied the Captain."
164 ""
164 "About ten miles south a solitary island rose to a height of one hundred"
164 "and four yards.  We made for it, but carefully, for the sea might be"
164 "strewn with banks.  One hour afterwards we had reached it, two hours"
164 "later we had made the round of it.  It measured four or five miles in"
164 "circumference.  A narrow canal separated it from a considerable stretch"
164 "of land, perhaps a continent, for we could not see its limits.  The"
164 "existence of this land seemed to give some colour to Maury's theory."
164 "The ingenious American has remarked that, between the South Pole and"
164 "the sixtieth parallel, the sea is covered with floating ice of enormous"
164 "size, which is never met with in the North Atlantic.  From this fact he"
164 "has drawn the conclusion that the Antarctic Circle encloses"
164 "considerable continents, as icebergs cannot form in open sea, but only"
164 "on the coasts.  According to these calculations, the mass of ice"
164 "surrounding the southern pole forms a vast cap, the circumference of"
164 "which must be, at least, 2,500 miles.  But the Nautilus, for fear of"
164 "running aground, had stopped about three cable-lengths from a strand"
164 "over which reared a superb heap of rocks.  The boat was launched; the"
164 "Captain, two of his men, bearing instruments, Conseil, and myself were"
164 "in it.  It was ten in the morning.  I had not seen Ned Land.  Doubtless"
164 "the Canadian did not wish to admit the presence of the South Pole.  A"
164 "few strokes of the oar brought us to the sand, where we ran ashore."
164 "Conseil was going to jump on to the land, when I held him back."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said I to Captain Nemo, \"to you belongs the honour of first"
164 "setting foot on this land.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir,\" said the Captain, \"and if I do not hesitate to tread this"
164 "South Pole, it is because, up to this time, no human being has left a"
164 "trace there.\""
164 ""
164 "Saying this, he jumped lightly on to the sand.  His heart beat with"
164 "emotion.  He climbed a rock, sloping to a little promontory, and there,"
164 "with his arms crossed, mute and motionless, and with an eager look, he"
164 "seemed to take possession of these southern regions.  After five"
164 "minutes passed in this ecstasy, he turned to us."
164 ""
164 "\"When you like, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "I landed, followed by Conseil, leaving the two men in the boat.  For a"
164 "long way the soil was composed of a reddish sandy stone, something like"
164 "crushed brick, scoriae, streams of lava, and pumice-stones. One could"
164 "not mistake its volcanic origin.  In some parts, slight curls of smoke"
164 "emitted a sulphurous smell, proving that the internal fires had lost"
164 "nothing of their expansive powers, though, having climbed a high"
164 "acclivity, I could see no volcano for a radius of several miles.  We"
164 "know that in those Antarctic countries, James Ross found two craters,"
164 "the Erebus and Terror, in full activity, on the 167th meridian,"
164 "latitude 77 deg. 32'. The vegetation of this desolate continent seemed"
164 "to me much restricted.  Some lichens lay upon the black rocks; some"
164 "microscopic plants, rudimentary diatomas, a kind of cells placed"
164 "between two quartz shells; long purple and scarlet weed, supported on"
164 "little swimming bladders, which the breaking of the waves brought to"
164 "the shore.  These constituted the meagre flora of this region.  The"
164 "shore was strewn with molluscs, little mussels, and limpets.  I also"
164 "saw myriads of northern clios, one-and-a-quarter inches long, of which"
164 "a whale would swallow a whole world at a mouthful; and some perfect"
164 "sea-butterflies, animating the waters on the skirts of the shore."
164 ""
164 "There appeared on the high bottoms some coral shrubs, of the kind"
164 "which, according to James Ross, live in the Antarctic seas to the depth"
164 "of more than 1,000 yards.  Then there were little kingfishers and"
164 "starfish studding the soil.  But where life abounded most was in the"
164 "air.  There thousands of birds fluttered and flew of all kinds,"
164 "deafening us with their cries; others crowded the rock, looking at us"
164 "as we passed by without fear, and pressing familiarly close by our"
164 "feet.  There were penguins, so agile in the water, heavy and awkward as"
164 "they are on the ground; they were uttering harsh cries, a large"
164 "assembly, sober in gesture, but extravagant in clamour.  Albatrosses"
164 "passed in the air, the expanse of their wings being at least four yards"
164 "and a half, and justly called the vultures of the ocean; some gigantic"
164 "petrels, and some damiers, a kind of small duck, the underpart of whose"
164 "body is black and white; then there were a whole series of petrels,"
164 "some whitish, with brown-bordered wings, others blue, peculiar to the"
164 "Antarctic seas, and so oily, as I told Conseil, that the inhabitants of"
164 "the Ferroe Islands had nothing to do before lighting them but to put a"
164 "wick in."
164 ""
164 "\"A little more,\" said Conseil, \"and they would be perfect lamps!  After"
164 "that, we cannot expect Nature to have previously furnished them with"
164 "wicks!\""
164 ""
164 "About half a mile farther on the soil was riddled with ruffs' nests, a"
164 "sort of laying-ground, out of which many birds were issuing.  Captain"
164 "Nemo had some hundreds hunted.  They uttered a cry like the braying of"
164 "an ass, were about the size of a goose, slate-colour on the body, white"
164 "beneath, with a yellow line round their throats; they allowed"
164 "themselves to be killed with a stone, never trying to escape.  But the"
164 "fog did not lift, and at eleven the sun had not yet shown itself.  Its"
164 "absence made me uneasy.  Without it no observations were possible."
164 "How, then, could we decide whether we had reached the pole?  When I"
164 "rejoined Captain Nemo, I found him leaning on a piece of rock, silently"
164 "watching the sky.  He seemed impatient and vexed.  But what was to be"
164 "done?  This rash and powerful man could not command the sun as he did"
164 "the sea.  Noon arrived without the orb of day showing itself for an"
164 "instant.  We could not even tell its position behind the curtain of"
164 "fog; and soon the fog turned to snow."
164 ""
164 "\"Till to-morrow,\" said the Captain, quietly, and we returned to the"
164 "Nautilus amid these atmospheric disturbances."
164 ""
164 "The tempest of snow continued till the next day.  It was impossible to"
164 "remain on the platform.  From the saloon, where I was taking notes of"
164 "incidents happening during this excursion to the polar continent, I"
164 "could hear the cries of petrels and albatrosses sporting in the midst"
164 "of this violent storm.  The Nautilus did not remain motionless, but"
164 "skirted the coast, advancing ten miles more to the south in the"
164 "half-light left by the sun as it skirted the edge of the horizon.  The"
164 "next day, the 20th of March, the snow had ceased.  The cold was a"
164 "little greater, the thermometer showing 2 deg. below zero.  The fog"
164 "was rising, and I hoped that that day our observations might be taken."
164 "Captain Nemo not having yet appeared, the boat took Conseil and myself"
164 "to land.  The soil was still of the same volcanic nature; everywhere"
164 "were traces of lava, scoriae, and basalt; but the crater which had"
164 "vomited them I could not see.  Here, as lower down, this continent was"
164 "alive with myriads of birds.  But their rule was now divided with large"
164 "troops of sea-mammals, looking at us with their soft eyes.  There were"
164 "several kinds of seals, some stretched on the earth, some on flakes of"
164 "ice, many going in and out of the sea.  They did not flee at our"
164 "approach, never having had anything to do with man; and I reckoned that"
164 "there were provisions there for hundreds of vessels."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said Conseil, \"will you tell me the names of these creatures?\""
164 ""
164 "\"They are seals and morses.\""
164 ""
164 "It was now eight in the morning.  Four hours remained to us before the"
164 "sun could be observed with advantage.  I directed our steps towards a"
164 "vast bay cut in the steep granite shore.  There, I can aver that earth"
164 "and ice were lost to sight by the numbers of sea-mammals covering them,"
164 "and I involuntarily sought for old Proteus, the mythological shepherd"
164 "who watched these immense flocks of Neptune.  There were more seals"
164 "than anything else, forming distinct groups, male and female, the"
164 "father watching over his family, the mother suckling her little ones,"
164 "some already strong enough to go a few steps.  When they wished to"
164 "change their place, they took little jumps, made by the contraction of"
164 "their bodies, and helped awkwardly enough by their imperfect fin,"
164 "which, as with the lamantin, their cousins, forms a perfect forearm.  I"
164 "should say that, in the water, which is their element--the spine of"
164 "these creatures is flexible; with smooth and close skin and webbed"
164 "feet--they swim admirably.  In resting on the earth they take the most"
164 "graceful attitudes.  Thus the ancients, observing their soft and"
164 "expressive looks, which cannot be surpassed by the most beautiful look"
164 "a woman can give, their clear voluptuous eyes, their charming"
164 "positions, and the poetry of their manners, metamorphosed them, the"
164 "male into a triton and the female into a mermaid.  I made Conseil"
164 "notice the considerable development of the lobes of the brain in these"
164 "interesting cetaceans.  No mammal, except man, has such a quantity of"
164 "brain matter; they are also capable of receiving a certain amount of"
164 "education, are easily domesticated, and I think, with other"
164 "naturalists, that if properly taught they would be of great service as"
164 "fishing-dogs.  The greater part of them slept on the rocks or on the"
164 "sand.  Amongst these seals, properly so called, which have no external"
164 "ears (in which they differ from the otter, whose ears are prominent), I"
164 "noticed several varieties of seals about three yards long, with a white"
164 "coat, bulldog heads, armed with teeth in both jaws, four incisors at"
164 "the top and four at the bottom, and two large canine teeth in the shape"
164 "of a fleur-de-lis. Amongst them glided sea-elephants, a kind of seal,"
164 "with short, flexible trunks.  The giants of this species measured"
164 "twenty feet round and ten yards and a half in length; but they did not"
164 "move as we approached."
164 ""
164 "\"These creatures are not dangerous?\" asked Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"No; not unless you attack them.  When they have to defend their young"
164 "their rage is terrible, and it is not uncommon for them to break the"
164 "fishing-boats to pieces.\""
164 ""
164 "\"They are quite right,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"I do not say they are not.\""
164 ""
164 "Two miles farther on we were stopped by the promontory which shelters"
164 "the bay from the southerly winds.  Beyond it we heard loud bellowings"
164 "such as a troop of ruminants would produce."
164 ""
164 "\"Good!\" said Conseil; \"a concert of bulls!\""
164 ""
164 "\"No; a concert of morses.\""
164 ""
164 "\"They are fighting!\""
164 ""
164 "\"They are either fighting or playing.\""
164 ""
164 "We now began to climb the blackish rocks, amid unforeseen stumbles, and"
164 "over stones which the ice made slippery.  More than once I rolled over"
164 "at the expense of my loins.  Conseil, more prudent or more steady, did"
164 "not stumble, and helped me up, saying:"
164 ""
164 "\"If, sir, you would have the kindness to take wider steps, you would"
164 "preserve your equilibrium better.\""
164 ""
164 "Arrived at the upper ridge of the promontory, I saw a vast white plain"
164 "covered with morses.  They were playing amongst themselves, and what we"
164 "heard were bellowings of pleasure, not of anger."
164 ""
164 "As I passed these curious animals I could examine them leisurely, for"
164 "they did not move.  Their skins were thick and rugged, of a yellowish"
164 "tint, approaching to red; their hair was short and scant.  Some of them"
164 "were four yards and a quarter long.  Quieter and less timid than their"
164 "cousins of the north, they did not, like them, place sentinels round"
164 "the outskirts of their encampment.  After examining this city of"
164 "morses, I began to think of returning.  It was eleven o'clock, and, if"
164 "Captain Nemo found the conditions favourable for observations, I wished"
164 "to be present at the operation.  We followed a narrow pathway running"
164 "along the summit of the steep shore.  At half-past eleven we had"
164 "reached the place where we landed.  The boat had run aground, bringing"
164 "the Captain.  I saw him standing on a block of basalt, his instruments"
164 "near him, his eyes fixed on the northern horizon, near which the sun"
164 "was then describing a lengthened curve.  I took my place beside him,"
164 "and waited without speaking.  Noon arrived, and, as before, the sun did"
164 "not appear.  It was a fatality.  Observations were still wanting.  If"
164 "not accomplished to-morrow, we must give up all idea of taking any.  We"
164 "were indeed exactly at the 20th of March.  To-morrow, the 21st, would"
164 "be the equinox; the sun would disappear behind the horizon for six"
164 "months, and with its disappearance the long polar night would begin."
164 "Since the September equinox it had emerged from the northern horizon,"
164 "rising by lengthened spirals up to the 21st of December.  At this"
164 "period, the summer solstice of the northern regions, it had begun to"
164 "descend; and to-morrow was to shed its last rays upon them.  I"
164 "communicated my fears and observations to Captain Nemo."
164 ""
164 "\"You are right, M. Aronnax,\" said he; \"if to-morrow I cannot take the"
164 "altitude of the sun, I shall not be able to do it for six months.  But"
164 "precisely because chance has led me into these seas on the 21st of"
164 "March, my bearings will be easy to take, if at twelve we can see the"
164 "sun.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Why, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Because then the orb of day described such lengthened curves that it"
164 "is difficult to measure exactly its height above the horizon, and grave"
164 "errors may be made with instruments.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What will you do then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I shall only use my chronometer,\" replied Captain Nemo.  \"If"
164 "to-morrow, the 21st of March, the disc of the sun, allowing for"
164 "refraction, is exactly cut by the northern horizon, it will show that I"
164 "am at the South Pole.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so,\" said I. \"But this statement is not mathematically correct,"
164 "because the equinox does not necessarily begin at noon.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very likely, sir; but the error will not be a hundred yards and we do"
164 "not want more.  Till to-morrow, then!\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo returned on board.  Conseil and I remained to survey the"
164 "shore, observing and studying until five o'clock. Then I went to bed,"
164 "not, however, without invoking, like the Indian, the favour of the"
164 "radiant orb.  The next day, the 21st of March, at five in the morning,"
164 "I mounted the platform.  I found Captain Nemo there."
164 ""
164 "\"The weather is lightening a little,\" said he.  \"I have some hope."
164 "After breakfast we will go on shore and choose a post for observation.\""
164 ""
164 "That point settled, I sought Ned Land.  I wanted to take him with me."
164 "But the obstinate Canadian refused, and I saw that his taciturnity and"
164 "his bad humour grew day by day.  After all, I was not sorry for his"
164 "obstinacy under the circumstances.  Indeed, there were too many seals"
164 "on shore, and we ought not to lay such temptation in this unreflecting"
164 "fisherman's way.  Breakfast over, we went on shore.  The Nautilus had"
164 "gone some miles further up in the night.  It was a whole league from"
164 "the coast, above which reared a sharp peak about five hundred yards"
164 "high.  The boat took with me Captain Nemo, two men of the crew, and the"
164 "instruments, which consisted of a chronometer, a telescope, and a"
164 "barometer.  While crossing, I saw numerous whales belonging to the"
164 "three kinds peculiar to the southern seas; the whale, or the English"
164 "\"right whale,\" which has no dorsal fin; the \"humpback,\" with reeved"
164 "chest and large, whitish fins, which, in spite of its name, do not form"
164 "wings; and the fin-back, of a yellowish brown, the liveliest of all the"
164 "cetacea.  This powerful creature is heard a long way off when he throws"
164 "to a great height columns of air and vapour, which look like whirlwinds"
164 "of smoke.  These different mammals were disporting themselves in troops"
164 "in the quiet waters; and I could see that this basin of the Antarctic"
164 "Pole serves as a place of refuge to the cetacea too closely tracked by"
164 "the hunters.  I also noticed large medusae floating between the reeds."
164 ""
164 "At nine we landed; the sky was brightening, the clouds were flying to"
164 "the south, and the fog seemed to be leaving the cold surface of the"
164 "waters.  Captain Nemo went towards the peak, which he doubtless meant"
164 "to be his observatory.  It was a painful ascent over the sharp lava and"
164 "the pumice-stones, in an atmosphere often impregnated with a sulphurous"
164 "smell from the smoking cracks.  For a man unaccustomed to walk on land,"
164 "the Captain climbed the steep slopes with an agility I never saw"
164 "equalled and which a hunter would have envied.  We were two hours"
164 "getting to the summit of this peak, which was half porphyry and half"
164 "basalt.  From thence we looked upon a vast sea which, towards the"
164 "north, distinctly traced its boundary line upon the sky.  At our feet"
164 "lay fields of dazzling whiteness.  Over our heads a pale azure, free"
164 "from fog.  To the north the disc of the sun seemed like a ball of fire,"
164 "already horned by the cutting of the horizon.  From the bosom of the"
164 "water rose sheaves of liquid jets by hundreds.  In the distance lay the"
164 "Nautilus like a cetacean asleep on the water.  Behind us, to the south"
164 "and east, an immense country and a chaotic heap of rocks and ice, the"
164 "limits of which were not visible.  On arriving at the summit Captain"
164 "Nemo carefully took the mean height of the barometer, for he would have"
164 "to consider that in taking his observations.  At a quarter to twelve"
164 "the sun, then seen only by refraction, looked like a golden disc"
164 "shedding its last rays upon this deserted continent and seas which"
164 "never man had yet ploughed.  Captain Nemo, furnished with a lenticular"
164 "glass which, by means of a mirror, corrected the refraction, watched"
164 "the orb sinking below the horizon by degrees, following a lengthened"
164 "diagonal.  I held the chronometer.  My heart beat fast.  If the"
164 "disappearance of the half-disc of the sun coincided with twelve o'clock"
164 "on the chronometer, we were at the pole itself."
164 ""
164 "\"Twelve!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"The South Pole!\" replied Captain Nemo, in a grave voice, handing me"
164 "the glass, which showed the orb cut in exactly equal parts by the"
164 "horizon."
164 ""
164 "I looked at the last rays crowning the peak, and the shadows mounting"
164 "by degrees up its slopes.  At that moment Captain Nemo, resting with"
164 "his hand on my shoulder, said:"
164 ""
164 "\"I, Captain Nemo, on this 21st day of March, 1868, have reached the"
164 "South Pole on the ninetieth degree; and I take possession of this part"
164 "of the globe, equal to one-sixth of the known continents.\""
164 ""
164 "\"In whose name, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"In my own, sir!\""
164 ""
164 "Saying which, Captain Nemo unfurled a black banner, bearing an \"N\" in"
164 "gold quartered on its bunting.  Then, turning towards the orb of day,"
164 "whose last rays lapped the horizon of the sea, he exclaimed:"
164 ""
164 "\"Adieu, sun!  Disappear, thou radiant orb! rest beneath this open sea,"
164 "and let a night of six months spread its shadows over my new domains!\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XV"
164 ""
164 "ACCIDENT OR INCIDENT?"
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 22nd of March, at six in the morning, preparations"
164 "for departure were begun.  The last gleams of twilight were melting"
164 "into night.  The cold was great, the constellations shone with"
164 "wonderful intensity.  In the zenith glittered that wondrous Southern"
164 "Cross--the polar bear of Antarctic regions.  The thermometer showed 120"
164 "below zero, and when the wind freshened it was most biting.  Flakes of"
164 "ice increased on the open water.  The sea seemed everywhere alike."
164 "Numerous blackish patches spread on the surface, showing the formation"
164 "of fresh ice.  Evidently the southern basin, frozen during the six"
164 "winter months, was absolutely inaccessible.  What became of the whales"
164 "in that time?  Doubtless they went beneath the icebergs, seeking more"
164 "practicable seas.  As to the seals and morses, accustomed to live in a"
164 "hard climate, they remained on these icy shores.  These creatures have"
164 "the instinct to break holes in the ice-field and to keep them open.  To"
164 "these holes they come for breath; when the birds, driven away by the"
164 "cold, have emigrated to the north, these sea mammals remain sole"
164 "masters of the polar continent.  But the reservoirs were filling with"
164 "water, and the Nautilus was slowly descending.  At 1,000 feet deep it"
164 "stopped; its screw beat the waves, and it advanced straight towards the"
164 "north at a speed of fifteen miles an hour.  Towards night it was"
164 "already floating under the immense body of the iceberg.  At three in"
164 "the morning I was awakened by a violent shock.  I sat up in my bed and"
164 "listened in the darkness, when I was thrown into the middle of the"
164 "room.  The Nautilus, after having struck, had rebounded violently.  I"
164 "groped along the partition, and by the staircase to the saloon, which"
164 "was lit by the luminous ceiling.  The furniture was upset.  Fortunately"
164 "the windows were firmly set, and had held fast.  The pictures on the"
164 "starboard side, from being no longer vertical, were clinging to the"
164 "paper, whilst those of the port side were hanging at least a foot from"
164 "the wall.  The Nautilus was lying on its starboard side perfectly"
164 "motionless.  I heard footsteps, and a confusion of voices; but Captain"
164 "Nemo did not appear.  As I was leaving the saloon, Ned Land and Conseil"
164 "entered."
164 ""
164 "\"What is the matter?\" said I, at once."
164 ""
164 "\"I came to ask you, sir,\" replied Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Confound it!\" exclaimed the Canadian, \"I know well enough!  The"
164 "Nautilus has struck; and, judging by the way she lies, I do not think"
164 "she will right herself as she did the first time in Torres Straits.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But,\" I asked, \"has she at least come to the surface of the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"We do not know,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"It is easy to decide,\" I answered.  I consulted the manometer.  To my"
164 "great surprise, it showed a depth of more than 180 fathoms.  \"What does"
164 "that mean?\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"We must ask Captain Nemo,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"But where shall we find him?\" said Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "\"Follow me,\" said I, to my companions."
164 ""
164 "We left the saloon.  There was no one in the library.  At the centre"
164 "staircase, by the berths of the ship's crew, there was no one.  I"
164 "thought that Captain Nemo must be in the pilot's cage.  It was best to"
164 "wait.  We all returned to the saloon.  For twenty minutes we remained"
164 "thus, trying to hear the slightest noise which might be made on board"
164 "the Nautilus, when Captain Nemo entered.  He seemed not to see us; his"
164 "face, generally so impassive, showed signs of uneasiness.  He watched"
164 "the compass silently, then the manometer; and, going to the"
164 "planisphere, placed his finger on a spot representing the southern"
164 "seas.  I would not interrupt him; but, some minutes later, when he"
164 "turned towards me, I said, using one of his own expressions in the"
164 "Torres Straits:"
164 ""
164 "\"An incident, Captain?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir; an accident this time.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Serious?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Is the danger immediate?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No.\""
164 ""
164 "\"The Nautilus has stranded?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And this has happened--how?\""
164 ""
164 "\"From a caprice of nature, not from the ignorance of man.  Not a"
164 "mistake has been made in the working.  But we cannot prevent"
164 "equilibrium from producing its effects.  We may brave human laws, but"
164 "we cannot resist natural ones.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo had chosen a strange moment for uttering this"
164 "philosophical reflection.  On the whole, his answer helped me little."
164 ""
164 "\"May I ask, sir, the cause of this accident?\""
164 ""
164 "\"An enormous block of ice, a whole mountain, has turned over,\" he"
164 "replied.  \"When icebergs are undermined at their base by warmer water"
164 "or reiterated shocks their centre of gravity rises, and the whole thing"
164 "turns over.  This is what has happened; one of these blocks, as it"
164 "fell, struck the Nautilus, then, gliding under its hull, raised it with"
164 "irresistible force, bringing it into beds which are not so thick, where"
164 "it is lying on its side.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But can we not get the Nautilus off by emptying its reservoirs, that"
164 "it might regain its equilibrium?\""
164 ""
164 "\"That, sir, is being done at this moment.  You can hear the pump"
164 "working.  Look at the needle of the manometer; it shows that the"
164 "Nautilus is rising, but the block of ice is floating with it; and,"
164 "until some obstacle stops its ascending motion, our position cannot be"
164 "altered.\""
164 ""
164 "Indeed, the Nautilus still held the same position to starboard;"
164 "doubtless it would right itself when the block stopped.  But at this"
164 "moment who knows if we may not be frightfully crushed between the two"
164 "glassy surfaces?  I reflected on all the consequences of our position."
164 "Captain Nemo never took his eyes off the manometer.  Since the fall of"
164 "the iceberg, the Nautilus had risen about a hundred and fifty feet, but"
164 "it still made the same angle with the perpendicular.  Suddenly a slight"
164 "movement was felt in the hold.  Evidently it was righting a little."
164 "Things hanging in the saloon were sensibly returning to their normal"
164 "position.  The partitions were nearing the upright.  No one spoke."
164 "With beating hearts we watched and felt the straightening.  The boards"
164 "became horizontal under our feet.  Ten minutes passed."
164 ""
164 "\"At last we have righted!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" said Captain Nemo, going to the door of the saloon."
164 ""
164 "\"But are we floating?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Certainly,\" he replied; \"since the reservoirs are not empty; and, when"
164 "empty, the Nautilus must rise to the surface of the sea.\""
164 ""
164 "We were in open sea; but at a distance of about ten yards, on either"
164 "side of the Nautilus, rose a dazzling wall of ice.  Above and beneath"
164 "the same wall.  Above, because the lower surface of the iceberg"
164 "stretched over us like an immense ceiling.  Beneath, because the"
164 "overturned block, having slid by degrees, had found a resting-place on"
164 "the lateral walls, which kept it in that position.  The Nautilus was"
164 "really imprisoned in a perfect tunnel of ice more than twenty yards in"
164 "breadth, filled with quiet water.  It was easy to get out of it by"
164 "going either forward or backward, and then make a free passage under"
164 "the iceberg, some hundreds of yards deeper.  The luminous ceiling had"
164 "been extinguished, but the saloon was still resplendent with intense"
164 "light.  It was the powerful reflection from the glass partition sent"
164 "violently back to the sheets of the lantern.  I cannot describe the"
164 "effect of the voltaic rays upon the great blocks so capriciously cut;"
164 "upon every angle, every ridge, every facet was thrown a different"
164 "light, according to the nature of the veins running through the ice; a"
164 "dazzling mine of gems, particularly of sapphires, their blue rays"
164 "crossing with the green of the emerald.  Here and there were opal"
164 "shades of wonderful softness, running through bright spots like"
164 "diamonds of fire, the brilliancy of which the eye could not bear.  The"
164 "power of the lantern seemed increased a hundredfold, like a lamp"
164 "through the lenticular plates of a first-class lighthouse."
164 ""
164 "\"How beautiful! how beautiful!\" cried Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" I said, \"it is a wonderful sight.  Is it not, Ned?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, confound it!  Yes,\" answered Ned Land, \"it is superb!  I am mad"
164 "at being obliged to admit it.  No one has ever seen anything like it;"
164 "but the sight may cost us dear.  And, if I must say all, I think we are"
164 "seeing here things which God never intended man to see.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned was right, it was too beautiful.  Suddenly a cry from Conseil made"
164 "me turn."
164 ""
164 "\"What is it?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Shut your eyes, sir!  Do not look, sir!\"  Saying which, Conseil"
164 "clapped his hands over his eyes."
164 ""
164 "\"But what is the matter, my boy?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I am dazzled, blinded.\""
164 ""
164 "My eyes turned involuntarily towards the glass, but I could not stand"
164 "the fire which seemed to devour them.  I understood what had happened."
164 "The Nautilus had put on full speed.  All the quiet lustre of the"
164 "ice-walls was at once changed into flashes of lightning.  The fire from"
164 "these myriads of diamonds was blinding.  It required some time to calm"
164 "our troubled looks.  At last the hands were taken down."
164 ""
164 "\"Faith, I should never have believed it,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "It was then five in the morning; and at that moment a shock was felt at"
164 "the bows of the Nautilus.  I knew that its spur had struck a block of"
164 "ice.  It must have been a false manoeuvre, for this submarine tunnel,"
164 "obstructed by blocks, was not very easy navigation.  I thought that"
164 "Captain Nemo, by changing his course, would either turn these obstacles"
164 "or else follow the windings of the tunnel.  In any case, the road"
164 "before us could not be entirely blocked.  But, contrary to my"
164 "expectations, the Nautilus took a decided retrograde motion."
164 ""
164 "\"We are going backwards?\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" I replied.  \"This end of the tunnel can have no egress.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Then,\" said I, \"the working is easy.  We must go back again, and go"
164 "out at the southern opening.  That is all.\""
164 ""
164 "In speaking thus, I wished to appear more confident than I really was."
164 "But the retrograde motion of the Nautilus was increasing; and,"
164 "reversing the screw, it carried us at great speed."
164 ""
164 "\"It will be a hindrance,\" said Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"What does it matter, some hours more or less, provided we get out at"
164 "last?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" repeated Ned Land, \"provided we do get out at last!\""
164 ""
164 "For a short time I walked from the saloon to the library.  My"
164 "companions were silent.  I soon threw myself on an ottoman, and took a"
164 "book, which my eyes overran mechanically.  A quarter of an hour after,"
164 "Conseil, approaching me, said, \"Is what you are reading very"
164 "interesting, sir?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very interesting!\"  I replied."
164 ""
164 "\"I should think so, sir.  It is your own book you are reading.\""
164 ""
164 "\"My book?\""
164 ""
164 "And indeed I was holding in my hand the work on the Great Submarine"
164 "Depths.  I did not even dream of it.  I closed the book and returned to"
164 "my walk.  Ned and Conseil rose to go."
164 ""
164 "\"Stay here, my friends,\" said I, detaining them.  \"Let us remain"
164 "together until we are out of this block.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As you please, sir,\" Conseil replied."
164 ""
164 "Some hours passed.  I often looked at the instruments hanging from the"
164 "partition.  The manometer showed that the Nautilus kept at a constant"
164 "depth of more than three hundred yards; the compass still pointed to"
164 "south; the log indicated a speed of twenty miles an hour, which, in"
164 "such a cramped space, was very great.  But Captain Nemo knew that he"
164 "could not hasten too much, and that minutes were worth ages to us.  At"
164 "twenty-five minutes past eight a second shock took place, this time"
164 "from behind.  I turned pale.  My companions were close by my side.  I"
164 "seized Conseil's hand.  Our looks expressed our feelings better than"
164 "words.  At this moment the Captain entered the saloon.  I went up to"
164 "him."
164 ""
164 "\"Our course is barred southward?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.  The iceberg has shifted and closed every outlet.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We are blocked up then?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes.\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVI"
164 ""
164 "WANT OF AIR"
164 ""
164 "Thus around the Nautilus, above and below, was an impenetrable wall of"
164 "ice.  We were prisoners to the iceberg.  I watched the Captain.  His"
164 "countenance had resumed its habitual imperturbability."
164 ""
164 "\"Gentlemen,\" he said calmly, \"there are two ways of dying in the"
164 "circumstances in which we are placed.\"  (This puzzling person had the"
164 "air of a mathematical professor lecturing to his pupils.) \"The first is"
164 "to be crushed; the second is to die of suffocation.  I do not speak of"
164 "the possibility of dying of hunger, for the supply of provisions in the"
164 "Nautilus will certainly last longer than we shall.  Let us, then,"
164 "calculate our chances.\""
164 ""
164 "\"As to suffocation, Captain,\" I replied, \"that is not to be feared,"
164 "because our reservoirs are full.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Just so; but they will only yield two days' supply of air.  Now, for"
164 "thirty-six hours we have been hidden under the water, and already the"
164 "heavy atmosphere of the Nautilus requires renewal.  In forty-eight"
164 "hours our reserve will be exhausted.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Captain, can we be delivered before forty-eight hours?\""
164 ""
164 "\"We will attempt it, at least, by piercing the wall that surrounds us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"On which side?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sound will tell us.  I am going to run the Nautilus aground on the"
164 "lower bank, and my men will attack the iceberg on the side that is"
164 "least thick.\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo went out.  Soon I discovered by a hissing noise that the"
164 "water was entering the reservoirs.  The Nautilus sank slowly, and"
164 "rested on the ice at a depth of 350 yards, the depth at which the lower"
164 "bank was immersed."
164 ""
164 "\"My friends,\" I said, \"our situation is serious, but I rely on your"
164 "courage and energy.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" replied the Canadian, \"I am ready to do anything for the general"
164 "safety.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good!  Ned,\" and I held out my hand to the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"I will add,\" he continued, \"that, being as handy with the pickaxe as"
164 "with the harpoon, if I can be useful to the Captain, he can command my"
164 "services.\""
164 ""
164 "\"He will not refuse your help.  Come, Ned!\""
164 ""
164 "I led him to the room where the crew of the Nautilus were putting on"
164 "their cork-jackets. I told the Captain of Ned's proposal, which he"
164 "accepted.  The Canadian put on his sea-costume, and was ready as soon"
164 "as his companions.  When Ned was dressed, I re-entered the"
164 "drawing-room, where the panes of glass were open, and, posted near"
164 "Conseil, I examined the ambient beds that supported the Nautilus.  Some"
164 "instants after, we saw a dozen of the crew set foot on the bank of ice,"
164 "and among them Ned Land, easily known by his stature.  Captain Nemo was"
164 "with them.  Before proceeding to dig the walls, he took the soundings,"
164 "to be sure of working in the right direction.  Long sounding lines were"
164 "sunk in the side walls, but after fifteen yards they were again stopped"
164 "by the thick wall.  It was useless to attack it on the ceiling-like"
164 "surface, since the iceberg itself measured more than 400 yards in"
164 "height.  Captain Nemo then sounded the lower surface.  There ten yards"
164 "of wall separated us from the water, so great was the thickness of the"
164 "ice-field. It was necessary, therefore, to cut from it a piece equal in"
164 "extent to the waterline of the Nautilus.  There were about 6,000 cubic"
164 "yards to detach, so as to dig a hole by which we could descend to the"
164 "ice-field. The work had begun immediately and carried on with"
164 "indefatigable energy.  Instead of digging round the Nautilus which"
164 "would have involved greater difficulty, Captain Nemo had an immense"
164 "trench made at eight yards from the port-quarter. Then the men set to"
164 "work simultaneously with their screws on several points of its"
164 "circumference.  Presently the pickaxe attacked this compact matter"
164 "vigorously, and large blocks were detached from the mass.  By a curious"
164 "effect of specific gravity, these blocks, lighter than water, fled, so"
164 "to speak, to the vault of the tunnel, that increased in thickness at"
164 "the top in proportion as it diminished at the base.  But that mattered"
164 "little, so long as the lower part grew thinner.  After two hours' hard"
164 "work, Ned Land came in exhausted.  He and his comrades were replaced by"
164 "new workers, whom Conseil and I joined.  The second lieutenant of the"
164 "Nautilus superintended us.  The water seemed singularly cold, but I"
164 "soon got warm handling the pickaxe.  My movements were free enough,"
164 "although they were made under a pressure of thirty atmospheres.  When I"
164 "re-entered, after working two hours, to take some food and rest, I"
164 "found a perceptible difference between the pure fluid with which the"
164 "Rouquayrol engine supplied me and the atmosphere of the Nautilus,"
164 "already charged with carbonic acid.  The air had not been renewed for"
164 "forty-eight hours, and its vivifying qualities were considerably"
164 "enfeebled.  However, after a lapse of twelve hours, we had only raised"
164 "a block of ice one yard thick, on the marked surface, which was about"
164 "600 cubic yards!  Reckoning that it took twelve hours to accomplish"
164 "this much it would take five nights and four days to bring this"
164 "enterprise to a satisfactory conclusion.  Five nights and four days!"
164 "And we have only air enough for two days in the reservoirs!  \"Without"
164 "taking into account,\" said Ned, \"that, even if we get out of this"
164 "infernal prison, we shall also be imprisoned under the iceberg, shut"
164 "out from all possible communication with the atmosphere.\" True enough!"
164 "Who could then foresee the minimum of time necessary for our"
164 "deliverance?  We might be suffocated before the Nautilus could regain"
164 "the surface of the waves?  Was it destined to perish in this ice-tomb,"
164 "with all those it enclosed?  The situation was terrible.  But everyone"
164 "had looked the danger in the face, and each was determined to do his"
164 "duty to the last."
164 ""
164 "As I expected, during the night a new block a yard square was carried"
164 "away, and still further sank the immense hollow.  But in the morning"
164 "when, dressed in my cork-jacket, I traversed the slushy mass at a"
164 "temperature of six or seven degrees below zero, I remarked that the"
164 "side walls were gradually closing in.  The beds of water farthest from"
164 "the trench, that were not warmed by the men's work, showed a tendency"
164 "to solidification.  In presence of this new and imminent danger, what"
164 "would become of our chances of safety, and how hinder the"
164 "solidification of this liquid medium, that would burst the partitions"
164 "of the Nautilus like glass?"
164 ""
164 "I did not tell my companions of this new danger.  What was the good of"
164 "damping the energy they displayed in the painful work of escape?  But"
164 "when I went on board again, I told Captain Nemo of this grave"
164 "complication."
164 ""
164 "\"I know it,\" he said, in that calm tone which could counteract the most"
164 "terrible apprehensions.  \"It is one danger more; but I see no way of"
164 "escaping it; the only chance of safety is to go quicker than"
