164 "solidification.  We must be beforehand with it, that is all.\""
164 ""
164 "On this day for several hours I used my pickaxe vigorously.  The work"
164 "kept me up.  Besides, to work was to quit the Nautilus, and breathe"
164 "directly the pure air drawn from the reservoirs, and supplied by our"
164 "apparatus, and to quit the impoverished and vitiated atmosphere."
164 "Towards evening the trench was dug one yard deeper.  When I returned on"
164 "board, I was nearly suffocated by the carbonic acid with which the air"
164 "was filled--ah! if we had only the chemical means to drive away this"
164 "deleterious gas.  We had plenty of oxygen; all this water contained a"
164 "considerable quantity, and by dissolving it with our powerful piles, it"
164 "would restore the vivifying fluid.  I had thought well over it; but of"
164 "what good was that, since the carbonic acid produced by our respiration"
164 "had invaded every part of the vessel?  To absorb it, it was necessary"
164 "to fill some jars with caustic potash, and to shake them incessantly."
164 "Now this substance was wanting on board, and nothing could replace it."
164 "On that evening, Captain Nemo ought to open the taps of his reservoirs,"
164 "and let some pure air into the interior of the Nautilus; without this"
164 "precaution we could not get rid of the sense of suffocation.  The next"
164 "day, March 26th, I resumed my miner's work in beginning the fifth yard."
164 "The side walls and the lower surface of the iceberg thickened visibly."
164 "It was evident that they would meet before the Nautilus was able to"
164 "disengage itself.  Despair seized me for an instant; my pickaxe nearly"
164 "fell from my hands.  What was the good of digging if I must be"
164 "suffocated, crushed by the water that was turning into stone?--a"
164 "punishment that the ferocity of the savages even would not have"
164 "invented!  Just then Captain Nemo passed near me.  I touched his hand"
164 "and showed him the walls of our prison.  The wall to port had advanced"
164 "to at least four yards from the hull of the Nautilus.  The Captain"
164 "understood me, and signed me to follow him.  We went on board.  I took"
164 "off my cork-jacket and accompanied him into the drawing-room."
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax, we must attempt some desperate means, or we shall be"
164 "sealed up in this solidified water as in cement.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; but what is to be done?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Ah! if my Nautilus were strong enough to bear this pressure without"
164 "being crushed!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well?\"  I asked, not catching the Captain's idea."
164 ""
164 "\"Do you not understand,\" he replied, \"that this congelation of water"
164 "will help us?  Do you not see that by its solidification, it would"
164 "burst through this field of ice that imprisons us, as, when it freezes,"
164 "it bursts the hardest stones?  Do you not perceive that it would be an"
164 "agent of safety instead of destruction?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Captain, perhaps.  But, whatever resistance to crushing the"
164 "Nautilus possesses, it could not support this terrible pressure, and"
164 "would be flattened like an iron plate.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I know it, sir.  Therefore we must not reckon on the aid of nature,"
164 "but on our own exertions.  We must stop this solidification.  Not only"
164 "will the side walls be pressed together; but there is not ten feet of"
164 "water before or behind the Nautilus.  The congelation gains on us on"
164 "all sides.\""
164 ""
164 "\"How long will the air in the reservoirs last for us to breathe on"
164 "board?\""
164 ""
164 "The Captain looked in my face.  \"After to-morrow they will be empty!\""
164 ""
164 "A cold sweat came over me.  However, ought I to have been astonished at"
164 "the answer?  On March 22, the Nautilus was in the open polar seas.  We"
164 "were at 26 deg.. For five days we had lived on the reserve on board."
164 "And what was left of the respirable air must be kept for the workers."
164 "Even now, as I write, my recollection is still so vivid that an"
164 "involuntary terror seizes me and my lungs seem to be without air."
164 "Meanwhile, Captain Nemo reflected silently, and evidently an idea had"
164 "struck him; but he seemed to reject it.  At last, these words escaped"
164 "his lips:"
164 ""
164 "\"Boiling water!\" he muttered."
164 ""
164 "\"Boiling water?\"  I cried."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.  We are enclosed in a space that is relatively confined."
164 "Would not jets of boiling water, constantly injected by the pumps,"
164 "raise the temperature in this part and stay the congelation?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Let us try it,\" I said resolutely."
164 ""
164 "\"Let us try it, Professor.\""
164 ""
164 "The thermometer then stood at 7 deg. outside.  Captain Nemo took me to"
164 "the galleys, where the vast distillatory machines stood that furnished"
164 "the drinkable water by evaporation.  They filled these with water, and"
164 "all the electric heat from the piles was thrown through the worms"
164 "bathed in the liquid.  In a few minutes this water reached 100 deg.. It"
164 "was directed towards the pumps, while fresh water replaced it in"
164 "proportion.  The heat developed by the troughs was such that cold"
164 "water, drawn up from the sea after only having gone through the"
164 "machines, came boiling into the body of the pump.  The injection was"
164 "begun, and three hours after the thermometer marked 6 deg. below zero"
164 "outside.  One degree was gained.  Two hours later the thermometer only"
164 "marked 4 deg.."
164 ""
164 "\"We shall succeed,\" I said to the Captain, after having anxiously"
164 "watched the result of the operation."
164 ""
164 "\"I think,\" he answered, \"that we shall not be crushed.  We have no more"
164 "suffocation to fear.\""
164 ""
164 "During the night the temperature of the water rose to 1 deg. below"
164 "zero.  The injections could not carry it to a higher point.  But, as"
164 "the congelation of the sea-water produces at least 2 deg., I was at"
164 "least reassured against the dangers of solidification."
164 ""
164 "The next day, March 27th, six yards of ice had been cleared, twelve"
164 "feet only remaining to be cleared away.  There was yet forty-eight"
164 "hours' work.  The air could not be renewed in the interior of the"
164 "Nautilus.  And this day would make it worse.  An intolerable weight"
164 "oppressed me.  Towards three o'clock in the evening this feeling rose"
164 "to a violent degree.  Yawns dislocated my jaws.  My lungs panted as"
164 "they inhaled this burning fluid, which became rarefied more and more."
164 "A moral torpor took hold of me.  I was powerless, almost unconscious."
164 "My brave Conseil, though exhibiting the same symptoms and suffering in"
164 "the same manner, never left me.  He took my hand and encouraged me, and"
164 "I heard him murmur, \"Oh! if I could only not breathe, so as to leave"
164 "more air for my master!\""
164 ""
164 "Tears came into my eyes on hearing him speak thus.  If our situation to"
164 "all was intolerable in the interior, with what haste and gladness would"
164 "we put on our cork-jackets to work in our turn!  Pickaxes sounded on"
164 "the frozen ice-beds. Our arms ached, the skin was torn off our hands."
164 "But what were these fatigues, what did the wounds matter?  Vital air"
164 "came to the lungs!  We breathed! we breathed!"
164 ""
164 "All this time no one prolonged his voluntary task beyond the prescribed"
164 "time.  His task accomplished, each one handed in turn to his panting"
164 "companions the apparatus that supplied him with life.  Captain Nemo set"
164 "the example, and submitted first to this severe discipline.  When the"
164 "time came, he gave up his apparatus to another and returned to the"
164 "vitiated air on board, calm, unflinching, unmurmuring."
164 ""
164 "On that day the ordinary work was accomplished with unusual vigour."
164 "Only two yards remained to be raised from the surface.  Two yards only"
164 "separated us from the open sea.  But the reservoirs were nearly emptied"
164 "of air.  The little that remained ought to be kept for the workers; not"
164 "a particle for the Nautilus.  When I went back on board, I was half"
164 "suffocated.  What a night!  I know not how to describe it.  The next"
164 "day my breathing was oppressed.  Dizziness accompanied the pain in my"
164 "head and made me like a drunken man.  My companions showed the same"
164 "symptoms.  Some of the crew had rattling in the throat."
164 ""
164 "On that day, the sixth of our imprisonment, Captain Nemo, finding the"
164 "pickaxes work too slowly, resolved to crush the ice-bed that still"
164 "separated us from the liquid sheet.  This man's coolness and energy"
164 "never forsook him.  He subdued his physical pains by moral force."
164 ""
164 "By his orders the vessel was lightened, that is to say, raised from the"
164 "ice-bed by a change of specific gravity.  When it floated they towed it"
164 "so as to bring it above the immense trench made on the level of the"
164 "water-line. Then, filling his reservoirs of water, he descended and"
164 "shut himself up in the hole."
164 ""
164 "Just then all the crew came on board, and the double door of"
164 "communication was shut.  The Nautilus then rested on the bed of ice,"
164 "which was not one yard thick, and which the sounding leads had"
164 "perforated in a thousand places.  The taps of the reservoirs were then"
164 "opened, and a hundred cubic yards of water was let in, increasing the"
164 "weight of the Nautilus to 1,800 tons.  We waited, we listened,"
164 "forgetting our sufferings in hope.  Our safety depended on this last"
164 "chance.  Notwithstanding the buzzing in my head, I soon heard the"
164 "humming sound under the hull of the Nautilus.  The ice cracked with a"
164 "singular noise, like tearing paper, and the Nautilus sank."
164 ""
164 "\"We are off!\" murmured Conseil in my ear."
164 ""
164 "I could not answer him.  I seized his hand, and pressed it"
164 "convulsively.  All at once, carried away by its frightful overcharge,"
164 "the Nautilus sank like a bullet under the waters, that is to say, it"
164 "fell as if it was in a vacuum.  Then all the electric force was put on"
164 "the pumps, that soon began to let the water out of the reservoirs."
164 "After some minutes, our fall was stopped.  Soon, too, the manometer"
164 "indicated an ascending movement.  The screw, going at full speed, made"
164 "the iron hull tremble to its very bolts and drew us towards the north."
164 "But if this floating under the iceberg is to last another day before we"
164 "reach the open sea, I shall be dead first."
164 ""
164 "Half stretched upon a divan in the library, I was suffocating.  My face"
164 "was purple, my lips blue, my faculties suspended.  I neither saw nor"
164 "heard.  All notion of time had gone from my mind.  My muscles could not"
164 "contract.  I do not know how many hours passed thus, but I was"
164 "conscious of the agony that was coming over me.  I felt as if I was"
164 "going to die.  Suddenly I came to.  Some breaths of air penetrated my"
164 "lungs.  Had we risen to the surface of the waves?  Were we free of the"
164 "iceberg?  No!  Ned and Conseil, my two brave friends, were sacrificing"
164 "themselves to save me.  Some particles of air still remained at the"
164 "bottom of one apparatus.  Instead of using it, they had kept it for me,"
164 "and, while they were being suffocated, they gave me life, drop by drop."
164 "I wanted to push back the thing; they held my hands, and for some"
164 "moments I breathed freely.  I looked at the clock; it was eleven in the"
164 "morning.  It ought to be the 28th of March.  The Nautilus went at a"
164 "frightful pace, forty miles an hour.  It literally tore through the"
164 "water.  Where was Captain Nemo?  Had he succumbed?  Were his companions"
164 "dead with him?  At the moment the manometer indicated that we were not"
164 "more than twenty feet from the surface.  A mere plate of ice separated"
164 "us from the atmosphere.  Could we not break it?  Perhaps.  In any case"
164 "the Nautilus was going to attempt it.  I felt that it was in an oblique"
164 "position, lowering the stern, and raising the bows.  The introduction"
164 "of water had been the means of disturbing its equilibrium.  Then,"
164 "impelled by its powerful screw, it attacked the ice-field from beneath"
164 "like a formidable battering-ram.  It broke it by backing and then"
164 "rushing forward against the field, which gradually gave way; and at"
164 "last, dashing suddenly against it, shot forwards on the ice-field, that"
164 "crushed beneath its weight.  The panel was opened--one might say torn"
164 "off--and the pure air came in in abundance to all parts of the Nautilus."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVII"
164 ""
164 "FROM CAPE HORN TO THE AMAZON"
164 ""
164 "How I got on to the platform, I have no idea; perhaps the Canadian had"
164 "carried me there.  But I breathed, I inhaled the vivifying sea-air.  My"
164 "two companions were getting drunk with the fresh particles.  The other"
164 "unhappy men had been so long without food, that they could not with"
164 "impunity indulge in the simplest aliments that were given them.  We, on"
164 "the contrary, had no end to restrain ourselves; we could draw this air"
164 "freely into our lungs, and it was the breeze, the breeze alone, that"
164 "filled us with this keen enjoyment."
164 ""
164 "\"Ah!\" said Conseil, \"how delightful this oxygen is!  Master need not"
164 "fear to breathe it.  There is enough for everybody.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land did not speak, but he opened his jaws wide enough to frighten"
164 "a shark.  Our strength soon returned, and, when I looked round me, I"
164 "saw we were alone on the platform.  The foreign seamen in the Nautilus"
164 "were contented with the air that circulated in the interior; none of"
164 "them had come to drink in the open air."
164 ""
164 "The first words I spoke were words of gratitude and thankfulness to my"
164 "two companions.  Ned and Conseil had prolonged my life during the last"
164 "hours of this long agony.  All my gratitude could not repay such"
164 "devotion."
164 ""
164 "\"My friends,\" said I, \"we are bound one to the other for ever, and I am"
164 "under infinite obligations to you.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Which I shall take advantage of,\" exclaimed the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"What do you mean?\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"I mean that I shall take you with me when I leave this infernal"
164 "Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Well,\" said Conseil, \"after all this, are we going right?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes,\" I replied, \"for we are going the way of the sun, and here the"
164 "sun is in the north.\""
164 ""
164 "\"No doubt,\" said Ned Land; \"but it remains to be seen whether he will"
164 "bring the ship into the Pacific or the Atlantic Ocean, that is, into"
164 "frequented or deserted seas.\""
164 ""
164 "I could not answer that question, and I feared that Captain Nemo would"
164 "rather take us to the vast ocean that touches the coasts of Asia and"
164 "America at the same time.  He would thus complete the tour round the"
164 "submarine world, and return to those waters in which the Nautilus could"
164 "sail freely.  We ought, before long, to settle this important point."
164 "The Nautilus went at a rapid pace.  The polar circle was soon passed,"
164 "and the course shaped for Cape Horn.  We were off the American point,"
164 "March 31st, at seven o'clock in the evening.  Then all our past"
164 "sufferings were forgotten.  The remembrance of that imprisonment in the"
164 "ice was effaced from our minds.  We only thought of the future."
164 "Captain Nemo did not appear again either in the drawing-room or on the"
164 "platform.  The point shown each day on the planisphere, and, marked by"
164 "the lieutenant, showed me the exact direction of the Nautilus.  Now, on"
164 "that evening, it was evident, to, my great satisfaction, that we were"
164 "going back to the North by the Atlantic.  The next day, April 1st, when"
164 "the Nautilus ascended to the surface some minutes before noon, we"
164 "sighted land to the west.  It was Terra del Fuego, which the first"
164 "navigators named thus from seeing the quantity of smoke that rose from"
164 "the natives' huts.  The coast seemed low to me, but in the distance"
164 "rose high mountains.  I even thought I had a glimpse of Mount"
164 "Sarmiento, that rises 2,070 yards above the level of the sea, with a"
164 "very pointed summit, which, according as it is misty or clear, is a"
164 "sign of fine or of wet weather.  At this moment the peak was clearly"
164 "defined against the sky.  The Nautilus, diving again under the water,"
164 "approached the coast, which was only some few miles off.  From the"
164 "glass windows in the drawing-room, I saw long seaweeds and gigantic"
164 "fuci and varech, of which the open polar sea contains so many"
164 "specimens, with their sharp polished filaments; they measured about 300"
164 "yards in length--real cables, thicker than one's thumb; and, having"
164 "great tenacity, they are often used as ropes for vessels.  Another weed"
164 "known as velp, with leaves four feet long, buried in the coral"
164 "concretions, hung at the bottom.  It served as nest and food for"
164 "myriads of crustacea and molluscs, crabs, and cuttlefish.  There seals"
164 "and otters had splendid repasts, eating the flesh of fish with"
164 "sea-vegetables, according to the English fashion.  Over this fertile"
164 "and luxuriant ground the Nautilus passed with great rapidity.  Towards"
164 "evening it approached the Falkland group, the rough summits of which I"
164 "recognised the following day.  The depth of the sea was moderate.  On"
164 "the shores our nets brought in beautiful specimens of sea weed, and"
164 "particularly a certain fucus, the roots of which were filled with the"
164 "best mussels in the world.  Geese and ducks fell by dozens on the"
164 "platform, and soon took their places in the pantry on board."
164 ""
164 "When the last heights of the Falklands had disappeared from the"
164 "horizon, the Nautilus sank to between twenty and twenty-five yards, and"
164 "followed the American coast.  Captain Nemo did not show himself.  Until"
164 "the 3rd of April we did not quit the shores of Patagonia, sometimes"
164 "under the ocean, sometimes at the surface.  The Nautilus passed beyond"
164 "the large estuary formed by the Uraguay.  Its direction was northwards,"
164 "and followed the long windings of the coast of South America.  We had"
164 "then made 1,600 miles since our embarkation in the seas of Japan."
164 "About eleven o'clock in the morning the Tropic of Capricorn was crossed"
164 "on the thirty-seventh meridian, and we passed Cape Frio standing out to"
164 "sea.  Captain Nemo, to Ned Land's great displeasure, did not like the"
164 "neighbourhood of the inhabited coasts of Brazil, for we went at a giddy"
164 "speed.  Not a fish, not a bird of the swiftest kind could follow us,"
164 "and the natural curiosities of these seas escaped all observation."
164 ""
164 "This speed was kept up for several days, and in the evening of the 9th"
164 "of April we sighted the most westerly point of South America that forms"
164 "Cape San Roque.  But then the Nautilus swerved again, and sought the"
164 "lowest depth of a submarine valley which is between this Cape and"
164 "Sierra Leone on the African coast.  This valley bifurcates to the"
164 "parallel of the Antilles, and terminates at the mouth by the enormous"
164 "depression of 9,000 yards.  In this place, the geological basin of the"
164 "ocean forms, as far as the Lesser Antilles, a cliff to three and a half"
164 "miles perpendicular in height, and, at the parallel of the Cape Verde"
164 "Islands, an other wall not less considerable, that encloses thus all"
164 "the sunk continent of the Atlantic.  The bottom of this immense valley"
164 "is dotted with some mountains, that give to these submarine places a"
164 "picturesque aspect.  I speak, moreover, from the manuscript charts that"
164 "were in the library of the Nautilus--charts evidently due to Captain"
164 "Nemo's hand, and made after his personal observations.  For two days"
164 "the desert and deep waters were visited by means of the inclined"
164 "planes.  The Nautilus was furnished with long diagonal broadsides which"
164 "carried it to all elevations.  But on the 11th of April it rose"
164 "suddenly, and land appeared at the mouth of the Amazon River, a vast"
164 "estuary, the embouchure of which is so considerable that it freshens"
164 "the sea-water for the distance of several leagues."
164 ""
164 "The equator was crossed. Twenty miles to the west were the Guianas, a"
164 "French territory, on which we could have found an easy refuge; but a"
164 "stiff breeze was blowing, and the furious waves would not have allowed"
164 "a single boat to face them. Ned Land understood that, no doubt, for he"
164 "spoke not a word about it. For my part, I made no allusion to his"
164 "schemes of flight, for I would not urge him to make an attempt that"
164 "must inevitably fail. I made the time pass pleasantly by interesting"
164 "studies.  During the days of April 11th and 12th, the Nautilus did not"
164 "leave the surface of the sea, and the net brought in a marvellous haul"
164 "of Zoophytes, fish and reptiles. Some zoophytes had been fished up by"
164 "the chain of the nets; they were for the most part beautiful"
164 "phyctallines, belonging to the actinidian family, and among other"
164 "species the phyctalis protexta, peculiar to that part of the ocean,"
164 "with a little cylindrical trunk, ornamented With vertical lines,"
164 "speckled with red dots, crowning a marvellous blossoming of tentacles."
164 "As to the molluscs, they consisted of some I had already"
164 "observed--turritellas, olive porphyras, with regular lines"
164 "intercrossed, with red spots standing out plainly against the flesh;"
164 "odd pteroceras, like petrified scorpions; translucid hyaleas,"
164 "argonauts, cuttle-fish (excellent eating), and certain species of"
164 "calmars that naturalists of antiquity have classed amongst the"
164 "flying-fish, and that serve principally for bait for cod-fishing. I had"
164 "now an opportunity of studying several species of fish on these shores."
164 "Amongst the cartilaginous ones, petromyzons-pricka, a sort of eel,"
164 "fifteen inches long, with a greenish head, violet fins, grey-blue back,"
164 "brown belly, silvered and sown with bright spots, the pupil of the eye"
164 "encircled with gold--a curious animal, that the current of the Amazon"
164 "had drawn to the sea, for they inhabit fresh waters--tuberculated"
164 "streaks, with pointed snouts, and a long loose tail, armed with a long"
164 "jagged sting; little sharks, a yard long, grey and whitish skin, and"
164 "several rows of teeth, bent back, that are generally known by the name"
164 "of pantouffles; vespertilios, a kind of red isosceles triangle, half a"
164 "yard long, to which pectorals are attached by fleshy prolongations that"
164 "make them look like bats, but that their horny appendage, situated near"
164 "the nostrils, has given them the name of sea-unicorns; lastly, some"
164 "species of balistae, the curassavian, whose spots were of a brilliant"
164 "gold colour, and the capriscus of clear violet, and with varying shades"
164 "like a pigeon's throat."
164 ""
164 "I end here this catalogue, which is somewhat dry perhaps, but very"
164 "exact, with a series of bony fish that I observed in passing belonging"
164 "to the apteronotes, and whose snout is white as snow, the body of a"
164 "beautiful black, marked with a very long loose fleshy strip;"
164 "odontognathes, armed with spikes; sardines nine inches long, glittering"
164 "with a bright silver light; a species of mackerel provided with two"
164 "anal fins; centronotes of a blackish tint, that are fished for with"
164 "torches, long fish, two yards in length, with fat flesh, white and"
164 "firm, which, when they arc fresh, taste like eel, and when dry, like"
164 "smoked salmon; labres, half red, covered with scales only at the bottom"
164 "of the dorsal and anal fins; chrysoptera, on which gold and silver"
164 "blend their brightness with that of the ruby and topaz; golden-tailed"
164 "spares, the flesh of which is extremely delicate, and whose"
164 "phosphorescent properties betray them in the midst of the waters;"
164 "orange-coloured spares with long tongues; maigres, with gold caudal"
164 "fins, dark thorn-tails, anableps of Surinam, etc."
164 ""
164 "Notwithstanding this \"et cetera,\" I must not omit to mention fish that"
164 "Conseil will long remember, and with good reason. One of our nets had"
164 "hauled up a sort of very flat ray fish, which, with the tail cut off,"
164 "formed a perfect disc, and weighed twenty ounces. It was white"
164 "underneath, red above, with large round spots of dark blue encircled"
164 "with black, very glossy skin, terminating in a bilobed fin. Laid out on"
164 "the platform, it struggled, tried to turn itself by convulsive"
164 "movements, and made so many efforts, that one last turn had nearly sent"
164 "it into the sea. But Conseil, not wishing to let the fish go, rushed to"
164 "it, and, before I could prevent him, had seized it with both hands. In"
164 "a moment he was overthrown, his legs in the air, and half his body"
164 "paralysed, crying--"
164 ""
164 "\"Oh! master, master! help me!\""
164 ""
164 "It was the first time the poor boy had spoken to me so familiarly.  The"
164 "Canadian and I took him up, and rubbed his contracted arms till he"
164 "became sensible. The unfortunate Conseil had attacked a cramp-fish of"
164 "the most dangerous kind, the cumana. This odd animal, in a medium"
164 "conductor like water, strikes fish at several yards' distance, so great"
164 "is the power of its electric organ, the two principal surfaces of which"
164 "do not measure less than twenty-seven square feet. The next day, April"
164 "12th, the Nautilus approached the Dutch coast, near the mouth of the"
164 "Maroni. There several groups of sea-cows herded together; they were"
164 "manatees, that, like the dugong and the stellera, belong to the skenian"
164 "order. These beautiful animals, peaceable and inoffensive, from"
164 "eighteen to twenty-one feet in length, weigh at least sixteen"
164 "hundredweight. I told Ned Land and Conseil that provident nature had"
164 "assigned an important role to these mammalia. Indeed, they, like the"
164 "seals, are designed to graze on the submarine prairies, and thus"
164 "destroy the accumulation of weed that obstructs the tropical rivers."
164 ""
164 "\"And do you know,\" I added, \"what has been the result since men have"
164 "almost entirely annihilated this useful race? That the putrefied weeds"
164 "have poisoned the air, and the poisoned air causes the yellow fever,"
164 "that desolates these beautiful countries. Enormous vegetations are"
164 "multiplied under the torrid seas, and the evil is irresistibly"
164 "developed from the mouth of the Rio de la Plata to Florida. If we are"
164 "to believe Toussenel, this plague is nothing to what it would be if the"
164 "seas were cleaned of whales and seals. Then, infested with poulps,"
164 "medusae, and cuttle-fish, they would become immense centres of"
164 "infection, since their waves would not possess 'these vast stomachs"
164 "that God had charged to infest the surface of the seas.'\""
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XVIII"
164 ""
164 "THE POULPS"
164 ""
164 "For several days the Nautilus kept off from the American coast."
164 "Evidently it did not wish to risk the tides of the Gulf of Mexico or of"
164 "the sea of the Antilles.  April 16th, we sighted Martinique and"
164 "Guadaloupe from a distance of about thirty miles.  I saw their tall"
164 "peaks for an instant.  The Canadian, who counted on carrying out his"
164 "projects in the Gulf, by either landing or hailing one of the numerous"
164 "boats that coast from one island to another, was quite disheartened."
164 "Flight would have been quite practicable, if Ned Land had been able to"
164 "take possession of the boat without the Captain's knowledge.  But in"
164 "the open sea it could not be thought of.  The Canadian, Conseil, and I"
164 "had a long conversation on this subject.  For six months we had been"
164 "prisoners on board the Nautilus.  We had travelled 17,000 leagues; and,"
164 "as Ned Land said, there was no reason why it should come to an end.  We"
164 "could hope nothing from the Captain of the Nautilus, but only from"
164 "ourselves.  Besides, for some time past he had become graver, more"
164 "retired, less sociable.  He seemed to shun me.  I met him rarely."
164 "Formerly he was pleased to explain the submarine marvels to me; now he"
164 "left me to my studies, and came no more to the saloon.  What change had"
164 "come over him?  For what cause?  For my part, I did not wish to bury"
164 "with me my curious and novel studies.  I had now the power to write the"
164 "true book of the sea; and this book, sooner or later, I wished to see"
164 "daylight.  The land nearest us was the archipelago of the Bahamas."
164 "There rose high submarine cliffs covered with large weeds.  It was"
164 "about eleven o'clock when Ned Land drew my attention to a formidable"
164 "pricking, like the sting of an ant, which was produced by means of"
164 "large seaweeds."
164 ""
164 "\"Well,\" I said, \"these are proper caverns for poulps, and I should not"
164 "be astonished to see some of these monsters.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What!\" said Conseil; \"cuttlefish, real cuttlefish of the cephalopod"
164 "class?\""
164 ""
164 "\"No,\" I said, \"poulps of huge dimensions.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I will never believe that such animals exist,\" said Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"Well,\" said Conseil, with the most serious air in the world, \"I"
164 "remember perfectly to have seen a large vessel drawn under the waves by"
164 "an octopus's arm.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You saw that?\" said the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Ned.\""
164 ""
164 "\"With your own eyes?\""
164 ""
164 "\"With my own eyes.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Where, pray, might that be?\""
164 ""
164 "\"At St. Malo,\" answered Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"In the port?\" said Ned, ironically."
164 ""
164 "\"No; in a church,\" replied Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"In a church!\" cried the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; friend Ned.  In a picture representing the poulp in question.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Good!\" said Ned Land, bursting out laughing."
164 ""
164 "\"He is quite right,\" I said.  \"I have heard of this picture; but the"
164 "subject represented is taken from a legend, and you know what to think"
164 "of legends in the matter of natural history.  Besides, when it is a"
164 "question of monsters, the imagination is apt to run wild.  Not only is"
164 "it supposed that these poulps can draw down vessels, but a certain"
164 "Olaus Magnus speaks of an octopus a mile long that is more like an"
164 "island than an animal.  It is also said that the Bishop of Nidros was"
164 "building an altar on an immense rock.  Mass finished, the rock began to"
164 "walk, and returned to the sea.  The rock was a poulp.  Another Bishop,"
164 "Pontoppidan, speaks also of a poulp on which a regiment of cavalry"
164 "could manoeuvre.  Lastly, the ancient naturalists speak of monsters"
164 "whose mouths were like gulfs, and which were too large to pass through"
164 "the Straits of Gibraltar.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But how much is true of these stories?\" asked Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"Nothing, my friends; at least of that which passes the limit of truth"
164 "to get to fable or legend.  Nevertheless, there must be some ground for"
164 "the imagination of the story-tellers. One cannot deny that poulps and"
164 "cuttlefish exist of a large species, inferior, however, to the"
164 "cetaceans.  Aristotle has stated the dimensions of a cuttlefish as five"
164 "cubits, or nine feet two inches.  Our fishermen frequently see some"
164 "that are more than four feet long.  Some skeletons of poulps are"
164 "preserved in the museums of Trieste and Montpelier, that measure two"
164 "yards in length.  Besides, according to the calculations of some"
164 "naturalists, one of these animals only six feet long would have"
164 "tentacles twenty-seven feet long.  That would suffice to make a"
164 "formidable monster.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Do they fish for them in these days?\" asked Ned."
164 ""
164 "\"If they do not fish for them, sailors see them at least.  One of my"
164 "friends, Captain Paul Bos of Havre, has often affirmed that he met one"
164 "of these monsters of colossal dimensions in the Indian seas.  But the"
164 "most astonishing fact, and which does not permit of the denial of the"
164 "existence of these gigantic animals, happened some years ago, in 1861.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What is the fact?\" asked Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "\"This is it.  In 1861, to the north-east of Teneriffe, very nearly in"
164 "the same latitude we are in now, the crew of the despatch-boat Alector"
164 "perceived a monstrous cuttlefish swimming in the waters.  Captain"
164 "Bouguer went near to the animal, and attacked it with harpoon and guns,"
164 "without much success, for balls and harpoons glided over the soft"
164 "flesh.  After several fruitless attempts the crew tried to pass a"
164 "slip-knot round the body of the mollusc.  The noose slipped as far as"
164 "the tail fins and there stopped.  They tried then to haul it on board,"
164 "but its weight was so considerable that the tightness of the cord"
164 "separated the tail from the body, and, deprived of this ornament, he"
164 "disappeared under the water.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Indeed! is that a fact?\""
164 ""
164 "\"An indisputable fact, my good Ned.  They proposed to name this poulp"
164 "`Bouguer's cuttlefish.'\""
164 ""
164 "\"What length was it?\" asked the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "\"Did it not measure about six yards?\" said Conseil, who, posted at the"
164 "window, was examining again the irregular windings of the cliff."
164 ""
164 "\"Precisely,\" I replied."
164 ""
164 "\"Its head,\" rejoined Conseil, \"was it not crowned with eight tentacles,"
164 "that beat the water like a nest of serpents?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Precisely.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Had not its eyes, placed at the back of its head, considerable"
164 "development?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, Conseil.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And was not its mouth like a parrot's beak?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Exactly, Conseil.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Very well! no offence to master,\" he replied, quietly; \"if this is not"
164 "Bouguer's cuttlefish, it is, at least, one of its brothers.\""
164 ""
164 "I looked at Conseil.  Ned Land hurried to the window."
164 ""
164 "\"What a horrible beast!\" he cried."
164 ""
164 "I looked in my turn, and could not repress a gesture of disgust."
164 "Before my eyes was a horrible monster worthy to figure in the legends"
164 "of the marvellous.  It was an immense cuttlefish, being eight yards"
164 "long.  It swam crossways in the direction of the Nautilus with great"
164 "speed, watching us with its enormous staring green eyes.  Its eight"
164 "arms, or rather feet, fixed to its head, that have given the name of"
164 "cephalopod to these animals, were twice as long as its body, and were"
164 "twisted like the furies' hair.  One could see the 250 air holes on the"
164 "inner side of the tentacles.  The monster's mouth, a horned beak like a"
164 "parrot's, opened and shut vertically.  Its tongue, a horned substance,"
164 "furnished with several rows of pointed teeth, came out quivering from"
164 "this veritable pair of shears.  What a freak of nature, a bird's beak"
164 "on a mollusc!  Its spindle-like body formed a fleshy mass that might"
164 "weigh 4,000 to 5,000 lb.; the, varying colour changing with great"
164 "rapidity, according to the irritation of the animal, passed"
164 "successively from livid grey to reddish brown.  What irritated this"
164 "mollusc?  No doubt the presence of the Nautilus, more formidable than"
164 "itself, and on which its suckers or its jaws had no hold.  Yet, what"
164 "monsters these poulps are! what vitality the Creator has given them!"
164 "what vigour in their movements! and they possess three hearts!  Chance"
164 "had brought us in presence of this cuttlefish, and I did not wish to"
164 "lose the opportunity of carefully studying this specimen of"
164 "cephalopods.  I overcame the horror that inspired me, and, taking a"
164 "pencil, began to draw it."
164 ""
164 "\"Perhaps this is the same which the Alector saw,\" said Conseil."
164 ""
164 "\"No,\" replied the Canadian; \"for this is whole, and the other had lost"
164 "its tail.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is no reason,\" I replied.  \"The arms and tails of these animals"
164 "are re-formed by renewal; and in seven years the tail of Bouguer's"
164 "cuttlefish has no doubt had time to grow.\""
164 ""
164 "By this time other poulps appeared at the port light.  I counted seven."
164 "They formed a procession after the Nautilus, and I heard their beaks"
164 "gnashing against the iron hull.  I continued my work.  These monsters"
164 "kept in the water with such precision that they seemed immovable."
164 "Suddenly the Nautilus stopped.  A shock made it tremble in every plate."
164 ""
164 "\"Have we struck anything?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"In any case,\" replied the Canadian, \"we shall be free, for we are"
164 "floating.\""
164 ""
164 "The Nautilus was floating, no doubt, but it did not move.  A minute"
164 "passed.  Captain Nemo, followed by his lieutenant, entered the"
164 "drawing-room. I had not seen him for some time.  He seemed dull."
164 "Without noticing or speaking to us, he went to the panel, looked at the"
164 "poulps, and said something to his lieutenant.  The latter went out."
164 "Soon the panels were shut.  The ceiling was lighted.  I went towards"
164 "the Captain."
164 ""
164 "\"A curious collection of poulps?\"  I said."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, indeed, Mr. Naturalist,\" he replied; \"and we are going to fight"
164 "them, man to beast.\""
164 ""
164 "I looked at him.  I thought I had not heard aright."
164 ""
164 "\"Man to beast?\"  I repeated."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.  The screw is stopped.  I think that the horny jaws of one"
164 "of the cuttlefish is entangled in the blades.  That is what prevents"
164 "our moving.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What are you going to do?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Rise to the surface, and slaughter this vermin.\""
164 ""
164 "\"A difficult enterprise.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, indeed.  The electric bullets are powerless against the soft"
164 "flesh, where they do not find resistance enough to go off.  But we"
164 "shall attack them with the hatchet.\""
164 ""
164 "\"And the harpoon, sir,\" said the Canadian, \"if you do not refuse my"
164 "help.\""
164 ""
164 "\"I will accept it, Master Land.\""
164 ""
164 "\"We will follow you,\" I said, and, following Captain Nemo, we went"
164 "towards the central staircase."
164 ""
164 "There, about ten men with boarding-hatchets were ready for the attack."
164 "Conseil and I took two hatchets; Ned Land seized a harpoon.  The"
164 "Nautilus had then risen to the surface.  One of the sailors, posted on"
164 "the top ladderstep, unscrewed the bolts of the panels.  But hardly were"
164 "the screws loosed, when the panel rose with great violence, evidently"
164 "drawn by the suckers of a poulp's arm.  Immediately one of these arms"
164 "slid like a serpent down the opening and twenty others were above."
164 "With one blow of the axe, Captain Nemo cut this formidable tentacle,"
164 "that slid wriggling down the ladder.  Just as we were pressing one on"
164 "the other to reach the platform, two other arms, lashing the air, came"
164 "down on the seaman placed before Captain Nemo, and lifted him up with"
164 "irresistible power.  Captain Nemo uttered a cry, and rushed out.  We"
164 "hurried after him."
164 ""
164 "What a scene!  The unhappy man, seized by the tentacle and fixed to the"
164 "suckers, was balanced in the air at the caprice of this enormous trunk."
164 "He rattled in his throat, he was stifled, he cried, \"Help! help!\""
164 "These words, spoken in French, startled me!  I had a fellow-countryman"
164 "on board, perhaps several!  That heart-rending cry!  I shall hear it"
164 "all my life.  The unfortunate man was lost.  Who could rescue him from"
164 "that powerful pressure?  However, Captain Nemo had rushed to the poulp,"
164 "and with one blow of the axe had cut through one arm.  His lieutenant"
164 "struggled furiously against other monsters that crept on the flanks of"
164 "the Nautilus.  The crew fought with their axes.  The Canadian, Conseil,"
164 "and I buried our weapons in the fleshy masses; a strong smell of musk"
164 "penetrated the atmosphere.  It was horrible!"
164 ""
164 "For one instant, I thought the unhappy man, entangled with the poulp,"
164 "would be torn from its powerful suction.  Seven of the eight arms had"
164 "been cut off.  One only wriggled in the air, brandishing the victim"
164 "like a feather.  But just as Captain Nemo and his lieutenant threw"
164 "themselves on it, the animal ejected a stream of black liquid.  We were"
164 "blinded with it.  When the cloud dispersed, the cuttlefish had"
164 "disappeared, and my unfortunate countryman with it.  Ten or twelve"
164 "poulps now invaded the platform and sides of the Nautilus.  We rolled"
164 "pell-mell into the midst of this nest of serpents, that wriggled on the"
164 "platform in the waves of blood and ink.  It seemed as though these"
164 "slimy tentacles sprang up like the hydra's heads.  Ned Land's harpoon,"
164 "at each stroke, was plunged into the staring eyes of the cuttle fish."
164 "But my bold companion was suddenly overturned by the tentacles of a"
164 "monster he had not been able to avoid."
164 ""
164 "Ah! how my heart beat with emotion and horror!  The formidable beak of"
164 "a cuttlefish was open over Ned Land.  The unhappy man would be cut in"
164 "two.  I rushed to his succour.  But Captain Nemo was before me; his axe"
164 "disappeared between the two enormous jaws, and, miraculously saved, the"
164 "Canadian, rising, plunged his harpoon deep into the triple heart of the"
164 "poulp."
164 ""
164 "\"I owed myself this revenge!\" said the Captain to the Canadian."
164 ""
164 "Ned bowed without replying.  The combat had lasted a quarter of an"
164 "hour.  The monsters, vanquished and mutilated, left us at last, and"
164 "disappeared under the waves.  Captain Nemo, covered with blood, nearly"
164 "exhausted, gazed upon the sea that had swallowed up one of his"
164 "companions, and great tears gathered in his eyes."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XIX"
164 ""
164 "THE GULF STREAM"
164 ""
164 "This terrible scene of the 20th of April none of us can ever forget.  I"
164 "have written it under the influence of violent emotion.  Since then I"
164 "have revised the recital; I have read it to Conseil and to the"
164 "Canadian.  They found it exact as to facts, but insufficient as to"
164 "effect.  To paint such pictures, one must have the pen of the most"
164 "illustrious of our poets, the author of The Toilers of the Deep."
164 ""
164 "I have said that Captain Nemo wept while watching the waves; his grief"
164 "was great.  It was the second companion he had lost since our arrival"
164 "on board, and what a death!  That friend, crushed, stifled, bruised by"
164 "the dreadful arms of a poulp, pounded by his iron jaws, would not rest"
164 "with his comrades in the peaceful coral cemetery!  In the midst of the"
164 "struggle, it was the despairing cry uttered by the unfortunate man that"
164 "had torn my heart.  The poor Frenchman, forgetting his conventional"
164 "language, had taken to his own mother tongue, to utter a last appeal!"
164 "Amongst the crew of the Nautilus, associated with the body and soul of"
164 "the Captain, recoiling like him from all contact with men, I had a"
164 "fellow-countryman. Did he alone represent France in this mysterious"
164 "association, evidently composed of individuals of divers nationalities?"
164 "It was one of these insoluble problems that rose up unceasingly before"
164 "my mind!"
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo entered his room, and I saw him no more for some time."
164 "But that he was sad and irresolute I could see by the vessel, of which"
164 "he was the soul, and which received all his impressions.  The Nautilus"
164 "did not keep on in its settled course; it floated about like a corpse"
164 "at the will of the waves.  It went at random.  He could not tear"
164 "himself away from the scene of the last struggle, from this sea that"
164 "had devoured one of his men.  Ten days passed thus.  It was not till"
164 "the 1st of May that the Nautilus resumed its northerly course, after"
164 "having sighted the Bahamas at the mouth of the Bahama Canal.  We were"
164 "then following the current from the largest river to the sea, that has"
164 "its banks, its fish, and its proper temperatures.  I mean the Gulf"
164 "Stream.  It is really a river, that flows freely to the middle of the"
164 "Atlantic, and whose waters do not mix with the ocean waters.  It is a"
164 "salt river, salter than the surrounding sea.  Its mean depth is 1,500"
164 "fathoms, its mean breadth ten miles.  In certain places the current"
164 "flows with the speed of two miles and a half an hour.  The body of its"
164 "waters is more considerable than that of all the rivers in the globe."
164 "It was on this ocean river that the Nautilus then sailed."
164 ""
164 "I must add that, during the night, the phosphorescent waters of the"
164 "Gulf Stream rivalled the electric power of our watch-light, especially"
164 "in the stormy weather that threatened us so frequently.  May 8th, we"
164 "were still crossing Cape Hatteras, at the height of the North Caroline."
164 "The width of the Gulf Stream there is seventy-five miles, and its depth"
164 "210 yards.  The Nautilus still went at random; all supervision seemed"
164 "abandoned.  I thought that, under these circumstances, escape would be"
164 "possible.  Indeed, the inhabited shores offered anywhere an easy"
164 "refuge.  The sea was incessantly ploughed by the steamers that ply"
164 "between New York or Boston and the Gulf of Mexico, and overrun day and"
164 "night by the little schooners coasting about the several parts of the"
164 "American coast.  We could hope to be picked up.  It was a favourable"
164 "opportunity, notwithstanding the thirty miles that separated the"
164 "Nautilus from the coasts of the Union.  One unfortunate circumstance"
164 "thwarted the Canadian's plans.  The weather was very bad.  We were"
164 "nearing those shores where tempests are so frequent, that country of"
164 "waterspouts and cyclones actually engendered by the current of the Gulf"
164 "Stream.  To tempt the sea in a frail boat was certain destruction.  Ned"
164 "Land owned this himself.  He fretted, seized with nostalgia that flight"
164 "only could cure."
164 ""
164 "\"Master,\" he said that day to me, \"this must come to an end.  I must"
164 "make a clean breast of it.  This Nemo is leaving land and going up to"
164 "the north.  But I declare to you that I have had enough of the South"
164 "Pole, and I will not follow him to the North.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What is to be done, Ned, since flight is impracticable just now?\""
164 ""
164 "\"We must speak to the Captain,\" said he; \"you said nothing when we were"
164 "in your native seas.  I will speak, now we are in mine.  When I think"
164 "that before long the Nautilus will be by Nova Scotia, and that there"
164 "near New foundland is a large bay, and into that bay the St. Lawrence"
164 "empties itself, and that the St. Lawrence is my river, the river by"
164 "Quebec, my native town--when I think of this, I feel furious, it makes"
164 "my hair stand on end.  Sir, I would rather throw myself into the sea!"
164 "I will not stay here!  I am stifled!\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian was evidently losing all patience.  His vigorous nature"
164 "could not stand this prolonged imprisonment.  His face altered daily;"
164 "his temper became more surly.  I knew what he must suffer, for I was"
164 "seized with home-sickness myself.  Nearly seven months had passed"
164 "without our having had any news from land; Captain Nemo's isolation,"
164 "his altered spirits, especially since the fight with the poulps, his"
164 "taciturnity, all made me view things in a different light."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, sir?\" said Ned, seeing I did not reply."
164 ""
164 "\"Well, Ned, do you wish me to ask Captain Nemo his intentions"
164 "concerning us?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Although he has already made them known?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Yes; I wish it settled finally.  Speak for me, in my name only, if you"
164 "like.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But I so seldom meet him.  He avoids me.\""
164 ""
164 "\"That is all the more reason for you to go to see him.\""
164 ""
164 "I went to my room.  From thence I meant to go to Captain Nemo's.  It"
164 "would not do to let this opportunity of meeting him slip.  I knocked at"
164 "the door.  No answer.  I knocked again, then turned the handle.  The"
164 "door opened, I went in.  The Captain was there.  Bending over his"
164 "work-table, he had not heard me.  Resolved not to go without having"
164 "spoken, I approached him.  He raised his head quickly, frowned, and"
164 "said roughly, \"You here!  What do you want?\""
164 ""
164 "\"To speak to you, Captain.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But I am busy, sir; I am working.  I leave you at liberty to shut"
164 "yourself up; cannot I be allowed the same?\""
164 ""
164 "This reception was not encouraging; but I was determined to hear and"
164 "answer everything."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" I said coldly, \"I have to speak to you on a matter that admits"
164 "of no delay.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What is that, sir?\" he replied, ironically.  \"Have you discovered"
164 "something that has escaped me, or has the sea delivered up any new"
164 "secrets?\""
164 ""
164 "We were at cross-purposes. But, before I could reply, he showed me an"
164 "open manuscript on his table, and said, in a more serious tone, \"Here,"
164 "M. Aronnax, is a manuscript written in several languages.  It contains"
164 "the sum of my studies of the sea; and, if it please God, it shall not"
164 "perish with me.  This manuscript, signed with my name, complete with"
164 "the history of my life, will be shut up in a little floating case.  The"
164 "last survivor of all of us on board the Nautilus will throw this case"
164 "into the sea, and it will go whither it is borne by the waves.\""
164 ""
164 "This man's name! his history written by himself!  His mystery would"
164 "then be revealed some day."
164 ""
164 "\"Captain,\" I said, \"I can but approve of the idea that makes you act"
164 "thus.  The result of your studies must not be lost.  But the means you"
164 "employ seem to me to be primitive.  Who knows where the winds will"
164 "carry this case, and in whose hands it will fall?  Could you not use"
164 "some other means?  Could not you, or one of yours----\""
164 ""
164 "\"Never, sir!\" he said, hastily interrupting me."
164 ""
164 "\"But I and my companions are ready to keep this manuscript in store;"
164 "and, if you will put us at liberty----\""
164 ""
164 "\"At liberty?\" said the Captain, rising."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir; that is the subject on which I wish to question you.  For"
164 "seven months we have been here on board, and I ask you to-day, in the"
164 "name of my companions and in my own, if your intention is to keep us"
164 "here always?\""
164 ""
164 "\"M. Aronnax, I will answer you to-day as I did seven months ago:"
164 "Whoever enters the Nautilus, must never quit it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You impose actual slavery upon us!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Give it what name you please.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But everywhere the slave has the right to regain his liberty.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Who denies you this right?  Have I ever tried to chain you with an"
164 "oath?\""
164 ""
164 "He looked at me with his arms crossed."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" I said, \"to return a second time to this subject will be neither"
164 "to your nor to my taste; but, as we have entered upon it, let us go"
164 "through with it.  I repeat, it is not only myself whom it concerns."
164 "Study is to me a relief, a diversion, a passion that could make me"
164 "forget everything.  Like you, I am willing to live obscure, in the"
164 "frail hope of bequeathing one day, to future time, the result of my"
164 "labours.  But it is otherwise with Ned Land.  Every man, worthy of the"
164 "name, deserves some consideration.  Have you thought that love of"
164 "liberty, hatred of slavery, can give rise to schemes of revenge in a"
164 "nature like the Canadian's; that he could think, attempt, and try----\""
164 ""
164 "I was silenced; Captain Nemo rose."
164 ""
164 "\"Whatever Ned Land thinks of, attempts, or tries, what does it matter"
164 "to me?  I did not seek him!  It is not for my pleasure that I keep him"
164 "on board!  As for you, M. Aronnax, you are one of those who can"
164 "understand everything, even silence.  I have nothing more to say to"
164 "you.  Let this first time you have come to treat of this subject be the"
164 "last, for a second time I will not listen to you.\""
164 ""
164 "I retired.  Our situation was critical.  I related my conversation to"
164 "my two companions."
164 ""
164 "\"We know now,\" said Ned, \"that we can expect nothing from this man."
164 "The Nautilus is nearing Long Island.  We will escape, whatever the"
164 "weather may be.\""
164 ""
164 "But the sky became more and more threatening.  Symptoms of a hurricane"
164 "became manifest.  The atmosphere was becoming white and misty.  On the"
164 "horizon fine streaks of cirrhous clouds were succeeded by masses of"
164 "cumuli.  Other low clouds passed swiftly by.  The swollen sea rose in"
164 "huge billows.  The birds disappeared with the exception of the petrels,"
164 "those friends of the storm.  The barometer fell sensibly, and indicated"
164 "an extreme extension of the vapours.  The mixture of the storm glass"
164 "was decomposed under the influence of the electricity that pervaded the"
164 "atmosphere.  The tempest burst on the 18th of May, just as the Nautilus"
164 "was floating off Long Island, some miles from the port of New York.  I"
164 "can describe this strife of the elements! for, instead of fleeing to"
164 "the depths of the sea, Captain Nemo, by an unaccountable caprice, would"
164 "brave it at the surface.  The wind blew from the south-west at first."
164 "Captain Nemo, during the squalls, had taken his place on the platform."
164 "He had made himself fast, to prevent being washed overboard by the"
164 "monstrous waves.  I had hoisted myself up, and made myself fast also,"
164 "dividing my admiration between the tempest and this extraordinary man"
164 "who was coping with it.  The raging sea was swept by huge cloud-drifts,"
164 "which were actually saturated with the waves.  The Nautilus, sometimes"
164 "lying on its side, sometimes standing up like a mast, rolled and"
164 "pitched terribly.  About five o'clock a torrent of rain fell, that"
164 "lulled neither sea nor wind.  The hurri cane blew nearly forty leagues"
164 "an hour.  It is under these conditions that it overturns houses, breaks"
164 "iron gates, displaces twenty-four pounders.  However, the Nautilus, in"
164 "the midst of the tempest, confirmed the words of a clever engineer,"
164 "\"There is no well-constructed hull that cannot defy the sea.\" This was"
164 "not a resisting rock; it was a steel spindle, obedient and movable,"
164 "without rigging or masts, that braved its fury with impunity.  However,"
164 "I watched these raging waves attentively.  They measured fifteen feet"
164 "in height, and 150 to 175 yards long, and their speed of propagation"
164 "was thirty feet per second.  Their bulk and power increased with the"
164 "depth of the water.  Such waves as these, at the Hebrides, have"
164 "displaced a mass weighing 8,400 lb.  They are they which, in the"
164 "tempest of December 23rd, 1864, after destroying the town of Yeddo, in"
164 "Japan, broke the same day on the shores of America.  The intensity of"
164 "the tempest increased with the night.  The barometer, as in 1860 at"
164 "Reunion during a cyclone, fell seven-tenths at the close of day.  I saw"
164 "a large vessel pass the horizon struggling painfully.  She was trying"
164 "to lie to under half steam, to keep up above the waves.  It was"
164 "probably one of the steamers of the line from New York to Liverpool, or"
164 "Havre.  It soon disappeared in the gloom.  At ten o'clock in the"
164 "evening the sky was on fire.  The atmosphere was streaked with vivid"
164 "lightning.  I could not bear the brightness of it; while the captain,"
164 "looking at it, seemed to envy the spirit of the tempest.  A terrible"
164 "noise filled the air, a complex noise, made up of the howls of the"
164 "crushed waves, the roaring of the wind, and the claps of thunder.  The"
164 "wind veered suddenly to all points of the horizon; and the cyclone,"
164 "rising in the east, returned after passing by the north, west, and"
164 "south, in the inverse course pursued by the circular storm of the"
164 "southern hemisphere.  Ah, that Gulf Stream!  It deserves its name of"
164 "the King of Tempests.  It is that which causes those formidable"
164 "cyclones, by the difference of temperature between its air and its"
164 "currents.  A shower of fire had succeeded the rain.  The drops of water"
164 "were changed to sharp spikes.  One would have thought that Captain Nemo"
164 "was courting a death worthy of himself, a death by lightning.  As the"
164 "Nautilus, pitching dreadfully, raised its steel spur in the air, it"
164 "seemed to act as a conductor, and I saw long sparks burst from it."
164 "Crushed and without strength I crawled to the panel, opened it, and"
164 "descended to the saloon.  The storm was then at its height.  It was"
164 "impossible to stand upright in the interior of the Nautilus.  Captain"
164 "Nemo came down about twelve.  I heard the reservoirs filling by"
164 "degrees, and the Nautilus sank slowly beneath the waves.  Through the"
164 "open windows in the saloon I saw large fish terrified, passing like"
164 "phantoms in the water.  Some were struck before my eyes.  The Nautilus"
164 "was still descending.  I thought that at about eight fathoms deep we"
164 "should find a calm.  But no! the upper beds were too violently agitated"
164 "for that.  We had to seek repose at more than twenty-five fathoms in"
164 "the bowels of the deep.  But there, what quiet, what silence, what"
164 "peace!  Who could have told that such a hurricane had been let loose on"
164 "the surface of that ocean?"
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XX"
164 ""
164 "FROM LATITUDE 47 deg. 24' TO LONGITUDE 17 deg. 28'"
164 ""
164 "In consequence of the storm, we had been thrown eastward once more."
164 "All hope of escape on the shores of New York or St. Lawrence had faded"
164 "away; and poor Ned, in despair, had isolated himself like Captain Nemo."
164 "Conseil and I, however, never left each other.  I said that the"
164 "Nautilus had gone aside to the east.  I should have said (to be more"
164 "exact) the north-east. For some days, it wandered first on the surface,"
164 "and then beneath it, amid those fogs so dreaded by sailors.  What"
164 "accidents are due to these thick fogs!  What shocks upon these reefs"
164 "when the wind drowns the breaking of the waves!  What collisions"
164 "between vessels, in spite of their warning lights, whistles, and alarm"
164 "bells!  And the bottoms of these seas look like a field of battle,"
164 "where still lie all the conquered of the ocean; some old and already"
164 "encrusted, others fresh and reflecting from their iron bands and copper"
164 "plates the brilliancy of our lantern."
164 ""
164 "On the 15th of May we were at the extreme south of the Bank of"
164 "Newfoundland.  This bank consists of alluvia, or large heaps of organic"
164 "matter, brought either from the Equator by the Gulf Stream, or from the"
164 "North Pole by the counter-current of cold water which skirts the"
164 "American coast.  There also are heaped up those erratic blocks which"
164 "are carried along by the broken ice; and close by, a vast charnel-house"
164 "of molluscs, which perish here by millions.  The depth of the sea is"
164 "not great at Newfoundland--not more than some hundreds of fathoms; but"
164 "towards the south is a depression of 1,500 fathoms.  There the Gulf"
164 "Stream widens.  It loses some of its speed and some of its temperature,"
164 "but it becomes a sea."
164 ""
164 "It was on the 17th of May, about 500 miles from Heart's Content, at a"
164 "depth of more than 1,400 fathoms, that I saw the electric cable lying"
164 "on the bottom.  Conseil, to whom I had not mentioned it, thought at"
164 "first that it was a gigantic sea-serpent. But I undeceived the worthy"
164 "fellow, and by way of consolation related several particulars in the"
164 "laying of this cable.  The first one was laid in the years 1857 and"
164 "1858; but, after transmitting about 400 telegrams, would not act any"
164 "longer.  In 1863 the engineers constructed an other one, measuring"
164 "2,000 miles in length, and weighing 4,500 tons, which was embarked on"
164 "the Great Eastern.  This attempt also failed."
164 ""
164 "On the 25th of May the Nautilus, being at a depth of more than 1,918"
164 "fathoms, was on the precise spot where the rupture occurred which"
164 "ruined the enterprise.  It was within 638 miles of the coast of"
164 "Ireland; and at half-past two in the afternoon they discovered that"
164 "communication with Europe had ceased.  The electricians on board"
164 "resolved to cut the cable before fishing it up, and at eleven o'clock"
164 "at night they had recovered the damaged part.  They made another point"
164 "and spliced it, and it was once more submerged.  But some days after it"
164 "broke again, and in the depths of the ocean could not be recaptured."
164 "The Americans, however, were not discouraged.  Cyrus Field, the bold"
164 "promoter of the enterprise, as he had sunk all his own fortune, set a"
164 "new subscription on foot, which was at once answered, and another cable"
164 "was constructed on better principles.  The bundles of conducting wires"
164 "were each enveloped in gutta-percha, and protected by a wadding of"
164 "hemp, contained in a metallic covering.  The Great Eastern sailed on"
164 "the 13th of July, 1866.  The operation worked well.  But one incident"
164 "occurred.  Several times in unrolling the cable they observed that"
164 "nails had recently been forced into it, evidently with the motive of"
164 "destroying it.  Captain Anderson, the officers, and engineers consulted"
164 "together, and had it posted up that, if the offender was surprised on"
164 "board, he would be thrown without further trial into the sea.  From"
164 "that time the criminal attempt was never repeated."
164 ""
164 "On the 23rd of July the Great Eastern was not more than 500 miles from"
164 "Newfoundland, when they telegraphed from Ireland the news of the"
164 "armistice concluded between Prussia and Austria after Sadowa.  On the"
164 "27th, in the midst of heavy fogs, they reached the port of Heart's"
164 "Content.  The enterprise was successfully terminated; and for its first"
164 "despatch, young America addressed old Europe in these words of wisdom,"
164 "so rarely understood:  \"Glory to God in the highest, and on earth"
164 "peace, goodwill towards men.\""
164 ""
164 "I did not expect to find the electric cable in its primitive state,"
164 "such as it was on leaving the manufactory.  The long serpent, covered"
164 "with the remains of shells, bristling with foraminiferae, was encrusted"
164 "with a strong coating which served as a protection against all boring"
164 "molluscs.  It lay quietly sheltered from the motions of the sea, and"
164 "under a favourable pressure for the transmission of the electric spark"
164 "which passes from Europe to America in .32 of a second.  Doubtless this"
164 "cable will last for a great length of time, for they find that the"
164 "gutta-percha covering is improved by the sea-water. Besides, on this"
164 "level, so well chosen, the cable is never so deeply submerged as to"
164 "cause it to break.  The Nautilus followed it to the lowest depth, which"
164 "was more than 2,212 fathoms, and there it lay without any anchorage;"
164 "and then we reached the spot where the accident had taken place in"
164 "1863.  The bottom of the ocean then formed a valley about 100 miles"
164 "broad, in which Mont Blanc might have been placed without its summit"
164 "appearing above the waves.  This valley is closed at the east by a"
164 "perpendicular wall more than 2,000 yards high.  We arrived there on the"
164 "28th of May, and the Nautilus was then not more than 120 miles from"
164 "Ireland."
164 ""
164 "Was Captain Nemo going to land on the British Isles?  No. To my great"
164 "surprise he made for the south, once more coming back towards European"
164 "seas.  In rounding the Emerald Isle, for one instant I caught sight of"
164 "Cape Clear, and the light which guides the thousands of vessels leaving"
164 "Glasgow or Liverpool.  An important question then arose in my mind."
164 "Did the Nautilus dare entangle itself in the Manche?  Ned Land, who had"
164 "re-appeared since we had been nearing land, did not cease to question"
164 "me.  How could I answer?  Captain Nemo remained invisible.  After"
164 "having shown the Canadian a glimpse of American shores, was he going to"
164 "show me the coast of France?"
164 ""
164 "But the Nautilus was still going southward.  On the 30th of May, it"
164 "passed in sight of Land's End, between the extreme point of England and"
164 "the Scilly Isles, which were left to starboard.  If we wished to enter"
164 "the Manche, he must go straight to the east.  He did not do so."
164 ""
164 "During the whole of the 31st of May, the Nautilus described a series of"
164 "circles on the water, which greatly interested me.  It seemed to be"
164 "seeking a spot it had some trouble in finding.  At noon, Captain Nemo"
164 "himself came to work the ship's log.  He spoke no word to me, but"
164 "seemed gloomier than ever.  What could sadden him thus?  Was it his"
164 "proxim ity to European shores?  Had he some recollections of his"
164 "abandoned country?  If not, what did he feel?  Remorse or regret?  For"
164 "a long while this thought haunted my mind, and I had a kind of"
164 "presentiment that before long chance would betray the captain's secrets."
164 ""
164 "The next day, the 1st of June, the Nautilus continued the same process."
164 "It was evidently seeking some particular spot in the ocean.  Captain"
164 "Nemo took the sun's altitude as he had done the day before.  The sea"
164 "was beautiful, the sky clear.  About eight miles to the east, a large"
164 "steam vessel could be discerned on the horizon.  No flag fluttered from"
164 "its mast, and I could not discover its nationality.  Some minutes"
164 "before the sun passed the meridian, Captain Nemo took his sextant, and"
164 "watched with great attention.  The perfect rest of the water greatly"
164 "helped the operation.  The Nautilus was motionless; it neither rolled"
164 "nor pitched."
164 ""
164 "I was on the platform when the altitude was taken, and the Captain"
164 "pronounced these words:  \"It is here.\""
164 ""
164 "He turned and went below.  Had he seen the vessel which was changing"
164 "its course and seemed to be nearing us?  I could not tell.  I returned"
164 "to the saloon.  The panels closed, I heard the hissing of the water in"
164 "the reservoirs.  The Nautilus began to sink, following a vertical line,"
164 "for its screw communicated no motion to it.  Some minutes later it"
164 "stopped at a depth of more than 420 fathoms, resting on the ground."
164 "The luminous ceiling was darkened, then the panels were opened, and"
164 "through the glass I saw the sea brilliantly illuminated by the rays of"
164 "our lantern for at least half a mile round us."
164 ""
164 "I looked to the port side, and saw nothing but an immensity of quiet"
164 "waters.  But to starboard, on the bottom appeared a large protuberance,"
164 "which at once attracted my attention.  One would have thought it a ruin"
164 "buried under a coating of white shells, much resembling a covering of"
164 "snow.  Upon examining the mass attentively, I could recognise the"
164 "ever-thickening form of a vessel bare of its masts, which must have"
164 "sunk.  It certainly belonged to past times.  This wreck, to be thus"
164 "encrusted with the lime of the water, must already be able to count"
164 "many years passed at the bottom of the ocean."
164 ""
164 "What was this vessel?  Why did the Nautilus visit its tomb?  Could it"
164 "have been aught but a shipwreck which had drawn it under the water?  I"
164 "knew not what to think, when near me in a slow voice I heard Captain"
164 "Nemo say:"
164 ""
164 "\"At one time this ship was called the Marseillais.  It carried"
164 "seventy-four guns, and was launched in 1762.  In 1778, the 13th of"
164 "August, commanded by La Poype-Ver trieux, it fought boldly against the"
164 "Preston.  In 1779, on the 4th of July, it was at the taking of Grenada,"
164 "with the squadron of Admiral Estaing.  In 1781, on the 5th of"
164 "September, it took part in the battle of Comte de Grasse, in Chesapeake"
164 "Bay.  In 1794, the French Republic changed its name.  On the 16th of"
164 "April, in the same year, it joined the squadron of Villaret Joyeuse, at"
164 "Brest, being entrusted with the escort of a cargo of corn coming from"
164 "America, under the command of Admiral Van Stebel.  On the 11th and 12th"
164 "Prairal of the second year, this squadron fell in with an English"
164 "vessel.  Sir, to-day is the 13th Prairal, the first of June, 1868.  It"
164 "is now seventy-four years ago, day for day on this very spot, in"
164 "latitude 47 deg. 24', longitude 17 deg. 28', that this vessel, after"
164 "fighting heroically, losing its three masts, with the water in its"
164 "hold, and the third of its crew disabled, preferred sinking with its"
164 "356 sailors to surrendering; and, nailing its colours to the poop,"
164 "disappeared under the waves to the cry of `Long live the Republic!'\""
164 ""
164 "\"The Avenger!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"Yes, sir, the Avenger!  A good name!\" muttered Captain Nemo, crossing"
164 "his arms."
164 ""
164 ""
164 ""
164 "CHAPTER XXI"
164 ""
164 "A HECATOMB"
164 ""
164 "The way of describing this unlooked-for scene, the history of the"
164 "patriot ship, told at first so coldly, and the emotion with which this"
164 "strange man pronounced the last words, the name of the Avenger, the"
164 "significance of which could not escape me, all impressed itself deeply"
164 "on my mind.  My eyes did not leave the Captain, who, with his hand"
164 "stretched out to sea, was watching with a glowing eye the glorious"
164 "wreck.  Perhaps I was never to know who he was, from whence he came, or"
164 "where he was going to, but I saw the man move, and apart from the"
164 "_savant_.  It was no common misanthropy which had shut Captain Nemo and"
164 "his companions within the Nautilus, but a hatred, either monstrous or"
164 "sublime, which time could never weaken.  Did this hatred still seek for"
164 "vengeance?  The future would soon teach me that.  But the Nautilus was"
164 "rising slowly to the surface of the sea, and the form of the Avenger"
164 "disappeared by degrees from my sight.  Soon a slight rolling told me"
164 "that we were in the open air.  At that moment a dull boom was heard.  I"
164 "looked at the Captain.  He did not move."
164 ""
164 "\"Captain?\" said I."
164 ""
164 "He did not answer.  I left him and mounted the platform.  Conseil and"
164 "the Canadian were already there."
164 ""
164 "\"Where did that sound come from?\"  I asked."
164 ""
164 "\"It was a gunshot,\" replied Ned Land."
164 ""
164 "I looked in the direction of the vessel I had already seen.  It was"
164 "nearing the Nautilus, and we could see that it was putting on steam."
164 "It was within six miles of us."
164 ""
164 "\"What is that ship, Ned?\""
164 ""
164 "\"By its rigging, and the height of its lower masts,\" said the Canadian,"
164 "\"I bet she is a ship-of-war. May it reach us; and, if necessary, sink"
164 "this cursed Nautilus.\""
164 ""
164 "\"Friend Ned,\" replied Conseil, \"what harm can it do to the Nautilus?"
164 "Can it attack it beneath the waves?  Can its cannonade us at the bottom"
164 "of the sea?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Tell me, Ned,\" said I, \"can you recognise what country she belongs to?\""
164 ""
164 "The Canadian knitted his eyebrows, dropped his eyelids, and screwed up"
164 "the corners of his eyes, and for a few moments fixed a piercing look"
164 "upon the vessel."
164 ""
164 "\"No, sir,\" he replied; \"I cannot tell what nation she belongs to, for"
164 "she shows no colours.  But I can declare she is a man-of-war, for a"
164 "long pennant flutters from her main mast.\""
164 ""
164 "For a quarter of an hour we watched the ship which was steaming towards"
164 "us.  I could not, however, believe that she could see the Nautilus from"
164 "that distance; and still less that she could know what this submarine"
164 "engine was.  Soon the Canadian informed me that she was a large,"
164 "armoured, two-decker ram.  A thick black smoke was pouring from her two"
164 "funnels.  Her closely-furled sails were stopped to her yards.  She"
164 "hoisted no flag at her mizzen-peak. The distance prevented us from"
164 "distinguishing the colours of her pennant, which floated like a thin"
164 "ribbon.  She advanced rapidly.  If Captain Nemo allowed her to"
164 "approach, there was a chance of salvation for us."
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" said Ned Land, \"if that vessel passes within a mile of us I"
164 "shall throw myself into the sea, and I should advise you to do the"
164 "same.\""
164 ""
164 "I did not reply to the Canadian's suggestion, but continued watching"
164 "the ship.  Whether English, French, American, or Russian, she would be"
164 "sure to take us in if we could only reach her.  Presently a white smoke"
164 "burst from the fore part of the vessel; some seconds after, the water,"
164 "agitated by the fall of a heavy body, splashed the stern of the"
164 "Nautilus, and shortly afterwards a loud explosion struck my ear."
164 ""
164 "\"What! they are firing at us!\"  I exclaimed."
164 ""
164 "\"So please you, sir,\" said Ned, \"they have recognised the unicorn, and"
164 "they are firing at us.\""
164 ""
164 "\"But,\" I exclaimed, \"surely they can see that there are men in the"
164 "case?\""
164 ""
164 "\"It is, perhaps, because of that,\" replied Ned Land, looking at me."
164 ""
164 "A whole flood of light burst upon my mind.  Doubtless they knew now how"
164 "to believe the stories of the pretended monster.  No doubt, on board"
164 "the Abraham Lincoln, when the Canadian struck it with the harpoon,"
164 "Commander Farragut had recognised in the supposed narwhal a submarine"
164 "vessel, more dangerous than a supernatural cetacean.  Yes, it must have"
164 "been so; and on every sea they were now seeking this engine of"
164 "destruction.  Terrible indeed! if, as we supposed, Captain Nemo"
164 "employed the Nautilus in works of vengeance.  On the night when we were"
164 "imprisoned in that cell, in the midst of the Indian Ocean, had he not"
164 "attacked some vessel?  The man buried in the coral cemetery, had he not"
164 "been a victim to the shock caused by the Nautilus?  Yes, I repeat it,"
164 "it must be so.  One part of the mysterious existence of Captain Nemo"
164 "had been unveiled; and, if his identity had not been recognised, at"
164 "least, the nations united against him were no longer hunting a"
164 "chimerical creature, but a man who had vowed a deadly hatred against"
164 "them.  All the formidable past rose before me.  Instead of meeting"
164 "friends on board the approaching ship, we could only expect pitiless"
164 "enemies.  But the shot rattled about us.  Some of them struck the sea"
164 "and ricochetted, losing themselves in the distance.  But none touched"
164 "the Nautilus.  The vessel was not more than three miles from us.  In"
164 "spite of the serious cannonade, Captain Nemo did not appear on the"
164 "platform; but, if one of the conical projectiles had struck the shell"
164 "of the Nautilus, it would have been fatal.  The Canadian then said,"
164 "\"Sir, we must do all we can to get out of this dilemma.  Let us signal"
164 "them.  They will then, perhaps, understand that we are honest folks.\""
164 ""
164 "Ned Land took his handkerchief to wave in the air; but he had scarcely"
164 "displayed it, when he was struck down by an iron hand, and fell, in"
164 "spite of his great strength, upon the deck."
164 ""
164 "\"Fool!\" exclaimed the Captain, \"do you wish to be pierced by the spur"
164 "of the Nautilus before it is hurled at this vessel?\""
164 ""
164 "Captain Nemo was terrible to hear; he was still more terrible to see."
164 "His face was deadly pale, with a spasm at his heart.  For an instant it"
164 "must have ceased to beat.  His pupils were fearfully contracted.  He"
164 "did not speak, he roared, as, with his body thrown forward, he wrung"
164 "the Canadian's shoulders.  Then, leaving him, and turning to the ship"
164 "of war, whose shot was still raining around him, he exclaimed, with a"
164 "powerful voice, \"Ah, ship of an accursed nation, you know who I am!  I"
164 "do not want your colours to know you by!  Look! and I will show you"
164 "mine!\""
164 ""
164 "And on the fore part of the platform Captain Nemo unfurled a black"
164 "flag, similar to the one he had placed at the South Pole.  At that"
164 "moment a shot struck the shell of the Nautilus obliquely, without"
164 "piercing it; and, rebounding near the Captain, was lost in the sea.  He"
164 "shrugged his shoulders; and, addressing me, said shortly, \"Go down, you"
164 "and your companions, go down!\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir,\" I cried, \"are you going to attack this vessel?\""
164 ""
164 "\"Sir, I am going to sink it.\""
164 ""
164 "\"You will not do that?\""
164 ""
164 "\"I shall do it,\" he replied coldly.  \"And I advise you not to judge me,"
164 "sir.  Fate has shown you what you ought not to have seen.  The attack"
164 "has begun; go down.\""
164 ""
164 "\"What is this vessel?\""
164 ""
164 "\"You do not know?  Very well! so much the better!  Its nationality to"
164 "you, at least, will be a secret.  Go down!\""
164 ""
164 "We could but obey.  About fifteen of the sailors surrounded the"
164 "Captain, looking with implacable hatred at the vessel nearing them."
164 "One could feel that the same desire of vengeance animated every soul."
164 "I went down at the moment another projectile struck the Nautilus, and I"
164 "heard the Captain exclaim:"
164 ""
164 "\"Strike, mad vessel!  Shower your useless shot!  And then, you will not"
164 "escape the spur of the Nautilus.  But it is not here that you shall"
164 "perish!  I would not have your ruins mingle with those of the Avenger!\""
164 ""
164 "I reached my room.  The Captain and his second had remained on the"
164 "platform.  The screw was set in motion, and the Nautilus, moving with"
164 "speed, was soon beyond the reach of the ship's guns.  But the pursuit"
164 "continued, and Captain Nemo contented himself with keeping his distance."
164 ""
